# Abstract

Let $s$ be the sphere spectrum. We construct an associative, commutative, and unital smash product in a complete and cocomplete category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ of $_ s$ -modules" whose derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ is equivalent to the classical stable homotopy category. This allows a simple and algebraically manageable definition of $^ { \mathfrak { s } }$ algebras" and "commutative $S .$ algebras" in terms of associative, or associative and commutative, products $R \land s R \longrightarrow R$ These notions are essentially equivalent to the earlier notions of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, and the older notions feed naturally into the new framework to provide plentiful examples. There is an equally simple definition of $R$ -modules in terms of maps $R \land _ { S } M \longrightarrow M$ When $\scriptstyle { R }$ is commutative, the category $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules also has an associative, commutative, and unital smash product, and its derived category ${ \mathcal { D } } _ { R }$ has properties just like the stable homotopy category.

Working in the derived category ${ \mathcal { D } } _ { R }$ , we construct spectral sequences that specialize to give generalized universal coefficient and Künneth spectral sequences. Classical torsion products and $\mathbf { E x t }$ groups are obtained by specializing our constructions to Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra and passing to homotopy groups, and the derived category of a discrete ring $\scriptstyle { R }$ is equivalent to the derived category of its associated Eilenberg-Mac Lane $S \mathrm { . }$ algebra.

We also develop a homotopical theory of $\scriptstyle { R }$ ring spectra in ${ \mathcal { D } } _ { R }$ , analogous to the classical theory of ring spectra in the stable homotopy category, and we use it to give new constructions as $_ { M U }$ -ring spectra of a host of fundamentally important spectra whose earlier constructions were both more difficult and less precise.

Working in the module category $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ , we show that the category of finite cell modules over an $_ { s }$ algebra $\boldsymbol { R }$ gives rise to an associated algebraic $\kappa$ -theory spectrum $K R$ Specialized to the Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra of discrete rings, this recovers Quillen's algebraic $\kappa$ -theory of rings. Specialized to suspension spectra $\Sigma ^ { \infty } ( \Omega X ) _ { + }$ of loop spaces, it recovers Waldhausen's algebraic $\kappa$ -theory of spaces.

Replacing our ground ring $s$ by a commutative $S \mathrm { . }$ algebra $R$ , we define $\boldsymbol { R }$ algebras and commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras in terms of maps $A \land _ { R } A \longrightarrow A$ , and we show that the categories of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules, $R$ -algebras, and commutative $\scriptstyle R$ -algebras are all topological model categories. We use the model structures to study Bousfield localizations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules and $R _ { ☉ }$ algebras. In particular, we prove that $\kappa o$ and $\kappa U$ are commutative $k o$ and $k u \cdot$ algebras and therefore commutative $S .$ algebras.

We define the topological Hochschild homology $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $T H H ^ { R } ( A ; M )$ of $A$ with coefficients in an $( A , A )$ bimodule $M$ and give spectral sequences for the calculation of its homotopy and homology groups. Again, classical Hochschild homology and cohomology groups are obtained by specializing the constructions to Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra and passing to homotopy groups.

# Introduction

The last thirty years have seen the importation of more and more algebraic techniques into stable homotopy theory. Throughout this period, most work in stable homotopy theory has taken place in Boardman's stable homotopy category [6], or in Adams' variant of it [2], or, more recently, in Lewis and May's variant [38]. That category is analogous to the derived category obtained from the category of chain complexes over a commutative ring $k$ by inverting the quasiisomorphisms. The sphere spectrum $s$ plays the role of $\pmb { k }$ , the smash product ^ plays the role of the tensor product, and weak equivalences play the role of quasi-isomorphisms. A fundamental difference between the two situations is that the smash product on the underlying category of spectra is not associative and commutative, whereas the tensor product between chain complexes of $k$ -modules is associative and commutative. For this reason, topologists generally work with rings and modules in the stable homotopy category, with their products and actions defined only up to homotopy. In contrast, of course, algebraists generally work with differential graded $k \mathrm { . }$ -algebras that have associative point-set level multiplications.

We here introduce a new approach to stable homotopy theory that allows one to do point-set level algebra. We construct a new category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ of $_ { s }$ -modules that has an associative, commutative, and unital smash product $\wedge _ { S }$ . Its derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ is obtained by inverting the weak equivalences; $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ is equivalent to the classical stable homotopy category, and the equivalence preserves smash products. This allows us to rethink all of stable homotopy theory: all previous work in the subject might as well have been done in $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ . Working on the pointset level, in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ , we define an $S .$ -algebra to be an $S .$ -module $\scriptstyle { R }$ with an associative and unital product $R \land _ { S } R \longrightarrow R$ ; if the product is also commutative, we call $\mathscr { R }$ a commutative $S _ { \ l }$ -algebra. Although the definitions are now very simple, these are not new notions: they are refinements of the $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra that were introduced over twenty years ago by May, Quinn, and Ray [48]. In general, the latter need not satisfy the precise unital property that is enjoyed by our new $S .$ -algebras, but it is a simple matter to construct a weakly equivalent $S .$ algebra from an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum and a weakly equivalent commutative $S .$ algebra from an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum.

It is tempting to refer to (commutative) $S .$ -algebras as (commutative) ring spectra. However, this would introduce confusion since the term "ring spectrum" has had a definite meaning for thirty years as a stable homotopy category level notion. Ring spectra in the classical homotopical sense are not rendered obsolete by our theory since there are many examples that admit no $S .$ algebra structure. In any case, the term $S .$ -algebra more accurately describes our new concept. With our theory, and the new possibilities that it opens up, it becomes vitally important to keep track of when one is working on the point-set level and when one is working up to homotopy. In the absence (or ignorance) of a good point-set level category of spectra, topologists have tended to be sloppy about this. The dichotomy will run through our work. The terms "ring spectrum" and "module spectrum" will always refer to the classical homotopical notions. The terms $^ { 6 6 } S .$ . algebra" and $S .$ -module" will always refer to the strict point-set level notions.

We define a (left) module $M$ over an $S .$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ to be an $S ^ { }$ -module $M$ with an action $R \land _ { S } M \longrightarrow M$ such that the standard diagrams commute. We obtain a category $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ of (left) $R$ -modules and a derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ . There is a smash product $M \wedge _ { R } N$ of a right $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ and a left $\boldsymbol { R }$ -module $N$ , which is an $S .$ . module. For left $R$ -modules $M$ and $N$ , there is a function $S .$ -module $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ that enjoys properties just like modules of homomorphisms in algebra. Each $F _ { R } ( M , M )$ is an $S$ -algebra. If $\scriptstyle { R }$ is commutative, then $M \wedge _ { R } N$ and $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ are $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, and in this case $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ enjoy all of the properties of ${ \mathcal { M } } _ { S }$ and $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ . Thus each commutative $S \mathrm { . }$ algebra $R$ determines a derived category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules that has all of the structure that the stable homotopy category has. These new categories are of substantial intrinsic interest, and they give powerful new tools for the investigation of the classical stable homotopy category.

When we restrict to Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra, our topological theory subsumes a good deal of classical homological algebra. For a discrete ring $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $M$ and $N$ , we have

$$
\operatorname { T o r } _ { n } ^ { R } ( M , N ) \cong \pi _ { n } ( H M \wedge _ { H R } H N ) \quad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \quad \operatorname { E x t } _ { R } ^ { n } ( M , N ) \cong \pi _ { - n } F _ { H R } ( H M , H N ) .
$$

Here $\wedge _ { R }$ and $F _ { R }$ must be interpreted in the derived category; that is, $\mathbf { \nabla } _ { H M }$ must be a CW $H R$ -module. Moreover, the algebraic derived category ${ \mathcal { D } } _ { R }$ is equivalent to the topological derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { H R }$ .

In general, for an $\pmb { S }$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ , approximation of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules $M$ by weakly equivalent cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules is roughly analogous to forming projective resolutions in algebra. There is a much more precise analogy that involves developing the derived categories of modules over rings or, more generally, DGA's in terms of cell modules. It is presented in [35], which gives an algebraic theory of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ $k$ -algebras that closely parallels the present topological theory.

When we restrict to the sphere spectrum $_ { s }$ , the derived smash products $M \land _ { S } N$ and function spectra $F _ { S } ( M , N )$ have as their homotopy groups the homology and cohomology groups $N _ { * } ( M )$ and $N ^ { * } ( M )$ . This suggests the alternative notations

$$
\mathrm { T o r } _ { n } ^ { R } ( M , N ) = \pi _ { n } ( M \wedge _ { R } N ) = N _ { n } ^ { R } ( M )
$$

and

$$
\operatorname { E x t } _ { R } ^ { n } ( M , N ) = \pi _ { - n } F _ { R } ( M , N ) = N _ { R } ^ { n } ( M )
$$

for $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules $M$ and $N$ When $R$ is connective, there are ordinary homology and cohomology theories on $R$ -modules, represented by Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra that are $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, and there are Atiyah-Hirzebruch spectral sequences for the computation of generalized homology and cohomology theories on $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules.

The realization of algebraic Tor and Ext groups via Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra generalizes to spectral sequences

$$
E _ { p , q } ^ { 2 } = \mathrm { T o r } _ { p , q } ^ { R _ { * } } ( M _ { * } , N _ { * } ) \Longrightarrow \mathrm { T o r } _ { p + q } ^ { R } ( M , N )
$$

and

$$
E _ { 2 } ^ { p , q } = { \mathrm { E x t } } _ { R ^ { * } } ^ { p , q } ( M ^ { * } , N ^ { * } ) \Longrightarrow { \mathrm { E x t } } _ { R } ^ { p + q } ( M , N ) .
$$

These specialize to give Künneth and universal coefficient spectral sequences in classical generalized homology and cohomology theories. There are also Eilenberg-Moore type spectral sequences for the calculation of $E _ { * } ( M \land _ { R } N )$ under appropriate hypotheses on $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $E$ .

Thinking of $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ as a new stable homotopy category, where $\scriptstyle { R }$ is a commutative $\pmb { S } .$ -algebra, we can realize the action of an element $\boldsymbol { x } \in R _ { n }$ on an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ as a map of $R$ -modules $x : \Sigma ^ { n } M \longrightarrow M$ . We define $M / x M$ to be the cofiber of $_ x$ , and we define the localization $M [ x ^ { - 1 } ]$ to be the telescope of a countable iterate of desuspensions of $_ x$ , starting with $M \longrightarrow \Sigma ^ { - n } M$ By iteration, we can construct quotients by sequences of elements and localizations at sequences of elements. We define $R$ -ring spectra, associative $R$ -ring spectra, and commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectra in the homotopical sense, with products $A \land _ { R } A \longrightarrow A$ defined via maps in the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ , and it turns out to be quite simple to study when quotients and localizations of $R$ -ring spectra are again $R$ -ring spectra.

When we take $R = M U$ , we find easy direct constructions as $M U .$ modules of all of the various spectra $( M U / X ) [ Y ^ { - 1 } ]$ that are usually obtained by means of the Baas-Sullivan theory of manifolds with singularities or the Landweber exact functor theorem. When their homotopy groups are integral domains concentrated in degrees congruent to zero mod 4, these $M U$ -modules all admit canonical structures of associative and commutative $M U .$ -ring spectra. Remarkably, it is far simpler to prove the sharper statements that apply in the derived category of $M U$ -modules than the much weaker stable homotopy category level analogs that were obtainable before our theory.

Thinking of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ as a new category of point-set level modules, where $R$ is again a commutative $S .$ -algebra, we can define $R \mathrm { . }$ algebras $A$ via point-set level products $A \land _ { R } A \longrightarrow A$ such that the appropriate diagrams commute. For example, $F _ { R } ( M , M )$ is an $R$ -algebra for any $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ These have all of the good formal properties of $S _ { \ l }$ -algebras. We repeat the dichotomy for emphasis: The terms $^ { 6 6 } R$ -ring spectrum" and $^ { * } R$ -module spectrum" will always refer to the homotopical notions defined in the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ . The terms $^ { 6 6 }$ algebra" and ${ } ^ { 6 6 }$ -module" will always refer to the strict, point-set, level notions.

We shall construct Bousfield localizations of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules at a given $R .$ -module $E$ . In principle, this is a derived category notion, but we shall obtain precise point-set level constructions. Using different point-set level constructions, we shall prove that the Bousfield localizations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras can be constructed to be $R$ -algebras and the Bousfield localizations of commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras can be constructed to be commutative $\scriptstyle { R } \quad$ -algebras. In particular, the localization $R _ { E }$ of $\scriptstyle { R }$ at $\mathcal { E }$ is a commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra, and we shall see that the category of $R _ { E } .$ . modules plays an intrinsically central role in the study of Bousfield localizations.

As a very special case, this theory will imply that the spectra $K O$ and $K U$ that represent real and complex periodic $\kappa$ -theory can be constructed as commutative algebras over the $S .$ algebras $k o$ and $k u$ that represent real and complex connective $\kappa$ -theory. Therefore $K O$ and $K U$ are commutative $S .$ -algebras, as had long been conjectured in the earlier context of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra. Again, it is far simpler to prove the sharper $k o$ and $k u \cdot$ -algebra statements than to construct $S .$ -algebra structures directly.

For an $R$ algebra $A$ , we define the enveloping $R$ algebra $A ^ { e } = A \land _ { R } A ^ { o p }$ , and we define the topological Hochschild homology of $A$ with coefficients in an $( A , A )$ -bimodule $M$ to be the derived smash product

$$
{ \cal T } H H ^ { R } ( A ; M ) = M \wedge _ { A ^ { e } } A .
$$

This is the correct generalization from algebra to topology since, if $\scriptstyle { R }$ is a discrete commutative ring and $A$ is an $R$ -algebra that is flat as an $R$ -module, then the algebraic and topological Hochschild homology are isomorphic:

$$
H H _ { n } ^ { R } ( A ; M ) \equiv \mathrm { T o r } _ { n } ^ { A ^ { \bullet } } ( M , A ) \cong \mathrm { T o r } _ { n } ^ { H A ^ { \bullet } } ( H M , H A ) \equiv \pi _ { n } ( T H H ^ { R } ( A ; M ) ) .
$$

In general, for a commutative $S \mathrm { . }$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ , an $R \mathrm { . }$ algebra $A$ , and an $( A , A )$ bimodule $M$ , there is a spectral sequence

$$
E _ { p , q } ^ { 2 } = H H _ { p , q } ^ { R _ { * } } ( M _ { * } , A _ { * } ) \Longrightarrow \pi _ { p + q } ( T H H ^ { R } ( A ; M ) )
$$

under suitable flatness hypotheses. More generally, there are similar spectral sequences converging to $E _ { * } ( T H H ^ { R } ( A ; M ) )$ for a commutative ring spectrum $\pmb { { \cal E } }$

There is also a point-set level version $t h h ^ { R } ( A ; M )$ of topological Hochschild homology. It is obtained by mimicking topologically the standard complex for the calculation of algebraic Hochschild homology. When $M = A$ ,this construction has particularly nice formal properties, as was observed in [54] and as we shall explain: it is isomorphic to the tensor $A \otimes S ^ { 1 }$ . A key technical point is that the derived category and point-set level definitions become equivalent after replacing $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $A$ by suitable weakly equivalent approximations.

Our $S _ { \ l }$ -algebras and their modules are enough like ordinary rings and modules that we can construct the algebraic $\kappa$ -theory spectrum $K R$ associated to an $S .$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ by applying Waldhausen's $s$ -construction to the category of finite cell $R _ { ☉ }$ -modules. Applied to the Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectrum $H R$ of a discrete ring $\scriptstyle { R }$ , this gives a new construction of Quillen's algebraic $\kappa$ -theory. Applied to the suspension spectrum $\Sigma ^ { \infty } ( \Omega X ) _ { + }$ , this gives a new construction of Waldhausen's algebraic $K .$ -theory of the space $X$ . The resulting common framework for topological Hochschild homology and Quillen and Waldhausen algebraic $K \cdot$ . theory opens up several new directions and appears to bring a number of standing conjectures within reach. We merely lay the foundations here.

The technical heart of our theory is the problem of keeping our formal pointset level constructions under homotopical control. While we shall show by essentially formal categorical arguments that our various categories of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, $R$ -algebras, and commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras are cocomplete and complete, tensored and cotensored, topological model categories, this formal structure does not in itself address the problem: forgetful functors from more to less structured spectra rarely preserve cofibrant objects, and may well not do so even up to homotopy type. The problem requires deeper analysis, and a crucial aspect of our work is that our discussion of model categories gives sufficient control on the underlying homotopy types of cofibrant $R \mathrm { . }$ -algebras and cofibrant commutative $R \mathrm { . }$ algebras to allow the calculational use of bar constructions and topological Hochschild homology complexes. This is also crucial to our proof that Bousfield localizations of $R \mathrm { . }$ -algebras can be constructed as $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras.

Another tool in keeping homotopical control is the category of "tame" spectra. It is an intermediate category between the ground category of spectra, which is well-designed for formal point-set level work but not for homotopical analysis, and the category of CW spectra, which is well-designed for homotopical analysis but not for formal work. The homotopy category of tame spectra is symmetric monoidal under the smash product, and we can approximate any structured spectrum by a weakly equivalent tame structured spectrum by means of a "cylinder construction" defined using homotopy colimits. Actually, this tool will only be needed in Chapter I, since the smash product of $S _ { \ l }$ -modules turns out to better behaved under weak equivalences than the smash product of spectra.

The basic construction underlying all of our work is the "twisted half-smash product" $A \ltimes E$ of a suitable space $A$ and a spectrum $E$ This construction is defined with respect to a given map $_ \alpha$ from $A$ to an appropriate space of linear isometries. After the penultimate draft of this book was completed, Michael Cole came up with a new construction of twisted half-smash products, one that is much easier to understand than the original construction of Lewis and May and that allows much simpler proofs of some of our main technical results. In particular, we proved and Cole reproved that a homotopy equivalence $A ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow A$ , with homotopy inverse unrelated to $\alpha$ , induces a homotopy equivalence $A ^ { \prime } \bowtie$ $E \longrightarrow A \ltimes E$ when $E$ is tame. This invariance statement is the technical lynchpin of our theory. We have discarded our original proof in favor of Cole's, and he will present his new treatment of twisted half-smash products in the Appendix of this book.

The construction of thh, of bar constructions needed in our work, and of functorial homotopy colimits of spectra all require geometric realizations of simplicial spectra. This raises another technical problem. To understand geometric realization homotopically, the given simplicial spectra must satisfy certain cofibration conditions, and it is hard to verify that a map of spectra is a cofibration (satisfies the homotopy extension property). The solution to this problem is basic to the homotopical understanding of cofibrant $\scriptstyle R$ -algebras and commutative $R$ algebras.

The reader interested in using our theory need not be concerned with these matters, and most of the technical proofs are deferred until the last few chapters and the appendix. The first three chapters explain the foundations needed for the applications of the next three, which are independent of one another. Chapter VII explains the foundations needed for Chapters VIII and IX, which are independent of each other. Each chapter has its own brief introduction. References within a chapter are of the form "Lemma $3 . 4 ^ { \mathfrak { n } }$ ; references to results in other chapters are of the form $^ { 6 } \mathrm { I } . 3 . 4 ^ { 5 }$ or, in the case of the Appendix, A.3.4.

Our work is not independent of earlier work: the groundwork was laid in [38], and all of our ring, module, and algebra spectra are spectra in the sense of Lewis and May with additional structure. In view of Cole's new treatment of twisted half-smash products, only the first 75 pages or so of [38] are relevant, and we have given much more readable expositions of this background material in [23] and [52]. In [38], the focus was on equivariant stable homotopy theory, the study of spectra with actions by compact Lie groups $G$ We have chosen to write this book nonequivariantly in the interests of readability. However, we have kept a close eye on the equivariant generalization, and we have been careful to use only arguments that directly generalize to the equivariant setting. We state a metatheorem.

THEOREM O.1. All of the definitions and all of the general theory in this paper apply to $G$ -spectra for any compact Lie group $G$ .

This has been used by Greenlees and May [28] to prove a completion theorem for the calculation of $M _ { * } ( B G )$ and $M ^ { * } ( B G )$ for any $M U$ -module spectrum $M$ . Some of that work, together with some of ours, was described in the announcement [22]. The recent series of expository papers [23, 26, 27] gives a descriptive account of some of the present theory and its equivariant applications, including both equivariant and non-equivariant applications of the theory to localizations and completions of $R$ -modules at ideals in $\pi _ { * } ( R )$ . The survey volume [52], which is a companion volume to this one, gives a more leisurely and thorough expository account, together with full details of the definitional framework in the equivariant setting.

We warn the knowledgeable reader that this material has undergone several major revisions, and the final definitions and terminology are not those of earlier announcements and drafts. In particular, our $S .$ -modules enjoy a unital property that was not imposed on the $S \mathrm { . }$ -modules, here called $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, of the earlier versions written by Elmendorf, Kriz, and May alone. The fact that one can impose this unital property and still retain homotopical control is one of many new insights contributed by Mandell. This substantially sharpens and simplifies the theory. Paradoxically, however, one cannot impose such a unital property in the parallel algebraic theory of [35]. Therefore, to facilitate a comparison of the algebraic and topological theories, we run through a little of the previous variant of our theory in the last chapter.

The chapter on algebraic $K$ -theory has not been previously announced and is entirely work of Mandell; it is part of his Chicago PhD thesis in preparation. Similarly, the Appendix is entirely work of Cole and is part of his Chicago PhD thesis in preparation.

Two other Chicago students deserve thanks. Maria Basterra has carefully read several drafts and caught numerous soft spots of exposition; her Chicago PhD thesis in preparation will give a thorough treatment of the André-Quillen cohomology of commutative $S$ -algebras. Jerome Wolbert has made many helpful comments, and his Chicago PhD thesis in preparation will analyze the new derived categories associated to the various $K .$ -theory spectra.

# INTRODUCTION

It is a pleasure to thank Mike Hopkins, Gaunce Lewis, and Jim McClure for many helpful conversations and e-mails. We owe a critical lemma, namely I.5.4, to Hopkins [32]. Although trivial to prove, it broke a psychological barrier and played a pivotal role in our thinking. We should acknowledge the pioneering work on $A _ { \infty }$ ring and module spectra of Alan Robinson, which gave precursors of many of the results of Chapter IV. We learned the material of $\mathbf { I X \ S 3 }$ , on thh, from the application of our theory given in the paper [54] of McClure, Schwänzl, and Vogt. We learned many of the results on cocomplete and complete, tensored and cotensored, topological model categories in $\mathrm { I I } \ S 7$ and VII§§2,4 from Hopkins and McClure. Their foresight and insight have been inspirational.

# CHAPTER I

# Prologue: the category of L-spectra

In this prologue, we construct a category whose existence was previously thought to be impossible by at least two of the authors: a complete and cocomplete category of spectra, namely the I-spectra, with an associative and commutative smash product. This contrasts with the category constructed by Lewis and the fourth author in [48, 38], whose smash product is neither associative nor commutative (before passage to homotopy categories), and with the category constructed by the first author in [20], which is neither complete nor cocomplete. We will also give a function L-spectrum construction that is right adjoint to the new smash product. The category of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra has all of the properties that we desire except that its smash product, denoted by $\land _ { \mathcal { L } }$ , is not unital. It has a natural unit map $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ , which is often an isomorphism and always a weak equivalence.

The curtain will rise on our real focus of interest in the next chapter, where we will define an $\pmb { S }$ -module to be an L-spectrum $M$ such that $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ is an isomorphism. Restricting $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ to $S .$ -modules and renaming it $\wedge _ { S }$ , this will give us a symmetric monoidal category in which to develop stable topological algebra.

# 1. Background on spectra and the stable homotopy category

We begin by recalling the basic definitions in Lewis and May's approach to the stable category. We first recall the definition of a coordinate-free spectrum; see [38, I§2], [20, §2], or [52, Ch.XII] for further details. A coordinate-free spectrum is a spectrum that takes as its indexing set, instead of the integers, the set of finite dimensional subspaces of a "universe", namely a real inner product space $U \cong \mathbb { R } ^ { \infty }$ .Thus, a spectrum $\mathcal { E }$ assigns a based space $E V$ to each finite dimensional subspace $V$ of $\boldsymbol { U }$ , with (adjoint) structure maps

$$
\tilde { \sigma } _ { V , W } : E V { \stackrel { \cong } { \longrightarrow } } \Omega ^ { W - V } E W
$$

when $V \subset W$ .Here $W - V$ is the orthogonal complement of $V$ in $W$ and $\Omega ^ { W } X$ is the space of based maps $F ( S ^ { W } , X )$ , where $S ^ { W }$ is the one-point compactification of $W$ . These maps are required to be homeomorphisms and to satisfy an evident

associativity relation. A map of spectra $f : E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ is a collection of maps of based spaces $f _ { V } : E V \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } V$ for which each of the following diagrams commutes:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { E V \xrightarrow { \quad f _ { V } \quad } } & { { } E ^ { \prime } V } \\ { \tilde { \sigma } _ { V , W } \Bigg \downarrow \quad } & { { } \quad \downarrow \tilde { \sigma } _ { V , W } ^ { \prime } } \\ { \Omega ^ { W - V } E W \xrightarrow { \Omega ^ { W - V } f _ { W } } \xrightarrow { \quad \Omega ^ { W - V } E ^ { \prime } W . } \Omega ^ { W - V } E ^ { \prime } W . } \end{array}
$$

We obtain the category $\mathcal { S } U$ of spectra indexed on $\boldsymbol { U }$ . If we drop the requirement that the maps ${ \tilde { \sigma } } _ { V , W }$ be homeomorphisms, we obtain the notion of a prespectrum and the category $\mathcal { P } U$ of prespectra. The forgetful functor $\mathcal { S } U \longrightarrow \mathcal { P } U$ has a left adjoint $L$ , details of which are given in [38, App]. Functors on prespectra that do not preserve spectra are extended to spectra by applying the functor $\boldsymbol { L }$ . For example, for a based space $X$ and a prespectrum $\boldsymbol { E }$ , we have the prespectrum $E \wedge X$ specified by $( E \wedge X ) ( V ) = E V \wedge X$ When $\boldsymbol { \varepsilon }$ is a spectrum, the structure maps for this prespectrum level smash product are not homeomorphisms, and we understand the smash product $E \wedge X$ to be the spectrum $L ( E \land X )$ . For example, $\Sigma E = E \wedge S ^ { 1 }$ . Function spectra are easier. We set $F ( X , E ) ( V ) = F ( X , E V )$ and find that this functor on prespectra preserves spectra. For example, $\Omega E =$ $F ( S ^ { 1 } , E )$ . The following result is discussed in [38, p.13].

PROPOsITION 1.1. The category $\mathcal { S } U$ is complete and cocomplete.

Proor. Limits and colimits are computed on prespectra spacewise. Limits preserve spectra, and colimits of spectra are obtained by use of the functor $L$ .

A homotopy in the category of spectra is a map $E \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ , and we let $h \mathcal { S } U$ denote the homotopy category of spectra; its objects are spectra and its morphisms are homotopy classes of maps between them. We have cofiber and fiber sequences that behave exactly as in the category of spaces. The cofiber $C f$ of a map $f : E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ of spectra is the pushout $E ^ { \prime } \cup _ { f } C E$ ,where $C E = E \wedge I$ . A cofibration of spectra is a map $i : E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ that satisfies the homotopy extension property (HEP: a homotopy $h : E \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow F$ of a restriction of a map $f : E ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow F$ extends to a homotopy $\tilde { h } : E ^ { \prime } { \wedge } I _ { + } \longrightarrow F$ of $f$ ). The canonical maps $E \longrightarrow C E$ and $E ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow C f$ are examples. The fiber $F f$ of a map $f : E ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow E$ is the pullback $E ^ { \prime } \times _ { f } P E$ ,where $P E = F ( I , E )$ .A fibration of spectra is a map $p : E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ that satisfies the covering homotopy property (CHP: a homotopy $h : F \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ of a projection $p \circ f$ , $f : F \longrightarrow E$ , is covered by a homotopy $\tilde { h } : F \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow E$ of $f$ . The canonical maps $P E \longrightarrow E$ and $F f \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ are examples.

A map $f$ of spectra is a weak equivalence if each of its component maps $f _ { V }$ is a weak equivalence of spaces. The stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } U$ is constructed from the homotopy category $h \mathcal { S } U$ by adjoining formal inverses to the weak equivalences, a process that is made rigorous by CW approximation.

The Vth space functor from spectra to spaces has a left adjoint that we shall denote by $\Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty }$ , or $\Sigma _ { n } ^ { \infty }$ when $V = \mathbb { R } ^ { n }$ [38, I§4]. Its definition will be recalled in X.4.5. When $V = \{ 0 \}$ , this is the suspension spectrum functor $\Sigma ^ { \infty }$ For $n \geq 0$ , the sphere spectrum $S ^ { n }$ is the suspension spectrum ${ \boldsymbol { \Sigma } } ^ { \infty } S ^ { n }$ of the sphere space $S ^ { n }$ .For $n > 0$ , the sphere spectrum $S ^ { - n }$ is $\Sigma _ { n } ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 }$ . There are canonical isomorphisms $\Sigma ^ { m } S ^ { n } \cong S ^ { m + n }$ for $m \geq 0$ and integers $_ n$ and there are canonical isomorphisms $\Sigma _ { m } ^ { \infty } S ^ { n } \cong S ^ { n - m }$ for $m \geq 0$ and $n \geq 0$ Sphere spectra are used to define the homotopy groups of spectra, $\pi _ { n } ( E ) = h { \mathcal { S } } U ( S ^ { n } , E )$ , and a map of spectra is a weak equivalence if and only if it induces an isomorphism of spectrum-level homotopy groups.

Although we shall not introduce different notations for space level and spectrum level spheres, we shall generally write $S$ for the zero sphere spectrum, reserving the notation $S ^ { 0 }$ for the two-point space.

The theory of cell and CW spectra is developed by taking sphere spectra as the domains of attaching maps [38, 1§5]. The stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } U$ is equivalent to the homotopy category of CW spectra. It is important to remember that homotopy-preserving functors on spectra that do not preserve weak equivalences are transported to the stable category by first replacing their variables by weakly equivalent CW spectra.

# 2. External smash products and twisted half-smash products

The construction of our new smash product will start from the external smash product of spectra. This is an associative and commutative pairing

$$
\mathcal { S } U \times \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } ( U \oplus U ^ { \prime } )
$$

for any pair of universes $U$ and $U ^ { \prime }$ . It is constructed by starting with the prespectrum level definition

$$
( E \wedge E ^ { \prime } ) ( V \oplus V ^ { \prime } ) = E V \wedge E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } .
$$

The structure maps fail to be homeomorphisms when $E$ and $E ^ { \prime }$ are spectra, and we apply the spectrification functor $L$ to obtain the desired spectrum level smash product. This external smash product is the one used in [20].

There is an associated function spectrum functor

$$
F : ( \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ) ^ { o p } \times \mathcal { S } ( U \oplus U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U
$$

and an adjunction

$$
\mathcal { S } ( U \oplus U ^ { \prime } ) ( E \wedge E ^ { \prime } , E ^ { \prime \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { S } U ( E , F ( E ^ { \prime } , E ^ { \prime \prime } ) )
$$

for $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ , and $E ^ { \prime \prime } \in \mathcal { S } ( U \oplus U ^ { \prime } )$ ;see [38, p. 69].

Now let $\mathcal { I }$ denote the category whose objects are universes $U$ and whose morphisms are linear isometries. Universes are topologized as the unions of their finite dimensional subspaces, and the set $\pmb { \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ of linear isometries $U \longrightarrow U ^ { \prime }$ is given the function space topology; it is a contractible space [38, II.1.5]. The category $\mathcal { S }$ constructed in [20] augments to the category $\mathcal { I }$ Since $\mathcal { I }$ fails to have limits and colimits (it even fails to have coproducts), $\mathcal { S }$ suffers from the same defects.

In order to obtain smash products internal to a single universe $U$ , we shall exploit the "twisted half-smash product". The input data for this functor consist of two universes $U$ and $U ^ { \prime }$ (which may be the same), an unbased space $A$ with a given structure map $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathcal { S } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ , and a spectrum $E$ indexed on $U$ . The output is the spectrum $A \ltimes E$ , which is indexed on $U ^ { \prime }$ . It must be remembered that the construction depends on $\alpha$ and not just on $A$ When $A$ has CW homotopy type, different choices of $\alpha$ lead to equivalent functors on the level of stable categories, by A.7.6. The intuition is that the twisted half-smash product is a generalization to spectra of the "untwisted" functor $A _ { + } \land X$ on based spaces $X$ . This intuition is made precise by the following "untwisting formula" that relates twisted half-smash products and suspension spectra. A proof will be given in A.5.5, where the result will be generalized to shift desuspension functors $\Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty }$ .

PRoposiTIoN 2.1. For any map $A \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ and any $n \geq 0$ , there is an isomorphism of spectra

$$
A \ltimes \Sigma ^ { \infty } X \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( A _ { + } \land X )
$$

that is natural in spaces $A$ over ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ and based spaces $X$ .

Observe that the functor $\Sigma ^ { \infty }$ implicitly refers to the universe $U$ on the left and to the universe $U ^ { \prime }$ on the right. The twisted half-smash product enjoys the following formal properties, among others; their analogs for the space level functor $A _ { + } \land X$ are trivial. Proofs will be given in A.5.3 and $\mathtt { A } \ S 6$ .

ProposITIoN 2.2. The following statements hold.

(i) There is a canonical isomorphism $\{ { \mathrm { i d } } _ { U } \} \ltimes E \cong E$ .   
ii Let $A \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ and $B \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime \prime } )$ be given; let $B \times A$ have the structure map given by the composite

$$
B \times A \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime \prime } ) \times \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } ) \xrightarrow { \circ } \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime \prime } ) .
$$

Then there is a canonical isomorphism

$$
( B \times A ) \ltimes E \cong B \ltimes ( A \ltimes E ) .
$$

(iii) Let $A \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U _ { 1 } , U _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } )$ and $B \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathcal { S } } ( U _ { 2 } , U _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } )$ be given; let $A \times B$ have the structure map given by the composite

$$
A \times B \longrightarrow { \mathcal { I } } ( U _ { 1 } , U _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) \times { \mathcal { I } } ( U _ { 2 } , U _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } ) \xrightarrow { \oplus } { \mathcal { I } } ( U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } , U _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \oplus U _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

Let $E _ { 1 }$ and $E _ { 2 }$ be spectra indexed on $U _ { 1 }$ and $U _ { 2 }$ respectively. Then there is a canonical isomorphism

$$
( A \times B ) \ltimes ( E _ { 1 } \land E _ { 2 } ) \cong ( A \ltimes E _ { 1 } ) \land ( B \ltimes E _ { 2 } ) .
$$

iv) For $A \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ , $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , and a based space $X$ , there is a canonical isomorphism

$$
A \bowtie ( E \wedge X ) \cong ( A \ltimes E ) \wedge X .
$$

The functor $A \ltimes ( - \ l )$ is a left adjoint. Its right adjoint will be used in our construction of function $S$ -modules.

ProposiTIoN 2.3. For any space $A$ over ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ , the functor $A \ltimes ( - \ l )$ has $^ a$ right adjoint, which is denoted by $F [ A , - )$ and called a twisted function spectrum.

The functor $A \ltimes E$ is homotopy-preserving in $E$ , and it therefore preserves homotopy equivalences in the variable $\boldsymbol { \varepsilon }$ However, it only preserves homotopies over ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ in $A$ Nevertheless, it very often preserves homotopy equivalences in the variable $A$ . This fact will be essential in keeping control over the homotopical behavior of our point-set level constructions. To state it in proper generality, we need the following notion of a well-behaved spectrum.

DEFInITION 2.4. A prespectrum $D$ is $\Sigma$ -cofibrant if each of its structure maps $\sigma : \Sigma ^ { W } D V \longrightarrow D ( V \oplus W )$ is a cofibration of based spaces. A spectrum $E$ is $\Sigma$ -cofibrant if it is isomorphic to one of the form $\boldsymbol { L D }$ , where $D$ is a $\Sigma$ -cofibrant prespectrum. A spectrum $E$ is tame if it is homotopy equivalent to a $\Sigma \cdot$ -cofibrant spectrum.

We shall discuss such spectra in $\mathbf { X \ S 4 }$ , where we shall see that all shift desuspensions of based spaces are $\Sigma$ -cofibrant and that all CW spectra are tame. It follows that any spectrum is weakly equivalent to a tame one. We shall show in $\mathbf { X \ S 5 }$ that structured ring or module spectra can be approximated functorially by weakly equivalent $\Sigma$ -cofibrant spectra with the same structure. The following result will be restated and proven as A.7.4. It is central to our theory.

THEOREM 2.5. Let $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ be tame and let $A$ be a space over $\mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ . If $\phi : A ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow A$ is a homotopy equivalence, then $\phi \ltimes \mathrm { i d } : A ^ { \prime } \ltimes E \longrightarrow A \ltimes E$ is $^ a$ homotopy equivalence.

Here, for a general spectrum $E$ , we do not know whether or not $\phi \ltimes \mathrm { i d }$ is even a weak equivalence. By A.7.3, the result has the following consequence.

COROLLARY 2.6. Let $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ be a spectrum that has the homotopy type of a CW spectrum and let $A$ be a space over ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ that has the homotopy type of a CW complex. Then $A \ltimes E$ has the homotopy type of a CW spectrum.

# 3. The linear isometries operad and internal smash products

For the rest of the paper, we restrict attention to a particular universe $U$ ; the reader is welcome to consider it as notation for $\mathbb { R } ^ { \infty }$ . We agree to write $\mathcal { S }$ instead of $\mathcal { S } U$ for the category of spectra indexed on $U$ . Except where explicitly stated otherwise, all given spectra, whatever extra structure they may have, will be in $\mathcal { S }$ . We are especially interested in twisted half smash products defined in terms of the following spaces of linear isometries.

NOTatIonS 3.1. Let $U ^ { j }$ be the direct sum of $j$ copies of $U$ and let $\mathcal { L } ( j ) =$ ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U ^ { j } , U )$ . The space $\mathcal { L } ( 0 )$ is the point $i$ ,where $i : \{ 0 \} \longrightarrow U$ ,and $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ contains the identity map $1 = \operatorname { i d } _ { U } : U \longrightarrow U$ The left action of $\Sigma _ { j }$ on $U ^ { j }$ by permutations induces a free right action of $\Sigma _ { j }$ on the contractible space $\mathcal { L } ( j )$ . Define maps

$$
\gamma : \mathcal { L } ( k ) \times \mathcal { L } ( j _ { 1 } ) \times \cdots \times \mathcal { L } ( j _ { k } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( j _ { 1 } + \cdots + j _ { k } )
$$

$$
\gamma ( g ; f _ { 1 } , \ldots , f _ { k } ) = g \circ ( f _ { 1 } \oplus \cdot \cdot \cdot \oplus f _ { k } ) .
$$

The spaces $\mathcal { L } ( j )$ form an operad [45, p.1] with structural maps $\gamma$ ,called the linear isometries operad. Points $f \in \mathcal { L } ( j )$ give inclusions $\{ f \} \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( j )$ . The corresponding twisted half-smash product is denoted $f _ { * }$ ; it sends spectra indexed on $U ^ { j }$ to spectra indexed on $U$ Applied to a $j$ -fold external smash product $E _ { 1 } \wedge \dots \wedge E _ { j }$ , it gives an internal smash product $f _ { * } ( E _ { 1 } \wedge \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge E _ { j } )$ . All of these smash products become equivalent in the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ , but none of them are associative or commutative on the point set level. In fact, the following sharper version of this assertion holds.

THEOREM 3.2. Let $\mathcal { S } _ { t } \subset \mathcal { S }$ be the full subcategory of tame spectra and let $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ be the category of tame spectra and homotopy classes of maps. The internal smash products $f _ { * } ( E \wedge E ^ { \prime } )$ determined by varying $f \in \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ are canonically isomorphic in $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ , and $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ is symmetric monoidal under the internal smash product. For based spaces $X$ and tame spectra $E$ , there is a natural isomorphism $E \wedge X \simeq f _ { * } ( E \wedge \Sigma ^ { \infty } X )$ in $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ .

ProoF. The external and internal smash products of $\Sigma$ cofibrant spectra are $\Sigma$ -cofibrant by results in $\mathbf { X \ S 4 }$ By Theorem 2.5, for any $f \in \mathcal { L } ( j )$ and any spectra $E _ { i } \in \mathcal S _ { t }$ , the map

$$
f _ { * } ( E _ { 1 } \wedge \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge E _ { j } ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes ( E _ { 1 } \wedge \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge E _ { j } )
$$

induced by the inclusion $\{ f \} \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( j )$ is a homotopy equivalence. Taking $j = 2$ , this shows that the internal smash products obtained from varying $f$ are homotopy equivalent. Replacing $f$ by $f \circ \sigma$ , where $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { 2 }$ is the transposition, we obtain a natural homotopy equivalence

$$
f _ { * } ( E _ { 2 } \wedge E _ { 1 } ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes E _ { 1 } \wedge E _ { 2 } ,
$$

and this shows that the internal smash product is commutative up to homotopy. Similarly, for associativity, the inclusions of the points $\{ f ( 1 \oplus f ) \}$ and $\{ f ( f \oplus 1 ) \}$ in $\mathcal { L } ( 3 )$ induce natural homotopy equivalences

$$
f _ { * } ( E _ { 1 } \wedge f _ { * } ( E _ { 2 } \wedge E _ { 3 } ) ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 3 ) \ltimes ( E _ { 1 } \wedge E _ { 2 } \wedge E _ { 3 } ) \longleftarrow f _ { * } ( f _ { * } ( E _ { 1 } \wedge E _ { 2 } ) \wedge E _ { 3 } ) .
$$

It is natural to think of based spaces as spectra indexed on the universe $\{ 0 \}$ . Then $i _ { * }$ and the suspension spectrum functor are both left adjoint to the zeroth space functor, hence $i _ { * } X \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } X$ . The map $\mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ that sends $f$ to $f \circ ( 1 \oplus i )$ and the inclusion $\{ 1 \} \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ induce natural homotopy equivalences

$$
f _ { * } ( E \wedge \Sigma ^ { \infty } X ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes ( E \wedge X ) \longleftarrow E \wedge X .
$$

Thus, up to natural isomorphisms, the internal smash product determined by $f$ becomes commutative, associative, and unital with unit $S = \Sigma ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 }$ on passage to $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ . The commutativity of coherence diagrams that is required for the assertion that $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ is symmetric monoidal (see [43, p. 180]) can be checked by an elaboration of these arguments.

The following consequence strengthens the assertion [38, I.6.1] that the stable homotopy category really is a stable category, in the sense that the suspension and loop functors $\Sigma$ and $\Omega$ pass to inverse self-equivalences of $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ .

COROLLARY 3.3. For tame spectra $E$ and $f \in \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ , there is a natural homotopy equivalence between $\Omega E$ and $f _ { * } ( E \wedge S ^ { - 1 } )$ , and the unit $\eta : E \longrightarrow \Omega \Sigma E$ and counit $\varepsilon : \Sigma \Omega E \longrightarrow E$ of the $( \Sigma , \Omega )$ -adjunction are homotopy equivalences.

PRooF. For based spaces $X , \Sigma ^ { \infty } X$ is naturally isomorphic to $\Sigma ( \Sigma _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } X )$ since the structural homeomorphism $E _ { 0 } \longrightarrow \Omega E _ { 1 }$ gives a natural isomorphism between their right adjoints. Therefore, for $E \in \mathcal S _ { t }$ , there is a natural homotopy equivalence

$$
E = E \wedge S ^ { 0 } \simeq f _ { * } ( E \wedge \Sigma ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 } ) \cong f _ { * } ( E \wedge \Sigma ( \Sigma _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 } ) ) \cong \Sigma ( f _ { * } ( E \wedge S ^ { - 1 } ) ) ,
$$

where the last isomorphism is given by Proposition 2.2(iv). It follows that, on $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ , the functor $\Sigma$ is an adjoint equivalence with inverse given by the functor $f _ { * } ( E \wedge S ^ { - 1 } )$ . The rest is a formal consequence of the uniqueness of adjoints.

Note that only actual homotopy equivalences, not weak ones, are relevant to these results. For this and other reasons, $h \mathcal { S } _ { t }$ will be a technically convenient halfway house between $h \mathcal { S }$ and the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \bar { \mathcal { S } }$ , which is obtained from either of these homotopy categories by inverting the weak equivalences.

We can deduce that cofiber sequences give rise to long exact sequences of homotopy groups.

COROLLARY .4. er $E { \stackrel { f } { \longrightarrow } } E ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow C f$ of tame spectra gives rise to a long exact sequence of homotopy groups

$$
\therefore \cdot \cdot { \longrightarrow } \pi _ { q } ( E ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { q } ( E ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { q } ( C f ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { q - 1 } ( E ) \longrightarrow \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

Therefore the natural map $F f \longrightarrow \Omega C f$ is a weak equivalence.

PRoOF. Consider the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r c l l } { { } } & { { S ^ { q } \xrightarrow { \mathrm { ~ i d ~ } } > S ^ { q } \xrightarrow { \qquad \quad  \quad C S ^ { q } \longrightarrow \Sigma S ^ { q } \xrightarrow { \quad \mathrm { ~ i d ~ } \quad \mathrm { ~ i d ~ } \quad > \Sigma S ^ { q } } } { \Sigma S ^ { q } } } } & { { \Sigma S ^ { q } } } \\ { { } } & { { \Sigma ^ { - 1 } \gamma ! } } & { { \Biggl \downarrow \alpha } } & { { \stackrel { \mathrm { ~ i ~ } \beta } { \parallel } } } & { { \stackrel { \mathrm { ~ i ~ } \gamma } { \Sigma \alpha } } } \\ { { } } & { { \stackrel { \vee } { E } \xrightarrow { \quad f } \Sigma ^ { \prime } \xrightarrow { \quad i } \Sigma ^ { \big } f \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \forall } \Sigma ^ { \big } E \xrightarrow { \quad \Sigma f } \Sigma E ^ { \prime } . } } & { { \stackrel { \mathrm { ~ i ~ } \beta } { \Sigma ^ { \prime }  \Sigma E ^ { \prime } } . } } \end{array}
$$

Here $\alpha$ is given such that $i \circ \alpha \simeq 0$ A homotopy induces a map $\beta$ such that the second square commutes. The usual cofiber sequence argument gives $\gamma$ such that the right two squares homotopy commute. Since $\eta _ { E } : E \longrightarrow \Omega \Sigma E$ is a weak equivalence, there is a map $\Sigma ^ { - 1 } \gamma : S ^ { q } \longrightarrow E$ , unique up to homotopy, such that

$$
\eta _ { E } \circ \Sigma ^ { - 1 } \gamma \simeq \Omega \gamma \circ \eta _ { S ^ { q } } .
$$

Therefore

$$
\eta _ { E ^ { \prime } } \circ f \circ \Sigma ^ { - 1 } \gamma = \Omega \Sigma f \circ \eta _ { E } \circ \Sigma ^ { - 1 } \gamma \simeq \Omega \Sigma f \circ \Omega \gamma \circ \eta _ { S ^ { q } } \simeq \Omega \Sigma \alpha \circ \eta _ { S ^ { q } } = \eta _ { E ^ { \prime } } \circ \alpha .
$$

Since $\eta _ { E ^ { \prime } }$ is a weak equivalence, this implies that $f \circ \Sigma ^ { - 1 } \gamma \simeq \alpha$ The long exact sequence follows by extending the given cofiber sequence to the right, as usual. The last statement follows by the five lemma and a comparison of our cofiber sequence with the fiber sequence associated to $f$ Details of this may be found in [38, pp 128-130]. For later use, observe that we only used that the maps $\eta$ are weak equivalences, not that they are homotopy equivalences, in this proof.

It follows that cofiber sequences are essentially equivalent to fiber sequences. More precisely, the cofibrations and fibrations give "triangulations" of the stable homotopy category such that the negative of a cofibration triangle is a fibration triangle, and conversely [38, pp 128-130].

CoRoLLARY 3.5. Pushouts of tame spectra along cofibrations preserve weak equivalences. That is, for a commutative diagram of tame spectra

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { E < - i } & { D \xrightarrow { f } \succ F } \\ & { \beta \Bigg \downarrow } & { \downarrow \alpha \downarrow \gamma } \\ & { E ^ { \prime }    D ^ { \prime } \xrightarrow { f ^ { \prime } } \vdash F ^ { \prime } } \end{array}
$$

in which i and $i ^ { \prime }$ are cofibrations and $\alpha , ~ \beta$ ,and $\gamma$ are weak equivalences, the induced map $\delta : E \cup _ { D } F \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } \cup _ { D ^ { \prime } } F ^ { \prime }$ of pushouts is a weak equivalence.

Proof. As for spaces, $C i$ is homotopy equivalent to $E / D$ , the induced map $F \longrightarrow E \cup _ { D } F$ is a cofibration, and the induced map $E / D \longrightarrow E \cup _ { D } F / F$ is an isomorphism. The conclusion follows from the previous corollary by a diagram chase and the five lemma.

PRopositioN 3.6. If $E$ is a CW spectrum and $\phi : F \longrightarrow F ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence between tame spectra, then $f _ { * } ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \phi ) : f _ { * } ( E \wedge F ) \longrightarrow f _ { * } ( E \wedge F ^ { \prime } )$ is a weak equivalence.

PROOF. The functor $f _ { * } ( ( - ) \wedge F )$ preserves cofiber sequences. Therefore, by Corollary 3.5 and induction up the sequential filtration of $E$ (see III.2.1), the result will hold for general $E$ if it holds for $E = S ^ { n }$ . When $E = S$ , the conclusion holds by the unit equivalence $f _ { * } ( S \wedge F ) \simeq F$ of Theorem 3.2. For $n > 0$ ,we easily deduce isomorphisms

$$
f _ { * } ( S ^ { n } \wedge F ) \cong \Sigma ^ { n } f _ { * } ( S \wedge F ) \quad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \quad \Sigma ^ { n } f _ { * } ( S ^ { - n } \wedge F ) \cong f _ { * } ( S \wedge F )
$$

from Proposition $2 . 2 ( \mathrm { i v } )$ . In view of Corollary 3.3, the result for $E = S ^ { - n }$ and $E = S ^ { n }$ therefore follows from the result for $_ { s }$ .

It follows that for general spectra $\boldsymbol { E }$ and tame spectra $\boldsymbol { \mathsf { \Pi } } ^ { F }$ , the smash product $E \wedge F$ in the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ is represented by $f _ { * } ( \Gamma E \wedge F )$ , where $\Gamma E$ is a CW spectrum weakly equivalent to $E$ That is, we do not also have to apply CW approximation to $\boldsymbol { F }$ . The mild restriction to tame spectra serves to avoid pathological point-set behavior.

# 4. The category of L-spectra

We think of $\mathcal { L } ( j ) \propto ( E _ { 1 } \wedge \cdots \wedge E _ { j } )$ as a canonical $j$ -fold internal smash product. It is still not associative, but we shall construct a commutative and associative smash product by restricting to $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra and shrinking the fat out of the construction. To define $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, we focus attention on a small part of the operad $\mathcal { L }$ . Recall the notion of a monad in a category from [43, ch.VI] or [45, 2.1].

NOTaTIonS 4.1. Let $\mathbb { L }$ denote the monad in the category $\mathcal { S }$ that is specified by $\mathbb { L } E = \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes E$ sthe product

$$
\mu : \mathbb { L } \mathbb { L } E \cong ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes E \longrightarrow { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes E = \mathbb { L } E
$$

is induced by the product $\gamma : \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ and the unit

$$
\eta : E \cong \{ 1 \} \ltimes E \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes E = \mathbb { L } E
$$

is induced by the inclusion $\{ 1 \} \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ of the identity element.

DEfinitioN 4.2. An $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum is an L-algebra $M$ , that is, a spectrum $M$ together with an action $\pmb { \xi } : \mathbb { L } M \longrightarrow M$ by the monad L. Explicitly, the following diagrams are required to commute:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } { \mathbb { L L } M \xrightarrow { \mu } \mathbb { L } M } & { } & { \qquad M \xrightarrow { \eta } \mathbb { L } M } \\ { \mathbb { L } \xi \Bigg | \qquad } & { \Bigg \downarrow \xi \qquad } & { \mathrm { a n d } } \\ { \mathbb { L } M \xrightarrow { \xi \quad \xi \quad { M } } M } & { } & { \qquad M . } \end{array}
$$

A map $f : M \longrightarrow N$ of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra is a map of spectra such that the following diagram commutes:

$$
\begin{array} { c } { \mathbb { L } M \xrightarrow { \mathbb { L } f } \mathbb { L } N } \\ { \xi _ { M } \Biggl \downarrow \qquad } \\ { M \xrightarrow { \mathbb { L } \tilde { \mathbf { \Xi } } } N . } \end{array}
$$

We let ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ denote the category of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra.

There is a dual form of the definition that will occasionally be needed. It is based on the following standard categorical observation.

LEMMA 4.3. Let T be a monad in a category $\mathscr { C }$ , and suppose that the functor T has a right adjoint $\mathbb { T } ^ { \# }$ . Then $\mathbb { T } ^ { \# }$ is a comonad such that the categories of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras and of $\mathbb { T } ^ { \# }$ -coalgebras are isomorphic.

We shall consistently use the notation $\mathbb { T } ^ { \# }$ for the comonad associated to a monad $\mathbb { T }$ that has a right adjoint. In particular, by Proposition 2.3, we now have a comonad $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# }$ such that an $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# }$ -coalgebra is the same thing as an L-spectrum. This implies the following result.

ProPosiTIoN 4.4. The category of L-spectra is complete and cocomplete, with both limits and colimits created in the underlying category $\mathcal { S }$ If $X$ is a based space and $M$ is an L-spectrum, then $M \wedge X$ and $F ( X , M )$ are $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, and the spectrum level fiber and cofiber of a map of L-spectra are L-spectra.

PROOF. Since ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ is the category of algebras over the monad $\mathbb { L }$ , the forgetful functor ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow { \mathcal { S } }$ creates limits [43, VI.2, ex. 2]. Since $\mathcal { S }$ is complete, this implies the statement about limits. The statement about colimits follows similarly by use of the comonad $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# }$ . The last statement is immediate from the canonical isomorphism

$$
{ \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes ( M \land X ) \cong ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes M ) \land X
$$

of Proposition 2.2(iv) and its analog (A.6.1)

$$
F [ { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) , F ( X , M ) ) \cong F ( X , F [ { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) , M ) ) .
$$

LEMMA 4.5. The sphere spectrum $S$ is an L-spectrum. More generally, for based spaces $X$ , $\Sigma ^ { \infty } X \cong S \wedge X$ is naturally an L-spectrum.

ProoF. Recall from the proof of Theorem 3.2 that a based space $X$ may be viewed as a spectrum indexed on $\{ 0 \}$ and that $\Sigma ^ { \infty } X \cong i _ { * } X$ , $i : \{ 0 \} \longrightarrow U$ .We may rewrite this as $\Sigma ^ { \infty } X = { \mathcal { L } } ( 0 ) \ltimes X$ Then the structure map is given by

$$
\gamma \ltimes \mathrm { i d } : { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 0 ) \ltimes X ) \cong ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 0 ) ) \ltimes X \longrightarrow { \mathcal { L } } ( 0 ) \ltimes X .
$$

$\mathbf { I n } \cdot$ the middle, $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 0 )$ is regarded as a space over $\mathcal { L } ( 0 )$ via $\gamma$ ,and the isomorphism is given by an instance of Proposition 2.2(ii). Of course, $\gamma$ here is just the unique map from $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ to the one-point space $\mathcal { L } ( 0 )$ , and our structure map is just the composite

$$
{ \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes \Sigma ^ { \infty } X \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) _ { + } \wedge X ) \longrightarrow \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( S ^ { 0 } \wedge X ) \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } X ,
$$

where the first isomorphism is given by Proposition 2.1.

A homotopy in the category of L-spectra is a map $M \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow N$ A map of $\mathbb { L } _ {  }$ spectra is a weak equivalence if it is a weak equivalence as a map of spectra. The stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra is constructed from the homotopy category $h \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ by adjoining formal inverses to the weak equivalences; again, the process is made rigorous by CW approximation. Since the theory of cell and CW L-spectra is exactly like the theory of cell and CW spectra developed in [38, I§5], we shall not give details. The reader who would like to see an exposition is invited to look ahead to $\operatorname { I I I } \ S 2$ The theory of cell $R$ -modules to be presented there applies (with minor simplifications) to give what is needed. It is formal that the monad $\mathbb { L }$ may be viewed as specifying the free functor from spectra to $\mathbb { L }$ spectra. The sphere $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra that we take as the domains of attaching maps when defining cell $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra are the free $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $\mathbb { L } S ^ { n } = \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes S ^ { n }$ . A weak equivalence of cell $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra is a homotopy equivalence, any L-spectrum is weakly equivalent to a CW L-spectrum, and $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ is equivalent to the homotopy category of CW L-spectra. We warn the reader that, although $S$ itself is an $\mathbb { L } _ { \star }$ spectrum, it does not have the homotopy type of a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum (see Warning

6.8 below). The following comparison between CW spectra and CW L-spectra establishes an equivalence between $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ and $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .

THEOREM 4.6. The following conclusions hold.

(i) The free functor $\mathbb { L } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ carries CW spectra to CW L-spectra.   
(ii) The forgetful functor ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow { \mathcal { S } }$ carries $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra of the homotopy types of CW L-spectra to spectra of the homotopy types of CW spectra.   
(iii) Every CW L-spectrum $M$ is homotopy equivalent as an L-spectrum to $\mathbb { L } E$ for some $C W$ spectrum $E$ .   
(iv) The unit $\eta : E \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } E$ of the adjunction

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } E , M ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( E , M )
$$

is a homotopy equivalence if $E \in { \mathcal { S } } _ { t }$ , for example if $\boldsymbol { E }$ is a $C W$ spectrum.

(v) The counit $\pmb { \xi } : \mathbb { L } M \longrightarrow M$ of the adjunction is a homotopy equivalence of spectra if $M$ is tame and is a homotopy equivalence of L-spectra if M has the homotopy type of a CW L-spectrum.

The free and forgetful functors establish an adjoint equivalence between the stable homotopy categories $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ and $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .

PRooF. Part (i) is immediate by induction up the sequential filtration (see III.2.1). Part (iv) is immediate from Theorem 2.5 and, applied to sphere spectra, it implies (ii). Since $\xi \circ \eta = \mathrm { i d } : M \longrightarrow M$ for any $M$ , (iv) and the Whitehead theorem in the category of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra imply $( \mathbf { v } )$ . Part (iii) follows from (i) and (v) since there is a CW spectrum $E$ and a homotopy equivalence of spectra $E \longrightarrow M$ . It is a formal consequence of (i) that we have an induced adjunction

$$
\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } E , M ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( E , M )
$$

(see [38, I.5.13]), and its unit and counit are natural isomorphisms.

Observe that, dually, we can interpret $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# }$ as specifying the "cofree" functor from spectra to $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. That is, we have an adjunction

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , \mathbb { L } ^ { \# } E ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( M , E ) .
$$

By part (ii) of the theorem and [38, I.5.13], there results an induced adjunction

$$
\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , \mathbb { L } ^ { \# } E ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( M , E ) .
$$

It is an easy categorical observation that, in any adjoint equivalence of categories, the given left and right adjoints are also right and left adjoint to each other.

COROLLARY 4.8. The functors $\mathbb { L } : \bar { h } \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# } : \bar { h } \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ are naturally isomorphic.

# 5. The smash product of L-spectra

Via instances of the structural maps $\gamma$ of the operad $\mathcal { L }$ , we have a left action of the monoid $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ and a right action of the monoid $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ on $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ . These actions commute with each other. If $M$ and $N$ are L-spectra, then $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ acts from the left on the external smash product $M \wedge N$ via the map

$$
\xi : \left( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \right) \ltimes \left( M \wedge N \right) \cong \left( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes M \right) \wedge \left( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes N \right) \xrightarrow { \xi \wedge \xi } { M \wedge N } .
$$

To form the twisted half smash product on the left, we think of $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ as mapping to ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U ^ { 2 } , U ^ { 2 } )$ via direct sum of linear isometries. The smash product over $\mathcal { L }$ of $M$ and $N$ is simply the balanced product of the two ${ \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) .$ . actions.

DEFInITION 5.1. Let $M$ and $N$ be $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. Define the operadic smash product $M \land \ u { \mathscr { L } } N$ to be the coequalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes ( M \wedge N ) \frac { \gamma ^ { \mathrm { w i d } } } { \mathrm { i d } \kappa \xi } \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes ( M \wedge N ) \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N .
$$

Here we have implicitly used the isomorphism

$$
( { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) ) \times ( M \wedge N ) \cong { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes [ ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes ( M \wedge N ) ]
$$

given by Proposition 2.2(ii). The left action of $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ on $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ induces a left action of $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ on $M \land \_ N$ that gives it a structure of $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum.

We may mimic tensor product notation and write

$$
M \land \_ { \mathcal { L } } N = \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ ( M \land N ) .
$$

We will freely use such notations for coequalizers below. The commutativity of this smash product is immediate.

ProPosiTIoN 5.2. There is a natural commutativity isomorphism of L-spectra

$$
\tau : M \land \_ N \longrightarrow N \land _ { \mathcal { L } } M .
$$

PRooF. The permutation $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { 2 }$ acts on $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ by $f \sigma = f \circ t$ ,where $t :$ $U ^ { 2 } \longrightarrow U ^ { 2 }$ is the transposition isomorphism. We may regard $\sigma$ as a map of spaces over $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ from $\mathbf { d } : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ to $\sigma : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ . We have an evident isomorphism $\iota : t _ { * } ( M \wedge N ) \cong N \wedge M$ on external smash products and, by Proposition 2.2(ii), there results a canonical isomorphism

$$
\sigma \ltimes \iota : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes M \land N \cong \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes t _ { * } ( M \land N ) \cong \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes N \land M .
$$

There is an analogous isomorphism

$$
( \sigma \times t ) \ltimes \iota : ( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes ( M \wedge N ) \longrightarrow ( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes ( N \wedge M ) .
$$

These maps induce an isomorphism of coequalizer diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { ( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \times ( M \wedge N ) \frac { \gamma \times \mathrm { i d } } { \mathrm { i d } \kappa \xi } \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes ( M \wedge N ) \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N } \\ & { \qquad ( \sigma \times t ) \ltimes u \Bigg \downarrow } \\ & { ( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes ( N \wedge M ) \frac { \gamma \times \mathrm { i d } } { \mathrm { i d } \kappa \xi } \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes ( N \wedge M ) \longrightarrow N \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M . } \end{array}
$$

To show that this smash product is associative, we need some preliminary material on coequalizers. We first recall a standard categorical definition [43, VI.6].

DEFINITIoN 5.3. Working in an arbitrary category, suppose given a diagram

$$
A { \xrightarrow { \quad e \quad } } B { \xrightarrow { \quad g \quad } } C
$$

in which $g e = g f$ .The diagram is called a split coequalizer if there are maps

$$
h : C \longrightarrow B \quad { \mathrm { a n d } } \quad k : B \longrightarrow A
$$

such that $g h = \operatorname { i d } _ { C }$ , $f k = \operatorname { i d } _ { B }$ , and $e k = h g$ It follows that $g$ is the coequalizer of $e$ and $f$ .

Observe that, while covariant functors need not preserve coequalizers in general, they clearly do preserve split coequalizers. The next observation is crucial; we learned it from Hopkins [32]. Note that, via structural maps $\gamma$ $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ acts from the left on any $\mathcal { L } ( i )$ , hence $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ acts from the left on $\mathscr { L } ( i ) \times \mathscr { L } ( j )$ .

LEMMA 5.4 (HopKINS). For $i \geq 1$ and $j \geq 1$ , the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( i ) } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \mathrm { i d } \times \gamma ^ { 2 } \Bigg \Vert \Bigg \Vert _ { \gamma \times \mathrm { i d } } } \\ & { \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( i ) \times \mathcal { L } ( j ) } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \Bigg \Vert _ { \gamma } } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \mathcal { L } ( i + j ) } \end{array}
$$

is a split coequalizer of spaces. Therefore,

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \pmb { \mathcal { L } } ( i + j ) \cong \pmb { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \times _ { \pmb { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times \pmb { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) } \pmb { \mathcal { L } } ( i ) \times \pmb { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) . } \end{array}
$$

PROOF. Choose isomorphisms $s : U ^ { i } \longrightarrow U$ and $t : U ^ { j } \longrightarrow U$ and define

$$
h ( f ) = ( f \circ ( s \oplus t ) ^ { - 1 } , s , t )
$$

and

$$
k ( f ; g , g ^ { \prime } ) = ( f ; g \circ s ^ { - 1 } , g ^ { \prime } \circ t ^ { - 1 } ; s , t ) .
$$

It is trivial to check the identities of Definition 5.3.

THEOREM 5.5. There is a natural associativity isomorphism of L-spectra

$$
( M \land \mathcal { L } \ N ) \land _ { \mathcal { L } } P \cong M \land _ { \mathcal { L } } ( N \land _ { \mathcal { L } } P ) .
$$

Proof. Note that, for any $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $N$ , $N \cong \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } N$ since ${ \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes$ $N = \mathbb { L } N$ and, as with any monad [43, p. 148], we have a split coequalizer

$$
\mathbb { L } \mathbb { L } N \Longrightarrow \mathbb { L } N \longrightarrow N .
$$

We have the isomorphisms

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \left( M \wedge \_ { { \mathscr { L } } } N \right) \wedge \_ { { \mathscr { L } } } P \cong { \mathscr { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) ^ { 2 } } \left( { \mathscr { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) ^ { 2 } } \left( M \wedge N \right) \right) \wedge \left( { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes _ { { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) } P \right) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \left( { \mathscr { L } } ( 2 ) \times _ { { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) ^ { 2 } } { \mathscr { L } } ( 2 ) \times { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) \right) \ltimes _ { { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) ^ { 3 } } \left( M \wedge N \wedge P \right) } \\ & { \qquad \cong { \mathscr { L } } ( 3 ) \ltimes _ { { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) ^ { 3 } } M \wedge N \wedge P . } \end{array}
$$

The symmetric argument shows that this is also isomorphic to $M \land \_ { \mathcal { L } } \ ( N \land \_ { \mathcal { L } }$ $P$ .

In view of the generality of Lemma 5.4, the argument iterates to prove the following statement.

THeOREM 5.6. For any $j$ -tuple $M _ { 1 } , \dots , M _ { j }$ of L-spectra, there is a canonical isomorphism of L-spectra

$$
M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { \pm } \ldots \wedge _ { \pm } M _ { j } \cong { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes _ { { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) ^ { j } } ( M _ { 1 } \wedge \dots \wedge M _ { j } ) ,
$$

where the iterated smash product on the left is associated in any fashion.

# 6. The equivalence of the old and new smash products

We here show that the smash product $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ does in fact realize the classical smash product of spectra up to homotopy, in the sense that the equivalence between $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ and $\bar { h } \bar { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ preserves smash products.

Fix a linear isometric isomorphism $f : U ^ { 2 } \longrightarrow U$ (not just an isometry) and use it to define the internal smash product of spectra in this section. We begin the comparison of smash products of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra with smash products of spectra with the following observation.

PROPOsITION 6.1. For spectra $X$ and $Y$ , there are isomorphisms of L-spectra

$$
\mathbb { L } X \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } Y \cong \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes X \wedge Y \cong \mathbb { L } f _ { * } ( X \wedge Y ) .
$$

For CW L-spectra $M$ and $N$ , $M \land \_ N$ is a CW L-spectrum with one $( p + q )$ -cell for each $p$ -cell of $M$ and $q$ -cell of $N$ .

PRooF. The first isomorphism is immediate from the definition of $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ . Regarding $f$ as a point in $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ , we see that $\gamma : \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \{ f \} \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ is a homeomorphism since $f$ is an isomorphism. It follows from Proposition 2.2(ii) that

$$
\mathbb { L } f _ { * } ( X \wedge Y ) = { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes f _ { * } ( X \wedge Y ) \cong { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes ( X \wedge Y ) .
$$

When $X$ and $Y$ are sphere spectra, so is $f _ { * } ( X \wedge Y )$ [38, II.1.4]. The second statement now follows exactly as for the smash product of CW complexes or CW spectra.

The crux of our comparison of smash products is the following proposition, which implies that $\mathbb { L } S$ is the unit for the smash product in the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .We defer the proof to $\mathrm { X I \ S 3 }$ .

PROPosITiION 6.2. For L-spectra $N$ , there is a natural weak equivalence of L-spectra $\omega : \mathbb { L } S \land \_ N \longrightarrow N$ , and $\Sigma : \pi _ { n } ( N ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { n + 1 } ( \Sigma N )$ is an isomorphism for all integers $_ n$ .

If we knew a priori that $\Sigma$ preserved weak equivalences, we could derive the second clause from the first and the natural isomorphism of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra

$$
\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong \Sigma ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { - 1 } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N )
$$

by a formal uniqueness of adjoints argument (compare Corollary 3.3). It is a pleasant and surprising technical feature of our theory, immediate from the proposition, that $\Sigma$ preserves weak equivalences of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. That is, the $\mathbb { L }$ structure somehow has the effect of eliminating point-set pathology. Since $\Sigma$ on homotopy groups is induced by $\eta : N \longrightarrow \Omega \Sigma N$ , the proposition also has the following immediate consequence.

COROLLARY 6.3. For L-spectra $N$ , the unit $\eta : N \longrightarrow \Omega \Sigma N$ and counit $\varepsilon$ : $\Sigma \Omega N \longrightarrow N$ of the $( \Sigma , \Omega )$ -adjunction are weak equivalences.

COROLLARY 6.4. Any cofiber sequence $N { \xrightarrow { \ f \ } } N ^ { \prime } \ \longrightarrow \ C f$ of L-spectra gives rise to a long exact sequence of homotopy groups

$$
\therefore \cdot \cdot { \longrightarrow } \pi _ { q } ( N ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { q } ( N ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { q } ( C f ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { q - 1 } ( N ) \longrightarrow \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

Therefore the natural map $F f \longrightarrow \Omega C f$ is a weak equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra.

PRooF. This follows from Corollary 6.3 via the proof of Corollary 3.4.

CoROLLARY 6.5. Pushouts along cofibrations of L-spectra preserve weak equivalences.

PRooF. Since a cofibration of L-spectra is a cofibration of spectra, by the retraction of mapping cylinders criterion, this follows from Corollary 6.4 via the proof of Corollary 3.5.

PROPOsITION 6.6. If $M$ is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum and $\phi : N \longrightarrow N ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of L-spectra, then id $\wedge _ { \pm } \phi : M \wedge _ { \pm } N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \pm } N ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of L-spectra.

PROoF. The functor $( - ) \land \mathscr { L } N$ preserves cofiber sequences, hence the result for general $M$ follows from Corollary 6.4 and the result for $M = \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }$ Here the result for $n = 0$ follows from Proposition 6.2 and the result for $_ n$ and $- n , n > 0$ follows from the result for $n = 0$ as in the proof of Proposition 3.6.

Thus, for $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $M$ and $N$ , the smash product $M \land \_ N$ in the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ is represented by $\Gamma M \land \scriptscriptstyle { \mathcal { L } } N$ , where $\Gamma M$ is a CW L-spectrum weakly equivalent to $M$ ; here we do not need to assume that $N$ is tame.
