This is analogous to the situation in algebra. When transporting tensor products to algebraic derived categories, we need only apply cell approximation to one of the tensor factors, without any condition on the other [35].

THEoREM 6.7. For L-spectra $M$ and $N$ , there is a natural map of spectra $\alpha : f _ { * } ( M \wedge N ) \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ ,and $\alpha$ is a weak equivalence when $M$ is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum and $N$ is a tame spectrum. For any $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum $N$ , the composite of the derived functor $( - ) \wedge _ { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } N : \bar { h } \mathscr { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \bar { h } \mathscr { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and the forgetful functor $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \bar { h } \bar { \mathcal { S } }$ computes the derived internal smash product with $N$ .

PROOF. Define $\alpha$ to be the composite

$$
f _ { * } ( M \wedge N ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes M \wedge N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N
$$

given by the the inclusion of $\{ f \}$ in $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ and the definition of $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ . Let $M$ be a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum throughout the proof. We first show that $\pmb { \alpha }$ is an equivalence when $N$ is also a CW $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum. In this case, $M$ and $N$ have the homotopy types of CW spectra by Theorem 4.6 and are therefore tame by X.4.3. Thus the first map is a homotopy equivalence by Theorem 2.5. By Theorem 4.6(iii), we may assume without loss of generality that $M = \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes X$ and $N = \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes Y$ for CW spectra $X$ and $Y$ . The second arrow then reduces to the homotopy equivalence

$$
{ \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes X ) \wedge ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes Y ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes X \wedge Y
$$

induced by the homotopy equivalence $\gamma : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ via Theorem 2.5. For a general $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum $N$ , choose a weak equivalence $\gamma : \Gamma N \longrightarrow$ $N$ , where $\Gamma N$ is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum. If $N$ is tame, then Propositions 3.6 and 6.6 imply that the vertical arrows are weak equivalences in the commutative diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { f _ { * } ( M \wedge \Gamma N ) \xrightarrow { \alpha } \to M \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } \Gamma N } \\ & { } & { \quad \mathrm { i d } \wedge \gamma \Bigg \downarrow } \\ & { } & { f _ { * } ( M \wedge N ) \xrightarrow [ \alpha ] { } \to M \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } N . } \end{array}
$$

Thus the bottom arrow $\alpha$ is a weak equivalence since the top one is. For the last statement, simply note that the right-hand composite

$$
( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \gamma ) \circ \alpha : f _ { * } ( M \wedge \Gamma N ) \longrightarrow M \wedge \_ N
$$

in the diagram is a weak equivalence even when $N$ is not tame.

WARNING 6.8. As said before, the sphere spectrum $s$ does not have the homotopy type of a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum. To see this, assume that it did. Then the action $\xi : \mathbb { L } S \longrightarrow S$ would be a homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra, by the Whitehead theorem, and the $\Sigma _ { 2 }$ -equivariant map

$$
\xi \wedge _ { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } \xi : \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } \mathbb { L } S \longrightarrow S \wedge _ { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } S
$$

would be a $\Sigma _ { 2 }$ -equivariant homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra and thus of spectra. By Propositions 6.1 and 2.1, $\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S$ is isomorphic to $\Sigma ^ { \infty } ( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) _ { + } )$ , with $\Sigma _ { 2 }$ -action induced by that on $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ . By Proposition 8.2 below, $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S$ is isomorphic to $S = \Sigma ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 }$ and has trivial action by $\Sigma _ { 2 }$ .Under these isomorphisms, $\xi \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \xi$ coincides with $\Sigma ^ { \infty } \pi$ , where $\pi : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) _ { + } \longrightarrow S ^ { 0 }$ sends all of $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ to the non-basepoint. Since $\mathcal { L } ( 2 ) / \Sigma _ { 2 } \simeq B ( \Sigma _ { 2 } )$ , our assumption implies that we obtain a homotopy equivalence $\Sigma ^ { \infty } B ( \Sigma _ { 2 } ) _ { + } \longrightarrow \Sigma ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 }$ on passage to orbits from $\xi \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \xi$ , which is absurd. A different perspective on this warning will be given in II.1.10.

# 7. Function L-spectra

We here construct a functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } }$ on $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra that is related to the smash product $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ by an adjunction of the usual form and consider its homotopical behavior.

THEOREM 7.1. Let $M , N$ , and $P$ be L-spectra. There is a function L-spectrum functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N )$ , contravariant in $M$ and covariant in $N$ , such that

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M \land _ { \mathcal { L } } N , P ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( N , P ) ) .
$$

Given the adjunction, we can deduce the homotopical behavior of $F _ { \mathcal { L } }$ from that of $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ . We run through this before turning to the construction. The following result is a formal consequence of Proposition 6.1; see [38, I.5.13].

PROPOsITION 7.2. If $M$ is a CW L-spectrum and $\phi : N \longrightarrow N ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of L-spectra, then

$$
\begin{array} { r } { F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathrm { i d } , \phi ) : F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ^ { \prime } ) } \end{array}
$$

is a weak equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. There is an induced adjunction

$$
\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N , P ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( N , P ) ) .
$$

As in Section 6, we fix a linear isometric isomorphism $f : U ^ { 2 } \longrightarrow U$ and use the isomorphism $f _ { * } : \mathcal { S } U ^ { 2 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U$ to define internal smash products $f _ { * } ( M \land N )$ . Recall the external function spectrum $F ( M , - )$ and the adjunction displayed for it at the start of Section 2. We use the inverse isomorphism $f ^ { * } = f _ { * } ^ { - 1 } : \mathcal { S } U \longrightarrow$ ${ \mathcal { S } } U ^ { 2 }$ to define internal function spectra $F ( M , f ^ { * } N )$ , as in [38, II.3.11].

THEOREM 7.3. For L-spectra $M$ and $N$ , there is a natural map of spectra

$$
{ \tilde { \alpha } } : F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) \longrightarrow F ( M , f ^ { * } N ) ,
$$

and $\tilde { \alpha }$ is a weak equivalence if $M$ is a $C W \mathbb { L }$ -spectrum. Therefore the equivalence of categories $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ induced by the forgetful functor from L-spectra to spectra carries the function L-spectrum functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } }$ to the internal function spectrum functor $F$ .

PrOoF. In the category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ , $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N )$ means $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \Gamma M , N )$ where $\Gamma M$ is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum weakly equivalent to $M$ , hence the second statement will follow from the first. The desired map $\tilde { \alpha }$ is the adjoint of the composite

$$
f _ { * } ( F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) \land M ) { \overset { \alpha } { \longrightarrow } } F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) \land _ { \mathcal { L } } M { \overset { \varepsilon } { \longrightarrow } } N ,
$$

where $\alpha$ is given by Theorem 6.7. By that result, if $M$ is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum and $X$ is a CW spectrum, then $\alpha : f _ { * } ( \mathbb { L } X \wedge M ) \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } X \wedge \_ M$ is a weak equivalence of spectra, and it induces a weak equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra

$$
\mathbb { L } ( f _ { * } ( \mathbb { L } X \wedge M ) ) \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } X \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M .
$$

Diagram chases show that the map

$$
\tilde { \alpha } _ { * } : \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) ) \longrightarrow \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( X , F ( M , f ^ { * } N ) )
$$

coincides with the composite of the following chain of natural isomorphisms:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X \wedge \mathscr { L } M , N ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } ( f _ { * } ( \mathbb { L } X \wedge M ) ) , N ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( f _ { * } ( \mathbb { L } X \wedge M ) , N ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( \mathbb { L } X \wedge M , f ^ { * } N ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( \mathbb { L } X , F ( M , f ^ { * } N ) ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } ( X , F ( M , f ^ { * } N ) ) . \quad \sqsupset } \end{array}
$$

LEMMA 7.4. The adjoint $N \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathbb { L } S , N )$ of the unit weak equivalence $\omega$ . $\mathbb { L } S \land \_ N \longrightarrow N$ is a weak equivalence.

PRooF. This is immediate from the natural isomorphisms

$$
\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , N ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M , N ) \cong \bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathbb { L } S , N ) )
$$

We must still prove Theorem 7.1. The desired adjunction dictates the definition of $F _ { \mathcal { L } }$ , and the reader is invited to skip to the next section. It will be simplest to construct $F _ { \mathcal { L } }$ in two steps. Remember that

$$
M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N = \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } M \wedge N .
$$

In the first step we consider general spectra indexed on $U ^ { 2 }$ and acted upon by $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ , thought of as a space over ${ \mathcal { I } } ( U ^ { 2 } , U ^ { 2 } )$ via direct sum of isometries. We call these $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ -spectra and denote the category of such spectra by $\mathcal { S } [ \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ]$ . Of course, the examples we have in mind are of the form $M \wedge N$ . We use the twisted function spectrum construction $F [ A , - )$ of Proposition 2.3.

LEMMA 7.5. Let $N$ be an L-spectrum. There is an $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ -spectrum $F _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { 2 } )$ such that

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } P , N ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ] ( P , F _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) )
$$

for $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ -spectra $P$ .

PROOF. We construct $F _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N )$ as the equalizer of two maps

$$
F [ { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) , N ) \Longrightarrow F [ { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) , N ) .
$$

The first is induced by $\gamma : \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ . The second is the composite

$$
\begin{array} { r } { F [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) \longrightarrow F [ \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes N ) \xrightarrow { F [ 1 , \xi ] } F [ \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) ; } \end{array}
$$

here the unlabelled arrow is adjoint to

$\begin{array} { r } { ( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) ) \ltimes F [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) \cong \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes F [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \ltimes \varepsilon } \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes N , } \end{array}$ where $\varepsilon$ is the counit of the adjunction. The left action of $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ on $F _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N )$ is induced by its right action on $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ .

The second step lands us back in the category of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra.

LEMMA 7.6. Let $N$ be an $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum and $P$ be an $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ -spectrum. There is an L-spectrum $\hat { F } ( N , P )$ such that

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ] ( M \wedge N , P ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , \hat { F } ( N , P ) )
$$

for L-spectra $M$ .

PROOF. Again, we construct $\hat { F } ( N , P )$ as an equalizer, this time of two maps

$$
F ( N , P )  F ( \mathbb { L } N , P ) .
$$

The first is induced by the structure map $\mathbb { L } N \longrightarrow N$ . The second is the composite

$$
F ( N , P ) \longrightarrow F ( \mathbb { L } N , ( \{ 1 \} \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes P ) \longrightarrow F ( \mathbb { L } N , P ) ,
$$

where the second arrow is induced by the structure map of $P$ as an ${ \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) .$ . module and the first arrow is adjoint to

$$
\begin{array} { r } { F ( N , P ) \land \mathbb { L } N \cong ( \{ 1 \} \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes F ( N , P ) \land N \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \ltimes \varepsilon } ( \{ 1 \} \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes P . } \end{array}
$$

The structure of $\hat { F } ( N , P )$ as an $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum is induced by the action on $P$ of the first factor of $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ in $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ ;more precisely, the action $\mathbb { L } F ( N , P ) \longrightarrow$ $F ( N , P )$ is adjoint to the composite

$$
\begin{array} { r } { ( \mathbb { L } { F } ( N , P ) ) \wedge N \cong ( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \{ 1 \} ) \ltimes \left( { F } ( N , P ) \wedge N \right) \ \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \ltimes \varepsilon } \ \left( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \{ 1 \} \right) \ltimes P \ \overset { \xi } { \longrightarrow } P . } \end{array}
$$

We combine these two functorial constructions to define $F _ { \mathcal { L } }$ .

DEfinitioN 7.7. For $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $M$ and $N$ , define

$$
F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) = \hat { F } ( M , F _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } [ \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) , N ) ) .
$$

The adjunction of Theorem 7.1 is just the composite of the two adjunctions already obtained.

# 8. Unital properties of the smash product of L-spectra

As we have already seen, $\mathbb { L } S$ is a unit for the smash product $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ on $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ . However, for precision in the consideration of algebraic structures, we wish to work in a category of spectra that is actually symmetric monoidal under its smash product, with a point-set level unit isomorphism. The appropriate candidate for a unit object is not $\mathbb { L } S$ but $S$ itself, and at this point another special, and surprising, property of the linear isometries operad comes into play.

Consider the diagram

$$
\mathscr { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathscr { L } ( 0 ) \times \mathscr { L } ( 0 ) \underset { \mathrm { i d } \times \gamma ^ { 2 } } { \overset { \gamma \times \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } } \mathscr { L } ( 2 ) \times \mathscr { L } ( 0 ) \times \mathscr { L } ( 0 ) \xrightarrow { \gamma \to \mathscr { L } ( 0 ) } \mathscr { L } ( 0 ) .
$$

This is not a split coequalizer, but it turns out to be a coequalizer. The coequalizer of the parallel pair of arrows is the orbit space $\mathcal { L } ( 2 ) / \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ .

LEMMA 8.1. The orbit space $\mathcal { L } ( 2 ) / \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ consists of a single point.

This is far from obvious, and it is only possible because $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ is a monoid but not a group. We defer its proof to $\mathrm { X I } \ S 2$ . It has the following implication. Recall Lemma 4.5.

PRoPOsITIoN 8.2. There is an isomorphism of L-spectra $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \longrightarrow S$ such that $\lambda \tau = \lambda$ . For based spaces $X$ and $Y$ , there is a natural isomorphism of L-spectra

$$
\lambda : \Sigma ^ { \infty } X \land _ { \mathcal { L } } \Sigma ^ { \infty } Y \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( X \land Y ) .
$$

ProoF. The second statement follows from the first, or directly: $\gamma$ induces the isomorphism

$$
\mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } \left( \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \ltimes X \right) \wedge \left( \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \ltimes Y \right) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \ltimes X \wedge Y .
$$

The relation $\lambda \tau = \lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \longrightarrow S$ is clear since $\gamma \tau = \gamma$ .

This formalizes our intuition that the smash product should be a stabilized generalization of the smash product of based spaces. It is natural to try to generalize the resulting isomorphism $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \Sigma ^ { \infty } X \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } X$ to arbitrary $\mathbb { L } _ { - }$ spectra, and the map does generalize.

PROPOSITION 8.3. Let $M$ and $N$ be L-spectra. There is a natural map of L-spectra $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow N$ . The symmetrically defined map $M \land \mathcal { L } \ S \longrightarrow M$ coincides with the composite $\lambda \tau$ . Moreover, under the associativity isomorphism,

$$
\lambda \tau \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathrm { i d } = \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \lambda : M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ,
$$

and, under the commutativity isomorphism, these maps also agree with

$$
\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \right) \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N .
$$

PROOF. When $N$ is the free $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $\mathbb { L } X = { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes X$ generated by a spectrum $X , \lambda$ is given by the map

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } X = { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 0 ) \ltimes S ^ { 0 } ) \wedge ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes X ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong ( { \mathcal { L } } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } { \mathcal { L } } ( 0 ) \times { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) ) \ltimes ( S ^ { 0 } \wedge X ) } \\ & { \qquad \xrightarrow { \gamma \ltimes \mathrm { i d } } { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \ltimes X = \mathbb { L } X . } \end{array}
$$

For general $N$ , the map just constructed induces a map of coequalizer diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } \mathbb { L } N \Longrightarrow S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } N \longrightarrow S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N } & { { } } \\ { \Big \downarrow } & { { } } \\ { \mathbb { L } \mathbb { L } N \Longrightarrow \mathbb { L } N \longrightarrow N . } & { { } \longrightarrow N . } \end{array}
$$

The symmetry is clear when $M$ is free and follows in general by an easy comparison of coequalizer diagrams. Similarly, suppose that $M = \mathbb { L } X$ and $N = \mathbb { L } Y$ for spectra $X$ and $Y$ . Then, under the associativity isomorphisms of their domains given in the proof of Theorem 5.5, the two unit maps defined on $\mathbb { L } X \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } Y$ agree with the map

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { L } ( 3 ) \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) ^ { 3 } } \left( \left( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes X \right) \wedge \left( \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \ltimes S ^ { 0 } \right) \wedge \left( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes Y \right) \right) } \\ & { \quad \cong \left( \mathcal { L } ( 3 ) \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) ^ { 3 } } \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \right) \ltimes \left( X \wedge S ^ { 0 } \wedge Y \right) } \\ & { \qquad \xrightarrow { \gamma \ltimes \mathrm { i d } } \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes \left( X \wedge Y \right) \cong \mathbb { L } X \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } \mathbb { L } Y . } \end{array}
$$

The conclusion for general $M$ and $N$ follows by another comparison of coequalizer diagrams. The last statement can be proven similarly.

Any attempt to show that $S$ is a strict unit for general L-spectra founders on the fact that Lemma 5.4 fails if $i = 0$ or $j = 0$ and $i + j > 0$ However, we shall prove the following up to homotopy version of that lemma in XI.2.2.

LEMMA 8.4. The space

$$
\hat { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \equiv \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \times _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )
$$

is contractible. Therefore $\gamma : \hat { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ is a homotopy equivalence.

Again, this assertion is far from obvious. It leads us to the following crucial result.

THEOREM 8.5. Let $M$ be an L-spectrum and consider $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$

) If $M = \mathbb { L } X$ for a tame spectrum $X$ , then $\lambda$ is a homotopy equivalence of spectra and thus a weak equivalence of L-spectra.   
(ii) If M is a CWL-spectrum, then $\lambda$ is a homotopy equivalence of L-spectra.   
(iii) For any $M$ , $\lambda$ is a weak equivalence of L-spectra.

PROOF. Since $\lambda = \gamma \bowtie$ id on free $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $\mathbb { L } X$ , Theorem 2.5 and the lemma give (i). By Theorem 4.6(iii), (i) applies to show that $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ is a weak equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra when $M$ is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum. By the Whitehead theorem for CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, there is a map of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $\xi : M \longrightarrow S \land _ { \mathcal { L } } M$ such that $\lambda \circ \xi \simeq \mathrm { i d }$ To complete the proof of (ii), we must show that $\xi \circ \lambda \simeq \mathrm { i d }$ , and the following commutative diagram identifies this composite with id $\land \pmb { \mathscr { x } } ( \lambda \circ \xi )$ :

$$
\begin{array} { l } { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \xi } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \frac { \lambda } { \beta } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M } } \\ { ~ \overset { \lambda } { \underset { \cal { M } } { \wedge } } \qquad } \\ { M \xrightarrow { \xi } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \widehat { M } . } \end{array}
$$

The rectangle commutes by the naturality of $\lambda$ and the triangle commutes by Proposition 8.3. For (iii), let $M$ be arbitrary and consider the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { \pi _ { n } ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ) \cong h { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ) \xrightarrow { \lambda ^ { * } } h { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ) } \\ & { } & { \qquad \downarrow , } \\ & { } & { \pi _ { n } ( M ) \cong h { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , M ) \xrightarrow { \lambda ^ { * } } h { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , M ) . } \end{array}
$$

By (ii), $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }$ is a homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra, hence the horizontal arrows are isomorphisms. The right vertical arrow is an isomorphism since, for $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $K$ ,

$$
\lambda _ { * } : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } K , S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } K , M )
$$

is a natural isomorphism; its inverse sends $f : S \land _ { \mathcal { L } } K \longrightarrow M$ to the composite

$$
\begin{array} { r } { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } K \stackrel { \lambda ^ { - 1 } \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } K \stackrel { \mathrm { \ i d } \wedge f } { \longrightarrow } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M . } \end{array}
$$

(Compare II.1.3 below). Therefore the left vertical arrow is an isomorphism.

REMARK 8.6. The weak equivalence $\omega : \mathbb { L } S \land _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ of Proposition 6.2 is just the composite

$$
\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \pm } M { \xrightarrow { \xi \wedge { \mathrm { i d } } } } S \wedge _ { \pm } M { \xrightarrow { \lambda } } \qquad M .
$$

Therefore $\xi \wedge$ id is also a weak equivalence for all $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $M$ .

COROLLARy 8.7. For any L-spectrum $M$ $\begin{array} { r } { I , \tilde { \lambda } : M \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) } \end{array}$ is a weak equivalence of L-spectra.

PRooF. For a spectrum $X$ $\cdot , \tilde { \lambda } _ { * } : \mathcal { S } ( X , M ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } ( X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) )$ can be identified with $\tilde { \lambda } _ { * } : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X , M ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) )$ .In turn, by naturality and adjunction, this can be identified with

$$
\lambda ^ { * } : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X , M ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } X , M ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) ) .
$$

If $X$ is a CW spectrum, then $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } X \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } X$ is a homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, hence the displayed maps all induce isomorphisms on passage to homotopy classes of maps. The conclusion follows by letting $X$ run through the sphere spectra.

# CHAPTER II

# Structured ring and module spectra

We can now define and study our basic algebraic objects. We begin with the $S .$ -modules, which we think of as analogs of modules over a fixed commutative ring $k$ Since the category of $S .$ -modules is symmetric monoidal under its smash product, we can define $S \mathrm { . }$ -algebras and commutative $S .$ -algebras exactly as we define (associative and unital) $k \mathrm { . }$ -algebras and commutative $k \mathrm { . }$ algebras. Intuitively, $S ^ { }$ -algebras are as close as one can get to $k \mathrm { . }$ -algebras in stable homotopy theory, and commutative $S$ -algebras are as close as one can get to commutative $k$ -algebras. These basic definitions are established in the first three sections, and the material of the rest of the chapter will not be used again until Chapter VII. The reader is invited to skip directly from Section 3 to the applications in Chapters III-VI.

By analyzing free objects, we demonstrate that our new definitions are unital sharpenings of the definitions of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra that were first given in [48]. This allows us to use [48, 50] to supply examples and is therefore fundamentally important to the theory. We give a parallel analysis of the definitions of modules over $S _ { \ l }$ -algebras and over $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra. Our new definitions drastically simplify the study of these algebraic structures. For example, in a final categorical section, we prove that the new definitions lead to elementary categorical proofs that the categories of $S .$ -algebras and of commutative $S .$ -algebras are cocomplete, as was first proven by Hopkins and McClure [32] for the categories of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

# 1. The category of $S$ -modules

Here, finally, is the promised definition of $S \mathrm { . }$ -modules.

DEFiNitioN 1.1. Define an $S$ -module to be an L-spectrum $M$ which is unital in the sense that $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ is an isomorphism. Let $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ denote the full subcategory of ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ whose objects are the $S$ -modules. For $S$ -modules $M$ and $N$ , define

$$
M \wedge _ { S } N = M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \quad \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } \quad F _ { S } ( M , N ) = S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) .
$$

The justification for the name $^ { 6 6 } S$ -module" is given by the commutative diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { S \wedge _ { S } S \wedge _ { S } M \xrightarrow { \lambda \wedge \mathrm { i d } } \qquad S \wedge _ { S } M } & { { } \qquad M \xrightarrow { \lambda ^ { - 1 } } S \wedge _ { S } M } \\ { \overset { \mathrm { i d } } { \underset { { \scriptstyle \bigwedge } S } { \mathrm { i d } } M } \overset { \biggr | } { \underset { { \scriptstyle \bigwedge } \atop { \scriptstyle \bigwedge } } } } & { { } \qquad \overset { \biggr | { \lambda } } { \underset { { \scriptstyle \bigwedge } } { \mathrm { i d } } } } & { { } \qquad \overset { \mathrm { a n d } } { \underset { { \scriptstyle \bigwedge } } { \mathrm { i d } } } } \\ { S \wedge _ { S } M \xrightarrow { \lambda } \qquad M } & { { } } \end{array}
$$

For the definition to be useful, we need examples, and I.8.2 and I.8.3 provide many. We consistently retain the notation $M \land \_ N$ when the given $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $M$ and $N$ are not restricted to be $S .$ -modules.

PRoPosITIoN 1.2. For any based space $X$ , $\Sigma ^ { \infty } X$ is an $S$ -module, and

$$
\Sigma ^ { \infty } X \wedge _ { S } \Sigma ^ { \infty } Y \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( X \wedge Y ) .
$$

For any $S$ -module $M$ and any L-spectrum $N$ , $M \land \ u { x } \lor$ is an $\boldsymbol { S }$ -module. In particular, $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ is an $S$ -module for any L-spectrum $N$ .

ProoF. For the second statement, I.8.3 gives that $\lambda$ for $M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ is determined by $\lambda$ for $M$ and is therefore an isomorphism.

We have the following categorical relationship between ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and ${ \mathcal { M } } _ { S }$ .

LEMMA 1.3. The functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - ) : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ is left adjoint to the functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , - ) : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and right adjoint to the inclusion $\ell : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow$ ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .

ProoF. The first adjunction is immediate from I.7.1. For the second, let $M$ be an $S$ -module and $N$ be an $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum. A map $f : M \longrightarrow S \land \mathcal { \bot } N$ of $S .$ . modules determines a map $\lambda \circ f : M \longrightarrow N$ of L-spectra, and a map $g : M \longrightarrow N$ of L-spectra determines a map $( \operatorname { i d } \wedge g ) \circ \lambda ^ { - 1 } : M \longrightarrow S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ of $S .$ modules. Using I.8.3, we see that these are inverse bijections.

This implies that to lift right adjoint functors from ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ to $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ , we must first forget down to ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ , next apply the given functor, and then apply the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ . For example, limits in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ are created in this fashion.

ProposITIoN 1.4. The category of $S$ -modules is complete and cocomplete. Its colimits are created in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ . Its limits are created by applying the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to limits in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ . If $X$ is a based space and $M$ is an $S$ -module, then $M \wedge X$ is an $S$ -module, and the spectrum level cofiber of a map of $S$ -modules is an $S$ -module. For a based space $X$ and $S$ -modules $M$ and $N$ ,

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( M \wedge X , N ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( M , S \wedge _ { { \mathcal { L } } } F ( X , N ) ) .
$$

Moreover,

$$
M \wedge X \cong M \wedge _ { S } \Sigma ^ { \infty } X ~ a n d ~ S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F ( X , M ) \cong F _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } X , M ) .
$$

REMARK 1.5. By the path $s$ -module of an $\boldsymbol { s }$ -module $N$ we must understand $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } P N$ By the fiber of a map $f : M \longrightarrow N$ of $S \mathrm { . }$ -modules, we must understand $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F f$ . Lemma 1.3 implies that the following square of $S .$ -modules is a pullback and that its vertical arrows satisfy the CHP in the category of $S .$ -modules.

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { S \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } F f \longrightarrow S \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } P N } } \\ { { \Big \downarrow \qquad } } \\ { { M \qquad \qquad \longrightarrow N . } } \end{array}
$$

The resulting fiber sequences of $S \mathrm { \Omega }$ -modules behave in exactly the same fashion as fiber sequences of spaces or spectra.

Lemma 1.3 also explains our definition of function $S .$ -modules. Its second adjunction and the adjunction of Theorem 7.1 compose to give the adjunction displayed in the following theorem.

THEOREM 1.6. The category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ is symmetric monoidal under $\wedge _ { S }$ , and

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( M \land _ { S } N , P ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( M , F _ { S } ( N , P ) )
$$

for $_ { s }$ -modules $M , N$ ,and $P$ .

A homotopy in the category of $S _ { \mathbf { \Psi } }$ -modules is a map $M \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow N$ A map of $S .$ . modules is a weak equivalence if it is a weak equivalence as a map of spectra. The derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ of $S$ -modules is constructed from the homotopy category $h \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ by adjoining formal inverses to the weak equivalences; again, the process is made rigorous by CW approximation. The free $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $\mathbb { L } X$ are not $S .$ -modules, and we define sphere $S$ -modules by

$$
S _ { S } ^ { n } \equiv S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }
$$

and use them as the domains of attaching maps when defining cell and CW $S .$ -modules. Observe that, by I.8.7 and Lemma 1.3, we have

$$
\pi _ { n } ( M ) \equiv h { \mathcal { S } } ( S ^ { n } , M ) \cong h { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , F _ { { \mathcal { L } } } ( S , M ) ) \cong h { \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( S _ { S } ^ { n } , M )
$$

for $S .$ modules $M$ From here, the theory of cell and CW $S \mathrm { . }$ -modules is exactly like the theory of cell and CW spectra and is obtained by specialization of the theory of cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules to be presented in Chapter III. A weak equivalence of cell $S$ -modules is a homotopy equivalence, any $S ^ { }$ -module is weakly equivalent to a CW $S .$ module, and $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ is equivalent to the homotopy category of CW $S .$ . modules. Again, as we shall explain in Remark 1.10, the $S .$ -module $s$ does not have the homotopy type of a CW $\boldsymbol { S }$ -module. When working homotopically, we replace it with $\mathit { S s } \equiv \mathit { S _ { S } ^ { 0 } }$ .

The following comparison between CW $s$ -modules and CW $\mathbb { L }$ spectra establishes an equivalence between $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ and $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and thus between $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ and $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ .

THEOREM 1.9. The following conclusions hold.

(i) The functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - ) : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ carries CW L-spectra to CW $_ { s }$ -modules.

(ii) The forgetful functor $\mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ carries $S$ -modules of the homotopy types of CW $s$ -modules to L-spectra of the homotopy types of CW Lspectra.   
(ii) Every CW $S$ -module $M$ is homotopy equivalent as an $s$ -module to $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ $N$ for some $C W \mathbb { L }$ -spectrum $N$ .   
(iv) The unit $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ is a weak equivalence for all L-spectra M and is a homotopy equivalence of L-spectra if $M$ has the homotopy type of a CW L-spectrum.

The functors $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ and the forgetful functor establish an adjoint equivalence between the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ . This equivalence of categories preserves smash products and function spectra.

ProoF. Part (i) is immediate by induction up the sequential filtration since the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ preserves spheres, cones, and colimits. Part (iv) is a recapitulation of I.8.5 and, applied to sphere $S .$ -modules, it implies part (ii). Part (iii) follows from (i) and (iv) since there is a CW $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $M ^ { \prime }$ and a homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $M ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow M$ The claimed adjoint equivalence of categories is immediate from part (iv). For smash products, the last statement is clear from (ii) and the fact that the smash product $M \land _ { S } N$ of $S .$ -modules is their smash product as $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. The statement for function spectra follows formally.

When doing classical homotopy theory, we can work interchangeably in $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ $\bar { h } \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ ,or $\mathcal { D } _ { S }$ . These three categories are equivalent, and the equivalences preserve all structure in sight. When working on the point set level, we have reached a nearly ideal situation with our construction of $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . We pause to comment on Lewis' observation [37] that there is no fully ideal situation.

REMARK 1.10. Suppose given a symmetric monoidal category of spectra with a suspension spectrum functor $\Sigma ^ { \infty }$ such that $S = \Sigma ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 }$ is the unit for the smash product, denoted $\wedge _ { S }$ , and there is a natural isomorphism

$$
\Sigma ^ { \infty } X \wedge _ { S } \Sigma ^ { \infty } Y \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( X \wedge Y )
$$

that is suitably compatible with the coherence isomorphisms for the unity, associativity, and commutativity of the respective smash products. Our category of $S .$ -modules satisfies all of these properties, and many other desiderata not included among Lewis's axioms. Suppose further that $\Sigma ^ { \infty }$ has a right adjoint ${ \mathfrak { s o } } \infty ^ { * }$ and let $Q X = \mathrm { c o l i m } \Omega ^ { n } \Sigma ^ { n } X$ . Then Lewis observes that there cannot be a natural weak equivalence

$$
\theta : { } ^ { \mathfrak { s o s } , \Sigma ^ { \infty } X \longrightarrow Q X }
$$

such that $\theta \circ \eta : X \longrightarrow Q X$ is the natural inclusion, where $\eta$ is the unit of the adjunction. In our context, we have the two adjunction homeomorphisms

$$
\mathscr { M } _ { S } ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } \Sigma ^ { \infty } X , M ) \cong \mathscr { T } ( X , \Omega ^ { \infty } F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) )
$$

and

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } , X , M ) \cong \mathcal { T } ( X , \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( S , M ) ) ,
$$

where $\mathcal { T }$ is the category of based spaces; see VII§2 for discussion of these topologized Hom sets and of the second of these adjunctions. It is a standard property of any symmetric monoidal category that the self-maps of the unit object form a commutative monoid under composition. In our situation $\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( S , S )$ is therefore a commutative topological monoid. It cannot be weakly equivalent to $\boldsymbol { Q } \boldsymbol { S } ^ { 0 }$ , but $\Omega ^ { \infty } F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , S )$ is weakly equivalent to $Q S ^ { 0 }$ Therefore the weak equivalence $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S \longrightarrow S$ cannot be a homotopy equivalence of $S$ -modules and $s$ cannot be of the homotopy type of a CW $S .$ -module.

# 2. The mirror image to the category of $S \cdot$ modules

The categorical picture becomes clearer when we realize that the category of $S .$ -modules has a "mirror image" category to which it is naturally equivalent. We find this material quite illuminating, but it will not be used until our discussion of Quillen model categories. The reader may prefer to skip it on a first reading.

DEFInITIoN 2.1. Define $\mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ to be the full subcategory of ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ whose objects are those L-spectra $N$ that are counital, in the sense that $\tilde { \lambda } : \bar { N } \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , N )$ is an isomorphism.

Looking through the mirror at Lemma 1.3 and noting that mirrors interchange left and right, we see the following reflection.

LEMMA 2.2. The functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , - ) : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ is right adjoint to the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( - \right) : \mathcal { M } ^ { s } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and left adjoint to the inclusion $r : \mathcal { M } ^ { S } \longrightarrow$ ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .

We agree to write

$$
f = F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , - ) : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ^ { S } \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad s = S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - ) : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }
$$

in the rest of this section. With this notation, Lemmas 1.3 and 2.2 give the following mirrored pairs of adjunctions, the upper arrow being left adjoint to the lower arrow in each case.

$$
\leq | \mathbb { L } | \frac { s } { \sum \limits _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } \mu _ { i } } \mathcal { M } _ { S } \frac { \ell } { \leq s } \mathcal { S } | \mathbb { L } | \quad \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } \quad \mathcal { S } | \mathbb { L } | \frac { f } { \leq \frac { f } { r } } \mathcal { M } ^ { S } \frac { \ell s r _ { * } } { \leq \frac { f } { f } } \mathcal { S } | \mathbb { L } | .
$$

The display makes new information visible. The composite of the first two left adjoints is just the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ and the composite of the second two right adjoints is just the functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , - )$ . Since these two endo-functors of ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ are left and right adjoint, they must be equivalent to their displayed composite adjoints.

LEMMA 2.5. For L-spectra $M$ , the maps

$$
\operatorname { i d } \wedge _ { \pm } \tilde { \lambda } : S \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } M \longrightarrow S \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } F _ { \mathscr { L } } ( S , M )
$$

and

$$
F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathrm { i d } , \lambda ) : F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ) \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M )
$$

are natural isomorphisms.

We now see that the reflection of a reflection is equivalent to the original.

PROposiTION 2.6. The functors

$$
f \ell : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ^ { S } \quad a n d \quad s r : \mathcal { M } ^ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }
$$

are inverse equivalences of categories. More precisely

$$
\varepsilon : s r f \ell M = S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) \longrightarrow M
$$

is an isomorphism for $M \in \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ , and

$$
\eta : N \longrightarrow F _ { { \mathcal { L } } } ( S , S \wedge _ { { \mathcal { L } } } N ) = f \ell s r N
$$

is an isomorphism for $N \in \mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ , where $\varepsilon$ and $\eta$ are the unit and counit of the $( S \land { \mathcal { \ L } } ( - ) , F { \mathcal { \ L } } ( S , - ) )$ adjunction.

PROoF. The functor $s \ell : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ is an equivalence, and it is left adjoint to the composite $s r f \ell : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . The functor $f r : \mathcal { M } ^ { s } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ^ { s }$ is an equivalence, and it is right adjoint to the composite $f \ell s r$ . Therefore these two composites are natural equivalences. A little diagram chase from the previous lemma gives the more precise statement. □

PRoposiTION 2.7. The category $\mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ , hence also the category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ , is equivalent to the category of algebras over the monad $r f$ in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ determined by the adjunction $( f , r )$ . The category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ , hence also the category $\mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ , is equivalent to the category of coalgebras over the comonad ls in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ determined by the adjunction $( \ell , s )$ .

PROOF. The unit of the monad $r f$ is $\tilde { \lambda } : { \cal M } \longrightarrow { \cal F } _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , { \cal M } ) = r f { \cal M }$ and its product is the natural isomorphism

$$
\mu : r f r f M = F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) ) \cong F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) = r f M
$$

implied by the isomorphism $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \cong S$ Clearly, if $\tilde { \lambda }$ is an isomorphism, then $M$ is an $r f .$ algebra with action $\tilde { \lambda } ^ { - 1 }$ . Conversely if $\xi : r f M \longrightarrow M$ is an action, then $\xi \circ \tilde { \lambda } = \mathrm { i d }$ and the following is a split coequalizer diagram in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .

$$
r f r f M \xrightarrow [ \mu ] { r f \xi } r f M \xrightarrow [ ] { \xi } M .
$$

Applying $f$ , we obtain a split coequalizer diagram in $\mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ . Since the counit $f r \longrightarrow \mathrm { i d }$ of the adjunction is an isomorphism, it induces an isomorphism of diagrams

$$
( f r f r f M \longrightarrow f r f M ) \qquad \longrightarrow \qquad ( f r f M \longrightarrow f M ) .
$$

Applying $r , \ r f M$ is the (split) coequalizer of the first and $M$ is the (split) coequalizer of the second. The resulting isomorphism $r f M \longrightarrow M$ is just the map $\xi$ ,hence is an isomorphism of $r f$ algebras.

# 3. $S$ -algebras and their modules

Let $\mathscr { C }$ be any symmetric monoidal category, with product $\sqcap$ and unit object $\boldsymbol { I }$ Then a monoid in $\mathscr { C }$ is an object $R$ together with maps $\eta : I \longrightarrow R$ and $\phi : R \boxed { { \bf { \mathscr { A } } } \longrightarrow R }$ such that the evident associativity and unity diagrams commute; $R$ is a commutative monoid if the evident commutativity diagram also commutes. A left $R$ -object over a monoid $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an object $M$ of $\mathscr { C }$ with a map $\mu : R \square M \longrightarrow M$ such that the evident unity and associativity diagrams commute, and right $R \mathrm { . }$ . objects are defined by symmetry. These definitions apply to our symmetric monoidal category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ .

Definition 3.1. An $S$ -algebra is a monoid in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ A commutative $S .$ -algebra is a commutative monoid in ${ \mathcal { M } } _ { S }$ . For an $S .$ -algebra or commutative $S$ algebra $R$ , a left or right $R _ { ☉ }$ -module is a left or right $R$ -object in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . Modules will mean left modules unless otherwise specified, and we let $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ denote the category of left $R$ -modules.

Observe that if $R$ is a commutative $S$ -algebra, then an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module is just a module over $R$ regarded as an $S$ -algebra, as in module theory in algebra. For this reason, even though our main interest is in the much richer commutative context, we work with general $S .$ -algebras wherever possible.

We insert the following lemma for later reference. It records specializations of observations that apply to monoids in any symmetric monoidal category.

LEMMA 3.2. Let $R$ be an $S$ -algebra and $M$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. Then the following diagrams of $s$ -modules are split coequalizers:

$$
R \wedge _ { S } R \wedge _ { S } R \underset { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \phi } { \xrightarrow { \phi \wedge \mathrm { i d } } } \quad \underset { \ r { \rtimes } \mathscr { S } } { \ m } R \wedge _ { S } R \underset { \ r { \longrightarrow } \mathscr { R } } { \longrightarrow } R .
$$

and

$$
R \wedge _ { S } R \wedge _ { S } M \frac { \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { \mu } } { \phi \wedge \mathrm { i d } } \quad \sharp R \wedge _ { S } M \longrightarrow M .
$$

While we have given the most conceptual form of the definitions, it is worthwhile to write out the relevant diagrams explicitly. We find that they make perfect sense for $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra that might not be $S .$ -modules, and this leads us back to the earlier notions of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and their modules.

Definition 3.3. An $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum is an L-spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ with a unit map $\eta : S \longrightarrow R$ and a product $\phi : R \land \mathcal { L } R \longrightarrow R$ such that the following diagrams commute:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \overset { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \underline { { \overset { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \underline { { \overset { \mathrm { i d } } {  } } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S } { \neg } } \\ { \overset { \not \downarrow } { \longrightarrow } \qquad \overset { \not \downarrow } { \longrightarrow } \qquad \lambda _ { \mathcal { T } } } \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { R \wedge \_ { \mathcal { L } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \stackrel { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \phi } { \longrightarrow } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R } \\ & { } & { \phi \wedge \mathrm { i d } \Bigg \downarrow \qquad } \\ & { } & { R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \longrightarrow R ; \qquad \phi \qquad \ R ; } \end{array}
$$

$R$ is an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum if the following diagram also commutes:

$$
\frac { R \land \mathbf { \mathcal { L } } R \operatorname { \mathcal { R } } \longrightarrow R \land \mathbf { \mathcal { L } } R } { \displaystyle \sum _ { \phi \setminus \mathbf { \mathcal { L } } _ { R } } \int _ { \phi } ^ { \phi } }
$$

A module over an $A _ { \infty }$ or $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $M$ with a map $\mu : R \land _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ such that the following diagrams commute:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \underset { \mathbf { \mu } \times \mathbf { \dot { \pi } } \times \mathbf { \pi } \times \mathcal { L } } { \underbrace { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M } & { \qquad \ R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \underset { \mathbf { \mu } \times \mathbf { \pi } \times \mathbf { \pi } \times \mathbf { \pi } \times \mathcal { L } } { \underbrace { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \mu } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M } \\ { \overset { \psi } { M } } & { \qquad \mathrm { a n d } \qquad } \\ { \overset { R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } } { M } } & { \qquad \ R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M . } \end{array}
$$

LEMMA 3.4. An $S$ -algebra or commutative $S$ -algebra is an $A _ { \infty }$ or $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum which is also an $_ { s }$ -module. A module over an $s$ -algebra or commutative $s$ -algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ is a module over $\scriptstyle { R }$ , regarded as an $A _ { \infty }$ or $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum, which is also an $S$ -module.

In view of Proposition 1.2, this leads to the following observations.

PRoPosITIoN 3.5. The following statements hold.

(i) $S$ is a commutative $\boldsymbol { S }$ -algebra with unit id and product $\lambda$ .   
ii) If $R$ and $R ^ { \prime }$ are $A _ { \infty }$ or $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, then so is $R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime }$ ; if either $\scriptstyle { R }$ or $R ^ { \prime }$ is an $S$ -algebra, then so is $R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime }$ .   
iv) If $R$ and $R ^ { \prime }$ are $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, $M$ is an $R$ -module and $M ^ { \prime }$ is an $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ - module, then $M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ^ { \prime }$ is an $R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime }$ -module.

In particular, we have a functorial way to replace $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and their modules by $S _ { \ l }$ -algebras and commutative $S .$ -algebras and their modules.

CoroLLAry 3.6. For an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ , $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R$ is an $S$ -algebra and $\lambda : S \land \mathcal { L } \ R \longrightarrow R$ is a weak equivalence of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, and similarly in the $E _ { \infty }$ case. If $M$ is an $R$ -module, then $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M$ is an $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R$ -module and $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow M$ is a weak equivalence of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and of modules over $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R$ regarded as an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum.

Recall that the tensor product of commutative rings is their coproduct in the category of commutative rings. The proof consists of categorical diagram chases that apply to commutative monoids in any symmetric monoidal category.

PROpOsiTION 3.7. If $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $R ^ { \prime }$ are commutative $S$ -algebras, then $R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime }$ is the coproduct of $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $R ^ { \prime }$ in the category of commutative $S$ -algebras.

We shall construct coproducts in the category of $S$ -algebras in Section 7, where we show more generally that the categories of $s$ -algebras and of commutative $S$ -algebras are cocomplete.

There is a version of the proposition that is true for $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, but this is not obvious. We shall return to this point in Chapter XIII, where we show that the category of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra under $\boldsymbol { S }$ is symmetric monoidal under a modified smash product $\star s$ and that $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra are exactly the monoids and commutative monoids in that symmetric monoidal category. This was the starting point for the earlier version of the present theory announced in [23].

# 4. Free $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra; comparisons of definitions

We focus on $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra here. It was proven in [48, 50] that various Thom spectra, Eilenberg-Mac Lane spectra, and connective algebraic and topological $\kappa$ -theory spectra are $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra. Using the results stated in the previous section, we can convert these $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra to weakly equivalent commutative $S .$ -algebras. However, on the face of it, the original definitions of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra appear to be different from those that we have given here. As in algebra, it is important to understand free $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, and we shall use this understanding to verify that our present definitions agree with the original ones.

There is no difficulty in constructing the relevant monads. In fact, we shall construct two pairs of monads and then relate them. The first is defined on the ground category of spectra and is transparently related to the earlier definitions. The second is defined on the ground category of $S$ -modules and is transparently related to the present definitions. The connection between them will establish the required equivalence of definitions. In effect, our new definition of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra is obtained from the old one simply by factoring the original defining monad $\mathbb { C }$ in $\mathcal { S }$ through a new defining monad $\mathbb { P }$ in the more highly structured category ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ .

ConsTRucTIoN 4.1. Construct monads $\mathbb { B }$ and $\mathbb { C }$ in the category of spectra as follows. Let $X$ be a spectrum and let $X ^ { j }$ be its $j$ -fold external smash power, with $X ^ { 0 } = S ^ { 0 }$ . Define

$$
{ \mathbb { B } } X \cong \bigvee _ { j \geq 0 } { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes X ^ { j }
$$

and

$$
\mathbb { C } X \cong \bigvee _ { j \geq 0 } \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes \Sigma _ { j } X ^ { j } ,
$$

where ${ \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes \Sigma _ { j } X ^ { j }$ is the orbit spectrum $( \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes X ^ { j } ) / \Sigma _ { j }$ . The units of these monads are induced by the unit maps ${ \cal X } \cong \{ 1 \} \ltimes { \cal X } \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes { \cal X }$ Their products are induced by wedge sums of maps induced by the structure maps $\gamma$ of the linear isometries operad $\mathcal { L }$ .

The notion of an $\mathcal { L }$ -spectrum was defined in [38, VII.2.1]. The definition used permutations, and there is a corresponding notion of a non- $\Sigma$ $\mathcal { L }$ -spectrum. An immediate comparison of definitions gives the following result.

PROPosITION 4.2. The category of $\mathbb { B }$ -algebras is isomorphic to the category of non $\Sigma$ $\mathcal { L }$ -spectra. The category of $\mathbb { C }$ -algebras is isomorphic to the category of $\mathcal { L }$ -spectra.

Actually, $\mathcal { O }$ -spectra were defined in [38, VII.2.1] for any operad $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ that is augmented over $\mathcal { L }$ . An $E _ { \infty }$ operad is one such that each $\mathcal { O } ( j )$ is $\Sigma _ { j }$ -free and contractible. In earlier work, $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra were understood to mean ${ \mathcal { O } } .$ . spectra for any $E _ { \infty }$ operad $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ augmented over $\mathcal { L }$ . The present theory is based on properties that are special to $\mathcal { L }$ .The following result, which will be proven in $\mathrm { { X I I \ S 1 } }$ , shows that restriction to $\mathcal { L }$ results in no loss of generality. There is an analogue for $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra that is obtained by forgetting about permutations.

PROPOSITION 4.3. Let $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ be an $E _ { \infty }$ operad over $\mathcal { L }$ . There is a functor $V$ that assigns a weakly equivalent $\mathcal { L }$ -spectrum $V R$ to an $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ -spectrum $R$ .

ConsTRucTIoN 4.4. Construct monads $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ in the category of L-spectra as follows. Let $M$ be an L-spectrum and let $M ^ { j }$ be its $j { \cdot }$ -fold power with respect to $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ , with $M ^ { 0 } = S$ Define

$$
\mathbb { T } M \cong \bigcup _ { j \geq 0 } M ^ { j }
$$

and

$$
\mathbb { P } M \cong \bigvee _ { j \geq 0 } M ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j } .
$$

Here passage to orbits preserves $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra since it is a finite colimit. The unit is the inclusion of $M = M ^ { 1 }$ . The product is induced by the maps

$$
M ^ { j _ { 1 } } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \dots \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M ^ { j _ { k } } \longrightarrow M ^ { j _ { 1 } + \dots + j _ { k } }
$$

that are given by the evident identifications if each $j _ { r } \geq 1$ and by use of the unit map $\lambda$ if any $j _ { r } = 0$ Observe that $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ restrict to monads in the category of $S$ -modules.

The letters $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ are mnemonic for "tensor algebra" and "polynomial" (or symmetric) algebra. As is clear for $S$ -modules and will be made explicit in Definition 7.1, the definitions fit into a general categorical framework that includes those constructions. The following result is an easy direct consequence of our definitions.

PRoPosITIoN 4.5. The categories of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and of $S$ -algebras are isomorphic to the categories of T-algebras in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and of T-algebras in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ The categories of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and of commutative $S$ -algebras are isomorphic to the categories of P-algebras in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and of $\mathbb { P }$ -algebras in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ .

To relate the monads $\mathbb { B }$ and $\mathbb { C }$ to the monads $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ , recall from I.4.2 that the category of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra is the category of $\mathbb { L }$ algebras in $\mathcal { S }$ Together with Propositions 4.2 and 4.5, the following result gives the promised comparison between the old and new definitions of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

PropositIoN 4.6. The monads $\mathbb { B }$ and TL are isomorphic, hence the categories of non $\boldsymbol { \Sigma }$ $\mathcal { L }$ -spectra and of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra are isomorphic. The monads $\mathbb { C }$ and $\mathbb { P L }$ are isomorphic, hence the categories of $\mathcal { L }$ -spectra and of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra are isomorphic.

ProoF. The isomorphisms on objects are immediate from I.5.6 applied to L-spectra $M _ { i } = \mathbb { L } X _ { i }$ . Since these isomorphisms are induced from the structure maps $\gamma$ of $\mathcal { L }$ , the comparison of monad structures is immediate. In both statements, the second clause is a categorical consequence of the first, as we shall show in Lemma 6.1 below.

REMARK 4.7. Observe that we have quotient maps of monads $\mathbb { B } \longrightarrow \mathbb { C }$ and $\mathbb { T } \longrightarrow \mathbb { P }$ . In Section 6, we shall give categorical definitions that show how to exploit these maps to construct an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\mathbb { C } \otimes _ { \mathbb { B } } R$ (or $\mathbb { P } \otimes _ { \mathbb { T } } R )$ from an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ by "passage to quotients", just as we construct commutative algebras as quotients of associative algebras; see Lemma 6.7 and Corollary 7.3. Formally, $\mathbb { C } \otimes _ { \mathbb { B } } R$ is a coequalizer of a right action of $\mathbb { B }$ on $\mathbb { C }$ and the given action of $\mathbb { B }$ on $\scriptstyle { R }$ .

REMARK 4.8. Passage to orbits and passage to coequalizers are often hard to analyze homotopically. We show how to deal with the first difficulty in III§5, where we show that symmetric powers and extended powers of $S$ -modules (and, more generally, $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules) are essentially equivalent. One often circumvents the second difficulty by replacing a construction like $\mathbb { C } \otimes _ { \mathbb { B } } R$ with its associated bar construction $B ( \mathbb { C } , \mathbb { B } , R )$ , which we shall introduce in $\mathrm { { X I I \ S 1 } }$ .

REMARK 4.9. There are reduced monads $\tilde { \mathbb { B } }$ and $\tilde { \mathbb { C } }$ in the category $\mathcal { S } \backslash S$ of spectra under $S$ and $\tilde { \mathbb { T } }$ and $\tilde { \mathbb { P } }$ in the category $\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \backslash S$ of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra under $S$ . They are constructed from the unreduced monads by unit map identifications similar to the basepoint identifications in the James construction or the infinite symmetric product. Observe that $\mathcal { S } \backslash S$ is the category of algebras over the monad $\mathbb { U }$ that is specified by $\mathbb { U } X = X \vee S$ , with product given by the folding map $S \vee S \longrightarrow S$ , and similarly for ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] \backslash S$ In all four cases, the unreduced monad is the composite of the reduced monad with $\mathbb { U }$ ,hence, by Lemma 6.1 below, the reduced and unreduced monads have the same algebras. The difference is that, when considering the reduced monad, one is considering the unit map $S \longrightarrow R$ as preassigned and then ensuring that the unit map created by the monad action coincides with it. It follows that the monad $\mathbb { U }$ acts from the right on the unreduced monads, and it is easy to write down this action directly. The reduced monad $\tilde { \mathbb { C } }$ can then be constructed from $\mathbb { C }$ by setting ${ \tilde { \mathbb { C } } } X = \mathbb { C } \otimes _ { \mathbb { U } } { \tilde { X } }$ for a spectrum $X$ under $S$ , with structure maps induced by passage to coequalizers, and similarly for our other monads. A more explicit description is given in [38, VII§3], where $\tilde { \mathbb { C } }$ is denoted by $\mathbb { C }$ While the monad $\mathbb { C }$ is more convenient for formal work, the monad $\tilde { \mathbb { C } }$ is of far greater homotopical interest.

# 5. Free modules over $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra

There is an analogue for modules of the original explicit definition of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra in terms of twisted half-smash products, and there is an analogous comparison of definitions.

ProposITIoN 5.1. The category of modules over an $\mathcal { L }$ -spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ is isomorphic to the category of spectra $M$ together with associative, unital, and, in the $E _ { \infty }$ context, equivariant systems of action maps

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes \left( R ^ { j - 1 } \wedge M \right) \longrightarrow M . } \end{array}
$$

Since we shall not need the details, we shall not write out the relevant diagrams. They make sense for any operad $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ augmented over $\mathcal { L }$ , and they are exact analogs of diagrams that are written out in the context of algebraic operads in [35, I.4.1]. Remarkably, with this alternative form of the definition, it is far from obvious that a module over an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ is the same thing as a module over $\scriptstyle { R }$ regarded as an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum. In fact, this appears to be false in the context of modules over an ${ \mathcal { O } } .$ -spectrum $R$ for a general $E _ { \infty }$ operad $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ augmented over $\mathcal { L }$ .However, we have the following analogue of Proposition 4.3, which will be proven in $\mathrm { { X I I \ S 1 } }$ . Again, there is an analogue for $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and modules that is obtained by forgetting about permutations.

PROPOSITION 5.2. Let $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ be an $E _ { \infty }$ operad over $\mathcal { L }$ and $R$ be an $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ -spectrum. There is a functor $V$ that assigns a weakly equivalent $V R$ -module to an $R$ -module $M$ , where $V R$ is the $\mathcal { L }$ -spectrum of Proposition 4.3.

There is a conceptual monadic proof of Proposition 5.1 that is based on analogs of Propositions 4.2, 4.5, and 4.6. To carry out this argument, we need to know that there is a free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module functor. This is obvious enough when we are considering $S$ -modules: $R \wedge _ { S } M$ is then the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module generated by an $S$ -module $M$ . For a general $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ and an L-spectrum $M$ , $R \land \dotsc M$ is an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module but, since $M$ need not be isomorphic to $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M$ , it is not the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module generated by $M$ .

DEFINITION 5.3. For an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ and an L-spectrum $M$ , define an $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum $\mathbb { R } M$ and maps of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $\pi : R \land \_ { \mathcal { L } } M \longrightarrow \mathbb { R } M$ and $\eta : M \longrightarrow$ $\mathbb { R } M$ by the pushout diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M \xrightarrow { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M } \\ { \bigwedge _ { \begin{array} { c } { \lambda } \\ { M } \end{array} } } \end{array}
$$

Dually, define an L-spectrum $\mathbb { R } ^ { \# } M$ by the pullback diagram

$$
\begin{array} { c c } { \mathbb { R } ^ { \# } M \longrightarrow } { \cal M }  \\ { \downarrow } \\ { F _ { { \mathcal { L } } } ( R , M ) \xrightarrow [ F ( \eta , \mathbf { i } \overline { { \mathbf { d } } } ) ] { } F _ { { \mathcal { L } } } ( S , M ) . } \end{array}
$$

These are special cases of general constructions to be studied in Chapter XIII. Such constructions permeated earlier versions of the present theory. Of course, $\pi$ is an isomorphism if $M$ is an $S _ { \ l }$ -module. As will be generalized in XIII.1.4, we deduce the following homotopical property by applying the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to the defining pushout diagram.

PROPOsITION 5.4. The map $\pi : R \land \mathcal { L } M \longrightarrow \mathbb { R } M$ is a weak equivalence for any L-spectrum $M$ .

The unit diagram of an $R$ -module $M$ ensures that its product factors through a map $\mathbb { R } M \longrightarrow M$ . More formally, elementary inspections of definitions give the following result.

PROPOSITION 5.5. Let $R$ be an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum. Then $\mathbb { R }$ is a monad in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ with unit $\eta : M \longrightarrow \mathbb { R } M$ and with product $\mu : \mathbb { R R } \longrightarrow \mathbb { R }$ induced from the product $\phi : R \land \mathcal { L } \ R \longrightarrow R$ . A left $R$ -module is an algebra over the monad $\mathbb { R }$ and, for an L-spectrum $M$ , RM is the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module generated by $M$ The functor $\mathbb { R } ^ { \# }$ is right adjoint to $\mathbb { R }$ and is therefore a comonad in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ such that an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module is a coalgebra over $\mathbb { R } ^ { \# }$ .

It is logical to denote the category of $\scriptstyle R$ -modules by ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] [ \mathbb { R } ]$ , reserving the notation $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ for the case when $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an $S .$ -algebra and $R$ -modules are required to be $S .$ -modules. We have freeness and cofreeness adjunctions

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] [ \mathbb { R } ] ( \mathbb { R } M , N ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , N )
$$

and

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] [ \mathbb { R } ] ( N , \mathbb { R } ^ { \# } M ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( N , M )
$$

for $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $M$ and $R$ -modules $N$ .

Clearly there results a composite adjunction that starts with spectra.

PRopositIoN 5.6. For a spectrum $X$ , define $\mathbb { F } X = \mathbb { R } X X$ . Then $\mathbb { F } X$ is the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module generated by $X$ . Thus

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] [ \mathbb { R } ] ( \mathbb { F } X , N ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( X , N )
$$

for an $R$ -module $N$ . Dually, define $\mathbb { F } ^ { \# } X = \mathbb { R } ^ { \# } \mathbb { L } ^ { \# } X$ .Then $\mathbb { F } ^ { \# } X$ is the cofree $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module generated by $X$ , so that

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] [ \mathbb { R } ] ( N , \mathbb { F } ^ { \# } X ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( N , X ) .
$$

In Construction 6.2, we shall show how to combine the monads of the previous section with these free module constructions to obtain monads $\mathbb { B } [ 1 ]$ and $\mathbb { C } [ 1 ]$ in the category of pairs of spectra such that a $\mathbb { B } [ 1 ]$ -algebra or $\mathbb { C } [ 1 ]$ algebra $( R ; M )$ is an $A _ { \infty }$ or $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ together with an $R$ -module $M$ in the alternative operad action sense described in Proposition 5.1. The construction will also give monads $\mathbb { T } [ 1 ]$ and $\mathbb { P } [ 1 ]$ in the category of pairs of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra such that a $\mathbb { T } [ 1 ] .$ . algebra or $\mathbb { P } [ 1 ]$ -algebra $( R ; M )$ is an $A _ { \infty }$ or $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ together with an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ in the sense of Definition 3.3. The monad $\mathbb { B } [ 1 ]$ has the general form

$$
\mathbb { B } [ 1 ] ( X ; Y ) = ( \mathbb { B } X ; \mathbb { B } ( X ; Y ) ) ,
$$

and similarly in the other three cases. Propositions 4.6 and 5.5, together with inspection of the cited construction, directly imply the following analogue of Proposition 4.6. By Lemma 6.1, this in turn implies Proposition 5.1.

PropositION 5.7. The monads $\mathbb { B } [ 1 ]$ and $\mathbb { T } [ 1 ] \circ ( \mathbb { L } , \mathbb { L } )$ are isomorphic. The monads C[1] and $\mathbb { P } [ 1 ] \circ ( \mathbb { L } , \mathbb { L } )$ are isomorphic. The second coordinates of the four monads are given explicitly as follows. Applied to a pair of spectra $( X ; Y )$ ,

$$
{ \mathbb { B } } ( X ; Y ) = \bigvee _ { j \geq 1 } { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes ( X ^ { j - 1 } \wedge Y )
$$

and

$$
\mathbb { C } ( X ; Y ) = \bigvee _ { j \geq 1 } \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes \Sigma _ { j - 1 } \big ( X ^ { j - 1 } \wedge Y \big ) .
$$

Applied to a pair of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $( M ; N )$ ,

$$
\mathbb { T } ( M ; N ) = \bigvee _ { j \geq 1 } M ^ { j - 1 } \wedge s N
$$

and

$$
\mathbb { P } ( M ; N ) = \bigvee _ { j \geq 1 } ( M ^ { j - 1 } / \Sigma _ { j - 1 } ) \wedge _ { S } N .
$$

If $N$ is an $S$ -module, then so are $\mathbb { T } ( M ; N )$ and $\mathbb { P } ( M ; N )$ .

ReMARK 5.8. Construction 6.2 applies equally well to give reduced versions of our four monads, giving monads in the category of pairs (of spectra or $\mathbb { L } _ { - }$ spectra), the first coordinate of which lies under $S$ The monad $\tilde { \mathbb { B } } [ 1 ]$ has the form

$$
\tilde { \mathbb { B } } [ 1 ] ( X ; Y ) = ( \tilde { \mathbb { B } } X ; \tilde { \mathbb { B } } ( X ; Y ) )
$$

and similarly in the other three cases. Inspection of definitions shows that

$$
\tilde { \mathbb { B } } S = \tilde { \mathbb { C } } S = S \quad \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } \quad \tilde { \mathbb { B } } ( S ; Y ) = \tilde { \mathbb { C } } ( S ; Y ) = \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes Y .
$$

This fact dictates our original definition of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra and is thus the conceptual starting point of our entire theory.

# 6. Composites of monads and monadic tensor products

In this section and the next, we collect a number of purely categorical observations and constructions that are needed in our work. We shall return to these topics in Chapter VII, but we shall make no further use of this material until then. The reader may prefer to skip these sections on a first reading. We here give the description of algebras over composite monads that was at the heart of our comparisons of definitions and formalize the tensor product construction that appeared briefly in Section 4.

LEMMA 6.1. Let S be a monad in a category $\mathcal { C }$ and let T be a monad in the category C[S] of S-algebras. Then the category $\mathscr { C }$ [S][T] of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras in ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ is isomorphic to the category ${ \mathcal { C } } [ \mathbb { T } \mathbb { S } ]$ of algebras over the composite monad TS in $\mathscr { C }$ . Moreover, the unit of T defines a map $\mathbb { S } \longrightarrow \mathbb { T S }$ of monads in $\mathscr { C }$ . An analogous assertion holds for comonads.

ProoF. Strictly speaking, in constructing $\mathbb { T S }$ , we are regarding $\mathbb { S }$ as the free ${ \mathbb S } .$ -algebra functor $\mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { S } ]$ , applying the functor $\mathbb { T } .$ ,and then applying the forgetful functor back to $\mathscr { C }$ . We continue to neglect notation for forgetful functors and to write $\mathbb { S }$ and $\mathbb { T }$ ambiguously for both the given monads and the resulting free functors. The unit of $\mathbb { T S }$ is given by the composite of unit maps

$$
X \longrightarrow \mathbb { S } X \longrightarrow \mathbb { T S } X .
$$

The product of $\mathbb { T S }$ is given by the composite maps

$$
\mathbb { T S } \mathbb { T S } X \longrightarrow \mathbb { T T S } X \longrightarrow \mathbb { T S } X ,
$$

where the second arrow is given by the product of $\mathbb { T }$ and the first is obtained by application of $\mathbb { T }$ to the action $\mathbb { S } \mathbb { T S } X \longrightarrow \mathbb { T S } X$ given by the fact that $\mathbb { T }$ takes $\mathbb { S }$ -algebras to $\mathbb { S }$ algebras. If $\scriptstyle R$ is a $\mathbb { T }$ algebra in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { S } ]$ , with action $\xi$ by $\mathbb { S }$ and action $\chi$ by $\mathbb { T }$ , then it is a $\mathbb { T S } .$ -algebra with action the composite

$$
\mathbb { T S } R \xrightarrow { \mathbb { T } \xi } \mathbb { T } R \xrightarrow { \ x } \mathbb { R } .
$$

If $Q$ is a $\mathbb { T S }$ -algebra with action $\omega$ , then $Q$ is a $\mathbb { T }$ algebra in ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ with actions the composites

$$
\begin{array} { r l r l r l r } { \Sigma Q \xrightarrow { \eta } \mathbb { T S } Q \xrightarrow { \omega } \quad } & { } & & { \mathrm { a n d } } & { } & & { \mathbb { T } Q \xrightarrow { \mathbb { T } \eta } \mathbb { T S } Q \xrightarrow { \omega } \quad \times \mathbb { Q } . } \end{array}
$$

These correspondences establish the required isomorphism of categories. Easy diagram chases show that $\mathbb { S } \longrightarrow \mathbb { T S }$ is a map of monads.

When applying this to modules, we used the following construction.

ConsTructIon 6.2. For a category $\mathscr { C }$ , let ${ \mathcal { C } } [ 1 ]$ be the category of pairs $( X ; Y )$ in $\mathscr { C }$ and pairs of maps. Let $\mathbb { S }$ be any of the monads $\mathbb { B } , \ \mathbb { C } , \ \mathbb { T }$ ,or $\mathbb { P }$ , and let $\mathscr { C }$ be its ground category ${ \mathcal { S } } , { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ , or $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . Construct a monad $\mathbb { S } [ 1 ]$ in $\mathcal { C } [ 1 ]$ as follows. On a pair $( X ; Y )$ , the functor $\mathbb { S } [ 1 ]$ is given by

$$
{ \mathbb S } [ 1 ] ( X ; Y ) = ( { \mathbb S } X ; { \mathbb S } ( X ; Y ) ) ,
$$

where $\mathbb { S } ( X ; Y )$ is the free $\mathbb { S } X$ -module generated by $\mathrm { Y }$ This functor factors through the evident category of pairs

$$
\left( { \mathbb { S } } { \mathrm { - a l g e b r a } } ; \ { \mathrm { o b j e c t ~ o f ~ } } { \mathcal { C } } \right)
$$

as the composite of $( \mathbb { S } ; \mathrm { i d } )$ and (id; free module), where the free module functor is that associated to the algebra in the first variable. Since the identity functor is a monad in a trivial way, each of these functors is a monad. Therefore, by Lemma 6.1, their composite $\mathbb { S } [ 1 ]$ is a monad such that an S[1]-algebra $( R ; M )$ is an $\mathbb { S }$ -algebra $R$ together with an $R$ -module $M$ .
