We used the following definition in our construction of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra from $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

DEfInitIoN 6.3. Let $( \mathbb { S } , \mu , \eta )$ be a monad in a cocomplete category $\mathscr { C }$ A (right) $\mathbb { S }$ -functor in a category $\mathcal { C } ^ { \prime }$ is a functor $F : \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { \prime }$ together with a natural transformation $\nu : F { \mathbb S } \longrightarrow F$ such that the following diagrams commute:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { F \mathbb { S } \prec \stackrel { F \eta } { \longrightarrow } F } & { \qquad F \mathbb { S } \mathbb { S } \xrightarrow { \nu S } \vdash F \mathbb { S } } \\ { \nu \Big \downarrow _ { \begin{array} { r } { r _ { \mathrm { i d } } } \\ { F } \end{array} } \quad } & { \mathrm { a n d } \quad } & { \quad F \mu \Big \downarrow } \\ { \qquad } & { \qquad \xrightarrow { F \mathbb { S } } \quad \xrightarrow { \nu } F . } \end{array}
$$

Given an $\mathbb { S }$ algebra $( R , \xi )$ , define ${ F \otimes _ { \mathbb { S } } R }$ to be the coequalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { F { \mathbb S } R \frac { \nu } { F \xi } \overline { { F R } } \longrightarrow F \otimes _ { \mathbb { S } } R . } \end{array}
$$

Given a monad $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ in ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \prime }$ and a left action $\lambda : \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } F \longrightarrow F$ ,we say that $\boldsymbol { \mathsf { \Pi } } ^ { F }$ is an $( \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { S } )$ -bifunctor if the following diagram commutes:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } F \mathbb { S } \xrightarrow { \lambda \mathbb { S } } \mathbb { F } \mathbb { S } } \\ { \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } \nu \Biggl \downarrow ^ { \nu } \qquad \downarrow ^ { \nu } } \\ { \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } F \xrightarrow { \lambda \quad }  F . } \end{array}
$$

EXAMPLE 6.4. The functor $\mathbb { S }$ is an $( \mathbb { S } , \mathbb { S } )$ -bifunctor, with both left and right action $\mu$ . If $\pi : \mathbb { S } \longrightarrow \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ is a map of monads in $\mathscr { C }$ , then $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ is an $( \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { S } )$ - bifunctor with right action $\nu = \mu ^ { \prime } \circ \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } \pi : \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } \mathbb { S } \longrightarrow \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ Observe that, for $X \in \mathcal { C }$ , ${ \mathbb S } ^ { \prime } \otimes _ { \mathbb S } { \mathbb S } X \cong { \mathbb S } ^ { \prime } X$ .

When ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \prime }$ in Definition 6.3 has a forgetful functor to the category of spectra, we shall construct a bar construction $B ( F , { \mathbb S } , R )$ that will give the appropriate homotopical version of $F \otimes _ { \mathbb { S } } R$ in $\mathrm { { X I I \ S 1 } }$ . Assuming that $\boldsymbol { F }$ is an $( \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { S } )$ -bifunctor for one of the monads constructed earlier in this chapter, we will find that $B ( F , { \mathbb S } , R )$ is an $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ -algebra. It is natural to ask whether or not $F \otimes _ { \mathbb { S } } R$ is itself an $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ -algebra. To answer this, we need another categorical definition.

DEFINITION 6.5. In any category $\mathscr { C }$ , a coequalizer diagram

$$
A { \underset { f } { \overset { e } { \to } } } B { \underset {  } { \overset { g } { \to } } } C .
$$

is said to be a reflexive coequalizer if there is a map $h : B \longrightarrow A$ such that $e \circ h = \mathrm { i d }$ and $f \circ h = \operatorname { i d }$ .

The following categorical observation is standard and easy. Although their stated hypotheses are different, the proofs of similar results in [43, p. 147] and [4, pp. 106-108] apply to give the first statement, and the second statement follows.

LEMMA 6.6. Let S be a monad in $\mathscr { C }$ such that $\mathbb { S }$ preserves reflexive coequalizers. If

$$
A { \underset { f } { \overset { e } { \to } } } B { \underset {  } { \overset { g } { \to } } } C
$$

is a reflexive coequalizer in $\mathscr { C }$ such that $A$ and $B$ are $\mathbb { S }$ -algebras and e and $f$ are maps of S-algebras, then $C$ has a unique structure of $\mathbb { S }$ -algebra such that $g$ is $^ { a }$

map of S-algebras, and $g$ is the coequalizer of e and $f$ in the category ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ . If, further, T is a monad in ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ such that T preserves reflexive coequalizers, then To S also preserves reflexive coequalizers.

Since the coequalizer diagram used to define ${ F \otimes } _ { \mathbb { S } } R$ is reflexive, via the map $F \eta : F R \longrightarrow F { \mathbb S R }$ , the first statement implies an answer to the question we asked originally.

LEMMA 6.7. Let S be a monad in $\mathscr { C }$ , $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ be a monad in ${ \pmb { \mathcal { C } } } ^ { \prime }$ , $\scriptstyle { R }$ be an S-algebra, and $F : \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { \prime }$ be an $( \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { S } )$ -bifunctor. If ${ \mathbb S } ^ { \prime }$ preserves reflexive coequalizers, then $F \otimes _ { \mathbb { S } } R$ is an $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ -algebra.

# 7. Limits and colimits of $S .$ algebras

We here prove that the categories of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and of $S .$ . algebras and commutative $S \mathrm { . }$ -algebras are complete and cocomplete. In fact, completeness follows immediately from Proposition 4.5. All four of our categories are categories of algebras over a monad in a complete category, and it follows that they are complete, with their limits created in their respective ground categories [43, VI.2, ex. 2]. The first statement of Lemma 6.6 applies to construct colimits, but to explain this properly we need some preliminary definitions that put our definitions of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra in perspective.

DEFINITIoN 7.1. A weak symmetric monoidal category $\mathscr { C }$ with product and unit object $\boldsymbol { I }$ is defined in exactly the same way as a symmetric monoidal category [43, p. 180], except that its unit map $\lambda : I \sqcap X \longrightarrow X$ is not required to be an isomorphism; $\mathscr { C }$ is said to be closed if the functor $( - ) \bigcirc Y$ has a right adjoint ${ \mathrm { H o m } } ( Y , - )$ for each $Y \in \mathcal { C }$ . Monoids and commutative monoids in $\mathscr { C }$ are defined in terms of diagrams of the form displayed in Definition 3.3. As in Construction 4.4 and Proposition 4.5, if $\mathscr { C }$ is cocomplete, then there are monads $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ in $\mathscr { C }$ whose algebras are the monoids and commutative monoids in $\mathscr { C }$ . For $X \in \mathcal { C }$ ,

$$
\mathbb { T } X \cong \coprod _ { j \geq 0 } X ^ { j } \quad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \quad \mathbb { P } X \cong \coprod _ { j \geq 0 } X ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j } .
$$

The proof of the following result is abstracted from an argument that Hopkins gave for the monad $\mathbb { C }$ [32]. He proceeded by reduction to a proof that the $j \cdot$ . fold symmetric powers of based spaces preserve reflexive coequalizers. With our new associative smash products, an abstraction of the latter proof makes the reduction unnecessary.

PROPOSITION 7.2. Let $\mathscr { C }$ be any cocomplete closed weak symmetric monoidal category. Then the monads T and $\mathbb { P }$ in $\mathscr { C }$ preserve reflexive coequalizers.

PRooF. For T, it suffices to prove that the $j$ -fold product $X _ { 1 } \sqcup \cdots \sqcup X _ { j }$ preserves reflexive coequalizers. Thus let

$$
X _ { i } \xrightarrow { e _ { i } } Y _ { i } \xrightarrow { g _ { i } } Z _ { i }
$$

be reflexive coequalizer diagrams in $\mathscr { C }$ , $1 \leq i \leq j$ ,and let $h _ { i } : Y _ { i } \longrightarrow X _ { i }$ satisfy $e _ { i } \circ h _ { i } = \mathrm { i d }$ and $f _ { i } \circ h _ { i } = \operatorname { i d }$ Let

$$
\varepsilon = e _ { 1 } \boxdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \boxdot e _ { j } , \quad \phi = f _ { 1 } \boxdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \boxdot f { f _ { j } , } \quad \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } \quad \gamma = g _ { 1 } \boxdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \boxdot g { g _ { j } . }
$$

Let $\beta : Y _ { 1 } \sqcup \dots \sqcup Y _ { j } \longrightarrow Z$ be the coequalizer of $\varepsilon$ and $\phi$ .Since $\gamma \varepsilon = \gamma \phi$ , there is a unique map $\pmb { \xi } : Z \longrightarrow Z _ { 1 } \sqcup \dots \sqcup Z _ { j }$ such that $\xi \circ \beta = \gamma$ We claim that $\xi$ is an isomorphism, and we proceed by induction on $j$ Let

$$
\varepsilon _ { i } = ( \mathrm { i } \mathrm { d } ) ^ { i - 1 } \bigcirc e _ { i } \bigcirc ( \mathrm { i } \mathrm { d } ) ^ { j - i } : Y _ { 1 } \bigcirc \cdot \cdot \cdot \bigcirc Y _ { i - 1 } \bigcirc X _ { i } \bigcirc Y _ { i + 1 } \bigcirc \cdot \cdot \cdot \bigcirc Y _ { j } \longrightarrow Y _ { 1 } \bigcirc \cdot \cdot \cdot \bigcirc Y _ { j } ,
$$

and, similarly, define $\phi _ { i } = ( \mathrm { i d } ) ^ { i - 1 } \square f _ { i } \square ( \mathrm { i d } ) ^ { j - i }$ .We observe first that $Z _ { 1 } \boldsymbol { \bigsqcup } \cdot \cdot \cdot \boldsymbol { \bigsqcup } Z _ { j }$ is the colimit of the diagram given by the $j$ pairs of maps $\{ \varepsilon _ { i } , \phi _ { i } \}$ Indeed, for any map $\alpha : Y _ { 1 } \sqcap \dots \sqcap Y _ { j } \longrightarrow W$ such that $\alpha \circ \varepsilon _ { i } = \alpha \circ \phi _ { i }$ for $1 \leq i \leq j$ , we obtain unique maps $\hat { \alpha }$ and $\tilde { \alpha }$ that make the following diagram commute by the induction hypothesis and the fact that the $\sqsupset$ product preserves colimits and epimorphisms:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { Y _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup Y _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangleup X _ { j } \frac { g _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup \dots \bigtriangledown g _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangleup \mathrm { d } } { \bigtriangleup } Z _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup \dots \bigtriangleup Z _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangledown X _ { j } } \\ & { \qquad \mathrm { i d } \bigtriangleup f _ { j } \Bigg | \Bigg | { \bf { i d } } \bigtriangledown f _ { j } } \\ & { Y _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup Y _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangleup Y _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangledown Y _ { j } \frac { g _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup \dots \bigtriangledown g _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangleup \mathrm { d } } { \bigtriangleup } Z _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup \dots \bigtriangleup Z _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangledown Y _ { j } } \\ & { \qquad \mathrm { a } \Bigg | } \\ & { \qquad k \not \equiv \mathrm { --- ~ \ --- ~ \sp \alpha ~  ~ \bigtriangleup ~ } \Bigg | { \bf { i d } } \bigtriangledown g _ { j } } \\ & { \qquad W \not \equiv \mathrm { --- ~ \ --- ~ \sp \alpha ~  ~ \bigtriangleup ~ } - Z _ { 1 } \bigtriangleup \mathrm { ~ \dots ~ \bigtriangleup ~ } Z _ { j - 1 } \bigtriangleup Z _ { j } . } \end{array}
$$

Now let $k _ { i } = h _ { 1 } \sqcap \cdots \sqcap h _ { i - 1 }$ id $\boxed { \varLambda } h _ { i + 1 } \boxed { \varLambda } \cdots \boxed { \varLambda } h _ { j }$ Visibly

$$
\varepsilon _ { i } = \varepsilon \circ k _ { i } \quad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \quad \phi _ { i } = \phi \circ k _ { i } .
$$

Since $\beta \varepsilon = \beta \phi$ , $\beta \varepsilon _ { i } = \beta \phi _ { i }$ for $1 \leq i \leq j$ and the universal property gives a map $\zeta : Z _ { 1 } \sqcup \cdots \sqcup Z _ { j } \longrightarrow Z$ It is easy to check from the universal properties that $\zeta$ and $\xi$ are inverse isomorphisms. In the symmetric case, we may take our $j$ given coequalizer diagrams to be the same and compose the $j \cdot$ -fold power, regarded as a functor to the category of $\Sigma _ { j }$ -objects in $\mathscr { C }$ , with the orbit functor. The latter is constructed as a coequalizer in $\mathscr { C }$ and is a left adjoint, so preserves coequalizers.

COROLLARY 7.3. The functors $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ on ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ , their restrictions to functors $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ on $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ , and the functors $\mathbb { B }$ and $\mathbb { C }$ on $\mathcal { S }$ preserve reflexive coequalizers.

PROOF. This is immediate since $\mathbb { B } = \mathbb { T }$ , $\mathbb { C } = \mathbb { P } \mathbb { L }$ , the functor $\mathbb { L } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow$ ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ preserves colimits, and colimits in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ and in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ are created in $\mathcal { S }$ .

Our claim that the categories of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and of $S .$ algebras and commutative $S .$ -algebras are cocomplete is now an immediate corollary of the following known result, which we also learned from Hopkins.

PRopositIoN 7.4. Let S be a monad in a cocomplete category $\mathscr { C }$ . If $\mathbb { S }$ preserves reflexive coequalizers, then ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ is cocomplete.

PRooF. Consider a diagram $\left\{ R _ { i } \right\}$ of $\mathbb { S }$ -algebras. Let colim $R _ { i }$ be its colimit in $\mathscr { C }$ and let $\iota _ { i } : R _ { i } \longrightarrow \cos \mathrm { R } _ { i }$ be the natural maps. Let

$$
\alpha : \mathrm { c o l i m } \mathbb { S } R _ { i } \longrightarrow \mathbb { S } \mathrm { c o l i m } R _ { i }
$$

be the unique map in $\mathscr { C }$ whose composite with the natural map $\mathbb { S } R _ { i } \longrightarrow \mathrm { c o l i m } \mathbb { S } R _ { i }$ is $\mathbb { S } \iota _ { i }$ for each $_ i$ Define colims $R _ { i }$ by the following coequalizer diagram in $\mathscr { C }$ :

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { S } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } \mathbb { S } R _ { i } ) \frac { \mathbb { S } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } \xi _ { i } ) } { \mu \circ \mathbb { S } \alpha } \mathbb { S } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } R _ { i } ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { 8 } R _ { i } . } \end{array}
$$

This is a reflexive coequalizer, via $\mathbb { S } ( \mathsf { c o l i m } \eta _ { i } )$ . Thus, by Lemma 6.6, colims $R _ { i }$ is an $\mathbb { S }$ -algebra such that the displayed diagram is a coequalizer in ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ . It follows easily that colims $R _ { i }$ is the colimit of $\{ R _ { i } \}$ in ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ .

This result is closely related to the following standard result of Linton [41] (see also [4, Thm 2, p. 319]).

THEOREM 7.5 (LINTON). Let $\mathbb { S }$ be a monad in a cocomplete category $\mathscr { C }$ .If ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ has coequalizers, then ${ \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { S } } ]$ is cocomplete.

# CHAPTER III

# The homotopy theory of $R ^ { }$ -modules

We here develop the homotopy theory of modules over an $S _ { \ l }$ algebra $R$ The classical theory of cell spectra generalizes to give a theory of cell modules over $R$ . The derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules is constructed from the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules by formally adjoining inverses to the weak equivalences, and it is equivalent to the homotopy category of cell $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules. We define the smash product over $R$ , $\wedge _ { R }$ , and the function $R$ -module functor, $F _ { R }$ , by direct mimicry of the definitions of tensor product and Hom functors for modules over an algebra. When specialized to commutative $S .$ -algebras, our smash product of $R \mathrm { . }$ modules is again an $\scriptstyle R .$ -module, and similarly for $F _ { R }$ . Here the category of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules has structure precisely like the category of $S \mathrm { . }$ -modules, and duality theory works exactly as it does for spectra. We assume familiarity with ${ \mathrm { I I } } \ S \ S 1 { , } 3$ and work in the ground category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ of $S _ { \ l }$ -modules.

# 1. The category of $R$ -modules; free and cofree $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules

Fix an $S$ -algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ We understand $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules to be left $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules unless otherwise specified. We first observe that the category $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ of $R .$ modules is closed under various constructions in the underlying categories of spectra and $S .$ -modules. As in algebra, an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module is the same thing as an algebra over the monad $R \land s ( - )$ in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ or, equivalently, a coalgebra over the adjoint comonad $F _ { S } ( R , - )$ in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . The functors $R \land s ( - )$ and $F _ { S } ( R , - )$ from $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ to $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ are left and right adjoint to the forgetful functor. That is, $R \land _ { S } ( - )$ and $F _ { S } ( R , - )$ are the free and cofree functors from $S _ { \ l }$ -modules to $R$ -modules. Together with II.1.4 and formal arguments exactly like those in algebra, this leads to the following result.

THEOREM 1.1. The category of $R$ -modules is complete and cocomplete, with both limits and colimits created in the underlying category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . Let $X$ be a based space, $\kappa$ be an $S$ -module, and $M$ and $N$ be $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Then the following conclusions hold, where the displayed isomorphisms are obtained by restriction of the corresponding isomorphisms for $S$ -modules.

(i) $M \wedge X$ is an $R$ -module and the spectrum level cofiber of a map of Rmodules is an R-module.

(ii) $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F ( X , N )$ is an $R$ -module and

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M \wedge X , N ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M , S \wedge _ { { \mathcal { L } } } F ( X , N ) ) .
$$

(iii) $M \land _ { S } K$ and $F _ { S } ( K , N )$ are $R$ -modules and

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M \wedge _ { S } K , N ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M , F _ { S } ( K , N ) ) .
$$

(iv) $F _ { S } ( M , K )$ is a right $\scriptstyle R$ -module. (v) As $R$ modules,

$$
M \wedge X \cong M \wedge _ { S } \Sigma ^ { \infty } X ~ a n d ~ S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F ( X , N ) \cong F _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } X , N ) .
$$

The cofiber and fiber of a map of $R$ -modules are $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, where the fiber is understood to be obtained by application of the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to the fiber constructed in the category of spectra.

ProoF. The only point that might need comment is the $R$ -module structure on $S \land _ { \mathcal { L } } F ( X , N )$ The evaluation map $\varepsilon : F ( X , N ) \land X \longrightarrow N$ is a map of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. The adjoint of $R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \varepsilon$ is a map of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra

$$
\tilde { \varepsilon } : R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F ( X , N ) \longrightarrow F ( X , R \wedge _ { S } N ) ,
$$

and we obtain the desired action upon applying $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \tilde { \varepsilon }$ and using the given action of $\scriptstyle { R }$ on $N$ . This leads to the $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module structure on the specified fiber of a map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules; compare II.1.5.

The free $R$ -module functor on spectra is the starting point of cellular theory.

DEFINITIoN 1.2. Define the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module generated by a spectrum $X$ to be

$$
\mathbb { F } _ { R } X = R \wedge _ { S } \mathbb { F } _ { S } X ,
$$

where $\mathbb { F } _ { S } X = S \wedge _ { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } \mathbb { X }$ Equivalently, since $R \land _ { S } S \cong R$ ,

$$
\mathbb { F } _ { R } X = R \wedge _ { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } \mathbb { L } X .
$$

We abbreviate $\mathbb { F } X = \mathbb { F } _ { R } X$ when $\scriptstyle { R }$ is clear from the context.

The term "free" is technically a misnomer, since $\mathbb { F }$ is not left adjoint to the forgetful functor. However, it is nearly so.

PROposITION 1.3. The functor $\mathbb { F } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { R }$ is left adjoint to the functor that sends an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ to the spectrum $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M )$ , and there is a natural map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $\pmb { \xi } : \mathbb { F } M \longrightarrow M$ whose adjoint $M \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M )$ is a weak equivalence of spectra. Therefore

$$
\pi _ { n } ( M ) \cong h \mathcal M _ { R } ( \mathbb { F } S ^ { n } , M ) .
$$

PRoOF. In view of II.1.3, we have the chain of isomorphisms

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { R } ( \mathbb { F } _ { R } X , N ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( \mathbb { F } _ { S } X , N ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , N ) ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , N ) ) .
$$

By I.8.7, we have a natural weak equivalence $\tilde { \lambda } : M \longrightarrow F \mathcal { L } ( S , M )$ of ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] .$ . spectra. Thought of as a map of spectra, its adjoint is the required $\boldsymbol { R }$ map $\xi$ The statement about the homotopy groups $\pi _ { n } ( M ) = h { \mathcal { S } } ( S ^ { n } , M )$ is clear; compare II.1.8.

The following central theorem shows that we have homotopical control on $\mathbb { F } X$ without any hypotheses (such as tameness or CW homotopy type) on $\boldsymbol { R }$ .

THEOREM 1.4. In the stable homotopy category $\bar { h } \mathcal { S }$ , FX is naturally isomorphic to the internal smash product $R \wedge X$ . Moreover, the composite

$$
\zeta : \mathbb { F } S \xrightarrow { \mathbb { F } \eta } \mathbb { F } R \xrightarrow { \xi } R
$$

is a weak equivalence of $R$ -modules.

ProoF. The first statement is clear from II.1.9 and I.6.7, but we point out a variant proof that makes clear that the weak equivalence is one of $R _ { ☉ }$ -module spectra (in the homotopical sense). In ${ \tt X } \ S 5$ , we shall construct a tame $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $\kappa R$ and a weak equivalence of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra $r : K R \longrightarrow R$ Since we are working in the stable homotopy category, we may take $X$ to be a CW spectrum. Then, by I.4.6 and I.6.6,

$$
r \wedge \_ { \mathsf { L } } { \mathrm { ~ i d : ~ } } K R \wedge \_ { \mathsf { L } } \mathbb { L } X \longrightarrow R \wedge \_ { \mathsf { L } } X = \mathbb { F } X
$$

is a weak equivalence. By I.4.6 and I.6.7, there are natural weak equivalences

$$
K R \wedge X \longrightarrow K R \wedge \mathbb { L } X \longrightarrow K R \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } X .
$$

For the second statement, observe that $\zeta$ is the common composite in the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { R \wedge \underbrace { \mathcal { L } \mathbb { L } S } _ { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \mathbb { L } } \frac { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \xi _ { * } } { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ (  \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { \ell } \alpha ) ^ { \ell } ~ } } R \wedge \mathcal { L } \mathrm { \normalfont ~ S } } \\ & { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ i d } \wedge \mathbb { L } \eta \Bigg \downarrow } \\ & { R \wedge \mathcal { L } \mathbb { L } R \underset { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \xi } { \longrightarrow } R \wedge \mathcal { L } R \underset { \phi } { \longrightarrow } R . } \end{array}
$$

By I.8.6, the top map id ${ \wedge } \xi$ is a weak equivalence.

COROLLARY 1.5. If $X$ is a wedge of sphere spectra, then $\pi _ { * } ( \mathbb { F } X )$ is the free $\pi _ { * } ( R )$ -module with one generator of degree n for each wedge summand $S ^ { n }$ .

We shall need one further fundamental property of free $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules.

DEFINITIoN 1.6. A compact spectrum is one of the form $\Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X$ for a compact space $X$ and an indexing space $V \subset U$ A compact $R$ -module is one of the form $\mathbb { F } K$ for a compact spectrum $K$ .

PROPOSITION 1.7. Let $L$ be a finite colimit of compact $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and let $\{ M _ { i } \}$ be $^ { a }$ sequence of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and (spacewise) inclusions $M _ { i } \longrightarrow M _ { i + 1 }$ . Then

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { R } ( L , \operatorname { c o l i m } M _ { i } ) \cong \operatorname { c o l i m } \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( L , M _ { i } ) .
$$

The generalization from compact $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules to their finite colimits is immediate. The compact case would be elementary if the free functor were left adjoint to the forgetful functor, and we shall show in $\mathbf { X I \ S 2 }$ that this is near enough to being true to give the conclusion.

While they play a less central role, we shall also make use of cofree $R$ -modules. Recall from 1§4 that $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S }$ is the right adjoint of $\mathbb { L }$ and gives a comonad whose coalgebras are the $\mathbb { L }$ spectra. In particular, $\mathbb { L } ^ { \# X }$ is an $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum for any spectrum $X$ .

DEFINITION 1.8. Define the cofree $S .$ -module generated by a spectrum $X$ to be $\mathbb { F } _ { S } ^ { \# } X = S \wedge _ { \mathbb { \ell } } \mathbb { L } ^ { \# } X$ .Then define the cofree $R$ module generated by $X$ to be

$$
\mathbb { F } _ { R } ^ { \# } X = F _ { S } ( R , \mathbb { F } _ { S } ^ { \# } X )
$$

with left action of $R$ induced by the right action of $R$ on itself. We abbreviate $\mathbb { F } ^ { \# } X = \mathbb { F } _ { R } ^ { \# } X$ when $\mathcal { R }$ is clear from the context.

The term "cofree" is not a misnomer, since here we do have the expected adjunction.

PROPOSITION 1.9. The functor $\mathbb { F } _ { R } ^ { \# } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { R }$ is right adjoint to the forgetful functor $\mathcal { M } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S }$ .

PROOF. Let $M$ be an $R$ -module and $X$ be a spectrum. Lemma 5.5(ii) below gives the first of the following isomorphisms, and II.1.3 and I.4.7 give the others:

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { R } ( M , \mathbb { F } _ { R } ^ { \# } X ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( M , \mathbb { F } _ { S } ^ { \# } X ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( M , \mathbb { L } ^ { \# } X ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( M , X )
$$

THEOREM 1.10. In the stable homotopy category ${ \bar { h } } { \mathcal { S } } , \mathbb { F } ^ { \# } X$ is naturally isomorphic to the internal function spectrum $F ( R , X )$ .

PRoOF. This is immediate from II.1.9, I.7.3, and I.4.8.

# 2. Cell and CW $R$ -modules; the derived category of $R$ -modules

To develop cell and CW theories for $R$ -modules, we think of the free $R .$ . modules $S _ { R } ^ { n } \equiv \mathbb { F } S ^ { n }$ as "sphere $R$ -modules". This is consistent with the sphere $\boldsymbol { S }$ -modules of II.1.7. For cells, we note that the cone functor $C X = X \wedge I$ commutes with $\mathbb { F }$ , so that $C \mathbb { F } S ^ { n } \cong \mathbb { F } C S ^ { n }$ . Since $\mathbb { F }$ has a right adjoint, maps out of sphere $R$ -modules and their cones are induced by maps on the spectrum level; the fact that the right adjoint is not the obvious forgetful functor will create no difficulties. In fact, we can simply parrot the cell theory of spectra from [38, I§5], reducing proofs to those given there via adjunction.

DEFInITIons 2.1. We define cell and relative cell $R$ -modules.

(i) A cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ is the union of an expanding sequence of sub $R$ . modules $M _ { n }$ such that $M _ { 0 } = *$ and $M _ { n + 1 }$ is the cofiber of a map $\phi _ { n }$ : $F _ { n } \longrightarrow M _ { n }$ , where $F _ { n }$ is a (possibly empty) wedge of sphere modules $S _ { R } ^ { q }$ (of varying dimensions). The restriction $\phi _ { n }$ to a wedge summand $S _ { R } ^ { q }$ is called an attaching map. The induced map ${ \cal C S } _ { R } ^ { q } \longrightarrow { \cal M } _ { n + 1 } \subset { \cal M }$ is called a cell. The sequence $\{ M _ { n } \}$ is called the sequential filtration of $M$ .   
(ii) For an $R$ -module $L$ , a relative cell $R$ -module $( M , L )$ is an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ specified as in (i), but with $M _ { 0 } = L$ .   
(iii) A map $f : M \longrightarrow N$ between cell $R$ -modules is sequentially cellular if $f ( M _ { n } ) \subset N _ { n }$ for all $_ n$ :   
(iv) A submodule $L$ of a cell $R$ -module $M$ is a cell submodule if $L$ is a cell $R$ -module such that $L _ { n } \subset M _ { n }$ and the composite of each attaching map $S _ { R } ^ { q } \longrightarrow L _ { n }$ of $L$ with the inclusion ${ \cal L } _ { n } \longrightarrow M _ { n }$ is an attaching map of $M$ Thus every cell of $L$ is a cell of $M$ Observe that $( M , L )$ may be viewed as a relative cell $R$ -module.   
(v) A cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module is finite dimensional if it has cells in finitely many dimensions. It is finite if it has finitely many cells.

The sequential filtration is essential for inductive arguments, but it should be regarded as flexible and subject to change whenever convenient. It merely records the order in which cells are attached and, as long as the cells to which new cells are attached are already present, it doesn't matter in what order cells are attached.

LEMMA 2.2. Let $f : M \longrightarrow N$ be an $R$ -map between cell $R$ -modules. Then M admits a new sequential filtration with respect to which $f$ is sequentially cellular.

PROOF. Assume inductively that $M _ { n }$ has been given a filtration as a cell $R$ . module $M _ { n } = \cup M _ { q } ^ { \prime }$ such that $f ( M _ { q } ^ { \prime } ) \subset N _ { q }$ for all $q$ .Let $\chi : S _ { R } ^ { r } \longrightarrow M _ { n }$ be an attaching map for the construction f $M _ { n + 1 }$ from $M _ { n }$ and let $\tilde { \chi } : { \cal C S } _ { R } ^ { r } \longrightarrow { \cal M } _ { n + 1 }$ be the corresponding cell. By Proposition 1.7, there is a minimal $q$ such that both

$$
\mathrm { I m } ( \chi ) \subset M _ { q } ^ { \prime } \mathrm { a n d } \mathrm { I m } ( f \circ \tilde { \chi } ) \subset N _ { q + 1 } .
$$

Extend the filtration of $M _ { n }$ to $M _ { n + 1 }$ by taking $\chi$ to be a typical attaching map of a cell of $M _ { q + 1 } ^ { \prime }$ .

We shall occasionally need the following two reassuring results. Their proofs are similar to those of their spectrum level analogs [38, pp 494495] and depend on Proposition 1.7 and its proof.

LEMMA 2.3. A map from a compact $R$ -module to a cell $R$ -module has image contained in a finite subcomplex, and a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module is the colimit of its finite subcomplexes.

If $\kappa$ and $L$ are subcomplexes of a cell $R \mathrm { . }$ -module $M$ , then we understand their intersection and union in the combinatorial sense. That is, $K \cap L$ is the cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module constructed from the attaching maps and cells that are in both $\kappa$ and $L$ and $K \cup L$ is the cell $R$ -module constructed from the attaching maps and cells that are in either $\kappa$ or $L$ . However, we also have their categorical intersection and union, namely the pullback of the inclusions of $\kappa$ and $L$ in $M$ and the pushout of the resulting maps from the categorical intersection to $\kappa$ and to $L$ .

LeMMA 2.4. For subcomplexes $\boldsymbol { K }$ and $L$ of a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ , the canonical map from the combinatorial intersection to the categorical intersection and from the categorical union to the combinatorial union of $K$ and $L$ are isomorphisms of R-modules.

DEfiniTioN 2.5. A cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ is said to be a CW $R _ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ -module if each cell is attached only to cells of lower dimension. The $\mathscr { n }$ -skeleton $M ^ { n }$ of a CW $R$ -module is the union of its cells of dimension at most $^ n$ A map $f : M \longrightarrow N$ between CW $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules is cellular if $f ( M ^ { n } ) \subset N ^ { n }$ for all $_ n$ . We do not require that $f$ also be sequentially cellular but, by Lemma 2.2, that can always be arranged by changing the order in which cells are attached. Relative CW $R \mathrm { . }$ modules $( M , L )$ are defined similarly, with each cell attached only to the union of $L$ and the cells of lower dimension.

PROPosITIoN 2.6. The collection of cell $R$ -modules enjoys the following closure properties.

(i) A wedge of cell R-modules is a cell R-module.   
(ii) The pushout of a map along the inclusion of a cell submodule is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module.   
(iii) The union of a sequence of inclusions of cell submodules is a cell $R$ module.   
(iv) The smash product of a cell $R$ -module and a based cell space (with based attaching maps) is a cell R-module.   
(v) The smash product over $S$ of a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module and a cell $s$ -module is a cell R-module.

The same statements hold with "cell" replaced by "CW", provided that, in (ii), the given map is cellular.

ProoF. In (ii), we apply Lemma 2.2 to ensure that the given map is sequentially cellular. Part (v) follows from I.6.1, which implies that the smash product of a sphere $R$ -module and a sphere $S .$ -module is a sphere $R$ -module. Otherwise the proofs are the same as for cell and CW spectra [38, 1§5].

The following result is the "Homotopy Extension and Lifting Property".

THEOREM 2.7 (HELP). Let $( M , L )$ be a relative cell $R$ -module and let e : $N \longrightarrow P$ be a weak equivalence of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Then, given maps $f : M \longrightarrow P$ , $g : L \longrightarrow N$ , and $h : L \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow P$ such that $f | _ { L } \ = \ h i _ { 0 }$ and $e g \ = \ h i _ { 1 }$ in the following diagram, there are maps $\tilde { g }$ and $\tilde { h }$ that make the entire diagram commute.

$$
\begin{array} { r } { L \longrightarrow L \setminus I _ { + } \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc L } \\ { M \longrightarrow P _ { \stackrel { R } { \underset { \stackrel { \ddots } { h } } { \longrightarrow } } \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc } ^ { h } N _ { \stackrel { \ddots } { \vartheta } } \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc } \\ { M \longrightarrow \stackrel { \tilde { g } } { \longrightarrow } M \wedge I _ { + } \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc \dotsc } & { M } \end{array}
$$

PROOF. This is proven for $( M , L ) = ( C S _ { R } ^ { q } , S _ { R } ^ { q } )$ by reduction to the spectrum level analog. Technically, we use that the fact that our spheres are obtained from sphere spectra by applying a functor that is left adjoint to a functor that preserves weak equivalences (even though it is not the obvious forgetful functor). The general case follows by induction up the sequential filtration, and the inductive step reduces directly to the case of $( C S _ { R } ^ { q } , S _ { R } ^ { q } )$ already handled.

The Whitehead theorem is a formal consequence.

THeorEM 2.8 (WHITEheAd). If $M$ is a cell $\boldsymbol { R }$ -module and $e : N \longrightarrow P$ is a weak equivalence of $R$ -modules, then $e _ { * } \ : \ h \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( M , N ) \ \longrightarrow \ h \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( M , P )$ is an isomorphism. Therefore a weak equivalence between cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules is $^ { a }$ homotopy equivalence.

Recall that a spectrum is "connective" if it is $( - 1 )$ -connected. When $\scriptstyle { R }$ is connective, $\pi _ { q } ( N / N ^ { q } ) = 0$ for any CW $R \mathrm { . }$ -module and we can prove the following cellular approximation theorem exactly as in [38, I.5.8]. For non-connective $R$ , this result fails and we must content ourselves with cell $R$ -modules. For connective $R$ , there is no significant loss of information if we restrict attention to CW $R .$ -modules.

THEOrEM 2.9 (CELLULAR APPRoxiMaTION). AssUme that $\scriptstyle { R }$ is connective and let $( M , L )$ and $( M ^ { \prime } , L ^ { \prime } )$ be relative CW $R$ -modules. Then any map $f :$ $( M , L ) \longrightarrow ( M ^ { \prime } , L ^ { \prime } )$ is homotopic relative to $L$ to a cellular map. Therefore, for cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $M$ and $M ^ { \prime }$ , any map $M \longrightarrow M ^ { \prime }$ is homotopic to a cellular map, and any two homotopic cellular maps are cellularly homotopic.

THEOREM 2.10 (ApPRoXIMaTION BY CELL MODULES). For any $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ , there is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module ΓM and a weak equivalence $\gamma : \Gamma M \longrightarrow M$ .If $R$ is connective, then IM can be chosen to be a CW $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module.

ProoF. Choose a wedge of sphere $\scriptstyle R$ -modules $N _ { 0 }$ and a map $\gamma _ { 0 } : N _ { 0 } \longrightarrow M$ that induces an epimorphism on homotopy groups. Given $\gamma _ { n } : N _ { n } \longrightarrow M$ ,we construct $N _ { n + 1 }$ from $N _ { n }$ as a homotopy coequalizer of pairs of representative maps for all pairs of unequal elements of any $\pi _ { q } ( N _ { n } )$ that map to the same element in $\pi _ { q } ( M )$ . We have homotopies that allow us to extend $\gamma _ { n }$ to $\gamma _ { n + 1 }$ .We let $\Gamma M$ be the union of the $N _ { n }$ , and the $\gamma _ { n }$ give a map $\gamma : \Gamma M \longrightarrow M$ We deduce from Proposition 1.7 that $\gamma$ is a weak equivalence, and we deduce from Proposition 2.6 that $\Gamma M$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. If $\scriptstyle { R }$ is connective, we may take our representative maps to be cellular, and $\Gamma N$ is then a CW $R .$ module.

CONsTRucTION 2.11. For each $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ , choose a cell $R .$ -module $\Gamma M$ and a weak equivalence $\gamma : \Gamma M \longrightarrow M$ By the Whitehead theorem, for a map $f : M \longrightarrow N$ , there is a map $\Gamma f : \Gamma M \longrightarrow \Gamma N$ , unique up to homotopy, such that the following diagram is homotopy commutative:

$$
\begin{array} { l } { \Gamma M \xrightarrow { \Gamma f } \Gamma N } \\ { \gamma \Bigg \downarrow \qquad } \\ { M \xrightarrow { \Gamma f } \gamma N . } \end{array}
$$

Thus $\Gamma$ is a functor $h \mathcal { M } _ { R } \longrightarrow h \mathcal { M } _ { R }$ , and $\gamma$ is a natural transformation from $\Gamma$ to the identity. The derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ can be described as the category whose objects are the $R$ -modules and whose morphisms are specified by

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathcal { D } _ { R } ( M , N ) = h \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( \Gamma M , \Gamma N ) , } \end{array}
$$

with the evident composition. When $M$ is a cell $R$ -module,

$$
\mathcal { D } _ { R } ( M , N ) \cong h \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( M , N ) .
$$

Using the identity function on objects and $\Gamma$ on morphisms, we obtain a functor $i : h \mathcal { M } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { R }$ that sends weak equivalences to isomorphisms and is universal with this property. Let $\mathcal { C } _ { R }$ be the full subcategory of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ whose objects are the cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Then the functor $\Gamma$ induces an equivalence of categories $\mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow h \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ with inverse the composite of $i$ and the inclusion of $h \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ in $h \mathcal { M } _ { R }$ .

Therefore the derived category and the homotopy category of cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules can be used interchangeably. Homotopy-preserving functors on $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules that do not preserve weak equivalences are transported to the derived category by first applying $\Gamma$ ,then the given functor.

The category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ has all homotopy limits and colimits. They are created as the corresponding constructions on the underlying diagrams of $S .$ -modules; equivalently, homotopy colimits are created on the spectrum level and homotopy limits are created from spectrum level homotopy limits, which are ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ spectra, by applying the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ . Explicit functorial constructions will be given in $\mathbf { X } \ S 3$ . We have enough information to quote the categorical form of Brown's representability theorem given in [13].

THEOREM 2.12 (BROwN). A contravariant functor $k : { \mathcal { D } } _ { R } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { S } }$ Sets is representable in the form $k ( M ) \cong \mathcal { D } _ { R } ( M , N )$ for some $R$ -module $N$ if and only if $k$ converts wedges to products and converts homotopy pushouts to weak pullbacks.

REMARK 2.13. There is a variant of Brown's theorem, due to Adams [3], that applies to functors that are defined only on finite CW spectra. Working in an algebraic context, Neeman [56] observed that Adams' variant does not generalize so readily. Rather, it requires a countability hypothesis that is satisfied automatically in the classical context of finite CW spectra. In our context, Adams' variant applies provided that each homotopy group $\pi _ { n } ( R )$ is countable.

# 3. The smash product of $R$ modules

We mimic the definition of tensor products of modules over algebras.

DEFinItioN 3.1. Let $R$ be an $S .$ -algebra and let $M$ be a right and $N$ be a left $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. Define $M \land _ { R } N$ to be the coequalizer displayed in the following diagram of $S .$ -modules:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { M \wedge _ { S } R \wedge _ { S } N \frac { \mu \wedge _ { S } \mathrm { i d } } { \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { S } \nu } \quad \stackrel { \sharp } { \sharp } M \wedge _ { S } N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { R } N , } \end{array}
$$

where $\mu$ and $\nu$ are the given actions of $\scriptstyle { R }$ on $M$ and $N$ .

When $R = S$ , we are coequalizing the same isomorphism (see I.8.3). Therefore our new $M \land _ { S } N$ coincides with our old $M \land _ { S } N$ .

We shall shortly construct function $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules satisfying the usual adjunction. It will follow that the functor $\wedge _ { R }$ preserves colimits in each of its variables. It is clear that smash products with spaces commute with $\wedge _ { R }$ , in the sense that

$$
( X \wedge M ) \wedge _ { R } N \cong X \wedge ( M \wedge _ { R } N ) \cong ( M \wedge _ { R } N ) \wedge X \cong M \wedge _ { R } ( N \wedge X ) .
$$

Therefore the functor $\wedge _ { R }$ commutes with cofiber sequences in each of its variables. We also have the following adjunction, which complements Theorem 1.1(iv).

LEMMA 3.2. For an $S$ -module $\kappa$ ,

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( M \wedge _ { R } N , K ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( N , F _ { S } ( M , K ) ) .
$$

The commutativity, associativity, and unity properties of the smash product over $S$ and comparisons of coequalizer diagrams give commutativity, associativity, and unity properties of the smash product over $\scriptstyle { R }$ , exactly as in algebra. We state these properties in the generality of their algebraic counterparts.

An $S$ -algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ with product $\phi : R \land s R \longrightarrow R$ has an opposite $S .$ algebra $R ^ { o p }$ with product $\phi \circ \tau$ , and a left $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module with action $\mu$ is a right $R ^ { o p }$ -module with action $\mu \circ \tau$ .

LEMMA 3.3. For a right $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ and left $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $N$ ,

$$
{ \cal M } \wedge _ { R } { \cal N } \cong { \cal N } \wedge _ { R ^ { o p } } { \cal M } .
$$

For $S$ algebras $R$ and $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ , we define an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule to be a left $\scriptstyle { R }$ and right $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } } ^ { \prime }$ -module $M$ such that the evident diagram commutes:

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { R \wedge _ { S } M \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime } } } \\ { { \Big \downarrow } } \\ { { R \wedge _ { S } M \longrightarrow M . } } \end{array}
$$

As in algebra, an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule is the same thing as an $( R \wedge _ { S } ( R ^ { \prime } ) ^ { o p } )$ -module.

PROPOSITION 3.4. Let $M$ be an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule, $N$ be an $( R ^ { \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule, and $P$ be an $( R ^ { \prime \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule. Then $M \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } N$ is an $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule and

$$
( M \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } N ) \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P \cong M \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( N \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P )
$$

# as $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodules.

The unity isomorphism has already been displayed, in the guise of a split coequalizer diagram, in II.3.2. We restate the conclusion.

LEMMA 3.5. The action $\nu : R \land _ { S } N \longrightarrow N$ of an $R$ -module $N$ factors through an isomorphism of $R$ -modules $\lambda : R \wedge _ { R } N \longrightarrow N$ .

For an $S _ { \ l }$ -module $K$ , $R \wedge _ { S } K \cong K \wedge _ { S } R$ is an $( R , R )$ -bimodule. In particular, this applies to the free left $R .$ module $\mathbb { F } _ { R } X = R \wedge _ { S } \mathbb { F } _ { S } X$ generated by a spectrum $X$ , which may be identified with the free right $R$ -module generated by $X$ The following instances of the isomorphisms above will be used in conjunction with the weak equivalences of I.6.7 and II.1.9. They allow us to deduce homotopical properties of $\wedge _ { R }$ from corresponding properties of $\wedge _ { S }$ .

PROPOsITION 3.6. Let $K$ and $L$ be $S$ -modules and let $N$ be an $R$ -module. There is a natural isomorphism of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules

$$
( K \wedge _ { S } R ) \wedge _ { R } N \cong K \wedge _ { S } N .
$$

There is also a natural isomorphism of $( R , R )$ -bimodules

$$
( K \wedge _ { S } R ) \wedge _ { R } ( R \wedge _ { S } L ) \cong R \wedge _ { S } ( K \wedge _ { S } L ) .
$$

Using I.6.1, we obtain the following consequence, in which we use an isomorphism of universes $f : U \oplus U \longrightarrow U$ to define the internal smash product $f _ { * } ( X \wedge Y )$ .

COrOLLARY 3.7. Let $X$ and $Y$ be spectra and let $N$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. There is a natural isomorphism of $R$ -modules

$$
{ \mathbb { F } _ { R } X \wedge _ { R } N \cong \mathbb { F } _ { S } X \wedge _ { S } N } .
$$

There is also a natural isomorphism of $( R , R )$ -bimodules

$$
\mathbb { F } _ { R } X \wedge _ { R } \mathbb { F } _ { R } Y \cong \mathbb { F } _ { R } f _ { * } ( X \wedge Y ) .
$$

Theorem 3.8. If $M$ is a cell $R$ -module and $\phi : N \longrightarrow N ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of $R$ -modules, then

$$
\mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { R } \phi : M \wedge _ { R } N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { R } N ^ { \prime }
$$

is a weak equivalence of $S$ -modules.

PROOF. When $M = \mathbb { F } _ { R } X$ for a CW spectrum $X$ , the conclusion is immediate from the corollary and I.6.6. The general case follows from the case of sphere $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules by induction up the sequential filtration and passage to colimits.

We construct $\wedge _ { R }$ as a functor

$$
\boldsymbol { r } \mathcal { D } _ { R } \times \ell \mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { S }
$$

by approximating one of the variables by a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module; here $^ { \mathfrak { s } } r ^ { \mathfrak { n } }$ and $^ { 6 9 } \ell ^ { 9 }$ indicate right and left $R$ -modules. That is, the derived smash product of $M$ and $N$ is represented by $\Gamma M \land _ { R } N$ .

The following technical sharpening of Corollary 1.5 will be the starting point for our later construction of a spectral sequence for the calculation of $\pi _ { * } ( M \land _ { R } N )$ .

PROPOSITION 3.9. Let $X$ be a wedge of sphere spectra and let $N$ be a cell $R$ -module. Then there is an isomorphism

$$
\pi _ { * } ( \mathbb { F } _ { R } X \wedge _ { R } N ) \cong ( \pi _ { * } ( R ) \otimes H _ { * } ( X ) ) \otimes _ { \pi _ { * } ( R ) } \pi _ { * } ( N )
$$

that is natural in the $R$ -modules $\mathbb { F } _ { R } X$ and $N$ .

ProoF. The point is that naturality on general maps $g : \mathbb { F } _ { R } X \longrightarrow \mathbb { F } _ { R } X ^ { \prime }$ , with their induced maps $\pi _ { * } ( R ) \otimes H _ { * } ( X ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { * } ( R ) \otimes H _ { * } ( X ^ { \prime } ) ,$ and not just on maps of the form $\textstyle g = \operatorname { \mathbb { F } } _ { R } f$ , $f : X \longrightarrow X ^ { \prime }$ , will be essential in the cited application. The diagram

$$
\mathbb { F } _ { R } X \wedge _ { S } R \wedge _ { S } N { \Longrightarrow } \mathbb { F } _ { R } X \wedge _ { S } N { \longrightarrow } \mathbb { F } _ { R } X \wedge _ { R } N
$$

is a split coequalizer in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ and thus in $\mathcal { S }$ , and it is visibly natural in both $\mathbb { F } _ { R } X$ and $N$ . It remains a coequalizer on applying $\pi _ { * }$ , and the required naturality follows.

Finally, we record an analogue of the behavior of tensor products of modules with respect to tensor products of algebras.

PROPOsITION 3.10. Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $R ^ { \prime }$ be $S$ -algebras, $M$ and $N$ be right and left $R$ -modules, and $M ^ { \prime }$ and $N ^ { \prime }$ be right and left $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ -modules. Then there is a natural isomorphism of $S$ -modules

$$
( M \wedge _ { S } M ^ { \prime } ) \wedge _ { R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime } } ( N \wedge _ { S } N ^ { \prime } ) \cong ( M \wedge _ { R } N ) \wedge _ { S } ( M ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } N ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

If $M$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module and $N ^ { \prime }$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ -module, then $M \wedge _ { S } N ^ { \prime }$ is a cell $R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime }$ -module.

ProoF. The first statement is a comparison of coequalizer diagrams. The second statement holds since, on spheres, I.6.1 implies isomorphisms

$$
\left( \mathbb { L } S ^ { q } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R \right) \wedge _ { S } \left( R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { r } \right) \cong \left( R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime } \right) \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { q + r } .
$$

# 4. Change of $S$ algebras; $q \cdot$ cofibrant $S \mathrm { . }$ algebras

In this section, we assume given a map of $S .$ algebras

$$
\phi : R \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime } , \qquad 
$$

and we study the relationship between the categories of $R _ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ -modules and of $R ^ { \prime }$ modules. By pullback along $\phi$ , we obtain a functor $\phi ^ { * } : \mathcal { M } _ { R ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { R }$ . It preserves weak equivalences and thus induces a functor $\phi ^ { * } : { \mathcal { D } } _ { R ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { D } } _ { R }$ . It is vital to the theory that this functor is an equivalence of categories when $\phi$ is a weak equivalence. As we explain, this allows us to replace general $S$ -algebras by better behaved $^ { * } q$ -cofibrant" ones whenever convenient, without changing the derived category.

Regard $R ^ { \prime }$ as a right $R$ -module via the composite

$$
R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { S } R { \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \phi } } R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { S } R ^ { \prime } { \xrightarrow { \mu ^ { \prime } } } R ^ { \prime } .
$$

Observe that $R ^ { \prime }$ is an $( R ^ { \prime } , R )$ -bimodule with the evident left action by $R ^ { \prime }$ and that, for an $R$ -module $M$ , $R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } M$ is an $R ^ { \prime }$ -module.

PROPOSITION 4.1. Define $\phi _ { * } : \mathcal { M } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { R ^ { \prime } }$ by $\phi _ { * } M = R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } M$ . Then $\phi _ { * }$ is left adjoint to $\phi ^ { * }$ , and the adjunction induces a derived adjunction

$$
{ \mathcal { D } } _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( \phi _ { * } M , M ^ { \prime } ) \cong { \mathcal { D } } _ { R } ( M , \phi ^ { * } M ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

Moreover, the functor $\phi _ { * }$ preserves cell modules.

PRooF. The required isomorphism

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( \phi _ { * } M , M ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( M , \phi ^ { * } M ^ { \prime } )
$$

is proven exactly as in algebra. It sends an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -map $M \longrightarrow M ^ { \prime }$ to the induced composite

$$
R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } M ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } M ^ { \prime } \cong M ^ { \prime } ,
$$

and it sends an $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ -map $R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow M ^ { \prime }$ to its restriction along the canonical map $M \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime } \land _ { R } M$ . Since the functor $\phi ^ { * }$ preserves weak equivalences, it is formal that the functor $\phi _ { * }$ carries $R$ -modules of the homotopy types of cell modules to $R ^ { \prime }$ -modules of the homotopy types of cell modules and induces an adjunction on derived categories [38, I.5.13]. Clearly

$$
{ \cal R } ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { \cal R } ( { \cal R } \wedge _ { \cal S } { \cal L } ) \cong { \cal R } ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { \cal S } { \cal L }
$$

for an $_ { s }$ -module $L$ Therefore the functor $\phi _ { * }$ carries sphere $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules to sphere $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ -modules. Since, as a left adjoint, $\phi _ { * }$ preserves colimits, this implies that $\phi _ { * }$ preserves cell modules and not just homotopy types of cell modules.

THEOREM 4.2. Let $\phi : R \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime }$ be a weak equivalence of $\boldsymbol { S }$ -algebras. Then $\phi _ { * } : \mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { R ^ { \prime } }$ and $\phi ^ { * } : { \mathcal { D } } _ { R ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { D } } _ { R }$ are inverse adjoint equivalences of categories.

PRoOF. If $M$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module, then the unit

$$
\phi \wedge _ { R } \mathrm { i d } : M \cong R \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } M
$$

of the adjunction is a weak equivalence by Theorem 3.8. Now let $M ^ { \prime }$ be an $\pmb { R } ^ { \prime }$ -module. In the derived category, the composite $\phi _ { * } \phi ^ { * } M ^ { \prime }$ means $R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } \Gamma M ^ { \prime }$ , where $\Gamma M ^ { \prime }$ is a cell $R$ -module for which there is a weak equivalence of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $\gamma : \Gamma M ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow \phi ^ { * } M ^ { \prime }$ . The counit of the adjunction is given by

$$
\mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { \phi } \gamma : R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } \Gamma M ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } M ^ { \prime } \cong M ^ { \prime } .
$$

An easy diagram chase shows that the composite map of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules

$$
\Gamma M ^ { \prime } \cong R \wedge _ { R } \Gamma M ^ { \prime } { \xrightarrow { \phi \wedge _ { R } \mathrm { i d } } } R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } \Gamma M ^ { \prime } { \xrightarrow { \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { \phi } \gamma } } R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } M ^ { \prime } \cong M ^ { \prime }
$$

coincides with $\gamma$ Since $\phi \wedge _ { R }$ id is a weak equivalence, so is d $\wedge _ { \phi } \gamma$

We shall give the category of $S$ -algebras a Quillen (closed) model category structure in Chapter VII. We will then have the notion of a $^ { 6 6 } q$ -cofibrant $S .$ . algebra", which is a retract of a "cell $S \mathrm { . }$ -algebra". For any $S$ algebra $R$ , there is a weak equivalence $\lambda : \Lambda R \longrightarrow R$ where $\Lambda R$ is a cell $S ^ { }$ -algebra. By the previous result, $\lambda$ induces an adjoint equivalence between the categories $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { D } _ { \Lambda R }$ . Actually, we will have two quite different model categories, one for $S .$ . algebras and another for commutative $S$ -algebras. The comments that we have just made apply in either context. As we shall explain in $\mathrm { V I I } \ S 6$ , the forgetful functor from $R \mathrm { . }$ algebras to $R$ -modules is better behaved homotopically in the non-commutative case than in the commutative case. In fact, VII.6.2 will give the following result.

THeOreM 4.3. If $R$ is a $q$ -cofibrant $\boldsymbol { S }$ -algebra, then $( R , S )$ is a retract of a relative cell $s$ -module, the inclusion $S \longrightarrow R$ being the unit of $\scriptstyle { R }$ Therefore $( R , S )$ has the homotopy type of a relative CW $s$ -module.

Since we can approximate a commutative $S \mathrm { . }$ -algebra by a non-commutative cell $S _ { \ l }$ -algebra without changing the derived category of modules (up to equivalence), we can use the previous result to obtain homotopical information about the derived categories of commutative $S .$ algebras.

We illustrate the force of these ideas by using them to obtain a complementary adjunction to the case of Proposition 4.1 that is obtained by specializing to the unit $\eta : S \longrightarrow R$ of an $S _ { \ l }$ -algebra $R$ :

$$
{ \mathcal D } _ { R } ( R \wedge _ { S } M , N ) \cong { \mathcal D } _ { S } ( M , \eta ^ { * } N )
$$

for $S _ { \ l }$ -modules $M$ and $R$ -modules $N$ .

PRoPosITIoN 4.4. The forgetful functor $\eta ^ { * } : \mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { S }$ has a right adjoint $\eta ^ { \# } : \mathcal { D } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { R } _ { . }$ , so that

$$
\mathcal { D } _ { R } ( N , \eta ^ { \# } M ) \cong \mathcal { D } _ { S } ( \eta ^ { * } N , M )
$$

for $\boldsymbol { S }$ -modules $M$ and $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $N$ .

ProoF. On the point set level, we have the adjunction

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( \eta ^ { * } N , M ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( N , F _ { S } ( R , M ) ) .
$$

Here we regard $\scriptstyle { R }$ as an $( S , R )$ -bimodule, and the right action of $\scriptstyle { R }$ on itself induces a left action of $\scriptstyle { R }$ on $F _ { S } ( R , M )$ (as with Hom functors in algebra). However, there is no reason to believe that the functor $F _ { S } ( R , M )$ of $M$ preserves weak equivalences, so that it is not clear how to pass to derived categories. Let $\lambda : \Lambda R \longrightarrow R$ be a weak equivalence of $S _ { \ l }$ algebras, where $\Lambda R$ is a cell $S .$ algebra. It follows easily from the previous theorem that the functor

$$
F _ { S } ( \Lambda R , M ) : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { \Lambda R }
$$

of $M$ does preserve weak equivalences. We therefore have an adjunction

$$
\mathcal { D } _ { S } ( ( \eta ^ { \prime } ) ^ { * } N ^ { \prime } , M ) \cong \mathcal { D } _ { \Lambda R } ( N ^ { \prime } , F _ { S } ( \Lambda R , M ) )
$$

for $\Lambda R$ -modules $N ^ { \prime }$ and $_ { s }$ -modules $M$ , where $\eta ^ { \prime }$ is the unit of $\Lambda R$ Theorem 4.2 implies that we also have an adjunction

$$
\mathcal { D } _ { \Lambda R } ( { \lambda } ^ { * } N , N ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { D } _ { R } ( N , { \lambda } _ { * } N ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

Since $\eta = \lambda \circ \eta ^ { \prime } : S \longrightarrow R$ and these forgetful functors all preserve weak equivalences, $\eta ^ { * } = ( \eta ^ { \prime } ) ^ { * } \circ \lambda ^ { * } : \mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { S }$ We define $\eta ^ { \# } ( M ) = \lambda _ { * } F _ { S } ( \Lambda R , M )$ and obtain the desired adjunction as the composite of the adjunctions just given.

# 5. Symmetric and extended powers of $R$ -modules

Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ be a commutative $S$ -algebra and $M$ be an $R$ module. The $j$ th symmetric power of $M$ is defined to be $M ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j }$ and the $j$ th extended power of $M$ is defined to be

$$
{ \cal D } _ { j } { \cal M } = ( E \Sigma _ { j } ) _ { + } \wedge _ { \Sigma _ { j } } { \cal M } ^ { j } .
$$

In both notions, $M ^ { j }$ denotes the $j$ -th power of $M$ with respect to $\wedge _ { R }$ . One of the most striking features of our smash product of $R$ -modules is that, in the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ , these are essentially equivalent notions. This fact will give us homotopical control on free commutative $\scriptstyle { R } $ -algebras and will play an important role in our study of Bousfield localizations of commutative $R _ { ☉ }$ -algebras in Chapter VIII; it will not be needed before then.

To explain this fact, observe that I.5.6 implies that, for a spectrum $K$ , we have an equivariant isomorphism

$$
( \mathbb { L } K ) ^ { j } \cong \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes K ^ { j } ,
$$

where the $j$ -th power is taken with respect to $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ on the left and with respect to the external smash product on the right. Therefore

$$
( \mathbb { L } K ) ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j } \cong \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { j } } K ^ { j } .
$$

This is the core of the claimed equivalence between symmetric and extended powers of $R$ -modules. However, to retain sufficient homotopical control on our constructions to prove the equivalence, we must assume that $R$ is a $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrant commutative $S .$ -algebra and apply results to be proven in $\mathrm { V I I } \ S 6$ . Note that, as the initial object in the category of commutative $s$ -algebras, $S$ itself is $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrant.

THEOREM 5.1. Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ be a $q$ -cofibrant commutative $S$ -algebra. If $M$ is a cell $R$ -module, then the projection

$$
\pi : ( E \Sigma _ { j } ) _ { + } \wedge _ { \Sigma _ { j } } M ^ { j } \longrightarrow M ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j }
$$

is a homotopy equivalence of spectra.

ProoF. The conclusion is trivial for $j = 1$ and we may assume inductively that it holds for $i < j$ .

We first prove the result for any $j$ when $M$ is the free $R$ -module generated by a CW-spectrum $X$ Expanding definitions and commuting the smash product with $( E \Sigma _ { j } ) _ { + }$ through our constructions, we find that

$$
M ^ { j } \cong R \wedge _ { S } S \wedge _ { \pm } ( { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes X ^ { j } ) ,
$$

$$
( E \Sigma _ { j } ) _ { + } \wedge M ^ { j } \cong R \wedge _ { S } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( ( E \Sigma _ { j } \times \mathcal { L } ( j ) ) \ltimes X ^ { j } ) ,
$$

and $\pi$ is induced from the $\Sigma _ { j }$ -equivalence $E \Sigma _ { j } \times \mathcal { L } ( j ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( j )$ by passage to orbits. When $R = S$ , $\pi$ is a homotopy equivalence of spectra by I.8.5 and the equivariant version of I.2.5. For general $R$ ,VII.6.5 and VII.6.7 imply that the functor $R \wedge _ { S } ( - )$ carries this homotopy equivalence to a weak equivalence. However, the domain and target have the homotopy types of CW spectra, by VII.6.6.

Next, let $M$ be a subcomplex of a cell $R$ -module $N$ and assume that the conclusion holds for $M$ and $N / M$ As explained for the (external) smash power of spectra in [14, pp37-38] and works equally well for the (internal) smash power of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, we have a filtration of $N ^ { j }$ by $\Sigma _ { j }$ -cofibrations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules

$$
M ^ { j } = F _ { j } N ^ { j } \subset F _ { j - 1 } N ^ { j } \subset \dots \subset F _ { 1 } N ^ { j } \subset F _ { 0 } N ^ { j } = N ^ { j } .
$$

Here $F _ { i } N ^ { j }$ is the union of the subcomplexes $M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge _ { R } M _ { j }$ , where each $M _ { k }$ is either $M$ or $N$ and $_ i$ of the $M _ { k }$ are $M$ The subquotients can be identified equivariantly as

$$
F _ { i } N ^ { j } / F _ { i + 1 } N ^ { j } \cong \Sigma _ { j } \times _ { \Sigma _ { i } \times \Sigma _ { j - i } } ( M ^ { i } \wedge _ { R } ( N / M ) ^ { j - i } ) .
$$

As a $\left( \Sigma _ { i } \times \Sigma _ { j - i } \right)$ -space, $E \Sigma _ { j }$ is homotopy equivalent to $E \Sigma _ { i } \times E \Sigma _ { j - i }$ , and there result homotopy equivalences

$$
( E \Sigma _ { j } ) _ { + } \wedge _ { \Sigma _ { j } } F _ { i } N ^ { j } / F _ { i + 1 } N ^ { j } \simeq ( ( E \Sigma _ { i } ) _ { + } \wedge _ { \Sigma _ { i } } M ^ { i } ) \wedge _ { R } ( ( E \Sigma _ { j - i } ) _ { + } \wedge _ { \Sigma _ { j - i } } ( N / M ) ^ { j - i } ) .
$$

Applying the original induction hypothesis on $j$ and inducting up the filtration, we deduce the conclusion for $N$ .

Finally, turning to the general case, let $\{ M _ { n } \}$ be the sequential filtration of $M$ , with $M _ { 0 } = *$ .By the first step, the conclusion holds for each $M _ { n + 1 } / M _ { n }$ . By the second step, the conclusion for $M _ { n }$ implies the conclusion for $M _ { n + 1 }$ . Since $M ^ { j }$ is the colimit of the sequence of $\Sigma _ { j }$ -cofibrations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $( M _ { n } ) ^ { j } \longrightarrow ( M _ { n + 1 } ) ^ { j }$ the conclusion for $M$ follows.

# 6. Function $R$ -modules

Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ be an $S _ { \ l }$ -algebra. We have a function $R$ -module functor $F _ { R }$ to go with our smash product. Its definition is dictated by the expected adjunction.

DEFInITIoN 6.1. Let $M$ and $N$ be (left) $R _ { ☉ }$ modules. Define $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ to be the equalizer displayed in the following diagram of $S$ -modules:

$$
F _ { R } ( M , N ) { \longrightarrow } F _ { S } ( M , N ) { \xrightarrow { \mu ^ { * } } } F _ { S } ( R \wedge _ { S } M , N ) .
$$

Here $\mu ^ { * } = F _ { S } ( \mu , \mathrm { i d } )$ and $\omega$ is the adjoint of the composite

$$
R \wedge _ { S } \left( M \wedge _ { S } F _ { S } ( M , N ) \right) { \overset { \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { S } } { \longrightarrow } } R \wedge _ { S } N { \overset { \nu } { \longrightarrow } } N .
$$

When $R = S$ , our new and old function $S$ -modules $F _ { S } ( M , N )$ are identical. We state the expected adjunction in a general form, but we are most interested in the case $R ^ { \prime } = S$ .

LEMMA 6.2. Let M be an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule, $N$ be an $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ -module, and $P$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. Then

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } N , P ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( N , F _ { R } ( M , P ) ) .
$$

Proor. The general case follows from the case $R = S$ of Lemma 3.2 by use of the coequalizer definition of $\wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } }$ and the equalizer definition of $F _ { R }$ .

As in algebra, this leads to a function module analogue of Proposition 3.4.

PROPOSITION 6.3. Let $M$ be an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule, $N$ be an $( R ^ { \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule, and $P$ be an $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule. Then $F _ { R } ( M , P )$ is an $( R ^ { \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule, and

$$
{ \cal F } _ { R } ( M \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime } } N , { \cal P } ) \cong { \cal F } _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( N , { \cal F } _ { R } ( M , { \cal P } ) )
$$

as $( R ^ { \prime \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodules.

Similarly, the unit isomorphism of Lemma 3.5 implies a counit isomorphism.

LEMMA 6.4. The adjoint $\tilde { \lambda } : M \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( R , M )$ is an isomorphism.

We also have analogs of Proposition 3.6 and Corollary 3.7. While we are interested primarily in the versions relating $F _ { R }$ to the functor $\wedge _ { S }$ , there are also versions relating $F _ { R }$ to the functor $F _ { S }$ . The following lemma is needed for the latter versions. Its algebraic analogue is proven by a formal argument that applies equally well in topology.

LEMMA 6.5. Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $R ^ { \prime }$ be $S$ -algebras.

(i) Let $M$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module, $M ^ { \prime }$ be an $R ^ { \prime }$ -module and $P$ be an ${ \cal R } \Lambda _ { S } { \cal R } ^ { \prime }$ -module. Then there is a natural bijection

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M , F _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( M ^ { \prime } , P ) ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R \wedge s R ^ { \prime } } ( M \wedge _ { S } M ^ { \prime } , P ) .
$$

ii) Let $M$ be a left $R$ -module, $N$ be a right $R$ -module, and $K$ be an $S$ -module. Then there is a natural bijection

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M , F _ { S } ( N , K ) ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( N \wedge _ { R } M , K ) .
$$

Proof. It suffices to check (i) when $M = R \wedge _ { S } L$ and $M ^ { \prime } = R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { S } L ^ { \prime }$ are the free modules generated by $S$ -modules $L$ and $L ^ { \prime }$ Similarly, it suffices to check (ii) when $M = R \wedge _ { S } L$ These cases are easy consequences of our adjunctions.

PROPOSITION 6.6. Let $\boldsymbol { K }$ be an $S$ -module and $M$ be a left $R$ -module. There is a natural isomorphism of left $R$ -modules

$$
F _ { R } ( K \wedge _ { S } R , M ) \cong F _ { S } ( K , M )
$$

and a natural isomorphism of right $R$ -modules

$$
F _ { R } ( M , F _ { S } ( R , K ) ) \cong F _ { S } ( M , K ) .
$$

ProoF. The first isomorphism is immediate from the following chain of isomorphisms of represented functors on left $R$ -modules $N$ , which result from Proposition 6.3, Proposition 3.6, and Theorem 1.1(iii), respectively.

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( N , F _ { R } ( K \wedge _ { S } R , M ) ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( ( K \wedge _ { S } R ) \wedge _ { R } N , M ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( K \wedge _ { S } N , M ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( N , F _ { S } ( K , M ) ) . } \end{array}
$$

The second isomorphism results from the following chain of isomorphisms of represented functors on right $R$ -modules $N$ :

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { M } _ { R ^ { o p } } ( N , F _ { R } ( M , F _ { S } ( R , K ) ) ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { o p } } ( M \wedge _ { S } N , F _ { S } ( R , K ) ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( R \wedge _ { R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { o p } } ( M \wedge _ { S } N ) , K ) } \\ & { \qquad \cong \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( M \wedge _ { R ^ { o p } } N , K ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R ^ { o p } } ( N , F _ { S } ( M , K ) ) . } \end{array}
$$

The first two isomorphisms are instances of isomorphisms of the lemma. The third follows from the fact that there is a natural isomorphism

$$
R \wedge _ { R \wedge _ { S } R ^ { o p } } ( M \wedge _ { S } N ) \cong M \wedge _ { R ^ { o p } } N ,
$$

as is easily checked when $M$ and $N$ are free $R$ -modules and follows in general.

COROLLARY 6.7. Let $X$ be a spectrum and $M$ be an $R$ -module. There is a natural isomorphism of left $R$ -modules

$$
F _ { R } ( \mathbb { F } _ { R } X , M ) \cong F _ { S } ( \mathbb { F } _ { S } X , M )
$$

and a natural isomorphism of right $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules

$$
F _ { R } ( M , \mathbb { F } _ { R } ^ { \# } X ) \cong F _ { S } ( M , \mathbb { F } _ { S } ^ { \# } X ) ) .
$$

The functor $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ converts colimits and cofiber sequences in $M$ to limits and fiber sequences and it preserves limits and fiber sequences in $N$ , as we see formally on the spectrum level (compare [38, III.2.5]) and deduce in order on the levels of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra, $S$ -modules, and $R$ -modules (compare II.1.5 and Theorem 1.1). Using the previous corollary to deal with sphere $R$ -modules and proceeding by induction up the sequential filtration of $M$ , we obtain the analogue of Theorem 3.8.

THeOreM 6.8. If $M$ is a cell $R$ -module and $\phi : N \longrightarrow N ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of $R$ -modules, then

$$
F _ { R } ( \mathrm { i d } , \phi ) : F _ { R } ( { \cal M } , { \cal N } ) \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( { \cal M } , { \cal N ^ { \prime } } )
$$

is a weak equivalence.

In the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ , $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ means $F _ { R } ( \Gamma M , N )$ , where $\Gamma M$ is a cell approximation of $M$ We are entitled to conclude that

$$
{ \mathcal { D } } _ { R } ( M \land _ { S } N , P ) \cong { \mathcal { D } } _ { S } ( N , F _ { R } ( M , P ) ) .
$$

As in Proposition 3.9, we have the following calculational sharpening of Corollary 6.7. It will be the starting point for our later construction of a spectral sequence for the calculation of $\pi _ { * } ( F _ { R } ( M , N ) )$ .

COROLLARY 6.9. Let $X$ be a wedge of sphere spectra and $N$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. Then there is an isomorphism

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \pi _ { * } ( F _ { R } ( \mathbb { F } X , N ) ) \cong \mathrm { H o m } _ { \pi _ { * } ( R ) } ( \pi _ { * } ( R ) \otimes H _ { * } ( X ) , \pi _ { * } ( N ) ) } \end{array}
$$

that is natural in the $R$ -modules $\mathbb { F } X$ and $N$ .

We end this section by recording a composition pairing that is a formal implication of Lemma 6.2 and Proposition 6.3. This works exactly as with tensor products and Hom in algebra and, as there, it is convenient for this purpose to use the commutativity of the smash product over $s$ to rewrite our adjunctions and isomorphisms with their variables occurring in the same order on both sides, returning to our original conventions of $\mathrm { I } \ S 7$ Thus, for an $S$ -module $L$ and for $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $M$ and $N$ , we have the natural isomorphism of $S .$ -modules

$$
F _ { R } ( L \land _ { S } M , N ) \cong F _ { S } ( L , F _ { R } ( M , N ) ) .
$$

Let $P$ be another $\scriptstyle { R }$ module. Using the evaluation $\scriptstyle R$ -map

$$
\varepsilon : F _ { R } ( M , N ) \land _ { S } M \longrightarrow N ,
$$

we obtain a composite $\scriptstyle { R }$ -map

$$
F _ { R } ( N , P ) \land _ { S } F _ { R } ( M , N ) \land _ { S } M { \stackrel { \mathrm { i d } \land _ { S } \xi } { \longrightarrow } } F _ { R } ( N , P ) \land _ { S } N { \stackrel { \varepsilon } { \longrightarrow } } P .
$$

Its adjoint is a composition pairing of $S ^ { }$ -modules

$$
\pi : F _ { R } ( N , P ) \land _ { S } F _ { R } ( M , N ) \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( M , P ) .
$$

This pairing is unital and associative in the sense that the following diagrams commute; let $\eta : S \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( M , M )$ be the adjoint of $\lambda : S \wedge _ { S } M \longrightarrow M$ .

$$
\begin{array} { r }  F _ { R } ( N , P ) \underset { \substack { \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow } { \wedge _ { S } S } } \\ { F _ { R } ( N , P ) \underset { \substack { \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow F _ { R } ( N , N ) \underset { \substack { \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow \longleftrightarrow ( N , P ) , } } } } { \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( N , N ) } } \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { S \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( M , \underline { { N } } ) } & { { } } \\ { \eta \wedge _ { S } \mathsf { i d } \Big \downarrow \qquad } & { { } \bigwedge } \\ { F _ { R } ( N , N ) \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( M , N ) \qquad } & { { } \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( M , N ) , } \end{array}
$$

and, for another $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $L$ ,

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { F _ { R } ( N , P ) \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( M , N ) \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( L , M ) \overset { \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { S } \pi } { \longrightarrow } F _ { R } ( N , P ) \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( L , N ) } \\ & { \qquad \quad \overset { \pi \wedge _ { S } \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } \ V } \\ & { F _ { R } ( M , P ) \wedge _ { S } F _ { R } ( L , M ) \xrightarrow { \pi } F _ { R } ( L , P ) . } \end{array}
$$

This leads to a host of examples of $S .$ -algebras and their modules.

PROPOSITION 6.12. Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ be an $S$ -algebra and let $M$ and $N$ be (left) $\scriptstyle { R }$ . modules. Then $F _ { R } ( N , N )$ is an $\boldsymbol { S }$ -algebra with product $\pi$ and unit $\eta$ Moreover, $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ is an $( F _ { R } ( N , N ) , F _ { R } ( M , M ) )$ -bimodule with left and right actions given by $\pi$ .

# 7. Commutative $S$ -algebras and duality theory

We assume that $\scriptstyle R$ is a commutative $S .$ -algebra in this section, and we show that the study of modules over $R$ works in exactly the same way as the study of modules over commutative algebras. If $\mu : R \land _ { S } M \longrightarrow M$ gives $M$ a left $R$ -module structure, then $\mu \circ \tau : M \wedge _ { S } R \longrightarrow M$ gives $M$ a right $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module structure such that $M$ is an $( R , R )$ -bimodule. As in the study of modules over commutative algebras, this leads to the following important conclusion.

TheoreM 7.1. If $M$ and $N$ are $R$ -modules, then $M \land _ { R } N$ and $F _ { R } ( M , N )$ have canonical $R$ -module structures induced from the $R$ -module structure of $M$ or, equivalently, $N$ . The smash product over $\scriptstyle { R }$ is commutative, associative and unital. There is an adjunction

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( L \wedge _ { R } M , N ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( L , F _ { R } ( M , N ) ) .
$$

Moreover, the adjunction passes to derived categories.

We have the following consequence of Corollary 3.7.

PROPOsiTION 7.3. If $M$ and $M ^ { \prime }$ are cell $R$ -modules, then $M \wedge _ { R } M ^ { \prime }$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module with one $( p + q )$ -cell for each $p$ -cell of $M$ and $q$ -cell of $M ^ { \prime }$ .

For $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $L , M$ and $N$ , we have a natural isomorphism of $R$ -modules

$$
F _ { R } ( L \land _ { R } M , N ) \cong F _ { R } ( L , F _ { R } ( M , N ) )
$$

because both sides represent the same functor. Exactly as in the previous section, but working entirely with $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, we obtain a natural associative and $\scriptstyle { R } .$ . unital composition pairing

$$
\pi : F _ { R } ( { \cal M } , { \cal N } ) \wedge _ { R } F _ { R } ( { \cal L } , { \cal M } ) \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( { \cal L } , { \cal N } ) .
$$

The formal duality theory explained in [38, Ch. II] applies to the stable category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Define the dual of $M$ to be $D _ { R } M = F _ { R } ( M , R )$ . We have an evaluation map $\varepsilon : D _ { R } M \land _ { R } M \longrightarrow R$ and a map $\eta : R \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( { \cal M } , { \cal M } )$ , namely the adjoint of $\lambda : R \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow M$ There is also a natural map

$$
\nu : F _ { R } ( L , M ) \wedge _ { R } N \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( L , M \wedge _ { R } N ) .
$$

By composition with the isomorphism $F _ { R } ( \mathrm { i d } , \lambda )$ , $\nu$ specializes to a map

$$
\nu : D _ { R } M \land _ { R } M \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( M , M ) .
$$

We say that $M$ is "strongly dualizable", if it has a coevaluation map $\bar { \eta } : R \longrightarrow$ $M \wedge _ { R } D _ { R } M$ such that the following diagram commutes in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ :

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { R \xrightarrow { \tilde { \eta } } \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { } & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \eta \Biggl | \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { } & { F _ { R } ( M , M ) \xleftarrow { \nu } \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad D _ { R } M \wedge D _ { R } M . } \end{array}
$$

The definition has many purely formal implications. The map $\nu$ of (7.6) is an isomorphism in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ if either $L$ or $N$ is strongly dualizable. The map $\nu$ of
