We shall see in a moment that $M / X M$ is independent of the ordering of the elements of the set $X$ .If $I$ denotes the ideal generated by a regular sequence $X$ , then, by Corollary 2.10 below, $M / X M$ is independent of the choice of regular sequence (under reasonable hypotheses) and it is reasonable to define

$$
M / I M = M / X M .
$$

However, this notation must be used with caution since, if we fail to restrict attention to regular sequences $X$ , the homotopy type of $M / X M$ will depend on the set $X$ and not just on the ideal it generates. For example, quite different modules are obtained if we repeat a generator $x _ { i }$ of $I$ in our construction.

As in algebra, we can describe the construction on general $R$ -modules $M$ as the smash product of $M$ with the construction on $R$ regarded as an $R$ -module. We write $R / X$ or $R / I$ instead of $R / X R$ or $R / I R$ .

LEMMA 1.10. For a sequence $X$ of elements of $R _ { * }$ , there is a natural isomorphism in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$

$$
( R / X ) \land _ { R } M \longrightarrow M / X M .
$$

In particular, for  finie squence $X = \{ x _ { 1 } , \ldots , x _ { n } \}$ ,

$$
R / ( x _ { 1 } , \ldots , x _ { n } ) \simeq ( R / x _ { 1 } ) \wedge _ { R } \cdots \wedge _ { R } ( R / x _ { n } ) ,
$$

and $R / X$ is therefore independent of the ordering of the elements of $X$ .

ProoF. Working on the point-set level, we have an isomorphism of cofiber sequences :

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } { S _ { R } ^ { n } \wedge _ { R } R \wedge _ { R } M \xrightarrow { x \wedge \mathrm { i d } } R \wedge _ { R } M \xrightarrow { \rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } } ( R / x R ) \wedge _ { R } M } & \\ { \quad } & { { \mathrm { i d } } \wedge \lambda { \Bigg \downarrow } } \\ { S _ { R } ^ { n } \wedge _ { R } M \xrightarrow { x } } & { { \not \downarrow } \xrightarrow { { \nu } } M \xrightarrow { \rho } } & { { M / x M } . } \end{array}
$$

We only claim an isomorphism in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ since, working homotopically, we should replace $R$ by $S _ { R }$ and use the weak equivalence $\zeta \wedge \mathrm { i d } : S _ { R } \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow R \wedge _ { R } M$ to obtain a composite weak equivalence $( S _ { R } / x S _ { R } ) \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow ( R / x R ) \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow$ $M / x M$ The rest follows by iteration and use of the commutativity of $\wedge _ { R }$ .

We turn next to localizations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules at subsets $X = \{ x _ { i } \}$ of $R _ { * }$ . We restrict attention to countable sets for notational convenience, but this restriction can easily be removed. Let $\{ y _ { i } \}$ be any cofinal sequence of products of the $x _ { i }$ , such as that specified inductively by $y _ { 1 } = x _ { 1 }$ and $y _ { i } = x _ { 1 } \cdot \cdot \cdot x _ { i } y _ { i - 1 }$ . If $y _ { i } \in R _ { n _ { i } }$ , we may represent $y _ { i }$ by an $R$ map $S _ { R } ^ { 0 } \longrightarrow S _ { R } ^ { - n _ { i } }$ , which we also denote by $y _ { i }$ Let $q _ { 0 } = 0$ and, inductively, $q _ { i } = q _ { i - 1 } + n _ { i }$ . The map of $R .$ -modules

$$
S _ { R } ^ { 0 } \wedge _ { R } M { \overset { y _ { i } \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } } S _ { R } ^ { - n _ { i } } \wedge _ { R } M
$$

reprsents $y _ { i }$ Smashing over $\scriptstyle { R }$ with $S _ { R } ^ { - q _ { i - 1 } }$ and using equivalences (1 obtain a sequence of maps of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules

$$
S _ { R } ^ { - q _ { i - 1 } } \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow S _ { R } ^ { - q _ { i } } \wedge _ { R } M .
$$

DEFINITIoN 1.12. Define the localization of $M$ at $X$ , denoted $M [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ , to be the telescope of the sequence of maps (1.11). Since $M \simeq S _ { R } ^ { 0 } \wedge _ { R } M$ in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ , we may regard the inclusion of the initial stage $S _ { R } ^ { 0 } \wedge _ { R } M$ of the telescope as a natural map $\lambda : M \longrightarrow M [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ .

Again, we have an analogue of the standard universal property of localization in algebra.

LEMMA 1.13. Let $N$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module such that $x _ { i } : \Sigma ^ { k _ { i } } N \longrightarrow N$ , $\deg x _ { i } = k _ { i }$ , is an equivalence for each $_ i$ and let $\alpha : M \longrightarrow N$ be a map of $R$ -modules. Then there is a unique map of $R$ -modules ${ \tilde { \alpha } } : M [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \longrightarrow N$ such that $\tilde { \alpha } \circ \lambda = \alpha$ .

ProoF. Passage to telescopes gives ${ \tilde { \alpha } } : M [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \longrightarrow N [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \simeq N$ The $\mathrm { l i m } ^ { 1 }$ term is zero in the exact sequence

$$
0 \longrightarrow \operatorname * { l i m } ^ { 1 } [ \Sigma ^ { 1 - q _ { i } } M , N ] _ { R } \longrightarrow [ M [ X ^ { - 1 } ] , N ] _ { R } \longrightarrow \operatorname * { l i m } [ \Sigma ^ { - q _ { i } } M , N ] _ { R } \longrightarrow 0
$$

since the maps of the inverse system are isomorphisms. Therefore $\tilde { \alpha }$ is unique.

Since homotopy groups commute with localization, by III.1.7, we see immediately that $\lambda$ induces an isomorphism of $R _ { * }$ -modules

$$
\pi _ { * } ( M [ X ^ { - 1 } ] ) \cong \pi _ { * } ( M ) [ X ^ { - 1 } ] .
$$

Arguing as in Lemma 1.10, we see that the localization of $M$ is the smash product of $M$ with the localization of $R$ .

LEMMA 1.15. For a set $X$ of elements of $R _ { * }$ , there is a natural weak equivalence

$$
R [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow M [ X ^ { - 1 } ] .
$$

Moreover, $R [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ is independent of the ordering of the elements of $X$ . For sets $X$ and $Y$ , $R [ ( X \cup { \bar { Y } } ) ^ { - 1 } ]$ is equivalent to the composite localization $R [ X ^ { - 1 } ] [ Y ^ { - 1 } ]$ .

ProoF. The independence of ordering is shown by use of the union of any two given cofinal sequences. The last statement is shown by use of the usual Fubini type theorem for iterated homotopy colimits.

# 2. Localizations and quotients of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectra

Again, fix a commutative $S$ algebra $R$ Since $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ is a symmetric monoidal category under $\wedge _ { R }$ with unit $\scriptstyle { R }$ , we have the notion of a monoid or a commutative monoid in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ . These are the analogs of associative or of associative and commutative ring spectra in classical stable homotopy theory. As there, we must allow weaker structures.

DEfinitioN 2.1. An $R$ -ring spectrum $A$ is an $R$ -module $A$ with unit $\eta : R \longrightarrow$ $A$ and product $\phi : A \land _ { R } A \longrightarrow A$ in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ such that the following left and right unit diagram commutes in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ .

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { R \wedge _ { R } \underset {  \sum } { A \frac { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \eta } } { \longrightarrow } A \wedge _ { R } A \overset { \mathrm { i d } \wedge \eta } {  } A \wedge _ { R } R  } \\ & { \qquad \bigwedge ^ { \phi } _ { A . }  } \end{array}
$$

Of course, by neglect of structure, an $R \mathrm { . }$ ring spectrum $A$ is a ring spectrum in the sense of classical stable homotopy theory; its unit is the composite of the unit of $\scriptstyle { R }$ and the unit of $A$ and its product is the composite of the product of $A$ and the canonical map

$$
A \land A \simeq A \land _ { S } A \longrightarrow A \land _ { R } A .
$$

Similarly, for an $R$ -ring spectrum $A$ , we have the evident homotopical notion of an $A { \mathrm { . } }$ module spectrum $M$ Here, in conformity with the definition just given, we only require that the action $\mu : A \land _ { R } M \longrightarrow M$ satisfy the unit condition $\mu \circ ( \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } ) = \mathrm { i d }$ in $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ When $A$ is associative, it is conventional to insist that $M$ also satisfy the evident associativity condition. These structures play a role in the study of our new derived categories of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules that is analogous to the role played by ring spectra and their module spectra in classical stable homotopy theory. When $R = S$ ,I.6.7 and II.1.9 imply that $S .$ -ring spectra and their module spectra are equivalent to classical ring spectra and their module spectra.

LeMMA 2.2. If $A$ and $B$ are $R$ -ring spectra, then so is $A \land _ { R } B$ . If $A$ and $B$ are associative or commutative, then so is $A \land _ { R } B$ .

We ask the behavior of quotients and localizations with respect to $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring structures. For localizations, the answer is immediate. We shall give a sharper point-set level analogue of the following result in VIII§2.

PROPOSITION 2.3. Let $X$ be a set of elements of $R _ { * }$ . If $A$ is an $R$ -ring spectrum, then $A [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ is an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectrum such that $\lambda : A \longrightarrow A [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ is a map of $R$ -ring spectra. If $A$ is associative or commutative, then so is $A [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ .

PROOF. Since $A [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \simeq R [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \wedge _ { R } A$ , it suffices to prove that $R [ X ^ { - 1 } ]$ is an associative and commutative $R$ -ring spectrum with unit $\lambda$ . Lemma 1.15 gives an equivalence

$$
R [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \wedge _ { R } R [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \simeq R [ X ^ { - 1 } ] [ X ^ { - 1 } ] \simeq R [ X ^ { - 1 } ]
$$

under $R$ , and this equivalence is the required product.

This doesn't work for quotients since $( R / X ) / X$ is not equivalent to $R / X$ . However, we can analyze the problem by analyzing the deviation, and, by Lemma 1.10, we may as well work one element at a time. We have a necessary condition for $R / x$ to be an $R$ -ring spectrum that will be familiar from classical stable homotopy theory. We generally write $\eta$ and $\phi$ for the units and products of $\scriptstyle { R } \scriptscriptstyle .$ . ring spectra; as stated before, we write $\Sigma ^ { n }$ for the module theoretic suspension functor $S _ { R } ^ { n } \wedge _ { R } ( - )$ .

LEMMA 2.4. Let $A$ be an $R$ -ring spectrum. If $A / x A$ admits an $R$ -ring spectrum structure such that $\rho : A \longrightarrow A / x A$ is a map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectra, then $x : A / x A \longrightarrow A / x A$ is null homotopic as a map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules.

Proor. We have the following commutative diagram (where we omit suspension coordinates from the labels of maps):

$$
\Sigma ^ { n } R \wedge _ { R } ( A / x A ) \overset { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \longleftrightarrow } \Sigma ^ { n } ( A / x A ) \wedge _ { R } ( A / x A ) \overset { x \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } ( A / x A ) \wedge _ { R } ( A / x A )
$$

In view of the following commutative diagram, its top composite is null homotopic:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \underset { \sum ^ { n } R \brace { \eta \mathrm { \tiny \sum } \eta } ^ { \sum ^ { n } A } } { \sum ^ { n } A \longrightarrow } \overset { x } { A } } \\ { \sum ^ { n } R \underset { \eta } { \longrightarrow } \Sigma ^ { n } ( A / x A ) \underset { x } { \longrightarrow } A / x A . } \end{array}
$$

Thus, for example, the Moore spectrum $S / 2$ is not an $S .$ -algebra since the map $2 : S / 2 \longrightarrow S / 2$ is not null homotopic.

We need a lemma in order to obtain an $\scriptstyle { R } \quad$ -ring spectrum structure on $R / x$ in appropriate generality.

LEMMA 2.5. Let $\rho : R \longrightarrow M$ be any map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Then

$$
\left( \rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } \right) \circ \rho = \left( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \rho \right) \circ \rho : R \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { R } M .
$$

PROOF. Since $\lambda = \lambda \circ \tau : R \wedge _ { R } R \longrightarrow R$ the following diagram commutes:

![](images/18193643a1c6dba35d03f8bc187b82236125a2229ed427ac9906a7bf12defc8d.jpg)

THEOREM 2.6. Let $x \ \in \ { R _ { n } }$ , where $\pi _ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ and $\pi _ { 2 n + 1 } ( R / x ) ~ = ~ 0$ . Then $R / x$ admits a structure of $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } }$ -ring spectrum with unit $\rho : R \longrightarrow R / x$ . Therefore $A / X A$ admits $^ { a }$ structure of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectrum such that $\rho : A \longrightarrow$ $A / X A$ is a map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectra for every $\scriptstyle { R }$ -ring spectrum $A$ and every sequence $X$ of elements of $R _ { * }$ such that $\pi _ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ and $\pi _ { 2 n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ for all $x \in X$ , where deg $x = n$ .

PRoOF. Consider the following diagram in the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ .

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \Sigma ^ { 2 n + 1 } R } } \\ { { \lfloor x  } } \\ { { \lfloor x  } } \\ { {  \sum ^ { n + 1 } R - \stackrel { x } { \longrightarrow } \Sigma R   \qquad \quad \stackrel { \mu \sim } \lambda \stackrel { \mu \sim } { \sim } \mu ^ { - 1 } R } } \\ { { \Sigma ^ { n } ( R / x ) \stackrel { x  } { \longrightarrow } R / x \stackrel { \rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } } { \ll } ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( \stackrel { x } { R } / x ) \stackrel { x  } { \ll } \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) \stackrel { x  } { \longrightarrow } \Sigma ( R / x ) } } \\ { { \lfloor x ^ { \prime }  } } \\ { {  \qquad \forall ^ { \prime } { } ^ { n + 1 } R - \stackrel { x  } { \longrightarrow } \Sigma R  } } \\ { { \Sigma ^ { 2 n + 2 } R } } \end{array}
$$

The map $_ x$ is that specified by (1.3). The bottom row is the cofiber sequence that results from the equivalence

$$
( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( R / x ) \simeq ( R / x ) / x
$$

of Lemma 1.10, and the column is also a cofiber sequence. The composite $x \circ \rho$ is null homotopic since $\rho \circ x$ is null homotopic and the square commutes. Therefore there is a map $\nu$ such that $\pi \circ \nu = \rho$ ,and $\nu$ is unique since $\pi _ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ Since $\pi \circ \nu \circ x = \rho \circ x = 0$ $\nu \circ x$ factors through a map $\Sigma ^ { 2 n + 1 } R \longrightarrow R / x .$ Since $\pi _ { 2 n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ , such maps are null homotopic. Thus $\nu \circ x$ is null homotopic. Therefore there is a map $\sigma$ such that $\sigma \circ \rho = \nu$ .Now $\pi \circ \sigma \circ \rho = \pi \circ \nu = \rho$ . hence $( \pi \circ \sigma - \operatorname { i d } ) \rho = 0$ Therefore $\pi \circ \sigma - \mathrm { i d }$ factors through a map $\Sigma ^ { 2 n + 2 } R \longrightarrow$ $\Sigma ^ { n + 1 } ( R / x )$ . Again, such maps are null homotopic. Therefore $\pi \circ \sigma = \operatorname { i d }$ Thus the bottom cofiber sequence splits (proving in passing that $x : \Sigma ^ { n } ( R / x ) \longrightarrow R / x$ is null homotopic, as it must be). A choice $\phi$ of a splitting gives a product on $R / x$ The unit condition $\phi \circ ( \rho { \wedge } \mathrm { i d } ) = \mathrm { i d }$ is automatic. To see that $\phi \circ ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \rho ) = \mathrm { i d }$ , we observe that, by the lemma,

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left( \phi \circ ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \rho ) - \mathrm { i d } \right) \circ \rho = \phi \circ ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \rho - \rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } ) \circ \rho = 0 . } \end{array}
$$

Therefore $\phi \circ ( { \mathrm { i d } } \land \rho ) - { \mathrm { i d } }$ factors through a map $\Sigma ^ { n + 1 } R \longrightarrow R / x$ Again, such maps are null homotopic, hence $\phi \circ ( \operatorname { i d } \wedge \rho ) = \operatorname { i d }$ This completes the proof that $R / x$ is an $R \mathrm { . }$ -ring spectrum with unit $\rho$ .The rest follows from Lemmas 1.10 and 2.2.

The product on $R / x$ can be described a little more concretely. The wedge sum

$$
( \rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } ) \vee \sigma : ( R / x ) \vee \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) \longrightarrow ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( R / x )
$$

is an equivalence. The product $\phi$ restricts to the identity on the first wedge summand and to the trivial map on the second wedge summand. Thus the product is determined by the choice of $\sigma$ , and two choices of $\sigma$ differ by a composite

$$
\Sigma ^ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) \xrightarrow { \pi ^ { \prime } } \Sigma ^ { 2 n + 2 } R \longrightarrow ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( R / x ) .
$$

By the splitting (2.8) and the assumption that $\pi _ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ , we can view the second map as an element of $\pi _ { 2 n + 2 } ( R / x )$ . If $_ x$ is not a zero divisor, then $\pi _ { * } ^ { \prime } = 0$ on homotopy groups and any two products have the same effect on homotopy groups.

Before continuing our discussion of these $R$ -ring spectra, we insert the following easy consequence of the mere existence of the $R$ -ring structure.

COROLLARY 2.10. Assume that $R _ { i } = 0$ if $_ i$ is odd. Let $X$ and $Y$ be regular sequences in $R _ { * }$ that generate the same ideal. Then there is an equivalence of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $\xi : R / X \longrightarrow R / Y$ under $\scriptstyle { R }$ .

Proof. It suffices to construct a map $\xi$ under $\mathcal { R }$ since it will automatically induce an isomorphism on homotopy groups. Each $x _ { i }$ is an $\scriptstyle { R _ { * } }$ -linear combination of the $y _ { j }$ and each $y _ { j } : R / Y \longrightarrow R / Y$ is zero. By Lemma 1.5, we obtain a unique map $\xi _ { i } : R / x _ { i } \longrightarrow R / Y$ under $R$ Since $R / Y$ is an $R _ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ -ring spectrum, we may first take the smash product of these maps and then use the product (associated conveniently) on $R / Y$ and passage to telescopes (if $X$ is infinite) to obtain $\xi$ .

# 3. The associativity and commutativity of $R$ -ring spectra

We assume given an $R$ -ring spectrum $A$ For $x \in R _ { n }$ as in Theorem 2.6, we give $A / x A \simeq ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } A$ the product induced by one of our constructed products on $R / x$ and the given product on $A$ . We refer to any such product as a "canonical" product on $A / x A$ Of course, we do not claim that every product on $A / x A$ is canonical. Observe that, by first using the product on $A$ , the product on $A / x A$ can be factored through

$$
\phi \wedge _ { R } \mathrm { i d } : ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } A \longrightarrow ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } A .
$$

This allows us to smash any diagram giving information about the product on $R / x$ with $A$ and so obtain information about the product on $A / x A$ Obviously any diagram so constructed is a diagram of right $A { \mathrm { . } }$ -module spectra via the product action of $A$ on itself. This smashing with $A$ can kill obstructions. Clearly, a map of $A$ -modules $\Sigma ^ { q } A \longrightarrow M$ is determined by its restriction $S ^ { q } \longrightarrow M$ along the unit of $A$ regarded as a map of spectra (or $S ^ { }$ -modules), which is just an element of $\pi _ { q } ( M )$ . These considerations lead to the following result.

THEOREM 3.1. Let $x \in R _ { n }$ , where $\pi _ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ and $\pi _ { 2 n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ Let A be an $R$ -ring spectrum and assume that $\pi _ { 2 n + 2 } ( A / x A ) = 0$ . Then there is $^ a$ unique canonical product on $A / x A$ . If $A$ is commutative, then $A / x A$ is commutative. If $A$ is associative and $\pi _ { 3 n + 3 } ( { \cal A } / x { \cal A } ) = 0$ , then $A / x A$ is associative.

ProoF. The second arrow of (2.9) becomes zero after smashing with $A$ since it is then given by an element of $\pi _ { 2 n + 2 } ( { \cal A } / { x } { \cal A } ) = 0$ . This proves the uniqueness statement. The commutativity statement follows since if $\phi$ is a canonical product on $A / x A$ , then so is $\phi \tau$ .However, it may be worth displaying the obstruction that lies in $\pi _ { 2 n + 2 } ( A / x A )$ . Looking at the splitting (2.8), we see that $\phi$ is commutative on the wedge summand $R / x$ by the unit property. For the summand $\Sigma ^ { n + 1 } R$ , consider the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \sum ^ { n + 1 } R \xrightarrow { \rho } \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) \xrightarrow { ( \phi - \phi \circ \tau ) \circ \sigma } R / x . } \\ { \xrightarrow [ ] { } \pi ^ { \prime } } \\ { \sum ^ { 2 n + 2 } R } \end{array}
$$

The horizontal composite is null homotopic since $\pi _ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) = 0$ Thus there exists $\gamma$ such that the triangle commutes. It is the obstruction to the commutativity of $R / x$ , and smashing with $A$ gives the obstruction to the commutativity of $A / x A$ .

For the associativity, consider the splitting displayed in the following diagram:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \langle R / \alpha \rangle \nabla \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } \langle R / x \rangle \vee \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } \langle R / x \rangle } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \langle R / x \rangle \nabla \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } \langle R / x \rangle ] \vee \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } [ ( R / x ) \nabla \zeta ^ { n + 1 } \langle R / x \rangle ] } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \langle R / x \rangle \wedge _ { R } \langle R / x \rangle ] \vee \Sigma ^ { n + 1 } [ ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } \langle R / x \rangle ] } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \langle R / x \rangle \wedge _ { R } [ ( R / x ) \nabla \zeta ^ { n + 1 } ( R / x ) ] } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ &  \qquad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad  \end{array}
$$

The question of associativity can be considered separately on the restrictions of the iterated product to the four wedge summands. Via easy diagram chases, we

see that, under the splitting (2.8) and unit isomorphisms, the natural maps

$$
\rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } \wedge \mathrm { i d } : R \wedge _ { R } \left( R / x \right) \wedge _ { R } \left( R / x \right) \longrightarrow \left( R / x \right) \wedge _ { R } \left( R / x \right) \wedge _ { R } \left( R / x \right)
$$

and

$$
\mathrm { i d } \wedge \rho \wedge \mathrm { i d } : ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } R \wedge _ { R } ( R / x ) \longrightarrow ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( R / x ) \wedge _ { R } ( R / x )
$$

correspond to the inclusions of the first and third and first and second wedge summands, respectively. Therefore the unital property of $\phi$ and the unital and associativity properties of $\wedge _ { R }$ imply that the restriction of $\phi$ to the first three wedge summands is associative. Let $\omega$ denote the displayed inclusion of the fourth wedge summand and consider the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \Sigma ^ { 2 n + 2 } R \xrightarrow { \rho \mathrm { ~ }  \Sigma ^ { 2 n + 2 } ( R / x ) } \frac { [ \phi \circ ( \phi \wedge \mathrm { i d } ) - \phi \circ ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \phi ) ] \circ \omega } { R / x }  R / x } & { } \\ { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \sum ^ { 3 n + 3 } R } \end{array}
$$

Call the horizontal composite $_ \alpha$ .If $\alpha$ is nullhomotopic then the deviation from associativity $\left[ \phi \circ ( \phi \wedge \mathrm { i d } ) - \phi \circ ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge \phi ) \right] \circ \omega$ factors through a map $\Sigma ^ { 3 n + 3 } R \longrightarrow R / x$ . Thus if $\pi _ { 3 n + 3 } ( R / x ) = 0$ , then the element $\alpha \in \pi _ { 2 n + 2 } ( R / x )$ is the obstruction to the associativity of $R / x$ . If both relevant homotopy groups become zero after smashing with $A$ , we can conclude that $A / x A$ is associative if $A$ is associative.

We can iterate the argument to arrive at the following fundamental conclusion.

THeORem 3.2. Assume that $R _ { i } = 0$ if $_ i$ is odd and let $X$ be a sequence of non zero divisors in $\scriptstyle { R _ { * } }$ such that $\pi _ { * } ( R / X )$ is concentrated in degrees congruent to zero mod 4. Then $R / X$ has a unique canonical structure of $\scriptstyle { R }$ ring spectrum, and it is commutative and associative.

ProoF. We first observe that for an element $x \in \pi _ { q } ( R )$ , an $R .$ -module $M$ , and an $\scriptstyle { R }$ module $N$ such that $x : \Sigma ^ { q } N \longrightarrow N$ is null homotopic, the map $\rho : M \longrightarrow M / x M$ induces an epimorphism

$$
\rho ^ { * } : [ M / x M , N ] _ { R } \longrightarrow [ M , N ] _ { R }
$$

since the action $x ^ { * } : [ M , N ] _ { R } \longrightarrow [ \Sigma ^ { q } M , N ] _ { R }$ can be computed from the action on $N$ and is therefore zero. Let $X _ { n }$ be the subsequence consisting of the first $_ n$ elements of the sequence $X$ Then $R / X$ is defined to be the telescope of the $R / X _ { n }$ , and Lemma 2.4 implies that multiplication by $x _ { n }$ is null homotopic on $R / X$ for each $_ n$ Since $R / X \wedge _ { R } R / X$ is equivalent to the telescope of the $R / X _ { n } \wedge _ { R } R / X _ { n }$ , we obtain a product on $R / X$ from a canonical product on the ${ \cal R } / X _ { n }$ by passage to telescopes. Moreover, by the Mittag-Leffler criterion, our first observation implies that all relevant $\mathrm { l i m } ^ { 1 }$ terms are zero. Thus it suffices to show that any two products on $X _ { n }$ become equal and the commutativity and associativity diagrams for ${ R \mathord { \left/ { \vphantom { R X _ { n } } } \right. \kern - delimiterspace } X _ { n } }$ become commutative upon composition with the map $R / X _ { n } \longrightarrow R / X$ , and we may proceed by induction on $_ n$ The conclusion follows since the obstructions to uniqueness, commutativity, and associativity of each ${ \cal R } / x _ { n }$ become trivial when we map to $R / X$ .

# 4. The specialization to $M U$ -modules and algebras

It was observed in [48] that $M U$ can be constructed as an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum, and we apply $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to convert it to a commutative $S \mathrm { . }$ -algebra. Of course, its homotopy groups are concentrated in even degrees, and every non-zero element is a non zero divisor. Thus Proposition 2.3, Theorem 2.6, and Theorem 3.2 combine to give the following result.

THEOREM 4.1. Let $X$ be a regular sequence in $M U _ { * }$ , let $\boldsymbol { { I } }$ be the ideal generated by $X$ , and let $Y$ be any sequence in $M U _ { * }$ . Then there is an $M U$ -ring spectrum $( M U / X ) [ Y ^ { - 1 } ]$ and a natural map of $M U$ -ring spectra (the unit map)

$$
\eta : M U \longrightarrow ( M U / X ) [ Y ^ { - 1 } ]
$$

such that

$$
\eta _ { * } : M U _ { * } \longrightarrow \pi _ { * } ( ( M U / X ) [ Y ^ { - 1 } ] )
$$

realizes the natural homomorphism of $M U _ { * }$ -algebras

$$
M U _ { * } \longrightarrow ( M U _ { * } / I ) [ Y ^ { - 1 } ] .
$$

If $M U _ { * } / I$ is concentrated in degrees congruent to zero mod 4, then there is a unique canonical product on $( M U / X ) [ Y ^ { - 1 } ]$ , and this product is commutative and associative.

In comparison with constructions of this sort based on the Baas-Sullivan theory of manifolds with singularities or on Landweber's exact functor theorem (where it applies), we have obtained a simpler proof of a substantially stronger result. We emphasize that an $M U .$ -ring spectrum is a much richer structure than just a ring spectrum and that commutativity and associativity in the $M U$ -ring spectrum sense are much more stringent conditions than mere commutativity and associativity of the underlying ring spectrum. Observe that the assumption that $X$ is regular is used only to obtain the calculational description of $\eta _ { * }$ .

We illustrate by explaining how $B P$ appears in this context. Fix a prime $p$ and write $( - ) _ { p }$ for localization at $p$ Let $B P$ be the Brown-Peterson spectrum at $p$ We are thinking of Quillen's idempotent construction, and we have the splitting maps $i : B P \longrightarrow M U _ { p }$ and $e : M U _ { p } \longrightarrow B P$ These are maps of commutative and associative ring spectra such that $e \circ i = \mathrm { i d }$ Let $I$ be the kernel of the composite

$$
M U _ { * } \longrightarrow M U _ { p * } \longrightarrow B P _ { * } .
$$

Then $I$ is generated by a regular sequence $X$ , and our $M U / X$ is a canonical integral version of $B P$ For the moment, let $B P ^ { \prime } = ( M U / X ) _ { p }$ . Let $\xi : B P \longrightarrow$ $B P ^ { \prime }$ be the composite

$$
B P { \stackrel { i } { \longrightarrow } } M U _ { p } { \stackrel { \zeta _ { p } } { \longrightarrow } } B P ^ { \prime } .
$$

It is immediate that $\xi$ is an equivalence. In effect, since we have arranged that $\zeta _ { p }$ has the same effect on homotopy groups as e, $\xi$ induces the identity map of $( M U _ { * } / I ) _ { p }$ on homotopy groups. By the splitting of $M U _ { p }$ and the fact that self-maps of $M U _ { p }$ are determined by their effect on homotopy groups [2, II.9.3], maps $M U _ { p } \longrightarrow B P$ are determined by their effect on homotopy groups. This implies that $\begin{array} { r } { \xi \circ e = \zeta _ { p } : M U _ { p } \longrightarrow B P ^ { \prime } } \end{array}$ The product on $B P$ is the composite

$$
B P \land B P \xrightarrow { i \land i } \ b { M U _ { p } \land M U _ { p } } \ b { \xrightarrow { \phi } } \ b { M U _ { p } \Vdash } \ b { \xrightarrow { e } } \ b { B P } .
$$

Since $\zeta _ { p }$ is a map of $M U .$ -ring spectra and thus of ring spectra, a trivial diagram chase now shows that the equivalence $\xi : B P \longrightarrow B P ^ { \prime }$ is a map of ring spectra.

We conclude that our $B P ^ { \prime }$ is a model for $B P$ that is an $M U$ -ring spectrum, commutative and associative if $p > 2$ .The situation for $p = 2$ is interesting. We conclude from the equivalence that $B P ^ { \prime }$ is commutative and associative as a ring spectrum, although we do not know that it is commutative or associative as an $M U$ algebra.

Recall that $\pi _ { * } ( B P ) = \mathbb { Z } _ { ( p ) } [ v _ { i } | d e g ( v _ { i } ) = 2 ( p ^ { i } - 1 ) ]$ , where the generators $v _ { i }$ come from $\pi _ { * } ( M U )$ (provided that we use the Hazewinkel generators). We list a few of the spectra derived from $B P$ , with their coefficient rings. Let $\mathbb { F } _ { p }$ denote the field with $p$ elements.

$$
\begin{array} { r l r l } { B P ( n ) } & { { } \mathbb { Z } _ { ( p ) } [ v _ { 1 } , \ldots , v _ { n } ] } & { } & { { } E ( n ) } & { { } \mathbb { Z } _ { ( p ) } [ v _ { 1 } , \ldots , v _ { n } , v _ { n } ^ { - 1 } ] } \\ { P ( n ) } & { { } \mathbb { F } _ { p } [ v _ { n } , v _ { n + 1 } , \ldots ] } & { } & { { } B ( n ) } & { { } \mathbb { F } _ { p } [ v _ { n } ^ { - 1 } , v _ { n } , v _ { n + 1 } , \ldots ] } \\ { k ( n ) } & { { } \mathbb { F } _ { p } [ v _ { n } ] } & { } & { { } K ( n ) } & { \mathbb { F } _ { p } [ v _ { n } , v _ { n } ^ { - 1 } ] } \end{array}
$$

By the method just illustrated, we can construct canonical integral versions of the $B P \langle n \rangle$ and $E ( n )$ . All of these spectra fit into the context of Theorem 4.1. If $p > 2$ , they all have unique canonical commutative and associative $M U .$ -ring spectra structures. Further study is needed when $p = 2$ , but we leave that to the interested reader. In any case, our theory makes it unnecessary to appeal to Baas-Sullivan theory or to Landweber's exact functor theorem for the construction and analysis of spectra such as these.

We have started with $M U$ because it appears in nature with a canonical structure as a commutative $S .$ -algebra. However, it is also possible to start with $B P$ , using the second author's result that $B P$ admits a commutative $S .$ algebra structure; in fact, it admits uncountably many different ones [34].

# CHAPTER VI

# Algebraic $\kappa { \mathrm { . } }$ theory of $S$ algebras

In this chapter we apply the basic constructions of algebraic $K .$ theory to the new categories of modules over $S .$ -algebras. We show how to construct a $K .$ theory spectrum $\kappa R$ for each $S .$ algebra $R$ in such a way that $K$ becomes a functor from the category of $S .$ -algebras to the stable category. When $\scriptstyle { R }$ is a connective commutative $S .$ -algebra, so is $K R$ We prove that weakly equivalent $S .$ -algebras have equivalent $K$ -theories, and we prove a Morita invariance result. When $\scriptstyle { R }$ is connective we are able to give an alternate description of this $K .$ theory in terms of Quillen's plus construction, a "plus equals $S _ { \bullet } { } ^ { \mathfrak { n } }$ theorem. When $R = H k$ is an Eilenberg-Mac Lane $S$ algebra, this $K .$ theory is essentially Quillen's algebraic $K .$ theory of the ring $k$ When $R = \Sigma ^ { \infty } | G S X | _ { + }$ is the suspension spectrum of the space obtained by adjoining a disjoint basepoint to the geometric realization of the loop group of the singular complex of a topological space $X$ , this $\kappa .$ theory is Waldhausen's algebraic $K .$ -theory of the space $X$ .

# 1. Waldhausen categories and algebraic $\kappa { \mathrm { . } }$ theory

We first review the basic definitions of Waldhausen [71] that we shall use.

DEFINItIoN 1.1. A category with w-cofibrations $\mathscr { C }$ is a (small) category with preferred zero object $" \ast > "$ , together with a chosen subcategory $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ that satisfies the following three axioms:

(i) Any isomorphism in $\mathcal { C }$ is a morphism in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ ; in particular, $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ contains all the objects of $\mathscr { C }$ .   
(ii) For every object $A$ in $\mathcal { C }$ , the unique map $* \longrightarrow A$ is in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ .   
iii) If $A \longrightarrow B$ is a map in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ , and $A \longrightarrow C$ is a map in $\mathscr { C }$ , then the pushout $B \amalg _ { A } C$ exists in $\mathscr { C }$ and the canonical map $C \longrightarrow B \amalg _ { A } C$ is in $\mathsf { c o } ( \mathcal { C } )$ ; in particular, $\mathscr { C }$ has finite coproducts.

We call the morphisms in $\mathsf { c o } ( \mathcal { C } )$ w-cofibrations, and often use the feathered arrow $\stackrel { 6 6 } { \longrightarrow } \stackrel { 9 9 }$ to denote them in diagrams. Although Waldhausen called these arrows "cofibrations" we will consistently use the term "w-cofibration" so that there will be no confusion with our standard use of the word "cofibration" to mean those maps that satisfy the homotopy extension property (HEP).

DEFINITIoN 1.2. A Waldhausen category (in [71], "a category with cofibrations and weak equivalences") is a category with w-cofibrations $\mathscr { C }$ and a chosen subcategory $\mathbf { w } ( { \pmb { \mathscr { C } } } )$ of $\mathscr { C }$ that satisfies the following axioms:

(i) Any isomorphism in $\mathscr { C }$ is a morphism in $\mathbf { w } ( { \pmb { \mathscr { C } } } )$ ; in particular, $\mathbf { w } ( { \pmb { \mathscr { C } } } )$ contains all the objects of $\mathscr { C }$ .   
(ii) Given any commutative diagram in $\mathscr { C }$

$$
\int \limits _ { C ^ { \prime }  } ^ { C  } \frac { A ^ { > } } { \int \limits _ { A ^ { \prime } > \infty } ^ { A } \int \limits _ { C ^ { \prime } } ^ { B } d x }
$$

in which the vertical maps are in $\mathbf { w } ( { \pmb { \mathscr { C } } } )$ and the feathered arrows are in $\mathsf { c o } ( \mathcal { C } )$ , the induced map $B \amalg _ { A } C \longrightarrow B ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A ^ { \prime } } C ^ { \prime }$ is in $\mathbf { w } ( { \pmb { \mathscr { C } } } )$ .

We call the morphisms in $\mathbf { w } ( \pmb { \mathcal { C } } )$ weak equivalences, and often use the arrows $^ { 6 6 } \longrightarrow ^ { 9 9 }$ to denote them. We say that the weak equivalences are saturated or that $\mathscr { C }$ is a saturated Waldhausen category if whenever $f$ and $g$ are composable arrows in $\mathscr { C }$ and any two of $f , g ,$ and $g f$ are weak equivalences then so is the third.

DeFINITIoN 1.3. A functor between Waldhausen categories is exact if it preserves all of the above structure; i.e. it must send w-cofibrations to w-cofibrations, weak equivalences to weak equivalences, the preferred zero object to the preferred zero object, and it must preserve the pushouts along a w-cofibration.

We now have all the necessary ingredients to describe Waldhausen's $S _ { \bullet }$ construction [71]. Let $\mathscr { C }$ be a Waldhausen category. For each $n \geq 0$ , define a category $S _ { n } \mathcal { C }$ as follows. An object of $S _ { n } \mathcal { C }$ consists of $_ n$ composible arrows in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ starting from the preferred zero object $^ *$ ,

$$
* = A _ { 0 } { \xrightarrow { \infty } } A _ { 1 } { \xrightarrow { \infty } } \cdots { \xrightarrow { \alpha _ { n - 1 } } } A _ { n } ,
$$

together with objects $A _ { i , j }$ for $0 \leq i \leq j \leq n$ and maps $a _ { i , j } \colon A _ { j } \longrightarrow A _ { i , j }$ such that $A _ { i , i } = *$ , $A _ { j } = A _ { 0 , j }$ with $a _ { 0 , j }$ the identity map, and the diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \underset { i > } { A _ { i } > } \xrightarrow { \alpha _ { j - 1 } > \dots > \alpha _ { i } } > A _ { j } } \\ & { \underset { * > } { \downarrow } } \end{array} \downarrow _ { a _ { i , j } }
$$

are pushouts for $0 \leq i < j \leq n$ A morphism of $S _ { n } \mathcal { C }$ from $\{ A _ { j } , A _ { i , j } , \alpha _ { j } , a _ { i , j } \}$ to $\{ A _ { j } ^ { \prime } , A _ { i , j } ^ { \prime } , \alpha _ { j } ^ { \prime } , a _ { i , j } ^ { \prime } \}$ is a sequence of maps $f _ { j } \colon A _ { j } \longrightarrow A _ { j } ^ { \prime }$ such that the diagram

$$
\begin{array}{c} \begin{array} { l } { { A _ { 0 } { > } \xrightarrow { \alpha _ { 0 } } \nonumber } } \\ { { f _ { 0 } \Biggl \downarrow \qquad \Biggl \downarrow _ { f _ { 1 } } } } \\ { { A _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } { > } \xrightarrow [ \alpha _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ] { } \nonumber } } \end{array}  \cdot \cdot \cdot { > } \xrightarrow [ \alpha _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } ] { \alpha _ { n } { > } } A _ { n } ^ { \prime }  \\ { { \nonumber } } \end{array}
$$

commutes. Observe that by the universal property of pushouts, we have induced maps $A _ { i , j } \ \longrightarrow \ A _ { i , j } ^ { \prime }$ making all the appropriate diagrams commute. We give $S _ { n } \mathcal { C }$ the structure of a Waldhausen category by defining a map $\{ f _ { 0 } , \ldots , f _ { n } \}$ to be a w-cofibration (resp. weak equivalence) if each $f _ { j }$ is a w-cofibration (resp. weak equivalence) of $\mathcal { C }$ Observe that when $\{ f _ { 0 } , \ldots , f _ { n } \}$ is a w-cofibration (resp. weak equivalence) all the induced maps $A _ { i , j } \longrightarrow A _ { i , j } ^ { \prime }$ are w-cofibrations (resp. weak equivalences). Notice that $S _ { 0 } \mathcal { C }$ is the trivial category and that $S _ { 1 } \mathcal { C }$ is isomorphic to $\mathscr { C }$ .

For $0 \leq i \leq n$ , define $d _ { k } \colon S _ { n } \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow S _ { n - 1 } \mathcal { C }$ to be the functor that drops the $k$ -th row and $k \mathrm { . }$ -th column from the matrix $\{ A _ { i , j } \}$ . More precisely, $d _ { 0 }$ sends the object $\{ A _ { j } , A _ { i , j } , \alpha _ { j } , a _ { i , j } \}$ of $S _ { n } \mathcal { C }$ to the object $\{ B _ { j } , B _ { i , j } , \beta _ { j } , b _ { i , j } \}$ of $S _ { n - 1 } \mathcal { C }$ where $B _ { j } = A _ { 1 , j + 1 }$ , $B _ { i , j } = A _ { i + 1 , j + 1 }$ , and the maps $\beta _ { j }$ and $b _ { i , j }$ are the maps induced from $\alpha _ { j + 1 }$ and $a _ { i + 1 , j + 1 }$ by the universal property of the pushout. For $k > 0$ . the functor $d _ { k }$ is defined similarly. For $0 \leq k \leq n$ , define $s _ { k } \colon S _ { n } \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow S _ { n + 1 } \mathcal { C }$ to be the functor that repeats the $k \mathrm { . }$ th row and $k$ -th column in the matrix $\left\{ A _ { i , j } \right\}$ . More precisely, $\scriptstyle { \pmb { s } } _ { k }$ sends the object $\{ A _ { j } , A _ { i , j } , \alpha _ { j } , a _ { i , j } \}$ of $S _ { n } \mathcal { C }$ to the object $\{ B _ { j } , B _ { i , j } , \beta _ { j } , b _ { i , j } \}$ of $S _ { n + 1 } { \mathcal { C } }$ where

$$
\begin{array} { l l l l } { B _ { j } } & { = } & { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { A _ { j } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j \leq k } \\ { A _ { j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j > k } \end{array} \right. } \\ { B _ { i , j } } & { = } & { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { A _ { i , j } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j \leq k } \\ { A _ { i , j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j > k \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } i \leq k } \end{array} \right. } \\ { A _ { i - 1 , j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j > k \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } i \leq k } \\ { \beta _ { j } } & { = } & { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { \alpha _ { j } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j < k } \\ { \mathrm { i d } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j = k } \end{array} \right. } \\ { \alpha _ { j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j > k } \\ { b _ { i , j } } & { = } & { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { a _ { i , j } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j \leq k } \\ { a _ { i , j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j > k } \end{array} \right. } \\ { a _ { i - 1 , j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } j > k \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } i \leq k } \\ { a _ { i - 1 , j - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } i > k . } \end{array}
$$

Observe that the functors $d _ { k }$ and $\scriptstyle { \pmb { s } } _ { k }$ satisfy the simplicial identities and the collection $\{ S _ { n } \mathcal { C } \}$ assembles into a simplicial category, which we denote $S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C }$ . Furthermore, the functors $d _ { k }$ and $\pmb { s _ { k } }$ are exact and $S _ { \bullet }$ has the structure of a simplicial Waldhausen category. In particular, we can iterate this construction to form the bisimplicial Waldhausen category $S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( 2 ) } \mathcal { C } = S _ { \bullet } S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C }$ and the polysimplicial Waldhausen categories $S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } = \bar { S _ { \bullet } } \cdot \cdot \cdot S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C }$ We abbreviate the notation for the category of weak equivalences in $S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C }$ to ${ w S } _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) }$ We are interested in the classifying spaces of these categories, the spaces $| w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } |$ Since $S _ { 0 } \mathcal { C }$ is the trivial category, we see that $| w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } |$ is connected, and it is not much harder to see that in general $| w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } |$ is $( n - 1 )$ -connected. Observe that the identifications of $\mathscr { C }$ with $S _ { 1 } \mathcal { C }$ and more generally $S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C }$ with $S _ { 1 } S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C }$ induce maps $\Sigma | w \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } |$ and $\Sigma | w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow | w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n + 1 ) } \mathcal { C } |$ which are inclusions of subcomplexes. It is a fundamental observation of [71, $\ S 1 . 3$ , 1.5.3] that in the sequence

$$
| w \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow \Omega | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow \Omega ^ { 2 } | w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( 2 ) } \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow \cdots .
$$

all maps beyond the first are homotopy equivalences. This motivates the following definition.

DEFINITION 1.4. The algebraic $K \cdot$ -theory of the Waldhausen category $\mathscr { C }$ is the spectrification of the $\Sigma$ -cofibrant prespectrum $\{ | w \mathcal { C } | , | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } | , | w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( 2 ) } \mathcal { C } | , \dots \}$ We denote this spectrum by the symbol $K \mathcal { C }$ The algebraic $K .$ groups of $\mathscr { C }$ are the homotopy groups of this spectrum, $K _ { n } { \mathcal { C } } = \pi _ { n } K { \mathcal { C } } = \pi _ { n + 1 } | w S _ { \bullet } { \mathcal { C } } |$ In particular, $K _ { n } { \pmb { \mathcal { C } } } = 0$ for $n < 0$ .

Waldhausen observes that in the special case when $\mathscr { C }$ is an exact category (where the w-cofibrations are the admissible monos, and the weak equivalences are the isomorphisms) the algebraic $\kappa$ -groups defined above agree with those defined by Quillen [58]. In fact, the basic properties of the $Q$ -construction are all easily provable in terms of the $S _ { \bullet }$ construction [67] (see also [53]).

Observe that an exact functor $\mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D }$ induces an exact functor $S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow$ $S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { D }$ and hence exact functors $S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { D }$ This induces a map spectra $\{ w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } \} \longrightarrow \{ w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { D } \}$ and hence a map of spectra $K \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow K \mathcal { D }$ If the map $| w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { D } |$ is a weak equivalence, then the maps $| w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { C } | \longrightarrow$ $| w S _ { \bullet } ^ { ( n ) } \mathcal { D } |$ are weak equivalences and therefore homotopy equivalences. Since these prespectra are $\Sigma$ -cofibrant, maps between them that are spacewise homotopy equivalences induce homotopy equivalences of their spectrifications. In other words, an exact functor that induces a weak equivalence on $| w S _ { \bullet } - |$ induces a homotopy equivalence of $K .$ -theory spectra. For this reason, although Waldhausen defines the algebraic $K .$ -theory of a Waldhausen category $\mathscr { C }$ to be the space $\Omega | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } |$ , all the results we use from [71] apply to the $K .$ theory spectra, even when they are stated only for the $\kappa .$ -theory spaces, and we will use them this way without further comment; moreover, whenever we assert a result about $K .$ -theory, we mean the result about the $K .$ -theory spectra unless otherwise noted.

# 2. Cylinders, homotopies, and approximation theorems

Let Mor $\mathscr { C }$ be the category whose objects are the morphisms of $\mathscr { C }$ and whose morphisms are the commutative diagrams. For $A , A ^ { \prime } , B ,$ $B ^ { \prime }$ objects of $\mathscr { C }$ and $a \colon A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ and $b \colon B \longrightarrow B ^ { \prime }$ maps of $\mathscr { C }$ $^ { a }$ and $b$ are objects of $\operatorname { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ If $f \colon A \longrightarrow B$ and $f ^ { \prime } \colon A ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow B ^ { \prime }$ are maps in $\mathscr { C }$ that make the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { l } { A \xrightarrow { f } B } \\ { a \Bigg \downarrow \Bigg \downarrow } \\ { A ^ { \prime } \xrightarrow { f ^ { \prime } } B ^ { \prime } } \end{array}
$$

commute, then $( f , f ^ { \prime } )$ is a map in $\operatorname { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ When $\mathscr { C }$ is a Waldhausen category, we can give $\operatorname { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ the structure of a Waldhausen category by saying that a map $( f , f ^ { \prime } )$ is a w-cofibration (resp. weak equivalence) of $\operatorname { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ if both $f$ and $f ^ { \prime }$ are w-cofibrations (resp weak equivalences) of $\mathscr { C }$ We shall also need to define a Waldhausen category $C \mathrm { M o R } { \mathcal { C } }$ , the underlying category of which is the full subcategory of MOR $\mathscr { C }$ of objects $a \colon A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ that are morphisms in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ . The weak equivalences in $C \mathbf { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ are the weak equivalences of Mor $\mathscr { C }$ that lie in

$C \mathbf { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ A map $( f , f ^ { \prime } )$ from $a \colon A \mapsto A ^ { \prime }$ to $b \colon B \mapsto B ^ { \prime }$ is a w-cofibration of $C \mathrm { M o R } { \mathcal { C } }$ if $f$ is a w-cofibration of $\mathscr { C }$ and the induced map $A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } B \longrightarrow B ^ { \prime }$ is a w-cofibration in $\mathscr { C }$ Verification that $C \operatorname { M o n } \mathcal { C }$ is a Waldhausen category can be found in [71, 1.1.1].

DEfINITion 2.1. (cf. [71, 1.6]) Let $\mathscr { C }$ be a Waldhausen category. A cylinder functor is a functor $T$ . $\mathbf { : } \ \mathbf { M o R } \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C }$ together with natural transformations $i _ { 1 }$ , $i _ { 2 }$ , and $p$ that make the following diagram commute for a morphism $f \colon A \longrightarrow B$ in $\mathscr { C }$ :

$$
A \underset { f \searrow \sqrt { 3 } } { \overset { i _ { 1 } } { \longleftrightarrow } } T f < \frac { i _ { 2 } } { \sum \limits _ { j } \cdots } B
$$

and that satisfies the following properties:

(i) $i _ { 1 } \amalg i _ { 2 } { : } A$ Ⅱ $B \mapsto T f$ is in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ .   
ii) The functor $( A \longrightarrow B ) \mapsto ( A \amalg B ^ { i _ { 1 } \amalg i _ { 2 } } T f )$ is an exact functor MoR $C \operatorname { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ .   
(iii) $T ( * \longrightarrow B ) = B$ , with $p$ and $i _ { 2 }$ the identity map.

We say that the cylinder functor satisfies the cylinder axiom if in addition $p \colon T f \longrightarrow B$ is in $\mathbf { w } ( { \pmb { \mathscr { C } } } )$ for all morphisms $f$ We will often refer to $i _ { 1 }$ and $i _ { 2 }$ as face maps and to $p$ as the collapse map.

The importance of cylinder functors is shown by Waldhausen's approximation theorem [71, 1.6.7].

THEOREM 2.2 (APPROXIMATION THEOREM). Let $\mathcal { A }$ and $\mathcal { B }$ be saturated Waldhausen categories, where $\pmb { \mathcal { A } }$ has a cylinder functor that satisfies the cylinder axiom. Let $F \colon \mathcal { A } \longrightarrow \mathcal { B }$ be an exact functor such that

i) If $f$ is a morphism in $\pmb { \mathcal { A } }$ such that $F ( f )$ is in $\mathbf { w } ( { \mathcal { B } } )$ , then $f$ is in $\mathbf { w } ( \pmb { \mathcal { A } } )$ .   
(ii) For any object $A \in { \mathcal { A } }$ and any map $f \colon F A \longrightarrow B$ , there exists a map $g \colon A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { A } }$ and a weak equivalence $h \colon F A ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow B$ in $\mathbf { w } ( { \mathcal { B } } )$ such that $f = h \circ F ( g )$ :

$$
\begin{array}{c} \begin{array} { c } { { F ( A ) \xrightarrow { f } B . } } \\ { { F ( g ) \Biggl \downarrow \qquad \begin{array} { l } { { } } \\ { { h } } \end{array} } } \\ { { F ( A ^ { \prime } ) } } \end{array}  \ : B .   \end{array}
$$

Then $F$ induces a homotopy equivalence $K { \mathcal { A } } \longrightarrow K { \mathcal { B } }$ .

REMARK 2.3. In [71] it is required that $g$ be a w-cofibration, but [68] points out that this requirement is unnecesary since we can use cylinders to replace an arbitrary map with a w-cofibration.

Often, we have the situation where it is easy to make the diagram in 2.2(ii) commute up to some kind of homotopy. By integrating the idea of homotopy into Waldhausen's language of $K \cdot$ -theory, we can prove two easy but extremely useful corollaries of Waldhausen's approximation theorem. To this end, we offer the following definitions.

DEfinitionS 2.4. Let $\mathscr { C }$ be a Waldhausen category with cylinder functor $_ T$ . Observe that $_ T$ gives an exact functor $I \colon { \mathcal { C } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { C } }$ by restriction along the exact functor $1 \colon \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathrm { M o R } \mathcal { C }$ that sends an object to its identity morphism. We call $( W , j _ { 1 } , j _ { 2 } , q )$ a cylinder object of the object $X$ if $W = I X$ , $q = p$ (the collapse map) and either $j _ { 1 } = i _ { 1 }$ and $j _ { 2 } = i _ { 2 }$ , or $\scriptstyle { j _ { 1 } = i _ { 2 } }$ and $j _ { 2 } = i _ { 1 }$ .We say that $( W , j _ { 1 } , j _ { 2 } , q )$ is a generalized cylinder object of the object $X$ , if $W$ is the pushout over alternate face maps of a sequence of cylinder objects, $j _ { 1 } , j _ { 2 } \colon X \mapsto W$ are the two unused face maps, and $q \colon W \longrightarrow X$ is the gluing of the collapse maps; in particular observe that $q \circ j _ { i } = 1 _ { X }$ for $i = 1 , 2$ .We call two maps $f _ { 1 } , f _ { 2 } \colon X \longrightarrow$ $Y$ homotopic if there exists a generalized cylinder object $W$ of $X$ and a map $\phi \colon W \longrightarrow Y$ such that $\phi \circ j _ { i } = f _ { i }$ for $i = 1 , 2$ . It is easy to see that this specifies an equivalence relation.

Let us say that an exact functor $F \colon \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D }$ between Waldhausen categories with cylinder functors preserves cylinder objects if there is a natural isomorphism $\alpha \colon F I _ { \mathcal { C } } \cong I _ { \mathcal { D } } F$ such that $\alpha \circ F ( i _ { k } ) = i _ { k }$ and $p \circ \alpha = F ( p )$ :

![](images/5c18483208ea85c14f285aa51374fce8c4b2c55f8c94a54b42ece8799c9df54d.jpg)

Observe that a functor that preserves cylinder objects also preserves generalized cylinder objects, in the sense that $\alpha$ gives an isomorphism of $F W$ to a generalized cylinder object $W ^ { \prime }$ with $\alpha \circ F ( j _ { k } ) = j _ { k } ^ { \prime }$ and $q ^ { \prime } \circ \alpha = F ( q )$ . It is easy to see that when $F$ preserves cylinder objects, $F$ also preserves the relation of homotopy of morphisms.

THeOreM 2.5 (HOMOTOPY APPROxIMaTION THEOrEM). Let $\mathcal { A }$ and $\mathcal { B }$ be small saturated Waldhausen categories with cylinder functors satisfying the cylinder axiom. Let $F \colon \mathcal { A } \longrightarrow \mathcal { B }$ be an exact functor that preserves cylinder objects and such that

i) If $f$ is a morphism in $\mathcal { A }$ such that $F ( f )$ is in $\mathbf { w } ( { \mathcal { B } } )$ , then $f$ is in $\mathbf { w } ( \mathcal { A } )$ .   
(ii) For any object $A \in { \mathcal { A } }$ and any map $f \colon F A \longrightarrow B$ , there exists a map $a \colon A \longrightarrow X$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { A } }$ and a weak equivalence $e \colon F X \longrightarrow B$ in $\mathbf { w } ( { \mathcal { B } } )$ such that $f$ is homotopic to $e \circ F ( a )$ .

Then $F$ induces a homotopy equivalence $K { \mathcal { A } } \longrightarrow K { \mathcal { B } }$ .

PRooF. We produce an object $A ^ { \prime }$ and maps $g , h$ that satisfy condition (ii) of Waldhausen's approximation theorem.

We have assumed that $f$ is homotopic to $e \circ F ( a )$ , so there exists a generalized cylinder object $( W ^ { \prime } , j _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , j _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } , q ^ { \prime } )$ of $F A$ and a map $\psi \colon W ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow B$ with $\psi \circ j _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } = f$ and $\psi \circ j _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } = e \circ F a$ . We can construct in $\mathcal { A }$ the generalized cylinder object $W$ with the same gluings of faces; then we have $\alpha \colon F W \cong W ^ { \prime }$ with $\alpha \circ F ( j _ { i } ) = j _ { i } ^ { \prime }$ , since $F$ preserves cylinder objects.

Let $A ^ { \prime } \ = \ W \ \amalg _ { a } \ X$ , and let $g$ be the evident map $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ induced by $j _ { 1 } \colon A \longrightarrow W$ .Then $\alpha$ induces an isomorphism $F A ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow W ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { F ( a ) } F X$ , which we will denote by $\tilde { \alpha }$ .Consider the map $\tilde { h } = \psi \amalg _ { F ( a ) } e \colon W ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { F ( a ) } F X \longrightarrow B$ .The inclusion $F X \longrightarrow W ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { F ( a ) } F X$ is a weak equivalence by property 1.2.(ii), since it is the pushout of the following weak equivalence of diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { F X \xleftarrow { F ( a ) } } \\ & { \sim | \operatorname* { i d } \qquad \sim | \operatorname* { i d } \qquad \sim | \begin{array} { l } { F A } \\ { \operatorname { i d } } \end{array}   } \\ & { F X \xleftarrow { F ( a ) } \xleftarrow { F A \gg }  W ^ { \prime } . } \end{array}
$$

The composite of this inclusion with $\tilde { h }$ is the weak equivalence $e$ , so we conclude that $\tilde { h }$ is a weak equivalence since $\mathcal { B }$ is saturated by assumption. Choosing $h$ to be the weak equivalence $\tilde { h } \circ \tilde { \alpha }$ makes diagram 2.2(ii) commute.

The next corollary of Waldhausen's approximation theorem requires some preliminary definitions.

DEFINITIoNs 2.6. We say that a map $f \colon X \longrightarrow Y$ is a homotopy equivalence if there exists a morphism $g \colon Y \longrightarrow X$ so that $f \circ g$ and $g \circ f$ are homotopic to the respective identity morphisms. In this case, we call $g$ a homotopy inverse to $f$ We say that $\mathscr { C }$ is a category with w-cofibrations and homotopy equivalences or a Waldhausen homotopy category (WH category for short) if $\mathscr { C }$ is a saturated Waldhausen category with cylinder functor satisfying the cylinder axiom such that the weak equivalences are the homotopy equivalences.

We need to make an observation about the "derived" category of a WH category, the category formed by inverting the homotopy equivalences. First note that if $f$ is a homotopy equivalence and $g$ a homotopy inverse to $f$ , then $g \circ f$ and $f \circ g$ are both identity morphisms in the derived category. From this, it is easy to see that every map in the derived category of $\mathscr { C }$ is represented by a map in $\mathscr { C }$ .

One can ask when two maps give the same map in the derived category. First observe that for homotopic maps $f _ { 1 } , f _ { 2 }$ and homotopic maps $g _ { 1 } , g _ { 2 }$ , the compositions $f _ { 1 } \circ g _ { 1 }$ and $f _ { 2 } \circ g _ { 2 }$ are homotopic; so we can form the homotopy category, $h \mathcal { C }$ , whose objects are the objects of $\mathscr { C }$ and whose maps are homotopy classes of maps. It is straightforward to verify that homotopic maps represent the same map in the derived category, and that if two maps represent the same map in the derived category then they are homotopic. We conclude that the natural map from $\mathscr { C }$ to its derived category factors through $h \mathcal { C }$ , and that the map from $h \mathcal { C }$ to the derived category is actually an isomorphism (not merely equivalence) of categories.

The next result is now an immediate corollary to Theorem 2.5.

COROLLARY 2.7. Let $\mathcal { A }$ and $\mathcal { B }$ be WH categories. Suppose $F \colon \mathcal { A } \longrightarrow \mathcal { B }$ is an exact functor that preserves cylinder objects and that passes to an equivalence on the derived categories. Then $\boldsymbol { \mathsf { \Pi } } ^ { F }$ induces a homotopy equivalence $K { \mathcal { A } } \longrightarrow K { \mathcal { B } }$ .

PRooF. We reduce to Theorem 2.5: Condition (i) is clear. For condition (ii), choose $X \in \mathcal A$ so that $F X$ is isomorphic to $B$ in the derived category of $\mathcal { B }$ . Since every map in the derived category of $\mathcal { B }$ is represented by an actual map in $\mathcal { B }$ , we can choose $e \colon F X \longrightarrow B$ that represents this isomorphism. Then $e$ is a homotopy equivalence; let $e ^ { \prime }$ be a homotopy inverse. Now there exists a map $a \colon A \longrightarrow X$ so that $F a \colon F A \longrightarrow F X$ represents the same map as $e ^ { \prime } \circ f$ in the derived category of $\mathcal { B }$ We conclude that $f$ is homotopic to $e \circ F a$ .

# 3. Application to categories of $R$ -modules

For an $S$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ , let $\mathcal { C } _ { R }$ be the full subcategory $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ consisting of the cell $R$ -modules, and ${ \mathcal { C W } } _ { R }$ the category of CW $R$ -modules and cellular maps. We denote by $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ the full subcategory of $\mathcal { C } _ { R }$ of finite cell $R$ -modules and $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ the full subcategory of ${ \mathcal { C W } } _ { R }$ of finite CW $R .$ -modules; more precisely, we must choose small full subcategories containing at least one object of each isomorphism class, but the fact that the category of spectra has canonical colimits allows a strict interpretation of the definition of cell and CW $R .$ -modules under which the categories $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ and $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ are already small. When $\mathscr { C }$ is one of the categories $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ or $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ , we can give $\mathscr { C }$ the structure of a WH category as follows. We define the category of w-cofibrations, $\mathsf { c o } ( \mathcal { C } )$ , to consist of those maps which are isomorphic (in MOR $\mathscr { C }$ ) to the inclusion of a subcomplex, and the category of weak equivalences $\mathbf { w } ( \pmb { \mathcal { C } } )$ to consist of those maps in $\mathscr { C }$ which are homotopy equivalences. We take as our cylinder functor the ordinary mapping cylinder.

ProPosITioN 3.1. These definitions specify structures of WH-categories on $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ and $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ and the inclusion $f \mathcal { C } \mathcal { W } _ { R } \longrightarrow f \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ is an exact functor which preserves cylinder objects. Furthermore, when $R$ is connective, this inclusion induces a homotopy equivalence of $K .$ -theory spectra.

ProoF. We check the definitions directly. Let $\mathcal { C }$ be either $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ or $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ with $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ and $\mathbf { w } ( { \mathcal { C } } )$ as above.

First we check that $\mathsf { c o } ( \mathcal { C } )$ is a category. Let $f \colon A \longrightarrow B$ and $g \colon B \longrightarrow C$ be arrows in $\mathscr { C }$ These are isomorphic to inclusions of subcomplexes, and without loss of generality we can assume that $f$ is the inclusion of a subcomplex and that $g$ is isomorphic to an inclusion $g ^ { \prime } \colon B ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow C$ with the isomorphism on the codomain $C$ the identity:

$$
\begin{array} { l c r } { { B \longrightarrow B ^ { \prime } } } \\ { { g \Bigg \downarrow } } \\ { { { \cal C } = { \cal C } . } } \end{array} \Bigg \downarrow _ { g ^ { \prime } } ^ { g ^ { \prime } }
$$

By choosing different sequential filtrations if necessary we can assume that the map $B \longrightarrow C$ is sequentially cellular and therefore so is the map $B \longrightarrow B ^ { \prime }$ (we adjust the sequential filtration on $A$ as well if necessary so that it remains a subcomplex of $B$ . Since $C$ is built from $B ^ { \prime }$ by attaching cells, we can form an isomorphic complex $D$ by attaching the same cells to $B$ via $b ^ { - 1 }$ In the CW case, $D$ is CW and the isomorphisms to and from $C$ are cellular because we have assumed that the isomorphisms $b$ and $b ^ { - 1 }$ are cellular. Now $A$ is a subcomplex of $B$ , which is a subcomplex of $D$ and the map $A \longrightarrow D$ is isomorphic to the map $A \longrightarrow C$ .

Properties 1.1(i) and (ii) and 1.2(i) are clear as will be 1.2(ii) once we show 1.1(iii). Given a diagram in $\mathcal { C }$

$$
\sum _ { \gamma } ^ { f  B }
$$

with $f$ an arrow in $\cos ( \mathcal { C } )$ , we show that we can find a pushout in $\mathscr { C }$ so that the map from $C$ is the inclusion of a subcomplex. We can assume without loss of generality that $f$ is the inclusion of a subcomplex and that the map $g$ is sequentially cellular. Since $B$ is built from $A$ by attaching cells, we can form a cell complex $D$ by attaching these cells to $C$ via $g \colon A \longrightarrow C$ .In the CW case, $D$ will be a CW complex since we have assumed that $g$ is cellular. For categorical reasons, $D$ must be a pushout of the above diagram, and by construction the map from $C$ is the inclusion of a subcomplex.

If $\scriptstyle { R }$ is connective, we can approximate any finite cell $R$ -module by a finite CW $R$ -module, and the last statement follows by Theorem 2.5.

DEFInITIoN 3.2. We define the algebraic $K \cdot$ theory of the $S .$ algebra $R$ to be the spectrum $K f { \mathscr C } _ { R }$ , and we denote it by $K R$ We define the algebraic $K \cdot$ groups of $\scriptstyle { R }$ to be the homotopy groups of this spectrum, $K _ { n } R = \pi _ { n } K R = K _ { n } f \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ .

Although the categories $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ and $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ seem the most natural choices for $K .$ -theory, there are many other possibilities. Indeed, since pushouts along cofibrations in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ preserve weak equivalences, it is easy to see that any subcategory of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ that is a category with w-cofibrations such that all of the w-cofibrations are cofibrations becomes a Waldhausen category by taking the weak equivalences to be the ordinary weak equivalences. In particular, when $\mathcal { X }$ is a small full subcategory of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ that contains the trivial $R _ { \mathbf { \delta } }$ -module and is closed under pushouts along cofibrations, then $\mathcal { X }$ is a category with w-cofibrations the set of all cofibrations in $\mathcal { X }$ , and in this way $\mathcal { X }$ becomes a Waldhausen category. We shall call the resulting Waldhausen category structure on $\mathcal { X }$ the standard Waldhausen structure. If $\mathcal { C }$ is a full subcategory of $\mathcal { C } _ { R }$ that is small, contains the trivial $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module, and is closed under pushouts along maps isomorphic to inclusions of subcomplexes, then $\mathscr { C }$ is a category with w-cofibrations the set of maps in $\mathscr { C }$ isomorphic to the inclusion of a subcomplex and in this way becomes a Waldhausen category. We shall call the resulting Waldhausen category structure on $\mathscr { C }$ the standard cellular Waldhausen structure. If $\mathcal { X }$ or $\mathscr { C }$ is closed under smashing with $I _ { + }$ , then the mapping cylinder gives a cylinder functor satisfying the cylinder axiom, which we shall call the standard cylinder functor. Since a standard cellular Waldhausen category with the the standard cylinder functor is a WH category, we shall call such a category a standard WH category. It might at first appear that the standard Waldhausen structures are somewhat rare, but the following remark demonstrates that they are actually quite common.

REMARK 3.3 (SMALLEST STANDARD WALDHAUSEN CATEGORIES). If $\mathcal { A }$ is a set of objects of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ that is not necessarily closed under pushouts along cofibrations, we can form a small category $\mathcal { B }$ containing $\mathcal { A }$ that is. We let $\mathcal { B }$ be the union of an expanding sequence of small categories $\mathcal { A } _ { 0 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { A } _ { 1 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { A } _ { 2 } \longrightarrow \cdots ,$ where $\mathrm { O b } ( \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ) = \mathcal { A }$ and $\mathcal { A } _ { n + 1 }$ is the full subcategory of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ of objects that are pushouts of diagrams (with one leg a cofibration) in $\mathcal { A } _ { n }$ (one choice of object for each such diagram). Since the set of all maps in $\mathcal { A } _ { n }$ is small (by induction), $\mathcal { A } _ { n + 1 }$ is a small category. It is easy to see that $\mathcal { B }$ has a kind of universal property: whenever a standard Waldhausen category contains a full subcategory equivalent to $\mathcal { A }$ (regarded as a full subcategory of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ ), it must contain a full subcategory compatibly equivalent to $\mathcal { B }$ .For this reason, we will refer to $\mathcal { B }$ as the smallest standard Waldhausen category containing $\pmb { \mathcal { A } }$ Observe that when all the objects of $\pmb { \mathcal { A } }$ have the weak homotopy types of finite cell complexes then so do all the objects of $\mathcal { B }$ (by Corollary I.6.5).

Often we will want our standard Waldhausen categories to have the standard cylinder functor. In forming $\mathcal { A } _ { n + 1 }$ from ${ \mathcal { A } } _ { n }$ above we could also include $X \wedge I _ { + }$ for each $X \ \in \ \mathcal A _ { n }$ . In this case, $\mathcal { A } _ { n + 1 }$ will still be small, but now $\mathcal { B }$ will be closed under smashing with $I _ { + }$ , and hence have the standard cylinder functor. It is easy to see that $\mathcal { B }$ will now have a similar universal property with respect to standard Waldhausen categories with the standard cylinder functor. For this reason we will refer to the category constructed in this way as the smallest standard Waldhausen category with standard cylinder functor containing $\mathcal { A }$ . Again, when all the objects of $\mathcal { A }$ have the weak homotopy types of finite cell complexes then so do all the objects of $\mathcal { B }$ .

If $\mathcal { A } \subset \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ , we can do a similar construction but using maps isomorphic to inclusions of subcomplexes in place of cofibrations. Then the resulting category $\mathcal { B } \subset \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ is a standard cellular Waldhausen category (a WH category if we include smashes with $I _ { + }$ ) and has a similar universal property with respect to standard cellular Waldhausen categories. We shall not actually use this construction, but we could call $\mathcal { B }$ in this case the smallest standard cellular Waldhausen category containing $\mathcal { A }$ (or if we include smashes with $I _ { + }$ , smallest standard WH category containing $\mathcal { A }$ ). Furthermore, observe that if all the objects of $\mathcal { A }$ have the homotopy types of finite cell complexes, then so do all the objects of $\mathcal { B }$ .

One advantage of the standard Waldhausen structures is that inclusions of subcategories are exact functors.

PROposITION 3.4. Suppose $\mathcal { X }$ is a subcategory of $\mathcal { V }$ . If $\mathcal { X }$ and $\mathcal { V }$ are both standard Waldhausen categories or both standard cellular Waldhausen categories, then the inclusion $\mathcal { X } \longrightarrow \mathcal { Y }$ is an exact functor. If $\mathcal { X }$ is a standard cellular Waldhausen category and $\mathcal { V }$ is a standard Waldhausen category, then the inclusion $\mathcal { X } \longrightarrow \mathcal { Y }$ is an exact functor.

Many standard Waldhausen categories have $K .$ -theory equivalent to $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ .The following proposition follows directly from Theorem 2.5 (and the Whitehead

Theorem) and will often apply to the smallest standard categories constructed above.

PROPOSITION 3.5. Let $\mathcal { X }$ be a standard Waldhausen category with standard cylinder functor or a standard WH category. If $\mathcal { X }$ contains $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ , and if furthermore each object of $\mathcal { X }$ is weakly equivalent to a finite cell complex, then the induced map of $K .$ -theory spectra is a homotopy equivalence.

The $\kappa .$ theory we consider, the $K .$ theory of $f { \pmb { \mathcal { C } } } _ { R }$ , is best thought of as analogous to the $K .$ -theory of finitely generated free modules: indeed, since all objects are constructed from the sphere $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules by a finite number of extensions by spheres, it follows immediately that the obvious homomorphism $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } \pi _ { 0 } R \stackrel { \cdot } { \longrightarrow } \bar { K } _ { 0 } R$ (induced by $[ n ] \mapsto \vee _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } S _ { R } )$ is surjective, where $" K _ { 0 } ^ { \bar { f } } \pi _ { 0 } R "$ denotes $K _ { 0 }$ of the finitely generated free modules of the ring $\pi _ { 0 } R$ When $\scriptstyle { R }$ is connective this homomorphism is an isomorphism whose inverse is given by the Euler characteristic of a CW object $X$ , the alternating sum of the classes of $C _ { n } ( X )$ , where $C _ { * }$ is the chain functor of $\operatorname { I V } \ S 3$ For this reason, categories that could be reasonable alternatives to the categories $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ and $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ would be those small categories of semi-finite cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules that are standard WH categories. When such a category contains $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ , it follows from [68, 1.10.1], [71, 1.5.9] and the argument of $[ 2 4 , \ \ S 1 ]$ (as observed in [68, 1.10.2]) that the inclusion will induce an isomorphism of homotopy groups of $K .$ -theory spectra except in dimension zero. Intuitively, whereas the $K .$ theory of $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ or $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R }$ is like the $K .$ -theory of the finitely generated free modules, we might think of the $K .$ theory of semi-finite objects as analogous to the $K .$ theory of the finitely generated projective modules.

We conclude this section by remarking that when $R$ is an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum but not an $S .$ -algebra, we can make analogous observations about the $K .$ theory of categories of its modules. However the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ is an exact functor that converts such a category to the corresponding category of $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R$ -modules and induces a homotopy equivalence of $K .$ -theory spectra by Theorem 2.5. Thus, results about the $K .$ theory of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra follow from results about the $K .$ theory of $S$ algebras.

# 4. Homotopy invariance and Quillen's algebraic $\kappa .$ theory of rings

In this section we prove some properties of the $K .$ -theory of the category $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ and compare with the $K \cdot$ theory of (discrete) rings.

We observe that $K .$ theory as defined above gives a functor from the category of $S .$ -algebras to the stable category which has nice homotopical properties.

PROPosiTIoN 4.1. If $\phi \colon A \longrightarrow B$ is a map of $S$ -algebras, then the functor $B \land _ { A } ( - ) \colon f { \mathcal { C } } _ { A } \longrightarrow f { \mathcal { C } } _ { B }$ (or $f { \mathcal { C W } } _ { A } { \longrightarrow } f { \mathcal { C W } } _ { B } )$ is exact and preserves cylinder objects. This association makes $K$ into a functor from the category of $S$ -algebras to the stable category.

PrOOF. The first statement follows from III.4.1, the second from the isomorphisms $C \wedge _ { B } \left( B \wedge _ { A } ( - ) \right) \cong C \wedge _ { A } ( - )$ and $A \wedge _ { A } ( - ) \cong \operatorname { i d } .$

PROposiTION 4.2. If $\phi \colon A \longrightarrow B$ is a map of $S$ -algebras that is a weak equivalence, then $K \phi$ is a homotopy equivalence.

PROOF. From III.4.2, $B \land _ { A } ( - )$ induces an equivalence of derived categories $\mathcal { D } _ { A } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { B }$ , which restricts to an equivalence of the derived categories of finite cell complexes by an easy application of the Whitehead theorem. The result follows from Corollary 2.7.

We compare this $K .$ -theory with Quillen's algebraic $K .$ theory. Let $k$ be a ring, and let $H k$ denote the Eilenberg-Mac Lane $S _ { \ l }$ -algebra of $k$ . We shall use the symbol $K ^ { f } k$ for the algebraic $K .$ -theory of the finitely generated free modules of $k$ , a covering spectrum of $\boldsymbol { K } \boldsymbol { k }$ .

THEOREM 4.3. KHk is homotopy equivalent to $K ^ { f } k$ , naturally up to homotopy in $k$ .

PROOF. We can identify $K ^ { f } k$ with the $K .$ -theory of the WH category of finite free $k \mathrm { . }$ -chain complexes with w-cofibrations the split monics, weak equivalences the quasi-isomorphisms, and the cylinder functor given by the usual mapping cylinder. (see, for example, [68, 1.11.7].) We will denote this WH category as $f ^ { \mathcal { C } } \mathcal { W } _ { k }$ .

The functor $C _ { * } : f \mathcal { C W } _ { H k } \longrightarrow f \mathcal { C W } _ { k }$ of $\mathrm { { I V } \ S 2 }$ is exact and preserves cylinder objects. By the Hurewicz theorem IV.3.6, a map between finite CW modules is a weak equivalence if and only if its image under $C _ { * }$ is a quasi-isomorphism, hence the theorem will follow from Theorem 2.2 if we can show that condition (ii) holds.

Given a finite free chain complex $M _ { * }$ , we can actually construct a CW $H k$ . module $X$ whose cellular chain complex $C _ { * } ( X )$ is isomorphic to $M _ { * }$ . We proceed by induction. Since $M _ { * }$ is finite, $M _ { i }$ is zero below some $m$ , and we take the $_ { i \cdot }$ skeleton of $X$ , $X ^ { i }$ to be the trivial $H k$ -module for $\textit { i } < m$ . Now assume that we have constructed $X ^ { n }$ and an isomorphism ${ \cal C } _ { * } ( X ^ { n } ) \longrightarrow M _ { * } ^ { \leq n }$ , where $M _ { * } ^ { \leq n }$ denotes the brutal $\boldsymbol { n }$ -truncation of $M _ { * }$ ,i.e. $M _ { i } ^ { \leq n } = M _ { i }$ for $i \leq n$ and $M _ { i } ^ { \leq n } = 0$ for $i > n$ .By IV.2.3, $\pi _ { n } ( X ^ { n } ) \cong H _ { n } ( M _ { * } ^ { \leq n } )$ , which is the kernel of the differential $d _ { n - 1 }$ , i.e. the cycles of $M _ { n }$ . Via this isomorphism and a choice of basis for $M _ { n + 1 }$ , the differential $d _ { n }$ specifies a homotopy class of maps from a wedge of $S _ { H k } ^ { n }$ to $X ^ { n }$ $X ^ { n + 1 }$ be ethe CW complex formed by attaching $( n + 1 )$ -cells along this map. By construction $C _ { n + 1 } ( X ^ { n + 1 } ) \cong M _ { n + 1 }$ , compatibly with the differentials.

Given an $H k$ -module $A$ and a map $f \colon C _ { * } ( A ) \longrightarrow M _ { * }$ , we show that we can find a map $a \colon A \longrightarrow X$ such that $C _ { * } ( a )$ agrees with $f$ via the isomorphism contructed above. Assume we have constructed this map as far as the $_ n$ -skeleton of $A$ , i.e. we have $a _ { n } : A ^ { n } \longrightarrow X$ such that $C _ { * } ( a _ { n } ) = f ^ { \leq n }$ . Now $A ^ { n + 1 }$ is formed from $A ^ { n }$ by attaching a fnite wedge $\vee C S _ { H k } ^ { n }$ along a map $\alpha$ $x \colon \vee S _ { H k } ^ { n } \longrightarrow A ^ { n }$ The map $f _ { n + 1 } \colon C _ { n + 1 } ( A ) \longrightarrow M _ { n + 1 }$ specifies a homotopy class of maps

$$
