\left( \lor C S _ { H k } ^ { n } , \lor S _ { H k } ^ { n } \right) \longrightarrow ( X ^ { n + 1 } , X ^ { n } ) \longrightarrow ( X , X ^ { n } ) ,
$$

whose class on $\vee S _ { H k } ^ { n }$ agrees with $\left[ a _ { n } \circ \alpha \right]$ , since $\pi _ { n } ( X _ { n } )$ coincides with the cycles of $M _ { n }$ . We choose a representative in the homotopy class whose restriction to

$S _ { H k } ^ { n }$ is $a _ { n } \circ \alpha$ This extends to a map

$$
a _ { n + 1 } \colon A ^ { n + 1 } \longrightarrow X ^ { n + 1 } \longrightarrow X ,
$$

and by construction $C _ { * } ( a _ { n + 1 } )$ agrees with $f ^ { \leq n + 1 }$ .

REMARK 4.4. For a connective $S .$ algebra $R$ with $k = \pi _ { 0 } ( R )$ , the functor $C _ { * }$ of $\operatorname { I V } \ S 3$ is an exact functor $f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { R } \longrightarrow f { \mathcal { C } } { \mathcal { W } } _ { k }$ .The induced map of $K \cdot$ theory can be thought of as "discretization" and factors as $K R \longrightarrow K H k \longrightarrow K ^ { f } k$ .

REMARk 4.5. Another question one may ask is how the $K \cdot$ theory of $k$ compares with the $K .$ theory of $k$ regarded as an $A _ { \infty }$ ring, i.e. the $K .$ theory of the category of finite cell $A _ { \infty }$ $k$ -modules (as constructed in [35]). In fact, Propositions 4.1 and 4.2 have exact analogs in the theory of discrete $A _ { \infty }$ rings (with close analogs of the proofs). In particular, it follows from Corollary 2.7 that the natural quasi-isomorphism of the ring $k$ with its $A _ { \infty }$ enveloping algebra induces a homotopy equivalence from the free $K .$ theory of $k$ to its $A _ { \infty }$ K-theory.

# 5. Morita equivalence

Next, we discuss Morita equivalence, the relationship of the category of $R .$ modules to the category of modules over the analogue of a matrix ring of $R$ We introduce the shorthand notation $\vee _ { n } X$ for $\vee _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } X$ , and we define

$$
M _ { n } R = F _ { R } ( \lor _ { n } R , \lor _ { n } R ) ,
$$

$$
M _ { n 1 } = F _ { R } ( R , \lor _ { n } R ) \cong \lor _ { n } R ,
$$

and

$$
M _ { 1 n } = F _ { R } ( \vee _ { n } R , R ) \cong \prod _ { n } R .
$$

By III.6.12, we see that $M _ { n } R$ is an $S$ -algebra, $M _ { n 1 }$ an $( M _ { n } R , R )$ -bimodule, and $M _ { 1 n }$ an $( R , M _ { n } R )$ -bimodule. Classical Morita equivalence is the theorem that for a (discrete) ring $R$ , tensoring with these two bimodules gives an equivalence between the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and the category of $M _ { n } R$ modules. The observation that this restricts to an equivalence between the categories of finitely generated projective modules proves that Quillen's algebraic $K .$ -theory is Morita invariant.

In the case we consider, it is unreasonable to hope for an equivalence between $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { M } _ { M _ { n } R }$ since products and coproducts are not isomorphic, but we can ask for an equivalence of $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R }$ . Furthermore, because our $K .$ theory is really the $K .$ theory of free modules, we cannot expect the induced map of $K .$ theory to give an isomorphism on $K _ { 0 }$ in general (since, for a discrete ring, the image of the free module of rank one is a projective but not free module for $n > 1$ ), but we can ask for an isomorphism of the higher $K \cdot$ groups. In this section we find affirmative answers to each of these questions in the following theorems.

THEOREM 5.1 (MoRITA EQUIVALENCE). The derived functors of $M _ { n 1 } \land _ { R } ( \ l - )$ and $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } ( - )$ give an equivalence of categories $\mathcal { D } _ { R } \simeq \mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , which restricts to an equivalence of the derived categories of semi-finite objects.

TheoreM 5.2 (MOrItA InVariance OF $K .$ THEORY). The point-set functor $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } ( - )$ induces a map of $K .$ -theory $K M _ { n } R \longrightarrow K R$ , which on homotopy groups $^ { \prime } K$ -groups) sends a generator in dimension zero to $n$ times a generator, and gives an isomorphism on the higher groups.

We prove Theorem 5.1 by imitating as much as possible the proof of classical Morita equivalence. The following lemma gives a good start in this direction.

LEMMA 5.3. The $( R , R )$ -bimodules $R$ and $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n 1 }$ are isomorphic.

ProoF. By a comparison of colimits, using the map of $S$ algebras $R \longrightarrow$ $M _ { n } R$ , it is not hard to see that the following diagram is a coequalizer (cf. VII.1.9):

$$
M _ { 1 n } \land _ { R } M _ { n } R \land _ { R } M _ { n 1 } \mathop { \longrightarrow } M _ { 1 n } \land _ { R } M _ { n 1 } \mathop { \longrightarrow } M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n 1 } } M _ { n 1 } .
$$

The evaluation map ${ \cal M } _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } { \cal M } _ { n 1 } \longrightarrow R$ coequalizes this diagram, so induces a map $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n 1 } \longrightarrow R$ , which is evidently an $( R , R )$ -bimodule map. We show that this is an isomorphism by observing that

$$
M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } M _ { n } R \wedge _ { R } M _ { n 1 } { \longrightarrow } M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } M _ { n 1 } { \longrightarrow } R
$$

is a split coequalizer of $S .$ -modules. The splitting is given by maps analogous to those in the discrete case: The map $R \cong R \wedge _ { R } R \longrightarrow M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } M _ { n 1 }$ is the smash product of the map $R \longrightarrow \mathsf { V } _ { n } R$ that includes it as the first wedge summand with the map $R \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( \vee _ { n } R , R )$ that is induced by the map $\lor _ { n } R \longrightarrow R$ that collapses onto the first summand. The map

$$
{ \cal M } _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } { \cal M } _ { n 1 } \cong { \cal M } _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } { \cal M } _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } { \cal R } \longrightarrow { \cal M } _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } { \cal M } _ { n } R \wedge _ { R } { \cal M } _ { n 1 }
$$

is the smash product of the identity on $M _ { 1 n }$ , the map $M _ { n 1 } \longrightarrow M _ { n } R$ induced by the map $\lor _ { n } R \longrightarrow R$ collapsing onto the first summand in the first variable, and the inclusion of $R$ as the first summand in $M _ { n 1 }$ .It is straightforward to verify that the composites are as required to split the diagram.

PROOF OF THEoREM 5.1. We verify that the composite $\mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R } \longrightarrow$ $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ is naturally isomorphic to the identity. Let $X$ be a cell $R \mathrm { . }$ -module, and let $Y$ be a cell $M _ { n } R$ -module approximation to $M _ { n 1 } \land _ { R } X$ ; we must show that the map

$$
M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } Y \longrightarrow M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } X \cong X
$$

is a weak equivalence. Observe that the obvious map $\lor _ { n } M _ { n 1 } \longrightarrow M _ { n } R$ is a weak equivalence and a map of $( M _ { n } R , R )$ -bimodules. Since $X$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -module, the map $\vee _ { n } M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } X \longrightarrow M _ { n } R \wedge _ { R } X$ is a weak equivalence and the composite map $\vee _ { n } Y \longrightarrow M _ { n } R \wedge _ { R } X$ is a homotopy equivalence. Now we conclude that $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } \lor _ { n } Y \longrightarrow \lor _ { n } X$ must be a weak equivalence, since the map $\lor _ { n } X \longrightarrow$ $\left( \prod _ { n } R \right) \wedge _ { R } X \cong M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { R } X$ is, but the induced map on homotopy groups is just the direct sum of $n$ copies of the map we are interested in, so this map must also be a weak equivalence.

The reverse composite $\smash { \mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R } }$ is simpler. Let $X$ be a cell $M _ { n } R$ module. Since $M _ { n 1 } \land _ { R } ( - )$ preserves weak equivalences, the composite functor can be represented by $X \mapsto M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } X$ Observe that the evaluation map $M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } M _ { 1 n } \longrightarrow M _ { n } R$ is a weak equivalence of $( M _ { n } R , M _ { n } R )$ bimodules. On the underlying $R$ -modules it is a map $\begin{array} { r } { \vee _ { n } \prod _ { n } R \longrightarrow \prod _ { n } \vee _ { n } R } \end{array}$ inducing an isomorphsim $\oplus \prod R _ { * } \longrightarrow \prod \oplus R _ { * }$ .This induces the natural isomorphism (in $\mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R } )$ to the identity.

Since the derived categories of semi-finite objects are full subcategories of the derived categories $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { D } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , we see that this equivalence restricts, since both functors send wedge summands of finite objects to wedge summands of finite objects.

Let $\mathscr { C }$ be the smallest standard Waldhausen category with standard cylinder functor containing $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ and the image of $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { M _ { n } R }$ . By Proposition 3.5, the $K .$ theory of $\mathscr { C }$ is homotopy equivalent to $K R$ Let $\mathcal { I }$ be the full subcategory of $\mathscr { C }$ of objects weakly equivalent to objects in the image of $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { M _ { n } R }$ Since pushouts along cofibrations are homotopy equivalent to homotopy pushouts, which $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } ( - )$ preserves, it is easy to check that $\mathcal { I }$ is closed under pushouts along cofibrations and is therefore a standard Waldhausen category with standard cylinder functor; moreover, the functor $M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } ( - ) \colon f \mathcal { C } _ { M _ { n } R } \longrightarrow \mathscr { s }$ is exact.

LeMMA 5.4. The exact functor ${ \cal M } _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { { \cal M } _ { n } R } ( - ) \colon f \mathcal { C } _ { { \cal M } _ { n } R } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { S } }$ induces a homotopy equivalence of $K .$ theory.

PRooF. We apply Theorem 2.5: given $A \in f \mathcal { C } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , $B \in \mathcal { I }$ , and a map $f \colon M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } A \longrightarrow B$ , we find $X \in f \mathcal { C } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , a weak equivalence $e \colon M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R }$ $X \longrightarrow B$ and a map $a \colon A \longrightarrow X$ , such that $e \circ M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } a$ is homotopic to $f$ By assumption, $B$ is weakly equivalent to the image of some $X \in f \mathcal { C } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , so $M _ { n 1 } \land _ { R } B$ is an $M _ { n } R$ -module weakly equivalent to $M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } X$ . which in turn is weakly equivalent to $X$ . Thus, by the Whitehead Theorem, there exists a weak equivalence $\epsilon \colon X \longrightarrow M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } B$ Since the natural map $i \colon M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } A \longrightarrow M _ { n } R \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } A \cong A$ is a weak equivalence, it has a homotopy retraction $r$ , and there exists a map $a \colon A \longrightarrow X$ such that $\epsilon \circ a$ is homotopic to $( M _ { n 1 } \land _ { R } f ) \circ r$ , again by the Whitehead theorem. Thus the solid line part of the following diagram commutes up to homotopy.

![](images/0d9e9b54136ac8e093f102047497f17c93b8ddbbd21b9173f9e6f6eb47009ec5.jpg)

We apply the functor $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } ( - )$ . The isomorphism constructed in Lemma 5.3 induces a natural transformation $\mu$ : id $\longrightarrow M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } ( - )$ , from

which we get the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } A } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { { \displaystyle ( M _ { 1 n } \wedge i ) \circ \not \{ | \begin{array} { l } { { { \cal M } _ { 1 n } \wedge r } } \\ { { \not \nabla } } \end{array} | } M _ { 1 n } \wedge \hat { r } } }  \\ { { { \displaystyle M _ { 1 n } \wedge M _ { n } R } A } } \\ { { { \displaystyle M _ { 1 n } \wedge a | } \ y \qquad \stackrel { \cong } { \widetilde { { { \cal M } _ { 1 n } } } } ( M _ { 1 n } \wedge \epsilon ) } } \\ { { { \displaystyle M _ { 1 n } \wedge M _ { n } R } X } } \end{array} { B } .
$$

By the associativity of the multiplication pairing, the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n 1 } \wedge _ { R } M _ { 1 n } \stackrel { \cong } { \longrightarrow } R \wedge _ { R } M _ { 1 n } } }  \\ & { } & { \qquad \quad M _ { 1 n } \wedge i \Bigg \downarrow \qquad } \\ & { } & { \qquad \quad \forall } \\ & { } & { M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n } R \longrightarrow \qquad \stackrel { \cong } { \longrightarrow } M _ { 1 n } } \end{array}
$$

must commute, and we conclude that $\left( M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } i \right) \circ \mu$ is the identity. Now, letting $e = \mu ^ { - 1 } \circ M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } \epsilon$ , we see that $e \circ M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } a$ is homotopic to $f$ as required.

# LEMMA 5.5. $\mathcal { F }$ is closed under extensions in $\mathscr { C }$ .

Proor. We need to show that for a cofibration sequence $A  B \twoheadrightarrow C$ in $\mathscr { C }$ , if $A$ and $C$ are in $\mathcal { I }$ , then $B$ is also in $\mathcal { I }$ . It suffices to consider the case when $A , B$ ,and $C$ are celluar $R$ -modules, since any cofibration sequence can be replaced by a weakly equivalent one of this form. Using the proof of the last lemma, the map $* \longrightarrow C$ allows us to find $X$ in $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { M _ { n } R }$ together with a weak equivalence $e \colon M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } X \longrightarrow C$ Composing with the map $c \colon C \longrightarrow \Sigma A$ implied by the cofibration sequence, and applying once again the proof of the last lemma, we find an object $Y$ in $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , a map $a \colon X \longrightarrow Y$ , and a weak equivalence $f \colon M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } Y \longrightarrow \Sigma A$ that make the following square homotopy commute:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } X \xrightarrow { M _ { 1 n } \wedge \alpha } M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } Y } } \\ & { \leq } & { \simeq \int _ { \ell } } \\ & { \underset { C } { \iint } } & { \simeq \sum A . } \end{array}
$$

We conclude that the induced map on cofibers $C ( M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } a ) \longrightarrow C ( c )$ is a weak equivalence. The functor $M _ { 1 n } \land _ { M _ { n } R } ( - )$ commutes with smashing over $S$ on the right with $S _ { S } ^ { - 1 }$ and smashing on the right with the space $S ^ { 1 }$ , both composites of which are homotopic to the identity in $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { M _ { n } R }$ .We conclude that the map $C ( M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } a ) \wedge _ { S } S _ { S } ^ { - 1 } \longrightarrow C ( c ) \wedge _ { S } S _ { S } ^ { - 1 }$ is a weak equivalence. But $C ( c ) \wedge _ { S } S _ { S } ^ { - 1 }$ is homotopy equivalent to $B$ and $\tilde { C } ( M _ { 1 n } ^ { \smile } \Lambda _ { M _ { n } R } a ) \wedge _ { S } S _ { S } ^ { - 1 } \cong M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } ( \tilde { C } ( a ) \wedge _ { S } \breve { S } _ { S } ^ { - 1 } )$ is in the image of $f { \mathcal { C } } _ { M _ { n } R }$ , hence $B$ is in $\mathcal { I }$

PROOF OF THeoREM 5.2. The inclusion ${ \mathcal { I } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { C } }$ is an exact functor. It is easy to see that on $K _ { 0 }$ it sends a generator $\left( S _ { M _ { n } R } \right)$ to $_ n$ times a generator $( M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { M _ { n } R } M _ { n } R \wedge _ { S } S _ { S } \cong M _ { 1 n } \wedge _ { S } S _ { S } \simeq \vee _ { n } S _ { R } )$ . We want to see that it induces an isomorphism of the higher $K \cdot$ groups. Let $\mathcal { J }$ be the full subcategory of $\mathscr { C }$ of all objects whose class in $K _ { 0 } \mathcal { C }$ is in the image of $K _ { 0 } \mathcal { I }$ (so in particular ${ \mathcal { F } } \subset { \mathcal { J } } )$ . It follows from the relations that define $K _ { 0 }$ that $\mathcal { J }$ inherits the structure of a standard Waldhausen category with standard cylinder functor. By Proposition 3.4, the inclusions ${ \mathcal { S } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { J } }$ and ${ \mathcal { J } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { C } }$ are exact functors.

We use the argument of [24, §1] to show that $\mathcal { I }$ is strictly cofinal in $\mathcal { J }$ (in the sense of [71, 1.5.9]). We define an equivalence relation on the objects of $\mathscr { C }$ by letting $A$ and $A ^ { \prime }$ be equivalent if there exists some $X \in \mathcal { I }$ such that $A \lor X$ is weakly equivalent to $A ^ { \prime } \lor X$ Let $G$ be the set of equivalence classes under this relation. Then $G$ is a group under the operation $^ { 6 6 } \vee ^ { 9 9 }$ with the inverse of $A$ represented by $\vee _ { n - 1 } A$ . We have an obvious homomorphism $G \longrightarrow K _ { 0 } \mathcal { C } / K _ { 0 } \mathcal { S }$ we construct an inverse to this homomorphism. If $A \longmapsto B \twoheadrightarrow C$ is a cofibration sequence in $\mathscr { C }$ , then $\lor _ { n } A  B \lor ( \lor _ { n - 1 } A ) \lor ( \lor _ { n - 1 } C )  \lor _ { n } C$ is a cofibration sequence. But $\vee _ { n } A$ and $\vee _ { n } C$ are in $\mathcal { I }$ , so $B \lor ( \lor _ { n - 1 } { A } ) \lor ( \lor _ { n - 1 } { C } )$ is in $\mathcal { I }$ since $\mathcal { I }$ is closed under extensions in $\mathscr { C }$ ; therefore, $B \lor ( \lor _ { n - 1 } A ) \lor ( \lor _ { n - 1 } C )$ represents the identity in $G$ and hence $B$ represents the same element as $A \lor C$ in $G$ If $A$ is weakly equivalent to $A ^ { \prime }$ then they represent the same element in $G$ We see that the association of an object to its class in $G$ satisfies the universal relations that define $K _ { 0 } \mathcal { C }$ and so specifies a map $K _ { 0 } { \mathcal { C } } \longrightarrow G$ This map clearly factors through a map $K _ { 0 } { \mathcal { C } } / K _ { 0 } { \mathcal { S } } \longrightarrow G$ that is evidently inverse to the map above. Thus, we see that $\mathcal { J }$ consists of the objects whose class in $G$ is the identity, so we conclude that for any $X \in { \mathcal { J } }$ , there exists $Y \in \mathcal { I }$ such that $X \vee Y$ is weakly equivalent to an object of $\mathcal { I }$ and hence $X \vee Y$ is an object of $\mathscr { F }$ .

Now by [71, 1.5.9], ${ \mathcal { I } } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { J } }$ induces a homotopy equivalence of $K .$ theory, but by [68, 1.10.1], $K _ { i } { \mathcal { J } } \longrightarrow K _ { i } \mathcal { C }$ is an isomorphism for $i > 0$ .

# 6. Multiplicative structure in the commutative case

In this section, we prove the following theorem (cf. [65]).

Theorem 6.1. If $R$ is a connective commutative $S$ -algebra then KR is homotopy equivalent to an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum and therefore weakly equivalent to a commutative $S$ -algebra.

This result and the results of the next section depend on the following technical lemma, which the reader may recognize as a simple application of the theory of [71, $\ S 1 . 6 \mathrm { - } 1 . 8 ]$ to our new categories. Although we believe that Theorem 6.1 may generalize to non-connective commutative $S .$ -algebras, this lemma is peculiar to the connective case and relies on the existence of the ordinary homology theories of $\operatorname { I V } \ S 3$ . For this lemma, we need $R$ to be a connective but not necessarily commutative $S$ -algebra. Let $\mathscr { C }$ be a standard Waldhausen category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules that contains $f { \pmb { \mathcal { C } } } _ { R }$ and that only contains objects of the weak homotopy type of finite cell $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules. We see by Proposition 3.5 that $K \mathcal { C }$ is homotopy equivalent to $\kappa R$ We denote by ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { m }$ the full subcategory of objects weakly equivalent to a finite wedge of $S _ { R } ^ { m }$ .Observe that for each $m$ , the category of weak equivalences of ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { m }$ is a symmetric monoidal category under the operation of wedge, and denote the associated spectrum as $k \mathcal { C } ^ { m }$ .Suspension induces a system of maps of spectra $k \mathcal { C } ^ { m } \longrightarrow k \mathcal { C } ^ { m + 1 }$ .

LEMMA 6.2. The homotopy colimit of the system $\{ k \mathcal { C } ^ { m } \}$ is homotopy equivalent to $K \mathcal { C }$ .

PRooF. The Hurewicz theorem IV.3.6 allows us to identify ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { m }$ with the full subcategory of $\mathscr { C }$ of objects whose ordinary homology $H _ { * } ^ { R }$ is zero in all dimensions except $m$ , and in dimension $_ m$ is a finitely generated free module. Let ${ \tilde { \mathcal { C } } } ^ { m }$ be the full subcategory of $\mathscr { C }$ of objects whose ordinary homology $H _ { * } ^ { R }$ is zero in all dimensions except $m$ and in dimension $m$ is a finitely generated stably free module, i.e. is isomorphic to the kernel of a surjective map of finitely generated free modules. Let ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \geq n }$ be the full subcategory of $\mathscr { C }$ of those objects which are $( n - 1 )$ -connected. By the Hurewicz theorem IV.3.6 these are exactly the objects whose homology is zero in dimensions less than $_ n$ We give the categories ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { m }$ and ${ \tilde { \mathcal { C } } } ^ { m }$ Waldhausen structures by defining the w-cofibrations to be the w-cofibrations of $\mathscr { C }$ whose quotients lie in the subcategory in question. The categories ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \geq n }$ have the structure of standard Waldhausen categories with the standard cylinder functor.

Suspension gives exact functors $\tilde { \ell } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \tilde { \ell } ^ { m + 1 }$ and $\mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n }$ For $m \geq n$ , the inclusion of ${ \tilde { \mathcal { C } } } ^ { m }$ in $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { C } } \geq n$ is an exact functor; for fixed $_ n$ , these inclusions induce a map

$$
\operatorname * { h o c o l i m } _ { m  \infty } | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { \tilde { C } } ^ { m } | \longrightarrow \operatorname { h o c o l i m } _ { \Sigma } | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n } | ,
$$

where the colimit on the right is taken over repeated application of suspension. Next observe that ordinary homology $H _ { * } ^ { R }$ restricted to $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { C } } \geq n$ is a homology theory in the sense of [71, $\ S 1 . 7 \}$ (at least after shifting the indexing), and that the categories ${ \tilde { \mathcal { C } } } ^ { m }$ form categories of "spherical objects" for $\mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n }$ for the class of finitely generated stably free modules. Since this theory satisfies the "Hypothesis" of [71, 1.7.1], we conclude that the map above is a weak equivalence. On the other hand the inclusions $\mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n + 1 }$ are exact functors which induce cofibrations $| w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n } | \longrightarrow | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } ^ { \geq n + 1 } |$ ,whose colimit is $| w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } |$ .Taking the colimit (over $_ n$ ) of the homotopy equivalence above, we get a homotopy equivalence

$$
\operatorname * { h o c o l i m } _ { m  \infty } | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { \tilde { C } } ^ { m } | \longrightarrow \mathrm { h o c o l i m } | w S _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } | .
$$

The maps on the right are all homotopy equivalences (by [71, 1.6.2]), so we conclude that there exists a homotopy equivalence hocolim $K \tilde { \mathcal { C } } ^ { m } \longrightarrow K \mathcal { C }$ .

We apply the Strict Cofinality Theorem [71, 1.5.9] to conclude that $K \tilde { \mathcal { C } } ^ { m }$ is homotopy equivalent to $K \mathcal { C } ^ { m }$ . Now we are reduced to comparing $K \mathcal { C } ^ { m }$ with $k \mathcal { C } ^ { m }$ . According to [71, 1.8.1], it suffices to observe that cofibrations in ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { m }$ are "splittable up to weak equivalence". Given a cofibration $A  B$ , we can find a basis of the free module $H _ { m } ( B )$ that represents the union of bases for $H _ { m } A$ and $H _ { m } ( B / A )$ . The Hurewicz theorem IV.3.6 now specifies homotopy classes of weak equivalences from the wedge of $A$ and a wedge of spheres to $B$ and to $A \lor B / A$ , relative to the maps from $A$ .

Proof Of Theorem 6.1 Let $\mathscr { C }$ be the smallest standard Waldhausen category with standard cylinder functor containing $R$ , $S _ { R } ^ { n }$ for all $_ n$ , and all finite smash products over $\scriptstyle { R }$ of these. It is easy to check that the bifunctor $( - ) \wedge _ { R } ( - )$

restricts to $\mathscr { C }$ (up to equivalence), so $\mathscr { C }$ is a symmetric bimonoidal category under coproduct and smash product over $R$ Let $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ be as in the lemma above. Then $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ is the full subcategory of $\mathcal { C }$ of objects weakly equivalent to a finite wedge of $S _ { R }$ . Since smash product over $R$ with $R$ and with $S _ { R } ^ { n }$ preserve weak equivalences, so do smash products over $R$ with any object of $\mathscr { C }$ , and the smash product over $R$ of objects in $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ is weakly equivalent to a finite wedge of $S _ { R }$ and therefore is an object of $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ . Thus the smash product over $\scriptstyle { R }$ restricts to a bifunctor on $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ that makes $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ a symmetric bimonoidal category. By the work of [50], we can construct $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ functorially as an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum.

Next observe that suspension and $S _ { R } ^ { - 1 } \wedge _ { R } ( - )$ give functors $\mathcal { C } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { m + 1 }$ and $\mathcal { C } ^ { m + 1 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { m }$ for which both composites are weakly equivalent to the identity. We conclude that suspension gives a homotopy equivalence $k \mathcal { C } ^ { m } \longrightarrow k \mathcal { C } ^ { m + 1 }$ , and that $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ is homotopy equivalent to $K \mathcal { C }$ by the previous lemma.

# 7. The plus construction description of $K R$

We have observed that the category $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ gives a $K .$ theory $K f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ that has some right to be called the algebraic $K \cdot$ theory of $\scriptstyle { R }$ . This section is devoted to a comparison with another possible definition, based on Quillen's plus construction. In what follows, $\scriptstyle { R }$ is a fixed connective $S$ -algebra, and $k = \pi _ { 0 } R$ . We shall make use of classifying spaces of the topological monoids ${ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( X , X )$ . Unfortunately even when $X = S _ { R }$ , we cannot guarantee that the inclusion of the identity element is a cofibration. There are well-known ways of overcoming this difficulty, e.g. whiskering the monoids [45] or using thickened realizations [66]. In this and the next section, we shall take advantage of such techniques implicitly wherever necessary without further comment.

Let $\tilde { M } _ { n } R$ be the topological space $f { \mathscr { C } _ { R } } ( \vee _ { n } S _ { R } , \vee _ { n } S _ { R } )$ then $\pi _ { 0 } { \tilde { M } } _ { n } R \cong M _ { n } ( k )$ the ordinary matrix ring of the ring $k$ . Let $\widetilde { G L } _ { n } R$ be the space consisting of those connected components of $\tilde { M } _ { n } R$ whose image in $M _ { n } ( k )$ is invertible. Then ${ \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R$ is a topological monoid; indeed, it is the monoid of homotopy equivalences in $\tilde { M } _ { n } R$ We can consider its classifying space $B { \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R$ .We have the inclusion $i _ { n } \colon \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R \longrightarrow \widetilde { G L } _ { n + 1 } R$ obtained by sending the last wedge summand to the last wedge summand via the identity map, and it induces $B i _ { n } \colon B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R \longrightarrow$ $B \widetilde { G L } _ { n + 1 } \breve { R }$ Let $B \widetilde { G L } R$ be the telescope of these maps.

Now $\pi _ { 1 } { \cal B } \widetilde { G L } { \cal R } \cong G L ( k )$ has a perfect normal subgroup, so we can form $B \widetilde { G L } R ^ { + }$ (Quillen's plus construction). We shall see shortly that $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times B \widetilde { G L } R ^ { + }$ is an infinite loop space. Define $K ^ { + } R$ to be the connective spectrum obtained by delooping $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } \bar { k } \times \widetilde { B G L } R ^ { + }$ . We prove the following "plus equals $S _ { \bullet } { } ^ { \mathfrak { n } }$ theorem.

Theorem 7.1. $K ^ { + } R$ is weakly equivalent to $K R$

First we need to specify the infinite loop space structure on $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times B \widetilde { G L } R ^ { + }$ . For this, we observe that $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times B \widetilde { G L } R ^ { + }$ is the group completion of the classifying space of the topological category $\pmb { \mathcal { W } }$ whose (discrete) set of objects is the finite wedges of $S _ { R }$ and whose space of morphisms is the set of homotopy equivalences topologized as a subspace of the space of morphisms of $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ Call this group completion $B$ .In the case when $K _ { 0 } ^ { \hat { f } } k$ is the integers, the classifying space of $\pmb { \mathcal { W } }$ is the disjoint union of the $B { \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R$ and we may apply the remarks of [66, §4] to conclude that we have a homology isomorphism to $B$ from the telescope of maps $\prod B { \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R$ to itself induced by the maps $B i _ { n } \colon B { \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R \longrightarrow B { \widetilde { G L } } _ { n + 1 } { R }$ . This telescope is easily seen to be $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times \mathrm { h o c o l i m } _ { n } B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R$ We conclude that $B \simeq K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times ( \mathrm { h o c o l i m } _ { n } B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R ) ^ { + }$ .

In the pathological case when $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k$ is not the integers, i.e. when there exists a homotopy equivalence $\vee _ { j } S _ { R } \simeq \vee _ { k } S _ { R }$ for $j \neq k$ , we still have a homology isomorphism to the group completion $B$ from the telescope $T$ of maps from $B { \# }$ to itself induced by addition of an identity map on the wedges of sphere $R \mathrm { . }$ . modules. Proposition 7.2 below allows us to see that $B \mathscr { W }$ is homotopy equivalent to a disjoint union of of some of the $B { \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R$ , one choice for each isomorphism class of finitely generated free $\pi _ { 0 } R$ -modules. Now we see that the telescope $_ T$ is homotopy equivalent to $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } \dot { k } \times \mathrm { h o c o l i m } _ { n } B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R$ , and we conclude that $B \simeq K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times ( \mathrm { h o c o l i m } _ { n } B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R ) ^ { + }$ .

To identify the homotopy type of $B { \mathcal { W } }$ in the pathological case above, we need the following proposition. We will need a similar result again later, and we have written this proposition in the minimal possible generality necessary to handle both cases. The proposition says essentially that if the morphisms in a category are all homotopy equivalences (in a certain sense), then the classifying space of the monoid of endomorphisms of any object is homotopy equivalent to its connected component in the classifying space of the category. Because this proposition has obvious generalizations with more general scope than its use in. this section, we break our rule of not mentioning the necessary cofibration assumptions. As always the reader has the choice of deleting the cofibration assumption by using a whiskering technique or employing the thickened realization.

PROPOSITION 7.2. (cf. [71, 2.2.7]) Let $\mathscr { C }$ be a topological category with discrete set of objects such that the identity morphism (from objects to morphisms) is a cofibration. Let $X$ be an object of $\mathscr { C }$ and denote by $\mathcal { C } _ { X }$ the full subcategory of $\mathscr { C }$ containing $X$ . Suppose that for each morphism $f \colon Y \longrightarrow Z$ in $\mathscr { C }$ , there is some $f ^ { \prime } \colon Z \longrightarrow Y$ so that $f ^ { \prime } \circ f$ and $f \circ f ^ { \prime }$ each lie in the same path component of ${ \mathcal { C } } ( Y , Y )$ and ${ \mathcal { C } } ( Z , Z )$ as the respective identity elements. Then the inclusion $\mathcal { C } _ { X } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C }$ induces a homotopy equivalence of the classifying space of $\mathcal { C } _ { X }$ with the connected component of its image in the classifying space of the category $\mathscr { C }$ .

PRooF. First observe that Quillen's "Theorem A" [58] holds with essentially the same proof for continuous functors between topological categories with discrete object sets whose identity map (objects to morphisms) is a cofibration.

Since the connected component of the image of $\mathcal { C } _ { X }$ in the classifying space of $\mathscr { C }$ is the classifying space of the connected component (as a graph) of $\mathscr { C }$ that contains $X$ , we can reduce to this smaller category and assume without loss of generality that $\mathscr { C }$ is connected (as a graph). Applying Quillen's Theorem A (dual formulation), we are reduced to showing that for every $Y$ in $\mathscr { C }$ ,the topological category $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y$ is contractible. But if $f \colon Y \longrightarrow Z$ is morphism in $\mathscr { C }$ , then we have $f ^ { \prime } \colon Z \longrightarrow Y$ and paths $\gamma \colon f ^ { \prime } \circ f  1 _ { Y }$ and $\gamma ^ { \prime } \colon f \circ f ^ { \prime }  1 _ { Z }$ . We can interpret the morphisms $f$ and $f ^ { \prime }$ as continuous functors $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z$ , $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y$ , and the paths $\gamma$ and $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ as continuous functors $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y \times I \longrightarrow$ $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y$ $, \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z \times I \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z$ Passing to the classifying spaces we see that the paths represent homotopies $B ( \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y ) \times I \longrightarrow B ( \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y )$ and $B ( \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z ) \times I \longrightarrow$ $B ( \mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z )$ from the composites $B f ^ { \prime } \circ B f$ and $B f \circ B f ^ { \prime }$ to the repective identities. In short, $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y$ and $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Z$ are homotopy equivalent. Since we have reduced to the case when $\mathscr { C }$ is connected (as a graph), we see that $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / Y$ is homotopy equivalent to $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / X$ . The lemma is established by the observation that $\mathcal { C } _ { X } / X$ has a final object and therefore is contractible.

We begin to compare $K ^ { + } R$ to $K f \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ . One obvious obstacle is that we have defined $K ^ { + } R$ in terms of a topological category and $K f { \mathscr C } _ { R }$ in terms of a discrete one. Let $\boldsymbol { w } \boldsymbol { \mathcal { C } } ^ { 0 }$ denote the (discrete) full subcategory of $\mathbf { w } ( f \mathcal { C } _ { R } )$ whose objects are homotopy equivalent to wedges of $S _ { R }$ ; the set of morphisms is the set of homotopy equivalences. Using arguments similar to [71, 2.2], we relate $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ to both $\pmb { \mathcal { W } }$ and $w S _ { \bullet } f \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ .

LEMMA 7.3. (cf. [71, 2.2.5]) There is a chain of weak equivalences relating the classifying spaces of the categories $\pmb { \mathcal { W } }$ and $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ . Each map in the chain is $^ a$ map of $E _ { \infty }$ spaces.

PROOF. For each $k$ , let $\mathcal { W } \Delta _ { k }$ be the (discrete) category whose objects are the objects of $\boldsymbol { w } \boldsymbol { \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } }$ and whose morphisms $\mathcal { W } \Delta _ { k } ( X , Y )$ consist of the set of continuous maps $\Delta [ k ] \longrightarrow f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R } ( X , Y )$ whose image lands in the component of a weak equivalence, where $\Delta [ k ]$ denotes the standard topological $k \mathrm { . }$ simplex. In light of the adjunction $\mathcal { T } ( \Delta [ k ] _ { + } , f \mathcal { C } _ { R } ( X , Y ) ) \cong f \mathcal { C } _ { R } ( X \wedge \Delta [ k ] _ { + } , Y )$ , we see that this is the same as the set of weak equivalences $X \wedge \Delta [ k ] _ { + } \longrightarrow Y$ . This is a simplicial category. Let $N _ { j , k }$ be the nerve of this category.

If we realize $N _ { j , k }$ in the $k$ direction, we obtain a simplicial space that is the nerve of a topological category with a discrete set of objects. We denote this category as $| \mathcal { W } \Delta |$ . In particular, the objects of $| \mathcal { W } \Delta |$ are the objects of $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ and the morphism space $| { \mathcal { W } } \Delta | ( X , Y )$ is the geometric realization of the total singular complex of the subspace of $f \mathcal { C } _ { R } ( X , Y )$ consisting of those components which contain homotopy equivalences. For each $X ~ \in ~ \mathcal { W }$ , let $| { \mathcal { W } } \Delta | _ { X }$ be the full subcategory of $| \mathcal { W } \Delta |$ consisting of the single object $X$ . By the previous proposition, the inclusion $| \mathcal { W } \Delta | _ { X } \longrightarrow | \mathcal { W } \Delta |$ induces a homotopy equivalence from the classifying space of $| \mathcal { W } \Delta | _ { X }$ to its connected component in the classifying space of $| \mathcal { W } \Delta |$ . On the other hand we have a natural weak equivalence of monoids $| { \mathcal { W } } \Delta | ( X , X ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { W } } ( X , X )$ , giving a weak equivalence of their classifying spaces. Let $| { \mathcal { W } } \Delta S _ { R } |$ be the full subcategory of $| \mathcal { W } \Delta |$ consisting of the finite wedges of $S _ { R }$ . Then we have weak equivalences $\lVert N _ { j , k } \rVert \stackrel { \sim } { \longrightarrow } \lVert \mathcal { W } \Delta S _ { R } \rVert \stackrel { \sim } { \longrightarrow } \lvert \mathcal { W } \rvert$ .

Next we produce a weak equivalence between $\boldsymbol { w } \boldsymbol { \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } }$ and $\mathcal { W } \Delta _ { * }$ The map $\Delta [ k ] _ { + } \longrightarrow S ^ { 0 }$ induces a functor $F \colon w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { W } \Delta _ { k }$ that is the identity on objects. Let $G \colon { \mathcal { W } } \Delta _ { k } \longrightarrow w { \mathcal { C } } ^ { 0 }$ be the functor induced by the map $S ^ { 0 } \longrightarrow \Delta [ k ] _ { + }$ that sends the non-basepoint to the zeroth vertex of $\Delta [ k ]$ . Then $G F$ is the identity functor on $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ . We show that $_ { F G }$ is homotopic to the identity. Let $H \colon \mathcal { W } \Delta _ { k } \longrightarrow \mathcal { W } \Delta _ { k }$ be the functor that takes $X$ to $X \wedge I _ { + }$ and that on morphisms is induced by a map $I \times \Delta [ k ] \longrightarrow \Delta [ k ]$ that is the identity on the bottom face $\{ 0 \} \times \Delta [ k ]$ and sends the whole top face $\{ 0 \} \times \Delta [ k ]$ to the zeroth vertex of $\Delta [ k ]$ . There are obvious natural transformations id $\longrightarrow H$ and $F G \longrightarrow H$ given by the inclusion of bottom face and the inclusion of top face, from which we conclude that $F G$ is homotopic to the identity. We may regard $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ as a simplicial category constant in the $k$ direction. The functors $F$ are compatible with the faces and degeneracies $( { \mathrm { i n ~ } } k )$ , and therefore assemble to a simplicial functor $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { W } \Delta _ { * }$ that induces a homotopy equivalence upon passage to classifying spaces.

It is easy to see that the simplicial maps above realize to maps of $E _ { \infty }$ spaces as they are induced by functors that preserve wedges.

PROOF OF THeoREM 7.1. If we let $\mathcal { C }$ be the category $f { \mathscr { C } } _ { R }$ , then $w \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ is exactly the subcategory of weak equivalences of the category $\mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ defined above Lemma 6.2, the associated spectrum of which we denoted $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ . Again suspension and $S _ { R } ^ { - 1 } \wedge _ { R } ( - )$ give functors $\mathcal { C } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { m + 1 }$ and $\mathcal { C } ^ { m + 1 } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C } ^ { m }$ whose composites are weakly equivalent to the identity. We conclude that the maps in the homotopy colimit are homotopy equivalences and that $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ is homotopy equivalent to $K R$ On the other hand, the previous proposition shows that $K ^ { + } R$ is weakly equivalent to $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ .

REMARk 7.4. Note that we only needed the connectivity hypothesis to show the relationship between $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ and $K f { \mathcal { C } } _ { R }$ .More generally we do have a homotopy equivalence $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } \simeq K ^ { + } R$ (the spectrum whose zeroth space is $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times B \widetilde { G L } R ^ { + } )$ , but there is no reason to expect that the map $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } \longrightarrow K f \mathcal { C } _ { R }$ will be a homotopy equivalence. In particular $k \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 }$ cannot see any relationships between spheres of different dimensions. For example, if $K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k = \dot { \mathbb { Z } }$ , but $S _ { R } \simeq S _ { R } ^ { 1 }$ , then $\vert w S _ { \bullet } f \mathcal { C } _ { R } \vert$ is contractible but $\lvert w N _ { \bullet } \mathcal { C } ^ { 0 } \rvert$ is not.

REMARK 7.5. We should also observe that this allows another interpretation of the discretization map: $\pi _ { 0 }$ applied to the simplicial space $N { \mathbf { \# } }$ gives an $E _ { \infty }$ map $K R ( 0 ) \simeq K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times \widetilde { B G L } R ^ { + } { \longrightarrow } K _ { 0 } ^ { f } k \times B G \bar { L } k ^ { + } \simeq \bar { K } ^ { f } k ( 0 )$ , which evidently coincides with the discretization map and is a weak equivalence in the case when $R = H k$ .

REMARK 7.6 (MONOMIAL MATRICES). Let $\pmb { \mathcal { V } }$ be the subcategory of $\pmb { \mathcal { W } }$ of those maps $\lor _ { n } S _ { R } \longrightarrow \lor _ { n } S _ { R }$ that are wedges of $_ n$ maps $S _ { R } \longrightarrow S _ { R }$ in any order. Thinking of ${ \pmb { \mathscr W } } ( \vee _ { n } S _ { R } , \vee _ { n } S _ { R } )$ as analogous to $G L _ { n } R$ , then $\mathcal { V } ( \vee _ { n } S _ { R } , \vee _ { n } S _ { R } )$ is analogous to the subgroup of monomial matrices, those matrices with a single non-zero entry in each row and column. Let $R ^ { \times }$ denote the monoid $\mathcal { V } ( S _ { R } , S _ { R } ) =$ ${ \mathcal { W } } ( S _ { R } , S _ { R } )$ . Then $\mathcal { V } ( \vee _ { n } S _ { R } , \vee _ { n } S _ { R } )$ is isomorphic to the monoid $\Sigma _ { n } \int R ^ { \times }$ and the classifying space of $\pmb { \mathcal { V } }$ is isomorphic to the disjoint union of the classifying spaces of these monoids; moreover, under this isomorphism the $E _ { \infty }$ space structure induced by wedge sums becomes the $E _ { \infty }$ space structure induced by block sums. We conclude that the group completion of the classifying space of $\pmb { \mathcal { V } }$ is homotopic to $Q B R ^ { \times } +$ , and that $\mathcal { Y } \longrightarrow \mathcal { W }$ induces a map of spectra $\Sigma ^ { \infty } B R ^ { \times } + \longrightarrow K R$ .

REMARK 7.7. (Naturality) Let $A \longrightarrow B$ be a map of $S .$ -algebras. We saw in Propostion 4.1 that the functor $B \wedge _ { A } ( - )$ induces a map of $K .$ theory spectra $K A \longrightarrow K B$ .This also restricts to a continuous functor of topological categories $\mathcal { W } _ { A } \longrightarrow \mathcal { W } _ { B }$ that induces a map of the plus contruction spectra above. We conclude that these two maps represent the same map in the stable category, since this functor commutes up to natural isomorphism with the functors used in comparing $K ^ { + }$ with $K$ .

# 8. Comparison with Waldhausen's $\kappa { \mathrm { . } }$ theory of spaces

Now we compare the new algebraic $K .$ -theory with Waldhausen's algebraic $K .$ . theory of spaces. For this, let $X$ be a connected pointed topological space, and let $G = | G S X |$ , the geometric realization of the Kan loop group of the based singular complex of $X$ .This is a topological group with non-degenerate identity. We let $R = \Sigma ^ { \infty } ( G _ { + } )$ (where the plus subscript is union with a disjoint basepoint) and we note that $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an $S$ algebra (IV.7.8) with $k = \pi _ { 0 } R = \mathbb { Z } [ \pi _ { 0 } G ]$ .

DEFInITioN 8.1. Let $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m }$ denote the topological monoid of pointed $G .$ -equivariant homotopy equivalences of $\mathsf { V } _ { n } \Sigma ^ { m } G _ { + }$ with itself, and let $B \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m }$ denote its $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m + 1 }$ and $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \mathcal { H } _ { n + 1 } ^ { m }$ which are induced by suspension and by addition of an identity map on the last wedge summand and which are cofibrations. The algebraic $K .$ theory of the space $X$ is defined to be the space $\begin{array} { r } { A ( X ) = K _ { 0 } ^ { f } \mathbb { Z } [ \pi _ { 0 } G ] \stackrel { - } { \times } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { m , n } B \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } ) ^ { + } } \end{array}$ This is obviously equivalent to Waldhausen's definition [71, 2.2.1]. We shall also use the symbol $A ( X )$ to denote the spectrum associated to its delooping, and under this interpretation we will prove the following result.

THeOReM 8.2. The spectra $K \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + }$ and $A ( X )$ are homotopy equivalent, naturally in $X$ .

Observe that the functors $\Sigma _ { m } ^ { \infty }$ give maps of topological monoids

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } ( \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + } , \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + } ) } \end{array}
$$

which are easily seen to be compatible with suspension and addition of an identity map. Composing with the functors $\mathbb { L }$ and $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ , we obtain maps of topological monoids

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( \bigvee _ { n } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + } , \bigvee _ { n } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + } ) . } \end{array}
$$

We denote this composite functor by $L _ { n } ^ { m }$ . The observation that the functor $G _ { + } \wedge ( - )$ is naturally isomorphic to the functor $R \land s ( - )$ immediately implies that $L _ { n } ^ { m }$ sends $G \mathrm { \Omega }$ -equivariant maps to $R$ -module maps; therefore, we can interpret $L _ { n } ^ { m }$ s map $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \tilde { M } _ { n } R$ Snce for $m \geq 2$ $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m }$ consis of the subspace of those connected components of $\begin{array} { r l } { \small } & { { } \operatorname { M a p } _ { G } ( \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { m } G _ { + } , \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { m } G _ { + } ) } \end{array}$ which $\pi _ { 0 }$ maps to $G L _ { n } ( R )$ , we see that $L _ { n } ^ { m }$ restricts to a map of monoids $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow$ ${ \widetilde { G L } } _ { n } R$ . We will show that in the colimit this map is a homotopy equivalence.

ProPosITIon 8.3. The map of topological monoids

$$
L _ { n } \colon \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { m } \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R
$$

is a homotopy equivalence.

PRooF. We have defined $L _ { n } ^ { m }$ via a composition of functors so that it would be easy to see that it is a map of monoids; we rewrite this composition to make it easier to analyze homotopically.

Consider the map of spaces

$$
f _ { m } \colon \mathcal { T } ( \bigvee _ { n } S ^ { m } , \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { m } G _ { + } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S } ( \bigvee _ { n } S _ { S } , \bigvee _ { n } S _ { R } )
$$

(for fixed $_ n$ ) induced by the composite of the functors $\Sigma _ { m } ^ { \infty }$ , $\mathbb { L }$ ,and $S \wedge _ { S } ( - )$ . The colimit of the $f _ { m }$ is the composite of the maps

$$
\begin{array} { l l l } { \dim _ { m } { \mathcal { T } } ( \bigvee _ { n } S ^ { m } , \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { m } G _ { + } ) } & { \longrightarrow } & { { \mathcal { S } } ( \bigvee _ { n } S , \bigvee _ { n } \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + } ) } \\ & { \longrightarrow } & { { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \bigvee _ { n } \mathbb { L } S , \bigvee _ { n } \mathbb { L } \Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + } ) } \\ & { \longrightarrow } & { { \mathcal { M } } _ { S } ( \bigvee _ { n } S _ { S } , \bigvee _ { n } S _ { R } ) , } \end{array}
$$

each of which is a homotopy equivalence. Via the obvious isomorphisms, the map $L _ { n }$ agrees with the restriction of this map to the connected components that consist of weak equivalences, and so it is also a homotopy equivalence.

Since the inclusion of the identity in $G$ is a cofibration, we see that induced map $\mathrm { c o l i m } _ { m } B \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } \longrightarrow B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R$ is a homotopy equivalence, and hence the induced map on the plus constructions of the telescopes is a homotopy equivalence.

ProoF OF THEoREM 8.1. We need to show that we have a map of spectra. But the infinite loop space structure on $A ( X )$ comes from the operation wedge on the colimit of the topological categories whose objects are finite wedges of $\Sigma ^ { m } G _ { + }$ (for each $_ m$ ) and whose maps are the $\mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m }$ . The functors $L _ { n } ^ { m }$ assemble to a continuous functor from this colimit to the category $\pmb { \mathcal { W } }$ which commutes with wedges and which coincides with the above homotopy equivalence on the plus constructions. We conclude that the map constructed above

$$
K _ { 0 } ^ { f } \mathbb { Z } [ \pi _ { 0 } G ] \times ( \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { m , n } B \mathcal { H } _ { n } ^ { m } ) ^ { + } \longrightarrow K _ { 0 } ^ { f } \mathbb { Z } [ \pi _ { 0 } G ] \times \widetilde { B G L } R ^ { + }
$$

is a map of $E _ { \infty }$ spaces.

Since $G$ is a CW space, $\Sigma ^ { \infty } G _ { + }$ is a CW spectrum, so $\tilde { M } _ { n } R , \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R , B \widetilde { G L } _ { n } R$ . $B \widetilde { G L } R$ , and $B \widetilde { G L } R ^ { + }$ have the homotopy type of CW spaces; therefore, the plus construction of the previous section produces a spectrum homotopy equivalent to $K R$ We conclude that the spectrum $A ( X )$ is homotopy equivalent to $K R$ .

REMARK 8.4 (LINEARIZATION). The map $R \longrightarrow H Z \land _ { S } R$ is a map of $S .$ algebras and a rational equivalence. The map

$$
H Z \wedge _ { S } ( - ) \colon \mathcal { W } _ { R } ( \vee _ { n } S _ { R } , \vee _ { m } S _ { R } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { W } _ { H Z \wedge _ { S } R } ( \vee _ { n } S _ { H Z \wedge _ { S } R } , \vee _ { m } S _ { H Z \wedge _ { S } R } )
$$

induces an equivalence on rational homology. We conclude that the induced map $K R \longrightarrow K ( H Z \land s R )$ is a rational equivalence. A comparison of the categories of modules for the $S$ algebra $H Z \land _ { S } R$ and the simplicial ring $\mathbb { Z } [ G S X ]$ would then give a linearization result. We save this and other observations along these lines for a future paper.

# CHAPTER VII

# $R \mathrm { . }$ -algebras and topological model categories

In Chapter II, we set up the ground category of $S$ -modules, and we developed the theory of $S .$ -algebras and their modules by exploiting the good formal properties of that category. In Chapter III, we set up a ground category of modules over a commutative $S .$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ that enjoys the same formal properties as the category of $\boldsymbol { S }$ -modules, and the previous three chapters gave applications of that theory. As we discuss in Section 1, we can go on to define $R$ -algebras and their modules simply by changing ground categories from $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ to $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ .

At this point, we face a homotopical problem. We want to use point-set level constructions, such as bar constructions and constructions of topological Hochschild homology, that involve taking smash powers of a commutative $R .$ . algebra $A$ . To make homotopical use of these constructions, we need to know that the underlying $R$ -modules of these smash powers represent their smash powers in the derived category of $R .$ -modules. However, $A$ need not have the homotopy type of a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module, so we must approximate it by a weakly equivalent $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra with better properties. We first attacked this problem by use of the bar construction of Chapter XII, but we'shall here deal with it by use of Quillen model categories.

Thus we shall prove that all of our various categories of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras, commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras, and modules over any of these are complete and cocomplete, tensored and cotensored, topologically enriched categories that admit canonical (closed) model structures in the sense of Quillen [57]. Since cofibrations and fibrations in the classical sense are important in our theory, we shall use the terms $q$ -cofibration and $q$ -fibration for the model category concepts.

The proofs that our categories are so richly structured are almost entirely formal, and these formal structures do not solve or even address the motivating homotopical problem since forgetful functors need not preserve $q$ -cofibrant homotopy types. However, we shall see that the problem can be solved by combining the formal theory with the homotopical analysis of the linear isometries operad.

Much of the formal theory in this chapter is based on ideas and results originally due to Hopkins and McClure (in part in [32], but we have also benefited from many profitable conversations) or to McClure, Schwänzl and Vogt [54].

# 1. $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras and their modules

We fix a commutative $s$ -algebra $R$ and work in the symmetric monoidal category $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ of $R$ -modules.

DEfInitioN 1.1. An $R$ algebra is a monoid in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ . A commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebra is a commutative monoid in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ .

As in algebra, we obtain free $R$ -algebras by "extension of scalars" from $S$ to $R$ . To show this, we use an alternative description of $R \mathrm { . }$ -algebras and commutative $R$ -algebras, which again is the same as in algebra. Say that a map $\eta : R \longrightarrow A$ of $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -algebras is central if the following diagram commutes:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \ R \wedge _ { S } A \longrightarrow \qquad \xrightarrow [ ] { \tau } A \wedge _ { S } R } \\ & { \underset { \rtimes [ ] { S } } { \mathrm { \wedge i d } } \Bigg \downarrow \qquad } \\ & { \ A \wedge _ { S } A \underset { \phi } { \longrightarrow } A \underset {  } { \longrightarrow } A \underset { \phi } { \longrightarrow } A \underset { \phi } { \mathrm { \wedge } } A } \end{array}
$$

We learned the following interpretation of this definition from McClure.

REMARK 1.2. The center of an associative $k \mathrm { . }$ -algebra $A$ with product $\phi$ can be written as the equalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
C ( A ) \longrightarrow A \frac { \tilde { \phi } } { \widetilde { \phi } \tau } \succeq \mathrm { H o m } _ { k } ( A , A ) ;
$$

here ${ \tilde { \phi } } ( a ) ( b ) = a b$ and $\widetilde { \phi \tau } ( a ) ( b ) = b a$ .This suggests that the center $C ( A )$ of an $S _ { \ l }$ algebra $A$ should be defined as the equalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { C ( A ) \longrightarrow A \frac { \tilde { \phi } } { \widetilde { \phi } \tau } \mp F _ { S } ( A , A ) . } \end{array}
$$

The definition of a central map $\eta : R \longrightarrow A$ then says precisely that $\eta$ factors through $C ( A )$ .

LEMMA 1.3. An $R$ -algebra $A$ is an $s$ -algebra with a central map $R \longrightarrow A$ of $S$ -algebras. A commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra $A$ is a commutative $S$ -algebra with a map $R \longrightarrow A$ of $S$ algebras.

PROoF. Trivially, if $A$ is an $R$ -algebra, then its unit $\eta : R \longrightarrow A$ is a central map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras. Conversely, if $A$ is an $S _ { \ l }$ -algebra and $\eta : R \longrightarrow A$ is a map of $S .$ -algebras, then $A$ is a left $R$ -module via the composite

$$
R \land _ { S } A { \xrightarrow { \eta \land { \mathrm { i d } } } } A \land _ { S } A { \xrightarrow { \phi } } A .
$$

There is a symmetrically defined right action of $\scriptstyle { R }$ on $A$ that makes $A$ an $( R , R )$ . bimodule. Centrality ensures that the left and right actions agree under the commutativity isomorphism of their domains. The product of $A$ therefore factors through $A \land _ { R } A$ to give the required $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra structure.

We leave the proofs of the next few results as exercises; as in the proofs above, one first writes down the proof of the algebraic analogue and then replaces tensor products with smash products.

PRopositIoN 1.4. If $Q$ is an $\boldsymbol { S }$ -algebra, then $R \wedge _ { S } Q$ is the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra generated by $Q$ , hence $R \wedge _ { S }$ TM is the free $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra generated by an $S$ -module $M$ . If $Q$ is $^ a$ commutative $s$ -algebra, then $R \land s Q$ is the free commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra generated by $Q$ , hence $R \wedge _ { S }$ PM is the free commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra generated by $M$ .

REMARK 1.5. We may think of $R \wedge _ { S }$ $( S \land \mathcal { L } \ \mathbb { B } X )$ as the "free" $R$ algebra generated by a spectrum $X$ and $R \land _ { S } ( S \land _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { C } X )$ as the "free" commutative $\scriptstyle { R } .$ . algebra generated by a spectrum $X$ . However, in view of II.1.3 (see also III§1), this is a misnomer since the right adjoints of these functors from the category of spectra to the category of $\scriptstyle R$ -algebras or commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras are weakly equivalent rather than equal to the obvious forgetful functors.

PROPOSITION 1.6. Let $f : R \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime }$ and $g : R \longrightarrow R ^ { \prime \prime }$ be maps of commutative $S$ -algebras. Then $R ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { R } R ^ { \prime \prime }$ is both the coproduct of $\scriptstyle { R ^ { \prime } }$ and $R ^ { \prime \prime }$ in the category of commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras and the pushout of $f$ and $g$ in the category of commutative $S$ -algebras. More generally, let $f : A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ and $g : A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime \prime }$ be maps of commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras. Then $A ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ is the pushout of $f$ and $g$ in the category of commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras.

As in algebra, we can define the notion of a module over an $R$ algebra $A$ , but it turns out to be equivalent to the notion of a module over $A$ regarded just as an $S .$ -algebra. Recall III.3.1.

DEfinitioN 1.7. Let $A$ be an $R$ algebra. A left or right $A { \mathrm { . } }$ -module is a left or right A-object in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ .

The free $A { \mathrm { . } }$ -module generated by an $S .$ -module $M$ is

$$
A \land _ { R } ( R \land _ { S } M ) \cong A \land _ { S } M .
$$

This gives an isomorphism of monads that implies the following result.

LEMMA 1.8. Let $A$ be an $R$ -algebra. A module over $A$ regarded as an $S$ . algebra is the same thing as a module over $A$ regarded as an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra. That is, an action $A \land _ { S } M \longrightarrow M$ necessarily factors through an action $A \land _ { R } M \longrightarrow M$ .

Similarly, if $M$ and $N$ are $A$ -modules, then $M \wedge _ { A } N$ is the same whether defined using a coequalizer diagram in the category of $R .$ -modules or in the category of $S .$ -modules.

LEMMA 1.9. Let $A$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra, and let $M$ be a right and $N$ a left $A$ . module. Then $M \wedge _ { A } N$ can be identified with the coequalizer $M \wedge _ { ( A , R ) } N$ displayed in the diagram

$$
M \wedge _ { R } A \wedge _ { R } N \frac { \mu \wedge _ { R } \mathrm { i d } } { \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { R } \nu } \quad \stackrel { \qquad } { \lesssim } M \wedge _ { R } N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { ( A , R ) } N ,
$$

The analogous result holds for function A-modules.

PRooF. The proof is a formal categorical chase of the following schematic diagram:

MAsRAsN   
MsAsN →MsN MAN Y   
MRARN MARN M (A,R) N.

Here the left vertical arrow is an epimorphism, and this implies that the diagonal dotted arrow factors through the dotted right vertical arrow.

Although we have only one notion of an $A$ -module, it is helpful to think of its study as divided into an "absolute theory", in which we take the ground ring to be $S$ , and a "relative theory", in which we take the ground ring to be $\scriptstyle { R }$ . The absolute theory is a special case of the study of modules over algebras that we developed in Chapter III. In particular, III.1.4 shows that $\mathbb { F } _ { A } X$ is weakly equivalent to $A \land X$ for a CW spectrum $X$ Here the free functor $\mathbb { F } _ { A }$ is isomorphic to the composite functor $A \wedge _ { R } ( R \wedge _ { S } \mathbb { F } _ { S } )$ from spectra to $A$ -modules. Again, the term free is a misnomer since the right adjoint of $\mathbb { F } _ { S }$ is only weakly equivalent to the forgetful functor. The theory of cell and CW $A { \mathrm { . } }$ -modules and the definition of the derived category of $A$ -modules are part of the absolute theory.

The previous lemma shows that the absolute smash product $\wedge _ { A }$ and function module functors $F _ { A }$ are isomorphic to the relative functors, so that $M \wedge _ { A } N$ and $F _ { A } ( M , N )$ are $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Of course, if $A$ is a commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra, then these are $A$ -modules and duality theory applies. In the relative theory, if we replace $( R , S )$ by $( A , R )$ , with concomitant changes of notations for various functors, then all of the statements in Chapter III which make sense remain true. Note, for example, that we have relative versions of III.3.10 and of the pairings discussed in $\mathrm { I I I } \ S 6$ The results on pairings give the following generalization of III.6.12.

PROPOSITION 1.10. Let $R$ be a commutative $S$ algebra, $A$ be an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra, and $M$ and $N$ be $A$ -modules. Then $F _ { A } ( M , M )$ is an $R$ -algebra and $F _ { A } ( M , N )$ is an $( F _ { A } ( N , N ) , F _ { A } ( M , M ) )$ -bimodule.

Of course, the case $A = R$ is of particular interest.

# 2. Tensored and cotensored categories of structured spectra

As in I§1, consider the categories $\mathcal { P }$ and $\mathcal { S }$ of prespectra and spectra indexed on a universe $U$ . It was proven in [38, p.17-18] that these categories are topologically enriched, in the sense that their Hom sets are based topological spaces such that composition is continuous. For prespectra $D$ and $D ^ { \prime }$ , $\mathcal { P } ( D , D ^ { \prime } )$ is topologized as a subspace of the product over indexing spaces $V$ of the function spaces $F ( D V , D ^ { \prime } V )$ . Since maps between spectra are just maps between their underlying prespectra, this fixes the topology on $\mathcal { S } ( E , E ^ { \prime } )$ . It was also observed in [38, p.18] that all of the functors introduced in that volume are continuous and all of the adjunctions proven in it are given by homeomorphisms of Hom sets.

For example, by [38, I.3.3], there are natural homeomorphisms

$$
\mathcal { S } ( E \wedge X , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { T } ( X , \mathcal { S } ( E , E ^ { \prime } ) ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( E , F ( X , E ^ { \prime } ) )
$$

for spaces $X$ and spectra $\boldsymbol { E }$ and $E ^ { \prime }$ , where $\mathcal { T }$ denotes the category of based spaces. In categorical language [33, $\ S 3 . 7 ]$ , (2.1) states that $\mathcal { S }$ is tensored with tensors $E \wedge X$ and cotensored with cotensors $F ( X , E )$ . Adjoining disjoint basepoints to unbased spaces $X$ , we obtain similar homeomorphisms involving the category $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { U } }$ of unbased spaces. We give a formal definition in the unbased context.

DEfiniTioN 2.2. Let $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ be a category enriched over the category $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { U } }$ of unbased spaces. Then $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ is tensored if there is a functor $\otimes _ { \mathcal { E } } : \mathcal { E } \times \mathcal { U } \longrightarrow \mathcal { E }$ , continuous in both variables, together with a natural homeomorphism

$$
{ \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ } ( E \otimes _ { { \mathcal { E } } } X , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong { \mathcal { U } } ( X , { \mathcal { E } } ^ { } ( E , E ^ { \prime } ) )
$$

for spaces $X$ and objects $\boldsymbol { E }$ and $E ^ { \prime }$ of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ We write $\otimes$ for $\otimes _ { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } }$ when $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ is clear from the context. Dually, $\pmb { \mathcal { E } }$ is cotensored if there is a functor $F _ { \mathcal { E } } : \mathcal { U } ^ { o p } \times \mathcal { E } \longrightarrow \mathcal { E }$ continuous in both variables, together with a natural homeomorphism

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathcal { U } ( X , \mathcal { \hat { \epsilon } } ( E , E ^ { \prime } ) ) \cong \mathcal { \hat { \epsilon } } ( E , F _ { \mathcal { \hat { \epsilon } } } ( X , E ^ { \prime } ) ) . } \end{array}
$$

As in the motivating example (2.1), $F _ { \delta }$ will always admit an explicit description. The tensors are more interesting and less familiar. We will give a way of describing them for many spaces $X$ in the next section.

Again, by the argument illustrated in [38, p.18-19], colimits and limits of spectra are continuous (a better term would be topological). This means that the isomorphisms

$$
\mathcal { S } ( \cos E _ { i } , F ) \cong \operatorname* { l i m } \mathcal { S } ( E _ { i } , F )
$$

and

$$
\mathcal { S } ( F , \operatorname* { l i m } E _ { i } ) \cong \operatorname* { l i m } \mathcal { S } ( F , E _ { i } )
$$

are homeomorphisms.

The continuity can also be deduced categorically. There are valuable general notions of indexed colimits and limits in enriched categories, which are defined and discussed in Kelly [33, §3.1]. Indexed colimits include tensors with spaces and continuous colimits as special cases, and dually for limits. We shall not repeat the general definition, since we shall not have occasion to use it, and we shall rely on the following result of Kelly [33, 3.69-3.73] to deduce the existence of indexed colimits and limits.

DEfinitioN 2.5. A category $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ enriched over $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { U } }$ is topologically cocomplete if it has all indexed colimits and topologically complete if it has all indexed limits.

THEOREM 2.6 (KELLY). Let $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ be a category enriched over the category of based or unbased spaces. Then $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ is topologically cocomplete if it is cocomplete and admits tensor products and is topologically complete if it is complete and admits cotensor products. In particular, the given colimits and limits are continuous.

Our various categories of structured ring, module, and algebra spectra inherit subspace topologies on their Hom sets. Thus they are all topologically enriched. All of the functors and adjunctions that we have constructed in this paper are continuous, by the cited arguments of [38, p.18-19]. We claim that our various categories of rings, modules, and algebras are topologically cocomplete and complete.

For modules, this is immediate from II.1.4, III.1.1, and inspection. If $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an $S$ algebra, $M$ is an $R _ { ☉ }$ module, and $X$ is a based space, then

$$
{ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M \wedge X , M ^ { \prime } ) \cong { \mathcal { T } } ( X , { \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M , M ^ { \prime } ) ) \cong { \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( M , S \wedge _ { { \mathcal { L } } } F ( X , M ^ { \prime } ) ) .
$$

We deduce the first isomorphism from the first isomorphism of (2.1) by first writing $\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( M , M ^ { \prime } )$ as the equalizer of a pair of maps $\mathcal { S } ( M , M ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } ( \mathbb { L } M , M ^ { \prime } )$ and then writing $\mathcal { M } _ { R } ( M , M ^ { \prime } )$ as the equalizer of a pair of maps $\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( M , M ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow$ $\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( R \land _ { S } M , M ^ { \prime } )$ . We deduce the second isomorphism from the first by use of the isomorphisms

$$
M \wedge X \cong M \wedge _ { S } \Sigma ^ { \infty } X \quad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \quad S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F ( X , M ) \cong F _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } X , M ) .
$$

PRopOsiTION 2.8. For any $S$ -algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ , $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ is topologically cocomplete and complete. Its tensors $M \wedge X$ and all other indexed colimits are created in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ or, equivalently, in $\mathcal { S }$ . Its cotensors $F _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } X , M )$ and all other indexed limits are created in $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ or, equivalently, by applying the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to indexed limits created in $\mathcal { S }$ .

Now consider the categories of $R _ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ -algebras and of commutative $R \mathrm { . }$ algebras. We agree to denote these categories by $\mathcal { A } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { C } \mathcal { A } _ { R }$ , respectively. We must enrich these categories over $\boldsymbol { \mathcal U }$ , since there are no "trivial maps" to take as basepoints of Hom sets. We have already observed in $\mathrm { I I } \ S 7$ that the categories $\mathcal { A } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { C A } _ { R }$ are complete and cocomplete. Continuing that discussion, we obtain the following result. The proof works equally well in the categories of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, where the result is due to Hopkins and McClure [32] and, in the $E _ { \infty }$ case, is the main technical result of McClure, Schwänzl, and Vogt [54, Thm A].

THeoReM 2.9. For any commutative $S$ -algebra $R$ , the categories $\mathcal { A } _ { R }$ of $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras and $\mathcal { C } \mathcal { A } _ { R }$ of commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras are topologically cocomplete and complete. Their cotensors and all other indexed limits are created in ${ \mathcal { M } } _ { S }$ or, equivalently, by applying the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to indexed limits created in $\mathcal { S }$ .

PROOF. By II.7.1 (compare II.4.5), we have monads $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ in the category of $R$ -modules whose algebras are the $R$ -algebras and commutative $R$ algebras, and these monads are continuous (e.g., by inspection when $R \ : = \ : S$ and use of Proposition 1.4). Now II.7.2 and II.7.4 apply to show that $\mathcal { A } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { C } \mathcal { A } _ { R }$ are cocomplete. In the commutative case, the construction of colimits is quite simple since Proposition 1.6 gives coproducts and pushouts, and it is trivial to construct coequalizers from them. Moreover, by an easy bootstrap argument from the continuity of colimits in the ground category of spectra, coequalizers in $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { A } } _ { R }$ and $\mathcal { C } \mathcal { A } _ { R }$ are continuous. Now II.7.2 and the following categorical result complete the proof.

PROPOsITION 2.10. Let $\mathbb { T } : \mathcal { C } \longrightarrow \mathcal { C }$ be a continuous monad defined on a topologically enriched category $\mathscr { C }$ and let $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ be the category of algebras over T. Assume that $\mathscr { C }$ is topologically cocomplete and complete.

(i) The forgetful functor $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { C }$ creates all indexed limits. (ii) If T preserves reflexive coequalizers, then $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ has all indexed

ProoF. Part (i) is the enriched version of [43, VI.2, Ex 2]. Our version of (ii) seems to be new, although part of the proof is due to Hopkins [32]. Reflexive coequalizers are defined in II.6.5. By II.7.4, we know that $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ is cocomplete in the ordinary sense. By Kelly's theorem (Theorem 2.6), we need only construct tensors in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ . Thus let $( C , \xi )$ be a $\mathbb { T }$ algebra and $X$ be a space. Let $C \otimes X$ denote their tensor in $\mathscr { C }$ . Define $\nu : \mathbb { T } C \otimes X \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } ( C \otimes X )$ to be the adjoint of the composite map of spaces

$$
X \longrightarrow { \mathcal { C } } ( C , C \otimes X ) \xrightarrow { \mathbb { T } } \{ \mathbb { T } C , \mathbb { T } ( C \otimes X ) ) ,
$$

where the first arrow is adjoint to the identity map $C \otimes X \longrightarrow C \otimes X$ . Define $C \otimes _ { \mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ] } X$ to be the coequalizer in $\mathscr { C }$ displayed in the following diagram:

$$
\mathbb { T } ( \mathbb { T } C \otimes X ) \frac { \mathbb { T } ( \xi \otimes \mathrm { i d } ) } { \mu \circ \mathbb { T } \nu } \mathbb { T } ( C \otimes X ) { \longrightarrow } C \otimes _ { \mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ] } X .
$$

Clearly the parallel arrows are both maps of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras. We claim that this diagram is a reflexive coequalizer in $\mathscr { C }$ It will follow from II.6.6 that the diagram is a coequalizer in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ . We check the adjunction homeomorphism

$$
{ \mathcal { C } } [ \mathbb { T } ] ( C \otimes _ { \mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ] } X , C ^ { \prime } ) \cong { \mathcal { U } } ( X , { \mathcal { C } } [ \mathbb { T } ] ( C , C ^ { \prime } ) )
$$

required of a tensor by using the fact that $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ] ( C , C ^ { \prime } )$ is the equalizer in $\boldsymbol { \mathcal U }$ of

$$
\mathcal { C } ( C , C ^ { \prime } ) \xrightarrow { \mathcal { C } ( \xi , \mathrm { i d } ) } \mathcal { C } ( \mathbb { T } C , C ^ { \prime } )
$$

and

$$
\mathscr { C } ( C , C ^ { \prime } ) \xrightarrow { \mathbb { T } } \mathscr { C } ( \mathbb { T } C , \mathbb { T } C ^ { \prime } ) \xrightarrow { \mathscr { C } ( \mathrm { i d } , \xi ^ { \prime } ) } \mathscr { C } ( \mathbb { T } C , C ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

To see that the displayed coequalizer is reflexive, as claimed, consider the map

$$
\mathbb { T } ( \eta \otimes \mathrm { i d } ) : \mathbb { T } ( C \otimes X ) \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } ( \mathbb { T } C \otimes X ) .
$$

Clearly $\mathbb { T } ( \xi \otimes \mathrm { i d } ) \circ \mathbb { T } ( \eta \otimes \mathrm { i d } ) = \mathrm { i d }$ Less obviously, $\mu \circ \mathbb { T } \nu \circ \mathbb { T } ( \eta \otimes \mathrm { i d } ) = \mathrm { i d }$ This follows by adjunction from the commutative diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { X \xrightarrow { \tilde { \eta } } \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \quad \quad \quad  \\ &  \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \ \end{array}
$$

Here the commutativity of the upper left triangle defines the adjoint $\tilde { \eta }$ and the lower right triangle commutes by the naturality, $\eta \circ f = \mathbb { T } f \circ \eta$ , of $\eta$ on maps $f : C \longrightarrow C \otimes X$ .Thus $\mathbb { T } ( \eta \otimes \mathrm { i d } )$ gives the required reflection.

In particular, $F _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } X _ { + } , A )$ is the cotensor of a space $X$ and an $R .$ algebra or commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebra $A$ The diagonal on $X$ and the product on $A$ induce the product on $F _ { S } ( \Sigma ^ { \infty } X _ { + } , A )$ .
