The following instance of a general categorical observation explains the relationship between the smash product $A \wedge X _ { + }$ in the category of $R$ -modules and the tensor $A \otimes X$ in the category of $R$ -algebras or commutative $\scriptstyle { R } .$ algebras.

PROPOSITION 2.11. For $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras $A$ and spaces $X$ there is a natural map of R-modules

$$
\omega : A \land X _ { + } \longrightarrow A \otimes X
$$

such that $\omega$ is the canonical isomorphism if $X = \{ * \}$ and the following transitivity diagrams commute:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { ( A \wedge X _ { + } ) \wedge Y _ { + } \frac { \omega \wedge i \mathrm { d } } { \omega } ( A \otimes X ) \wedge Y _ { + } \xrightarrow { \omega } ( A \otimes X ) \otimes Y } } \\ & { } &  \overset { \mathrm { \scriptsize ~ \cong ~ } } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset { \quad } { \ v { \ = } } \overset  \quad \ v  \end{array}
$$

For $x \in X$ , let $i _ { x } : A \longrightarrow A \land X _ { + }$ be the map induced by the inclusion $\{ x \} _ { + } \longrightarrow$ $X _ { + }$ . $A$ map $f : A \land X _ { + } \longrightarrow B$ of spectra into an $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebra $B$ such that each composite $f \circ i _ { x } : A \longrightarrow B$ is a map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras uniquely determines a map of $R$ -algebras ${ \tilde { f } } : A \otimes X \longrightarrow B$ such that $f = \tilde { \ b { f } } \circ \omega$ . The same statement holds for commutative $R$ algebras.

PROOF. We have a natural map

$$
\mathcal { A } _ { R } ( A \otimes X , B ) \cong \mathcal { U } ( X , \mathcal { A } _ { R } ( A , B ) ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { U } ( X , \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( A , B ) ) \cong \mathcal { M } _ { R } ( A \wedge X _ { + } , B ) ,
$$

and $\omega$ is the image of the identity map of $A \otimes X$ . The rest is easy diagram chasing, using the natural map ${ \mathcal { M } } _ { R } ( A \wedge X _ { + } , B ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { S } } ( A \wedge X _ { + } , B )$ for the last statement.

REMARK 2.12. For $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras $A$ and $B$ , the previous result says that a map $A \otimes X \longrightarrow B$ of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras determines and is determined by a map $A \land X _ { + } \longrightarrow B$ of spectra that is pointwise a map of $R$ -algebras. A similar construction and result apply whenever one has a tensored category $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ with a continuous forgetful functor to spectra. For objects $A$ and $B$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { b } }$ , we define a homotopy to be a map $h : A \otimes I \longrightarrow B$ . Then $h$ is induced by a homotopy $A \land I _ { + } \longrightarrow B$ through maps in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ .

# 3. Geometric realization and calculations of tensors

To prepare for our construction of model structures and our study of thh, we explain how to calculate tensors $E \otimes X$ for certain spaces $X$ , and we use this calculation to study pushouts and cofibrations in the context of $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras. Our main tool is geometric realization, and the reader is urged to read the first two sections of Chapter $\mathbf { X }$ , which give a down to earth study of the geometric realization of simplicial spectra, before reading this section.

Fix a topologically complete and cocomplete category $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ with a continuous forgetful functor to spectra. We have the notion of a simplicial object $\pmb { E } _ { \ast }$ in $\pmb { \delta } ^ { \circ }$ .There are two notions of the geometric realization of such an object. We can first forget down to the category of simplicial spectra and take the geometric realization $| E _ { * } |$ there, or we can rework the definition and carry out the construction entirely in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ , obtaining the internal geometric realization $| E _ { * } | _ { \mathcal { E } }$ . Explicitly, $| E _ { * } | _ { \mathcal { E } }$ is the coend

$$
| E _ { * } | _ { \mathcal { E } } = \int ^ { \Delta } E _ { q } \otimes _ { \mathcal { E } } \Delta _ { q } .
$$

The following relationships between these two kinds of geometric realization generalize and clarify observations of McClure, Schwänzl, and Vogt [54, 4.3, 4.4] about the category of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra. We defer the proofs to the end of the section.

PROPOSITION 3.2. Let $X _ { * }$ be a simplicial space and let $A \in { \mathcal { E } }$ . Then there is a natural isomorphism

$$
A \otimes _ { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } | X _ { * } | \cong | A \otimes _ { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } X _ { * } | _ { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } ,
$$

of objects of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$

The realization of underlying simplicial spectra is more amenable to homotopical analysis than the internal realization. In favorable cases, the realization $| E _ { * } |$ will again be an object of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ , but this is not formal. We shall prove in $\mathbf { X } \ S \boldsymbol { 1 }$ that this holds for all of the categories of interest to us. In such cases, the two geometric realizations are isomorphic. In particular, the following result holds.

PROPOSITION 3.3. Let $R$ be any commutative $s$ -algebra, such as $R = S$ .For simplicial $R$ -algebras $A _ { * }$ , there is a natural isomorphism of $R$ algebras

$$
| A _ { * } | \cong | A _ { * } | _ { \mathcal { A } _ { R } } ,
$$

and similarly for simplicial commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras.

COroLLAry 3.4. For $R$ -algebras $A$ and simplicial spaces $X _ { * }$ , there is a natural isomorphism of $R$ -algebras

$$
A \otimes _ { { \mathcal { A } } _ { R } } | X _ { * } | \cong | A \otimes _ { { \mathcal { A } } _ { R } } X _ { * } | ,
$$

and similarly for commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras.

In the following discussion, we let $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ denote either $\mathcal { A } _ { R }$ or $\mathcal { C } \mathcal { A } _ { R }$ and write $\otimes$ for $\otimes _ { \pmb { \mathscr { s } } }$ . We use the term $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra in either case. The computation of $A \otimes | X _ { * } |$ just given applies particularly effectively to simplicial sets $X _ { * }$ , regarded as discrete simplicial spaces. We have a categorical coproduct $\amalg$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ . This is $\wedge _ { R }$ in the commutative case, but it is the "free product" in the non-commutative case. In the commutative case, the codiagonal map $\nabla : A \amalg A \longrightarrow A$ is the product on $A$ . In both cases, the unit $\eta : R \longrightarrow A$ is the unique map from the initial object. Since a discrete set $\underline { n }$ with $_ n$ points is the coproduct of its elements and the functor $A \otimes ( - )$ preserves coproducts, $A \otimes \underline { { n } }$ is the coproduct of $_ n$ copies of $A$ . To calculate $A \otimes | X _ { * } |$ , we need only identify the induced face and degeneracy operators on coproducts of copies of $A$ in terms of the structure maps $\nabla$ and $\eta$ .

In order to understand homotopy theory in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ , we need to understand $A \otimes I$ . We shall describe it in terms of a bar construction that is defined on $R .$ algebras. Recall that we defined the bar construction $B ( M , R , N )$ for a commutative $S .$ . algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules $M$ and $N$ in IV.7.2. We shall later use the evident generalization in which we replace $R$ and its modules by a commutative $R .$ . algebra $A$ and its modules. We here introduce a variant that applies equally well to either commutative or non-commutative $R _ { ☉ }$ -algebras. In the commutative case, it is just the specialization of the cited generalization in which the given $A$ -modules are restricted to be commutative $A { \mathrm { . } }$ algebras.

DEfinitioN 3.5. Let $A$ be an $R$ -algebra, and let $f : A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ and $g : A \longrightarrow$ $A ^ { \prime \prime }$ be maps of $R$ -algebras. These maps and the identity maps of $A ^ { \prime }$ and $A ^ { \prime \prime }$ determine maps of $R$ algebras

$$
\mu : A ^ { \prime } \amalg A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime } \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad \nu : A \amalg A ^ { \prime \prime } \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime \prime }
$$

Define a simplicial $R$ algebra $\beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ by replacing $\wedge _ { S }$ and $\phi$ by $\amalg$ and $\nabla$ in IV.7.2. Then define an $R$ algebra $\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ by

$$
\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) = | \beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) | .
$$

There is an evident natural map of $R$ algebras

$$
\psi : \beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }
$$

from the bar construction to the displayed pushout.

Define the double mapping cylinder $R$ algebra $M ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ by

$$
M ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) = A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } ( A \otimes I ) \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }
$$

and observe that the map $I \longrightarrow \{ p t \}$ induces a collapse map

$$
\psi : { \cal M } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime } .
$$

We have the following identification of these two constructions.

PROPOSITION 3.7. Let A be an $R$ -algebra with given maps to $R$ -algebras $A ^ { \prime }$ and $A ^ { \prime \prime }$ . Then there is a natural isomorphism

$$
\beta ^ { R } ( A , A , A ) \cong A \otimes I
$$

of $R$ -algebras over $A$ and under $A$ Ⅱ $A$ , and there is a natural isomorphism

$$
\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \cong M ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )
$$

of $R$ -algebras over $A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ and under $A ^ { \prime }$ Ⅱ $A ^ { \prime \prime }$ .

PROOF. Let ${ { I } _ { * } }$ be the standard simplicial 1-simplex with realization $\boldsymbol { I }$ It has $p + 2 p$ -simplices, and a simple comparison of its face and degeneracy operations (e.g., [44, p.14]) with those of the bar construction shows that we have a natural identification of simplicial $R$ algebras

$$
\beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( A , A , A ) \cong A \otimes I _ { * } .
$$

In fact, one can see this quite directly, since the only non-degenerate simplices of $I _ { * }$ are a 1-simplex $\Delta _ { 1 }$ and its faces, and similarly for $\beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( A , A , A )$ . The rest follows.

We use this to obtain a result about cofibrations that will be at the heart of our construction of model structures on $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ in Section 6. Let $\mathbb { T } : \mathcal { M } _ { R } \longrightarrow \mathcal { E } ^ { \circ }$ be the free $R _ { ☉ }$ -algebra functor; thus $\mathbb { T }$ must be interpreted as $\mathbb { P }$ in the commutative case. We shall prove in XII.2.3 that the functor $\mathbb { T }$ preserves cofibrations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Since $\mathbb { T }$ preserves tensors and pushouts and since $R = \mathbb { T } ( * )$ , we have

$$
\mathbb { T } C M \cong R \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } ( \mathbb { T } M \otimes I ) .
$$

Proposition 3.8. For an $R$ -module $M$ and a map of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras $\mathbb { T } M \longrightarrow A$ , the natural map of $R$ -algebras

$$
\psi : M ( \mathbb { T } C M , \mathbb { T } M , A ) \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } C M \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } A
$$

is homotopic rel $A$ to an isomorphism.

ProoF. For a based space $X$ , it is trivial to see that the map

$$
C X \cup _ { X } ( X \wedge I _ { + } ) \longrightarrow C X
$$

that retracts the cylinder onto the base of the cone is homotopic to a homeomorphism. Working in the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, the same argument works with $X$ replaced by $M$ Applying the functor $\mathbb { T }$ , the cited map then becomes the map

$$
\rho : R \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } \left( \mathbb { T } M \otimes I \right) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } \left( \mathbb { T } M \otimes I \right) \longrightarrow R \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } \left( \mathbb { T } M \otimes I \right)
$$

that retracts the second copy of $\mathbb { T } M \otimes I$ onto the base of the first. We have

$$
M ( \mathbb { T } C M , \mathbb { T } M , A ) \cong R \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } ( \mathbb { T } M \otimes I ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } ( \mathbb { T } M \otimes I ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } M } A ,
$$

and $\psi$ is obtained by applying the functor $\left( - \right) \operatorname { I I } _ { \mathbb { T } M } A$ to $\rho$ The conclusion follows.

PRoposITioN 3.9. For any pushout diagram of $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras

$$
\begin{array} { c c } { { \mathbb { T } M \longrightarrow \ A } } \\ { { \downarrow } } \\ { { \mathbb { V } C M \longrightarrow B , } } \end{array}
$$

the map i is a cofibration of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and therefore of spectra.

Proor. The essential point is just that the unit map $\eta : R \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } C M$ is the inclusion of a wedge summand of $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } }$ -modules and a retract of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras. From this, we find that the induced map $A \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } C M$ Ⅱ $A$ of $R$ -algebras is also the inclusion of a wedge summand of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and a retract of $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras. By the previous lemma and proposition, the pushout is isomorphic under $A$ to the bar construction $\beta ^ { R } ( \mathbb { T } C M , \mathbb { T } M , A )$ . All of the degeneracy operators of $\beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( \mathbb { T } C M , \mathbb { T } M , A )$ are inclusion of wedge summands of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, and it follows that $\beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( \mathbb { T } C M , \mathbb { T } M , A )$ is proper in the sense of X.2.2. This implies that the map from the zero skeleton $\mathbb { T } C M \amalg A$ into $\beta ^ { R } ( \mathbb { T } C M , \mathbb { T } M , A )$ is a cofibration, and the conclusion follows.

We shall also need the following elementary complement.

LEMMA 3.10. Let $\left\{ A _ { i } \right\}$ be a sequence of maps of $R$ -algebras that are cofibrations of spectra. Then the underlying spectrum of the colimit of the sequence computed in the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras is the colimit of the sequence computed in the category of spectra.

ProoF. The colimit in the category of spectra computes the colimit in the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules and satisfies

$$
( \dim A _ { i } ) \wedge _ { R } ( \operatorname { c o l i m } A _ { i } ) \cong \operatorname { c o l i m } ( A _ { i } \wedge _ { R } A _ { i } ) .
$$

Therefore the spectrum level colimit inherits an $R$ -algebra structure from the $A _ { i }$ , and the universal property in the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras follows from the universal property in the category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules.

We must still prove Propositions 3.2 and 3.3. Let $s \mathcal { C }$ denote the category of simplicial objects in a category $\mathscr { C }$ .

Proof Of Proposition 3.2. For a space $Y$ , let $\mathcal { U } ( \Delta _ { * } , Y )$ be the evident simplicial space with $q$ -simplices $\mathcal { U } ( \Delta _ { q } , Y )$ . This functor of $Y$ is right adjoint to geometric realization,

$$
\mathcal { U } ( | X _ { * } | , Y ) \cong s \mathcal { U } ( X _ { * } , \mathcal { U } ( \Delta _ { * } , Y ) ) .
$$

Similarly, for an object $F$ of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ , let $F _ { \delta } ( \Delta _ { * } , F )$ be the evident simplicial object of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ with $q$ -simplices $F _ { \mathcal { E } } ( \Delta _ { q } , F )$ .This functor of $\boldsymbol { \mathsf { \Pi } } ^ { F }$ is right adjoint to the internal geometric realization,

$$
\delta ^ { \circ } ( | E _ { * } | _ { \mathcal { E } } , F ) \cong s \delta ^ { \circ } ( E _ { * } , F _ { \mathcal { E } } ( \Delta _ { * } , F ) ) .
$$

These adjunctions, together with tensor and cotensor adjunctions, give the chain of natural isomorphisms

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \mathcal { \bar { E } } ^ { { \circ } } ( E \otimes \varepsilon | X _ { * } | , F ) \cong \mathcal { U } ( | X _ { * } | , \mathcal { \bar { O } } ( E , F ) ) } & { } \\ { \ } & { \cong s \mathcal { U } ( X _ { * } , \mathcal { U } ( \Delta _ { * } , \mathcal { \bar { O } } ( E , F ) ) } \\ { \ } & { \cong s \mathcal { U } ( X _ { * } , \mathcal { \bar { O } } ( E , F _ { \mathcal { E } } ( \Delta _ { * } , F ) ) } \\ { \ } & { \cong s \mathcal { O } ^ { \circ } ( E \otimes \varepsilon ^ { \prime } ( E _ { * } , F _ { \mathcal { E } } ( \Delta _ { * } , F ) ) } \\ { \ } & { \cong \mathcal { O } ^ { \circ } ( | E \otimes \varepsilon ^ { \prime } ( E , F ) . } \end{array}
$$

The conclusion follows.

ProoF OF ProposiTioN 3.3. Our interest is in the examples $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } = \mathcal { A } _ { R }$ and $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ } = \pmb { \mathcal { C } } \pmb { \mathcal { A } } _ { R }$ , but the argument works more generally. In fact, it applies whenever realizations $| E _ { * } |$ inherit structure present in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ , with the induced structure "arising pointwise". To explain what this means, note that we have an adjunction like those of (3.11) and (3.12) for simplicial spectra $\kappa _ { * }$ and spectra $L$ , namely

$$
\mathcal { S } ( | K _ { * } | , L ) \cong s \mathcal { S } ( K _ { * } , F ( ( \Delta _ { * } ) _ { + } , L ) ) ,
$$

where $F ( ( \Delta _ { * } ) _ { + } , L )$ has $q$ -simplices $F ( ( \Delta _ { q } ) _ { + } , L )$ . Now let $E _ { * }$ be a simplicial object of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ and $F$ be an object of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ When $| E _ { * } |$ is again an object of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } }$ , we have the subspace

$$
\delta ^ { \circ } ( | E _ { * } | , F ) \subset \mathcal { S } ( | E _ { * } | , F )
$$

of maps in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ . We say that the induced structure on $| E _ { * } |$ arises pointwise if this subspace coincides under the adjunction (3.13) with the subspace of $\mathfrak { s s } \mathcal { S } ( E _ { * } , F ( ( \Delta _ { * } ) _ { + } , F ) )$ consisting of those points $f \ = \ \{ f _ { q } \}$ such that the adjoint $\tilde { f } _ { q } : E _ { q } \wedge ( \Delta _ { q } ) _ { + } \longrightarrow F$ of $f _ { q } : E _ { q } \longrightarrow F ( ( \Delta _ { q } ) _ { + } , F ) )$ restricts to a map $E _ { q } \longrightarrow F$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ on the copy of $E _ { q }$ in $E _ { q } \wedge ( \Delta _ { q } ) _ { + }$ determined by each point of $\Delta _ { q }$ . By Proposition 2.11 and Remark 2.12, such a map $\tilde { f } _ { q }$ extends uniquely to a map $\tilde { g _ { q } } : E _ { q } \otimes _ { \mathcal { E } } \Delta _ { q } \longrightarrow F$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ . In turn, under the tensor-cotensor adjunction, $\tilde { g _ { q } }$ corresponds to a map $g _ { q } : E _ { q } \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { E } } ( \Delta _ { q } , F )$ in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ The function $\{ f _ { q } \} \longrightarrow \{ g _ { q } \}$ determines an adjunction

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathcal { \delta } ^ { \circ } ( | E _ { * } | , F ) \cong s \mathcal { \delta } ^ { \circ } ( E _ { * } , F _ { \mathcal { E } } ( \Delta _ { * } , F ) ) . } \end{array}
$$

Comparison of (3.12) and (3.14) gives the conclusion. An alternative argument based on the properties of the monads $\mathbb { T }$ and $\mathbb { P }$ is also possible. The adjunctions above can be used to check that

$$
\mathbb { T } | A _ { * } | \cong | \mathbb { T } ( A _ { * } ) | _ { \mathcal { A } _ { R } } .
$$

The functor $\mathbb { T }$ commutes with $\big | \cdot \big |$ on simplicial $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules, the functor $| \cdot | _ { \mathcal { A } _ { R } }$ preserves coequalizers, and a comparison of coequalizer diagrams gives the result.

# 4. Model categories of ring, module, and algebra spectra

We shall prove that our various categories of structured spectra admit model structures. A more general, axiomatic, framework is possible; compare Blanc [5]. We assume familiarity with the language of model categories, by which we understand closed model categories in Quillen's original sense [57]. A good exposition is given in [18]. We explain our results in this section and prove them in the next.

In this paper, cofibrations and fibrations in any of our categories mean maps that satisfy the homotopy extension property (HEP) or covering homotopy property (CHP) in that category. Cofibrations in this sense will play a central role in the work of the next section. It is a pity that the language of model categories has, in the literature, been superimposed on the classical language, with resulting ambiguity. We shall use $q$ -cofibrations and $q \cdot$ -fibrations for the model theoretic terms.

In all of our model categories, the weak equivalences in the model sense will be those maps in the category which are weak equivalences of underlying spectra. We say that the weak equivalences are created in $\mathcal { S }$ .Observe that a retract of a weak equivalence is a weak equivalence. Recall that a $q$ -fibration or $q$ -cofibration in a model category is said to be acyclic if it is a weak equivalence.

Implicitly or explicitly, we must constantly think in terms of diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { l } { E \frac { \alpha  X } { \alpha } } \\ { \down _ { V } \underbrace { \mathcal { I } _ { \mathbf { \Phi } ^ { \prime } } } _ { \beta } \biggr \} p } \end{array}
$$

where the square is given to be commutative and we seek a lift $g$ that makes both triangles commute. We say that $_ i$ has the left lifting property (LLP) with respect to a class of morphisms $\mathcal { P }$ if there exists such a lift $g$ for any square in which $p \in \mathcal { P }$ . We say that $p$ satisfies the right lifting property (RLP) with respect to a class of morphisms $\mathcal { I }$ if there exists such a lift $g$ for any square in which $i \in \mathcal { I }$ .

For example, a Serre fibration of spectra is a map that satisfies the CHP with repect to the set of "cone spectra"

$$
\left\{ \left. \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n } \right| q \geq 0 \mathrm { a n d } n \geq 0 \right\} .
$$

This means that it is a map that satisfies the RLP with respect to the set of inclusions

$$
i _ { 0 } : \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n } \wedge I _ { + } .
$$

Again, a retract of a Serre fibration is a Serre fibration. The $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibrations in $\mathcal { S }$ will be the Serre fibrations.

The following definition will allow us to give succinct statements of our results.

DEFInITIoN 4.1. Let $\mathscr { C }$ be a model category with a forgetful functor to $\mathcal { S }$ that creates weak equivalences and let $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ be a category with a forgetful functor to $\mathscr { C }$ We say that $\mathscr { C }$ creates a model structure in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \diamond }$ if $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ is a model category whose weak equivalences are created in $\mathcal { S }$ and whose $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibrations are created in $\mathscr { C }$ That is, a map in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ is a $q$ -fibration if it is a $q$ -fibration when regarded as a map in $\mathscr { C }$ The $q$ -cofibrations in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ must then be those maps which satisfy the left lifting property with respect to the acyclic $q \mathrm { . }$ fibrations.

Our categories are enriched, and our model structures will reflect this. Quillen defined the notion of a simplicial model category in [57, II§2], and the appropriate topological analogue of his definition reads as follows.

DEfINITIoN 4.2. A model category $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ }$ is topological if it is topologically complete and cocomplete and if, for any $q$ -cofibration $i : E \longrightarrow F$ and $q$ -fibration $p : X \longrightarrow Y$ , the induced map

$$
( i ^ { * } , p _ { * } ) : \ell ^ { \circ } ( F , X ) \longrightarrow \ell ^ { \circ } ( E , X ) \times _ { \ell ^ { \circ } ( E , Y ) } \ell ^ { \circ } ( F , Y )
$$

is a Serre fibration of spaces which is acyclic if either $i$ or $p$ is acyclic.

THEOREM 4.4. The category $\mathcal { S }$ is a topological model category with respect to the weak equivalences and Serre fibrations. If $\mathbb { T } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S }$ is a continuous monad that preserves reflexive coequalizers and satisfies the "Cofibration Hypothesis", then $\mathcal { S }$ creates a topological model structure in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ .

We think of the first statement as the specialization to the identity monad of the second. We shall specify the "Cofibration Hypothesis" shortly. It will obviously be satisfied by the identity monad and by the monad $\mathbb { L }$ , and arguments like those of the previous section verify it for the monads $\mathbb { T L }$ and $\mathbb { P L }$ that define $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

COROLLARY 4.5. The categories of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra and of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra are topological model categories.

Of course, we are far more interested in our categories of modules and algebras. The crux of the proof of Theorem 4.4 is the adjunction

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { T } ] ( \mathbb { T } X , A ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( X , A )
$$

for spectra $X$ and $\mathbb { T } .$ algebras $A$ . By the adjunction, the $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibrations in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ are the maps that satisfy the RLP with respect to the set of inclusions

$$
\mathbb { T } i _ { 0 } : \mathbb { T } \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n } \wedge I _ { + } .
$$

That is, they satisfy the CHP with respect the set of "cone $\mathbb { T } .$ algebras" $\mathbb { T } \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n }$ These maps deserve to be called Serre fibrations of $\mathbb { T }$ algebras.

Similarly, we define a Serre fibration of $S$ -modules to be a map that satisfies the CHP with respect to the "cone $S$ modules" $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } C S ^ { n }$ .For $S _ { \mathbf { \partial } }$ modules, the adjunction above must be replaced by the adjunction

$$
\mathcal { M } _ { S } ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } X , M ) \cong \mathcal { S } ( X , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M ) )
$$

that we obtain by composing the first adjunction of II.2.2 with the freeness adjunction for the monad $\mathbb { L }$ . Thus, when interpreting Definition 4.1 for $S .$ . modules, we must change our forgetful functor from the obvious one to the functor $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , - )$ .Since $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M )$ is naturally weakly equivalent to $M$ , by I.8.7, the weak equivalences are unchanged. However, the $q$ -fibrations are changed.

THEOREM 4.6. The category $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ is a topological model category with weak equivalences created in $\mathcal { S }$ . Its $q$ -fibrations are the Serre fibrations of $S$ -modules, which are the maps $f : M \longrightarrow N$ of $S$ -modules such that

$$
F ( \mathrm { i d } , f ) : F _ { { \mathcal { L } } } ( S , M ) \longrightarrow F _ { { \mathcal { L } } } ( S , N )
$$

is a Serre fibration of spectra.

Although the functor $\mathbb { T } X = S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { \bot } X$ from spectra to $S$ -modules is not a monad, the proof of Theorem 4.4 nevertheless applies. To understand this, we think in terms of the "mirror image" category $\mathcal { M } ^ { S }$ of counital $\mathbb { L }$ spectra specified in II.2.1. By II.2.7 and composition (see II.6.1), we have a continuous monad $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , \mathbb { L } ( - ) )$ on $\mathcal { S }$ whose algebras are the counital L-spectra. We have a topological equivalence of categories $\mathcal { M } ^ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ that carries $N$ to $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ By II.2.5, $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , M )$ is naturally isomorphic to $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } M$ for any $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $M$ . Thus the monad that defines counital $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra is transported under the equivalence to the functor $\mathbb { T }$ relevant to the construction of the model structure on $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ . The equivalence has the effect of changing the forgetful functor.

The proof of Theorem 4.4 will apply equally well if we change our ground category to $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ .

TheoreM 4.7. If $\mathbb { T } : \mathcal { M } _ { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ is a continuous monad that preserves reflexive coequalizers and satisfies the "Cofibration Hypothesis", then $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ creates a topological model structure in $\mathcal { M } _ { S } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ .

Of course, the description of the $q$ -fibrations as maps $f$ such that $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , f )$ is a Serre fibration persists. Again, the Cofibration Hypothesis will be specified shortly and holds in our examples.

COROLLARY 4.8. The categories of $S$ -algebras, commutative $S$ -algebras, and modules over an $S$ -algebra $R$ are topological model categories.

Now that we have a model structure on $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ , we can generalize Theorem 4.7 by changing its ground category to $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ .

THEOREM 4.9. Let $R$ be a commutative $S$ -algebra. $l f \ \mathbb { T } : \ \mathcal { M } _ { R } \longrightarrow \ \mathcal { M } _ { R }$ is a continuous monad that preserves reflexive coequalizers and satisfies the "Cofibration Hypothesis", then $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ creates a topological model structure in $\mathcal { M } _ { R } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ .

CoROLLARY 4.10. The categories of algebras and commutative algebras over a commutative $S$ -algebra $R$ are topological model categories.

In fact, Theorems 4.7 and 4.9 both apply, and they give the same model structures since they give the same $q$ -fibrations and weak equivalences. We prefer to think of the model structure as created in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ , since that makes visible more information about the $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrations. While the model category theory dictates what the $q$ -cofibrations must be, the proofs of the theorems will lead to explicit descriptions.

DEFInITIoN 4.11. Let $\mathbb { T }$ be a monad in $\mathcal { S }$ as in Theorem 4.7. A relative cell $\mathbb { T }$ -algebra $Y$ under a $\mathbb { T }$ -algebra $X$ is a $\mathbb { T }$ -algebra $Y = \operatorname { c o l i m } Y _ { n }$ , where $Y _ { 0 } = X$ and $Y _ { n + 1 }$ is obtained from $Y _ { n }$ as the pushout of a sum of attaching maps $\mathbb { T } S ^ { q } \longrightarrow Y _ { n }$ along the coproduct of the natural maps $\mathbb { T } S ^ { q } \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } C S ^ { q }$ . When $X$ is an initial $\mathbb { T } .$ . algebra, we say that $Y$ is a cell $\mathbb { T }$ -algebra. Relative and absolute cell $\mathbb { T }$ algebras are defined in precisely the same way for a monad $\mathbb { T }$ in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ as in Theorem 4.9, except that the sphere spectra $S ^ { q }$ are replaced by the sphere $R .$ modules $S _ { R } ^ { q }$ .

REMARK 4.12. The functor $\mathbb { T } : \mathcal { S } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ , being a left adjoint, preserves coproducts. Thus, when attaching a coproduct of cells $\mathbb { T } C S ^ { q }$ to $Y _ { n }$ to obtain $Y _ { n + 1 }$ , we are considering a pushout in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ of the general form

![](images/99e789ff12b6393e56a7940c700653a42f11dc61d1ef111575957b297d579f6e.jpg)

where $\boldsymbol { E }$ is a wedge of spheres, and similarly when the ground category is $\mathcal { M } _ { S }$ or $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ .

The Cofibration Hypothesis is just the minimum condition necessary to obtain homotopical control over these pushout diagrams and their colimits. It holds in our examples by Proposition 3.9 and Lemma 3.10.

COFIBRaTION HyPOTHESIS. The map $_ i$ in any pushout of the form (4.13) is a cofibration of spectra (for Theorem 4.4) or of $S$ -modules (for Theorem 4.7) or of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules (for Theorem 4.9). The underlying spectrum of the $\mathbb { T } .$ algebra colimit of a sequence of cofibrations of $\mathbb { T } .$ -algebras is their colimit as a sequence of maps of spectra.

Actually, for the model structure in Theorems 4.7 and 4.9, we only need the maps $_ i$ to be cofibrations of spectra, or even just spacewise closed inclusions of spectra. However, the stronger $R$ -module cofibration condition holds in practice and is important in the applications.

THEOREM 4.14. Under the hypotheses of Theorems 4.4, 4.7, and 4.9, a map of T-algebras is a $q$ -cofibration if and only if it is a retract of a relative cell Talgebra. Moreover, any $q$ -cofibration is a cofibration of underlying spectra (in Theorem 4.4) or of underlying $s$ -modules (in Theorem 4.7) or of underlying $R$ -modules (in Theorem 4.9).

By the Cofibration Hypothesis, the second statement will follow from the first. In all of our categories of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras, the trivial spectrum is a terminal object and every $\mathbb { T }$ -algebra is $q$ -fibrant. By the previous result, a $\mathbb { T } .$ -algebra is $q$ -cofibrant if and only if it is a retract of a cell $\mathbb { T }$ -algebra. Note in particular that the unit $R \longrightarrow A$ of a $q$ -cofibrant $R \mathrm { . }$ -algebra or commutative $R$ -algebra is a cofibration of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules.

As in our discussion of Theorem 4.6, the proof of the previous theorem will apply to give the following expected conclusion.

THeOREM 4.15. For an $\boldsymbol { S }$ -algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ , such as $R = S$ , a map of $R$ -modules is a $q$ -cofibration if and only if it is a retract of a relative cell $R$ -module.

Thus, in the case of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, model category theory just brings us back to the cell theory that we took as our starting point. We can turn this around. We certainly want the weak equivalences and $q \mathrm { . }$ cofibrations in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ to be the weak equivalences of underlying spectra and the retracts of relative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules. Since the weak equivalences and $q$ -cofibrations determine the $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibrations, we see that the $q$ -fibrations specified in Theorem 4.6 are in fact forced on us by the cell theory that we began with.

Returning to the general context of Theorem 4.14, we also have that the natural notion of homotopy in any of our categories of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras, namely that discussed in Remark 2.12, agrees with the notion of homotopy that is dictated by our model category structures.

LEMMA 4.16. If $A$ is a $q$ -cofibrant T-algebra, then $A \otimes I$ is a cylinder object for $A$ in the sense of Quillen. That is, the folding map $\operatorname { i d } + \operatorname { i d } : A$ I $A \longrightarrow A$ factors as the composite of a $q$ -cofibration $A \amalg A \longrightarrow A \otimes I$ and a weak equivalence $A \otimes I \longrightarrow A$ .

# 5. The proofs of the model structure theorems

We must prove Theorems 4.4, 4.6, 4.7, 4.9, 4.14, and 4.15 and Lemma 4.16. For uniformity of treatment, let $\mathscr { C }$ be either $\mathcal { S }$ or $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ for a commutative $S .$ algebra $R$ Logically, of course, we should treat the case $R = S$ before going on to the general case. Let $\mathbb { T }$ be a continuous monad in $\mathscr { C }$ that preserves reflexive coequalizers. By Proposition 2.10, we already know that $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ is complete, cocomplete, tensored and cotensored, and indeed has all indexed limits and colimits. It is clear that if $g \circ f$ is defined and two of $f , g$ ,and $g \circ f$ are weak equivalences, then so is the third. It is also clear that the collections of $q$ -fibrations, $q$ -cofibrations, and weak equivalences are closed under composition and retracts and contain all isomorphisms. It remains to prove that arbitrary maps factor appropriately and that the $q$ -fibrations satisfy the right lifting property (RLP) with respect to the acyclic $q$ -cofibrations. The essential point is that Quillen's "small object argument" applies to construct the required factorizations. A general version of Quillen's original argument is given in [18, §6], and we shall give a modified version of that argument.

DEFInITIoN 5.1. For the purposes of this section, define a finite pair of spectra to be a pair of the form $( \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } B , \Sigma _ { q } ^ { \infty } A )$ , where $B$ is a finite based CW complex, $A$ is a subcomplex, and $q \geq 0$ : Define a finite pair of L-spectra to be a pair obtained by applying $\mathbb { L }$ to a finite pair of spectra. Define a finite pair of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules to be a pair obtained by applying $\mathbb { F } _ { R }$ to a finite pair of spectra.

As a matter of esoterica, we actually only need $A$ , not $B$ , to be finite in our arguments.

LEMMA 5.2. Let $\mathscr { F }$ be a set of maps in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ , each of which is of the form $\mathbb { T } E \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } F$ for some finite pair $( F , E )$ in $\mathscr { C }$ : Then any map $f : X \longrightarrow Y$ in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ factors as a composite

$$
X { \xrightarrow { i } } X ^ { \prime } { \xrightarrow { p } } Y ,
$$

where $p$ satisfies the RLP with respect to each map in $\mathscr { F }$ and i satisfies the LLP with respect to any map that satisfies the RLP with respect to each map in $\mathcal { F }$ .

PROOF. Let $X = X _ { 0 }$ . We construct a commutative diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } { X _ { 0 } \xrightarrow { i _ { 0 } } \ X _ { 1 } \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } } & { \cdots X _ { n } \xrightarrow { i _ { n } } X _ { n + 1 } \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad \quad } } & { \cdots \cdot } \\ { f = p _ { 0 } \Bigg \downarrow } & { { \Bigg \downarrow } p _ { 1 } } & { \quad \quad \quad } & { \Bigg \downarrow ^ { p _ { n } } } \\ { Y \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } } Y \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad \quad }  & { \cdots Y \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad } } & { Y \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad } } \end{array} \Bigg \downarrow . .  &  { }
$$

as follows. Suppose inductively that we have constructed ${ \mathfrak { p } } _ { n }$ . Consider all maps from a map in $\mathscr { F }$ to $p _ { n }$ . Each such map is a commutative diagram of the form

$$
\begin{array} { l } { \mathbb { T } E \xrightarrow { \alpha \xrightarrow { \alpha } \times X _ { n } } } \\ { \mathbb { \downarrow } _ { \mathbb { \Gamma } ^ { F } \xrightarrow { \alpha \xrightarrow { \alpha \xrightarrow { \alpha } \times X _ { n } } } \mathbb { \downarrow } _ { \mathbb { \Gamma } ^ { p _ { n } } } } } \\ { \mathbb { T } F \xrightarrow { \beta \xrightarrow { \alpha \xrightarrow { \beta \xrightarrow { \alpha } \times X _ { n } } } Y . } } \end{array}
$$

Summing over such diagrams, we construct a pushout diagram of the form

$$
\begin{array} { l }  \underset {  \begin{array} { c } { \mathrm { U } \mathbb { T } E  \cdots \times X _ { n } } \\ { \displaystyle \downarrow } \\ { \mathrm { U } \mathbb { T } F \longrightarrow X _ { n + 1 } . } \end{array}  } \end{array}
$$

The maps $\beta$ induce a map $p _ { n + 1 } : X _ { n + 1 } \longrightarrow Y$ such that $p _ { n + 1 } \circ i _ { n } = p _ { n }$ .Let $X ^ { \prime } = \operatorname { c o l i m } X _ { n }$ , let $i : X \longrightarrow X ^ { \prime }$ be the canonical map, and let $p : X ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow Y$ be obtained by passage to colimits from the $p _ { n }$ . Constructing lifts by passage to coproducts, pushouts, and colimits in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ , we see that each $i _ { n }$ and therefore also $_ i$ satisfies the LLP with respect to maps that satisfy the RLP with respect to maps in $\mathcal { F }$ Assume given a commutative square

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { \mathbb { T } E \frac { \alpha ^ { \prime } } { \beta } \hat {  } X ^ { \prime } } \\ & { } & { \underset { i \nmid \nmid \natop \mathbb { T } F } { \frac { g _ { \gamma } } { \beta } } \hat { \downarrow } p } \\ & { } & { \mathbb { T } F \hat {  } Y , } \end{array}
$$

where $_ i$ is in $\mathcal { F }$ To verify that $p$ satisfies the RLP with respect to $i$ , we must construct a map $g$ that makes the diagram commute. The Cofibration Hypothesis implies that $X ^ { \prime }$ is constructed as the colimit of a sequence of cofibrations of spectra. By [38, App.3.9], a cofibration of spectra is a spacewise closed inclusion.

Therefore, using that $\mathbb { T }$ is the free functor from $\mathscr { C }$ to $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ , we see by III.1.7 that the natural map

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { c o l i m } \ { \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { T } } ] ( { \mathbb { T } } E , X _ { n } ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { C } } [ { \mathbb { T } } ] ( { \mathbb { T } } E , X ^ { \prime } ) } \end{array}
$$

is a bijection. This ensures that $\alpha ^ { \prime } : \mathbb { T } E \longrightarrow X ^ { \prime }$ factors through some $X _ { n }$ , giving us one of the commutative squares (5.4) used in the construction of $X _ { n + 1 }$ . By construction, there is a map $\mathbb { T } F \longrightarrow X _ { n + 1 }$ whose composite with the natural map to $X ^ { \prime }$ gives a map $g$ as required.

LEMMA 5.6. Any map $f : X \longrightarrow Y$ in $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ factors as $p \circ i$ , where $_ i$ is an acyclic $q$ -cofibration that satisfies the $L L P$ with respect to any $q$ -fibration and $p$ is a $q$ -fibration.

PROOF. Let $\mathcal { F }$ be the set of pairs obtained by letting $( B , A )$ in Definition 5.1 run through all pairs of spaces $( C S ^ { n } \wedge I _ { + } , C S ^ { n } \wedge \{ 0 \} _ { + } )$ , $n \geq 0$ By chasing through adjunctions and using the definition of $q$ -fibrations and $q$ -cofibrations, we see that a map is a $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibration if and only if it satisfies the RLP with respect to every map in $\mathcal { F }$ and that every map in $\mathcal { F }$ is a $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibration, of course an acyclic one. Note the relevance of the first adjunction of II.2.2 when $\mathcal { C } = \mathcal { M } _ { S }$ : this is where the definition of $q$ -fibrations in Theorem 4.6 is forced on us. Now use Lemma 5.2 to factor $f$ Then that lemma says that $p$ is a $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibration and that $_ i$ satisfies the LLP with respect to all $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibrations. In particular, $_ i$ is a $q$ -cofibration. We use the cylinders $( - ) \otimes I$ to define homotopies in the category $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ , as discussed in Remark 2.12. Then the free functor $\mathbb { T }$ and the adjoint forgetful functor preserve homotopies. A formal argument shows that each $i _ { n }$ is the inclusion of a deformation retraction of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras, and it follows that $_ i$ is also a deformation retraction. Therefore $_ i$ is an acyclic $q$ cofibration.

LEMMA 5.7. The $q$ -fibrations satisfy the RLP with respect to the acyclic $q$ -- ibrations.

PRooF. This is formal. Let $f : E \longrightarrow F$ be any acyclic $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibration. We must show that $f$ satisfies the LLP with respect to $q$ -fibrations. By the previous lemma, we may factor $f$ as $f = p \circ i$ , where $i : E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ is an acyclic $q$ -cofibration that does satisfy the LLP with respect to $q$ fibrations and $p : E ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow F$ is a $q \cdot$ . fibration. Since $f$ and $_ i$ are weak equivalences, so is $\boldsymbol { p }$ Since $f$ satisfies the LLP with respect to acyclic $q$ -fibrations, there exists $g : F \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ such that $g \circ f = i$ and $p \circ g = \operatorname { i d } _ { F }$ . Clearly $p$ and $g$ , together with the identity map on $E$ , express $f$ as a retract of $_ i$ Since $i$ satisfies the LLP with respect to $q$ -fibrations, so does $f$ .

LEMMA 5.8. Any map $f : X \longrightarrow Y$ in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ factors as $p \circ i$ , where $i$ is a $q$ -cofibration and $p$ is an acyclic $q$ -fibration.

ProoF. This is another application of Lemma 5.2. Let $\mathcal { A } \mathcal { F }$ be the set of pairs obtained by letting $( B , A )$ in Definition 5.1 run through all pairs of spaces $( C S ^ { n } , S ^ { n } )$ , $n \geq 0$ . By tracing through adjunctions again, we see that a map of $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras is an acyclic $q$ -fibration if and only if it satisfies the RLP with respect to all maps in $\mathcal { A } \mathcal { F }$ and that each map in $\mathcal { A } \mathcal { F }$ is thus a $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibration. In the factorization $f = p \circ i$ that we now obtain from Lemma 5.2, that lemma says that $p$ is an acyclic $q$ -fibration and $i$ is a $q \mathrm { . }$ cofibration.

We must still prove that $\mathcal { C } [ \mathbb { T } ]$ is topological, in the sense of Definition 4.2. As in [57, SM7(a), p.II.2.3], the description of $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrations as retracts of relative cell $\mathbb { T }$ -algebras implies that we need only check that the map (4.3) is a Serre fibration when $i : E \longrightarrow F$ is in the set $\mathcal { F }$ defined in the proof of Lemma 5.6 and an acyclic Serre fibration when $i : E \longrightarrow F$ is in the set $\mathcal { A } \mathcal { F }$ defined in the proof of Lemma 5.8. The freeness adjunction for the monad $\mathbb { T }$ reduces this to the case of spectra or of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules, and further adjunctions then reduce it to its known space level analogue. This completes the proofs of Theorems 4.4, 4.6, 4.7, and 4.9.

PROOF OF THEoREM 4.14. As in the proof of Lemmas 5.2 and 5.8, a relative cell $\mathbb { T }$ algebra $E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ satisfies the LLP with respect to the acyclic $q \mathrm { . }$ -fibrations and is thus a $q$ -cofibration. Let $f : E \longrightarrow F$ be a $q$ -cofibration. The proof of Lemma 5.8 gives a factorization of $f$ as the composite of a relative cell $\mathbb { T }$ algebra $i : E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ and an acyclic $q$ -fibration $p : E ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow F$ As in the proof of Lemma 5.7, there exists $g : F \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ such that $g \circ f = i$ and $p \circ g = \operatorname { i d } _ { F }$ , and $p$ and $g$ express $f$ as a retract of $i$

PrOOF OF LEMMA 4.16. We may assume without loss of generality that $A$ is a cell $\mathbb { T }$ algebra. Write $A = \mathsf { c o l i m } A _ { n }$ , where $A _ { 0 } = \mathbb { T } ( \ast )$ and, for $n > 0$ , ${ \cal A } _ { n } = \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } ( E _ { n } ) } { \cal A } _ { n - 1 }$ for some wedge $E _ { n }$ of sphere objects of $\mathscr { C }$ Let $B _ { n } = A \amalg _ { A _ { n } } \left( A _ { n } \otimes I \right) \amalg _ { A _ { n } } A$ Since the functor $( - ) \otimes I$ commutes with colimits, we have

$$
A \otimes I \cong A \amalg _ { A } \left( A \otimes I \right) \amalg _ { A } A \cong \operatorname { c o l i m } B _ { n } .
$$

Clearly $B _ { 0 } \cong A \amalg A$ We have $\mathbb { T } ( E _ { n } \wedge I _ { + } ) \cong \mathbb { T } E _ { n } \otimes I$ and, for $n > 0 , B _ { n }$ is constructed from $B _ { n - 1 }$ by a pushout diagram of the form

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \mathbb { T } ( E _ { n } \wedge I _ { + } \cup C E _ { n } \wedge ( \partial I ) _ { + } ) \longrightarrow B _ { n - 1 } } } \\ { { \Big \downarrow } } \\ { { \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } \wedge I _ { + } ) \longrightarrow B _ { n } } } \end{array}
$$

It follows that $A \otimes I$ is the colimit of a sequence of inclusions of cell $\mathbb { T } .$ algebras relative to $A$ Ⅱ $A$ . The canonical weak equivalences $A _ { n } \otimes I \longrightarrow A _ { n }$ induce the canonical weak equivalence $A \otimes I \longrightarrow A$ on passage to colimits.

The reader should be convinced that the construction of model structures is a nearly formal consequence of the monadic descriptions of our various notions of structured ring, module, and algebra spectra.

REMARK 5.9. In [35], categories of $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ $k \mathrm { . }$ -algebras and their modules were defined, and derived categories of modules were constructed, using a cell theory based on "sphere and cone modules". Replacing the ground category $\mathcal { S }$ with the ground category $\mathcal { M } _ { k }$ of differential graded $k$ -modules, the arguments of this section apply to give model structures to the analogous algebraic categories of modules. Unlike the treatments in [57, 5, 18], with this approach there is not the slightest reason to restrict attention to bounded below $k$ -modules.

# 6. The underlying $R$ -modules of $q \mathrm { . }$ cofibrant $R$ algebras

Let $R$ be a fixed $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrant commutative $S .$ -algebra. We study the underlying $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules of $q$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebras and commutative $R \mathrm { . }$ algebras. The main point is to prove that the point-set level iterated smash products of $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebras represent their smash product in the derived category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ .

We begin with the simpler non-commutative case, and we do not need $R$ to be $q$ -cofibrant in the following two results. Recall from III.7.3 that smash products of cell $R$ -modules are cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules.

PROPOSITION 6.1. Let $A$ and $B$ be $R$ -algebras that are cell $R$ -modules relative to $\scriptstyle { R }$ . Then their coproduct All $B$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module relative to $R$ . In more detail, $A$ Ⅱ $B$ is the colimit of an expanding sequence of relative cell $R$ -modules $\left\{ C _ { n } \right\}$ such that $C _ { 0 } = R$ and, for $n \geq 1$ , $C _ { n } / C _ { n - 1 }$ is the wedge of the two monomial word modules of length $_ n$ in $A / R$ and $B / R$ .

Proor. The monomial word modules in $R$ -modules $M$ and $N$ are the smash products

$$
M \wedge _ { R } N \wedge _ { R } M \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \qquad \mathrm { a n d } \qquad N \wedge _ { R } M \wedge _ { R } N \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \ .
$$

By II.7.4, we see that $A$ I $B$ is constructed via a coequalizer diagram in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$

$$
\mathbb { T } ( \mathbb { T } A \lor \mathbb { T } B ) { \underset {  } { \longrightarrow } } \mathbb { T } ( A \lor B ) { \underset {  } { \longrightarrow } } A \amalg B .
$$

Writing out the source and target of the pair of parallel arrows as wedges of smash products and restricting to those wedge summands with at most $n$ smash factors, we define $C _ { n }$ to be the coequalizer of the resulting restricted parallel pair of arrows. Clearly there result compatible maps $C _ { n } \longrightarrow C _ { n + 1 }$ and $C _ { n } \longrightarrow A \amalg B$ such that $A \amalg B$ is the colimit of the $C _ { n }$ . In view of the use of the action maps $\mathbb { T } A \longrightarrow A$ and $\mathbb { T } B \longrightarrow B$ in II.7.4, we see that the wedge of the monomial words in $A$ and $B$ of length at most $_ n$ maps onto $C _ { n }$ . That is, elements of word monomials involving $A \land _ { R } A$ or $B \land _ { R } B$ are identified in the coequalizer with elements of word monomials of lower length.

Let $U _ { n }$ be the coproduct in the category of $R$ -modules under $\scriptstyle { R }$ of the two monomial words in $A$ and $B$ of length $_ n$ , so that the copies of $R$ in these $R \mathrm { . }$ . modules under $\scriptstyle { R }$ are identified. Then $U _ { n }$ is a relative cell $R \mathrm { . }$ module. Let $V _ { n }$ be the union of the subcomplexes of $U _ { n }$ that are obtained by replacing any one $A$ or $B$ in either of the monomial words by its submodule $R$ The isomorphisms $R \land _ { R } A \longrightarrow A$ and $R \land _ { R } B \longrightarrow B$ induce a map $V _ { n } \longrightarrow C _ { n }$ Inspection of the restricted coequalizer diagrams (and comparison with algebra for intuition) shows that there result pushout diagrams of $R$ -modules

$$
\begin{array} { c c c } { { V _ { n } \longrightarrow C _ { n } } } & { { } } & { { } } \\ { { { \Bigg \downarrow } } } & { { { } } } & { { { \Bigg \downarrow } } } \\ { { U _ { n + 1 } \longrightarrow C _ { n + 1 } . } } & { { { } } } & { { { } } } \end{array}
$$

Inductively, the $C _ { n }$ and $A \amalg B$ are cell $R$ -modules relative to $R$ .

THeOreM 6.2. If $A$ is a $q$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebra, then $A$ is a retract of a cell $R$ -module relative to $R$ .Thus the unit $R \longrightarrow A$ is a $q$ -cofibration of $R$ -modules.

Proof. If $M$ is a cell $R .$ -module, then $M ^ { j }$ is a cell $R$ -module for $j \geq 1$ and $( \mathbb { T } M , R )$ is a relative cell $R$ -module. Moreover, since $M \longrightarrow C M$ is cellular, $\mathbb { T } M \longrightarrow \mathbb { T } C M$ is the inclusion of a subcomplex in a relative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. Now suppose that $( A , R )$ is a relative cell $R$ -module and that we have a pushout diagram of $R \mathrm { . }$ -algebras

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { T } M \longrightarrow \ A } \\ { \Big \downarrow } \\ { \mathbb { T } C M \longrightarrow B . } \end{array}
$$

As in the proof of Proposition 3.9, $B$ is isomorphic to the geometric realization of a simplicial $R _ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ -module that is proper because its degeneracies are given by inclusions of wedge summands. The previous proposition implies that its $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module of $p$ -simplices is a cell $R$ -module relative to $R$ Moreover, the face and degeneracy maps are sequentially cellular. Therefore, by X.2.7, $( B , R )$ is isomorphic to a relative cell $R$ module, and $A$ is a subcomplex. By passage to colimits, any cell $R _ { \mathbf { \nu } }$ -algebra is a relative cell $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -module. The conclusion follows from Theorem 4.14.

In the commutative case, the argument fails because we must pass to orbits over actions of symmetric groups. Tracing the proof of III.7.3 back to that of I.6.1, we see that it depends on the homeomorphism $\mathcal { L } ( j ) \cong \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ induced by a linear isomorphism $f : U ^ { j } \longrightarrow U$ Since this homeomorphism is not $\Sigma _ { j }$ . equivariant, we cannot deduce that symmetric powers of CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra are, or even have the homotopy types of, CW L-spectra, although they do have the homotopy types of CW spectra. For this reason, we cannot conclude that the symmetric power $M ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j }$ of a cell $R$ -module $M$ has the homotopy type of a cell $R$ -module; we refer the reader to III.5.1 for an analysis of the homotopy type of its underlying spectrum. We get around this problem by use of the following result, which gives canonical CW $S .$ -module approximations of smash products of "extended powers" of CW spectra. Here the $j$ th extended power of $X$ is defined to be

$$
D _ { j } X = ( \mathbb { L } X ) ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j } \cong { \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { j } } X ^ { j } .
$$

We adopt the convention that $D _ { 0 } X = S$ .

THEOREM 6.3. Let $\{ X _ { 1 } , \dots , X _ { n } \}$ be $C W$ spectra, let $j _ { i } \geq 0$ , and consider the following commutative diagram of L-spectra:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \bigwedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } D _ { j _ { i } } X _ { i } ) \xrightarrow { \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \lambda } \bigwedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } D _ { j _ { i } } X _ { i } } \\ & { \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \xi ) \Bigg \downarrow } \\ & { \bigwedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } D _ { j _ { i } } X _ { i } ) \xrightarrow [ \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \lambda ] { \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } \lambda } X _ { i } . } \end{array}
$$

All spectra in the diagram have the homotopy types of $C W$ -spectra, all maps in the diagram are homotopy equivalences of spectra, and $\land s ( S \land \mathcal { L } \mathbb { L } D _ { j _ { i } } X _ { i } )$ has the homotopy type of a CW $S$ -module.

PROOF. By [38, VI.5.2 or VIII.2.4], $X ^ { j }$ has the homotopy type of a $\Sigma _ { j }$ -CW spectrum indexed on $U ^ { j }$ By XI.1.7, $\mathcal { L } ( j )$ has the homotopy type of a $\Sigma _ { j }$ . CW complex. Therefore, by the equivariant form of I.2.6, ${ \mathcal { L } } ( j ) \ltimes X ^ { j }$ has the homotopy type of a $\Sigma _ { j }$ CW spectrum indexed on $U$ .Thus, by [38, I.5.6], $D _ { j } X$ has the homotopy type of a CW-spectrum. By I.4.6 and II.1.9, $\mathbb { L } D _ { j } X$ has the homotopy type of a CW $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum and $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } D _ { j } X$ has the homotopy type of a CW $S .$ -module. These conclusions pass to smash products by I.6.1 and III.7.3. The top horizontal arrow is a homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra by I.4.6 and I.8.5, and the bottom horizontal arrow is a homotopy equivalence of spectra by XI.2.5. We claim that the right vertical arrow and therefore the left vertical arrow are also homotopy equivalences of spectra. Indeed, if all $j _ { i } \geq 1$ , then use of I.5.4 and I.5.6 shows that the right vertical arrow is isomorphic to

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { ( \mathcal { L } ( n ) \times \mathcal { L } ( j _ { 1 } ) \times \cdots \times \mathcal { L } ( j _ { n } ) ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { j _ { 1 } } \times \cdots \times \Sigma _ { j _ { n } } } ( X _ { 1 } ^ { j _ { 1 } } \wedge \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge X _ { n } ^ { j _ { n } } ) } \\ & { \qquad \biggr \downarrow \gamma \ltimes \mathrm { i d } } \\ & { \qquad \mathcal { L } ( j _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + j _ { n } ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { j _ { 1 } } \times \cdots \times \Sigma _ { j _ { n } } } ( X _ { 1 } ^ { j _ { 1 } } \wedge \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge X _ { n } ^ { j _ { n } } ) . } \end{array}
$$

Since $\gamma$ is a $( \Sigma _ { j _ { 1 } } \times \cdot \cdot \cdot \times \Sigma _ { j _ { n } } )$ -equivariant homotopy equivalence, the map before passage to orbits is an equivariant homotopy equivalence by the equivariant version of I.2.5. If any $j _ { i } = 0$ , then use of I.6.1 reduces us to the case when a single $j _ { i } = 0$ , and in this case the conclusion follows from I.8.6.

Now return to the study of our given $q$ -cofibrant commutative $S .$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ .

DEFInITION 6.4. Define $ { \mathcal { E } } _ { R }$ to be the collection of $R .$ -modules of the form

$$
R \land s ( S \land s { \cal D } _ { j } X ) ,
$$

where $X$ is any spectrum of the homotopy type of a CW-spectrum and $j \geq 0$ . Define $\bar { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } _ { R }$ to be the closure of $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { E } } _ { R }$ under finite $\wedge _ { R }$ -products, wedges, pushouts along cofibrations, colimits of countable sequences of cofibrations, and homotopy equivalences, where all of these operations are taken in the category of $\scriptstyle { R } .$ modules. That is, if $\{ M _ { 1 } , \ldots , M _ { n } \} \subset \bar { \mathcal { E } } _ { R }$ , then $M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge _ { R } M _ { n } \in \bar { \mathcal { E } } _ { R } ^ { o }$ ,and so forth.

Observe that $\bar { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } _ { R }$ contains all $R$ -modules of the homotopy types of cell $R \mathrm { . }$ . modules, that being the collection that would be obtained if we only allowed $j = 1$ in our initial class. One point of the definition is the following observation. Its proof is just like that of Theorem 6.2, and we shall say more about the commutative case shortly.

THEOREM 6.5. The underlying $R$ -module of a $q$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebra or commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra $A$ is in $\mathcal { \bar { E } } _ { R }$ .

Another point is the following reassuring consequence of Theorem 6.3 and the definition.

ProPosITIoN 6.6. The underlying spectrum of an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module in $\bar { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } _ { R }$ has the homotopy type of a CW-spectrum.

These lead to the main point, which is that we have control of the behavior of derived smash product of $R .$ -modules that are in $\bar { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } _ { R }$ .

THEOREM 6.7. Let $R$ be a $q$ -cofibrant commutative $S$ -algebra. Choose a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $\Gamma M$ and a weak equivalence of $R$ -modules $\gamma : \Gamma M \longrightarrow M$ for each $M \in \bar { \pmb { \mathcal { E } } } _ { R }$ . Then, for any finite subset $\{ M _ { 1 } , \ldots , M _ { n } \}$ of $\bar { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } _ { R }$ ,

$$
\gamma \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge _ { R } \gamma : \Gamma M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge _ { R } \Gamma M _ { n } \longrightarrow M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge _ { R } M _ { n }
$$

is a weak equivalence of $R$ -modules. That is, the derived smash product of the $M _ { i }$ in the category $\mathcal { D } _ { R }$ is represented by their point-set level smash product.

PROOF. When $R = S$ and each $M _ { i }$ is in $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } _ { S }$ , Theorem 6.3 gives the conclusion. The conclusion for general $M _ { i }$ follows by standard commutation formulas relating smash products to the chosen operations. For general $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $M _ { i } = R \wedge _ { S } N _ { i }$ ,where $N _ { i } \in \mathcal { E } _ { S }$ has CW $S .$ approximation $\Gamma N _ { i }$ , $R \land _ { S } \Gamma N _ { i }$ is a CW $\scriptstyle { R }$ approximation of $M _ { i }$ . Here we have the identification

$$
\bigl ( R \wedge _ { { \cal S } } N _ { 1 } \bigr ) \wedge _ { { \cal R } } \cdots \wedge _ { { \cal R } } \bigl ( R \wedge _ { { \cal S } } N _ { n } \bigr ) \cong { \cal R } \wedge _ { { \cal S } } \bigl ( N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { { \cal S } } \cdots \wedge _ { { \cal S } } N _ { n } \bigr ) ,
$$

and similarly and compatibly for the $\Gamma N _ { i }$ . By Theorem 6.5, $\scriptstyle { R }$ is in $\bar { \pmb { \mathscr { E } } } _ { S }$ , hence the result for $S$ implies the result for these $M _ { i }$ The result for general $M _ { i }$ follows as in the case $R = S$ .

Observe that the $\gamma$ and their smash products are necessarily homotopy equivalences of underlying spectra, since these are CW homotopy types.

REMARK 6.8. In any model category, the coproduct of $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrant objects is $q$ cofibrant. In particular, $A$ and $B$ are $q$ -cofibrant commutative $\scriptstyle { R } .$ algebras, then so is $A \land _ { R } B$ .

# 7. $q$ -Cofibrations and weak equivalences; cofibrations

Again, let $R$ be a fixed $q$ -cofibrant commutative $S .$ -algebra. We here prove several useful lemmas concerning the relationship between $\pmb q$ -cofibrations and weak equivalences. We also prove a key technical lemma about cofibrations. Recall the bar construction $\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , \dot { A ^ { \prime \prime } } )$ from Definition 3.5. The following result is a direct consequence of Proposition 3.7 and Lemma 4.16.

LEMMA 7.1. If $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ and $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime \prime }$ are maps of $q$ -cofibrant $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras, then $\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ is a $q$ -cofibrant $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra over $A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ , and the natural map $A ^ { \prime }$ $A ^ { \prime \prime } \longrightarrow \beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ is a $q$ -cofibration. The same statement holds for commutative $R$ -algebras.

This result gains force from the following two, which give good homotopical behavior of the functor $\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ and allow us to transport that behavior to the functor $A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ . Remember that $A ^ { \prime } \operatorname { I I } _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime } = A ^ { \prime } \wedge _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ in the commutative case.

LEMMA 7.2. If $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ is a map of $q$ -cofibrant $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras, then the functor $\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , - )$ preserves weak equivalences between $q$ -cofibrant $R$ algebras. The same statement holds for commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras.

ProoF. This is not obvious since the given weak equivalence of $R$ algebras will be a homotopy equivalence of $R$ -algebras but not necessarily of $R$ algebras under $A$ The functor $\beta ^ { R }$ is obtained by passage to geometric realization from a functor $\beta _ { * } ^ { R }$ to simplicial $R$ -modules. Since coproducts of weak equivalences between $q$ -cofibrant objects are weak equivalences in any model category, it is clear from Definition 3.5 that each functor $\beta _ { q } ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A - )$ preserves weak equivalences. By Lemma 7.4 below, $\beta _ { * } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ is a proper simplicial $R$ -module, and the conclusion follows from X.2.4.

LEMMA 7.3. If $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ is a $q$ -cofibration of $q$ -cofibrant $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras and $A ^ { \prime \prime }$ is a $q$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebra, then the natural map $\psi : \beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras. The same statement holds for commutative $R$ algebras.

Proor. Recall from Proposition 3.7 that we can identify $\beta ^ { R } ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ with the double mapping cylinder $M ( A ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ as an $\scriptstyle { R } .$ algebra over $A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ .Without loss of generality, we may assume that $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ is the inclusion of a relative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebra. Write $A ^ { \prime } = \operatorname { c o l i m } A _ { n } ^ { \prime }$ where $A _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } = A$ and $A _ { n } ^ { \prime } = \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) \mathrm { I I } _ { \mathbb { T } E _ { n } } A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime }$ for some wedge of sphere $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules $E _ { n }$ Then each map $A _ { n } ^ { \prime } ~ \amalg _ { A } ~ A ^ { \prime \prime } ~ \longrightarrow$ $A _ { n + 1 } ^ { \prime } ~ \amalg _ { A } ~ A ^ { \prime \prime }$ and $M ( A _ { n } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \longrightarrow M ( A _ { n + 1 } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } )$ is a $q \mathrm { . }$ cofibration of $\scriptstyle { R } \cdot$ . algebras and therefore a cofibration of $R _ { ☉ }$ -modules. Thus it suffices to show that each map $\psi : M ( A _ { n } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \longrightarrow A _ { n } ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ is a weak equivalence. This is clear for $n = 0$ ;assume inductively that it is true for $n - 1$ We have the following commutative diagram, in which the horizontal arrows are the evident isomorphisms:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } { M ( A _ { n } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \xrightarrow { \cong } \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } E _ { n } } M ( A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) } & { } & \\ { \psi \Bigg \downarrow } & { } & { \Bigg \downarrow \mathrm { d } \amalg \psi } \\ { A _ { n } ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime } \xrightarrow { \cong } \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } E _ { n } } A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime } . } & { } \end{array}
$$

We also have the following commutative diagram:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { M ( \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) , \mathbb { T } E _ { n } , M ( A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) ) \xrightarrow { \psi } \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } E _ { n } } M ( A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } , A , A ^ { \prime \prime } ) } \\ & { } & { M ( \mathrm { i d } , \mathrm { i d } , \psi ) \Bigg \downarrow } \\ & { } & { M ( \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) , \mathbb { T } E _ { n } , A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime } ) \xrightarrow [ \psi ] { \psi } \mathbb { T } ( C E _ { n } ) \amalg _ { \mathbb { T } E _ { n } } A _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime } . } \end{array}
$$

Here, by Proposition 3.8, the horizontal maps $\psi$ are homotopic to isomorphisms and are thus weak equivalences. The map $M ( \mathrm { i d } , \mathrm { i d } , \psi )$ is a weak equivalence by the previous lemma. Therefore id $\ Ḋ \amalg \psi Ḍ$ is a weak equivalence. The conclusion follows.

This has the following important consequence concerning cobase change.

PROPOSITION 7.4. If $A \longrightarrow A ^ { \prime }$ is a $q$ -cofibration of $q$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebras, then the functor $A ^ { \prime } \amalg _ { A } ( - )$ preserves weak equivalences between $q$ -cofibrant $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras. The same statement holds for commutative $R$ -algebras.

In fact, in the commutative case, we need only assume given a weak equivalence between $R$ -algebras that are $q \mathrm { . }$ -cofibrant as $S .$ -algebras, since the construction of $A \wedge _ { A } A ^ { \prime \prime }$ does not depend on $R$ .

We conclude with the following lemma on cofibrations. It will imply that the simplicial $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules used in the following chapters are proper, in the sense of X.2.2. As explained at the start of $\mathbf { X \ S 2 }$ , we abuse language by writing about unions, images, and inclusions when we should be writing more precisely about maps from a suitable coend to $A ^ { q }$ . The abuse is justified by the conclusion, since a cofibration of spectra is a spacewise closed inclusion [38, I.8.1].

LEMMA 7.5. Let $A$ be a $q$ -cofibrant $R$ -algebra or a $q$ -cofibrant commutative $R$ -algebra. Let $s A ^ { q } \subset A ^ { q }$ be the "union of the images" of the maps

$$
s _ { i } = ( \mathrm { i d } ) ^ { i } \wedge \eta \wedge ( \mathrm { i d } ) ^ { q - i } : A ^ { q - 1 } \longrightarrow A ^ { q } .
$$

Then the "inclusion" $s A ^ { q } \subset A ^ { q }$ is a cofibration of $R$ -modules. In particular, the unit $\eta : R \longrightarrow A$ is a cofibration of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules.

ProoF. In the non-commutative case, $( A , R )$ is a relative cell $R$ -module. Its qth smash power inherits such a structure, by III.7.3, and $s A ^ { q }$ is a subcomplex. In the commutative case, we can apply the same brief argument, once we observe that $( A , R )$ is a suitably general kind of relative cell $R$ -module. Thus we consider generalized relative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules that are constructed with (cell, sphere) pairs replaced by pairs of the form $( N \wedge B _ { + } ^ { q } , N \wedge S _ { + } ^ { q - 1 } )$ , where $N$ runs through all finite smash products over $\scriptstyle { R }$ of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules of the form $( S _ { R } ^ { n } ) ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j }$ or $( C S _ { R } ^ { n } ) ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j }$ for integers $_ n$ and for $j \geq 1$ . Here the $( B ^ { q } , S ^ { q - 1 } )$ are ordinary space level (cell, sphere) pairs. Observe that these $R$ -modules are finite colimits of compact $R \mathrm { . }$ . modules, so that III.1.7 applies to them. Let $\mathbb { P }$ be the monad in the category of $R$ -modules that defines commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras. Obviously $\mathbb { P } M$ and $\mathbb { P } C M$ are relative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules in this generalized sense when $M$ is a wedge of sphere $R _ { ☉ }$ -modules. Equally obviously, the smash product over $\scriptstyle { R }$ of two such generalized relative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules is another such. Suppose that $A$ is such a commutative $\scriptstyle R .$ -algebra and consider a pushout diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { P } M \longrightarrow A } \\ { \downarrow } \\ { \mathbb { P } C M \longrightarrow B . } \end{array}
$$

As explained in 3.53.9, $B$ is isomorphic to the geometric realization of the proper simplicial $R$ module $\beta _ { * } ^ { R } ( \mathbb { P } C M , \mathbb { P } M , A )$ .Remember that, here in the commutative case, the coproduct used in $\mathrm { V I I } \ S 3$ is the smash product over $\scriptstyle { R }$ We may construct the geometric realization by first using degeneracy identifications, which serve simply to eliminate redundant wedge summands from the relevant coend, and then face identifications; compare X.2.6. The latter identifications can be expressed by pushout diagrams of $R$ -modules

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \beta _ { q } ^ { R } ( \mathbb { P } C M , \mathbb { P } M , A ) \wedge \partial \Delta _ { q + } \xrightarrow { g } F _ { q - 1 } \beta ^ { R } ( \mathbb { P } C M , \mathbb { P } M , A ) } \\ & { \qquad \downarrow } \\ & { \beta _ { q } ^ { R } ( \mathbb { P } C M , \mathbb { P } M , A ) \wedge \Delta _ { q + } \xrightarrow { } F _ { q } \beta ^ { R } ( \mathbb { P } C M , \mathbb { P } M , A ) . } \end{array}
$$

Of course, we think of $( \Delta _ { q } , \partial \Delta _ { q } )$ as a model for $( B ^ { q } , S ^ { q - 1 } )$ . Proceeding inductively and using III.1.7 and the proof of III.2.2, we can make $g$ a sequentially cellular map and deduce that the qth filtration is a generalized relative cell $\scriptstyle { R } .$ . module. By passage to colimits, so is any commutative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebra. The same holds for smash powers. The inclusion of a subcomplex is a cofibration, by reduction to the obvious case of cell pairs, and the conclusion follows.

# CHAPTER VIII

# Bousfield localizations of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules and algebras

We study Bousfield localizations in this chapter. For any $S .$ algebra $\scriptstyle { R }$ and cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $\boldsymbol { E }$ , we show that $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ admits a new model category structure in which the weak equivalences are the $E$ -equivalences. With this model structure, a factorization of the trivial map $M \longrightarrow *$ as the composite of an $E$ acyclic $\pmb { E }$ . cofibration and an $\boldsymbol { E }$ -fibration constructs the localization of $M$ at $E$ .

Restricting to a $q$ -cofibrant commutative $S$ -algebra $R$ , we combine formal constructions with the homotopical analysis of the previous chapter to prove that localizations at $\boldsymbol { \varepsilon }$ of cell $R$ -algebras can be constructed as cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras, and similarly for commutative cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras. Of course, this applies quite generally since any $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebra is weakly equivalent to a cell $R _ { \mathbf { \nu } }$ algebra. That is, we can conclude that Bousfield localizations of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras and commutative $\scriptstyle R$ -algebras are again such. In the case $R = S$ , Hopkins and McClure had an unpublished argument, sketched in e-mails to us, that Bousfield localizations of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra are $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

We deduce that the derived category of $\boldsymbol { E }$ -local $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules is equivalent to the full subcategory of the derived category of $R _ { E }$ -modules whose objects are those $R _ { E }$ -modules that are $E$ -local as $R$ -modules. In particular our new derived categories of $S _ { E }$ -modules are intrinsically important to a complete understanding of the classical Bousfield localizations of spectra.

As a simple direct application, we deduce that $K O$ and $K U$ are commutative ko and $k u$ -algebras since they are Bousfield localizations of $k o$ and $k u$ obtained by inverting the Bott elements. By neglect of structure, they are therefore commutative $S$ -algebras. This solves a problem that was first studied in McClure's 1978 PhD thesis.

We refer the reader to [26] for a discussion of the special cases of Bousfield localization that give localizations and completions of $R$ -modules at ideals of the coefficient ring $R _ { * }$ .

# 1. Bousfield localizations of $R$ -modules

Let $R$ be an $S$ -algebra, such as $S$ itself, and suppose given a cell $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -module $E$ . We shall construct Bousfield localizations of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules at $\boldsymbol { E }$ The treatment

is based on Bousfield's papers [11, 12], but in the latter he was handicapped by working in a primitive category of spectra that did not admit a model category structure.

A map $f : M \longrightarrow N$ of $R \mathrm { . }$ -modules is said to be an $\boldsymbol { E }$ -equivalence if the induced map

$$
\mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { R } f : E \wedge _ { R } M \longrightarrow E \wedge _ { R } N
$$

is a weak equivalence. Homologically, we should call such maps $E _ { * } ^ { R }$ -equivalences, and we shall often refer to them as $E$ -acyclic maps. An $R \mathrm { . }$ -module $W$ is said to be $E .$ -acyclic if $E \wedge _ { R } W \simeq *$ , and a map $f$ is $E$ -acyclic if and only if its cofiber is $E .$ acyclic. We say that an $R$ -module $L$ is $E$ -local if $f ^ { * } : { \mathcal { D } } _ { R } ( N , L ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { D } } _ { R } ( M , L )$ is an isomorphism for any $E$ -equivalence $f$ or, equivalently, if $\mathcal { D } _ { R } ( W , L ) = 0$ for any $E$ -acyclic $R$ -module $W$ . Since this is a derived category criterion, it suffices to test it when $W$ is a cell $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module. A localization of $M$ at $\boldsymbol { \varepsilon }$ is a map $\lambda : M \longrightarrow M _ { E }$ such that $\lambda$ is an $E$ -equivalence and $M _ { E }$ is $E$ -local.
