j _ { 1 } = ( g \circ i _ { 1 } ) ^ { - 1 } \circ g ^ { \prime } \circ i _ { 1 } \mathrm { a n d } j _ { 2 } = ( g \circ i _ { 2 } ) ^ { - 1 } \circ g ^ { \prime } \circ i _ { 2 } ,
$$

then we find that (2.6) holds and can conclude that $\left( g ^ { \prime } , f ^ { \prime } \right) \sim \left( g , f \right)$ . Thus the injectivity will follow if we can show that $( V _ { 1 } , V _ { 2 } , h ) \sim ( V _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , V _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } , h ^ { \prime } )$ for any two triples such that $h = h ^ { \prime }$ . Choose infinite dimensional subspaces $W _ { 1 }$ of $V _ { 1 }$ and $W _ { 1 } ^ { \prime }$ of $V _ { 1 } ^ { \prime }$ such that $W _ { 1 } , W _ { 1 } ^ { \prime }$ , and $h ( U )$ are mutually orthogonal. Then

$$
( V _ { 1 } , V _ { 2 } , h ) \sim ( W _ { 1 } , h ( U ) , h ) \sim ( W _ { 1 } + W _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , h ( U ) , h ) \sim ( W _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , h ^ { \prime } ( U ) , h ^ { \prime } ) \sim ( V _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , V _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } , h ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

This proves the injectivity and thus the bijectivity of $\hat { \gamma }$ .

The contractibility of $\mathcal { L } ^ { 0 } ( 1 )$ is clear since it is closed under the homotopies described in Lemma 1.5. To see the contractibility of $\hat { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 )$ , write $U = U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 }$ , where $U _ { 1 }$ and $U _ { 2 }$ are isomorphic to $\boldsymbol { U }$ , and define

$$
\mathcal { H } ( 2 ) = \{ g | g ( \{ 0 \} \oplus U ) \subset U _ { 2 } \} \subset \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) ,
$$

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \hat { \pmb { \mathscr { H } } } ( 1 ) = \pmb { \mathscr { H } } ( 2 ) \times \pmb { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) \times \pmb { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) \times \pmb { \mathscr { L } } ( 0 ) \times \pmb { \mathscr { L } } ( 1 ) , } \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\mathcal { H } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) = \{ f | f ( U ) \subset U _ { 2 } \} \subset \mathcal { L } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) .
$$

Since $\mathcal { H } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) \cong \mathcal { I } ( U , U _ { 2 } )$ , it is contractible. Since $\mathcal { L } ^ { 0 } ( 1 )$ is also contractible, the inclusion $\mathcal { H } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ^ { 0 } ( 1 )$ is obviously a homotopy equivalence. We have the following commutative diagram, in which the vertical arrows are inclusions:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \hat { \mathcal { H } } ( 1 ) \xrightarrow { \hat { \beta } } \mathcal { H } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) } \\ { \Big \downarrow \qquad } \\ { \hat { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) \xrightarrow { \hat { \mathcal { H } } ( 1 ) { } } \mathcal { L } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) . } \end{array}
$$

Modifying the proof that $\hat { \gamma }$ is a bijection by restricting $V _ { 2 }$ to be contained in $U _ { 2 }$ , we see that $\hat { \beta }$ is also a bijection. Choose linear isometric isomorphisms $k _ { 1 } : U \longrightarrow U _ { 1 }$ and $k _ { 2 } : U \longrightarrow U _ { 2 }$ and define

$$
\sigma : \mathcal { H } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { H } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )
$$

by $\sigma ( f ) = ( k _ { 1 } \oplus k _ { 2 } , i , k _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } \circ f )$ Then $\sigma$ is a continuous section of

$$
\gamma : \mathcal { H } ( 2 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 0 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { H } ^ { 0 } ( 1 ) .
$$

It follows that $\hat { \beta }$ is a homeomorphism and in particular that $\hat { \mathcal { H } } ( 1 )$ is contractible. We claim that the inclusion $\iota : \mathcal { H } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ is a homotopy equivalence of right $\mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ -spaces. It will follow that the inclusion $\begin{array} { r } { \hat { \mathcal { K } } ( 1 ) \longrightarrow \hat { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 ) } \end{array}$ is a homotopy equivalence, proving the contractibility of $\hat { \mathcal { L } } ( 1 )$ .

Define $\rho : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { X } ( 2 )$ by $\rho ( g ) = i _ { 2 } \circ k _ { 2 } \circ g$ To prove our claim, it suffices to find a homotopy $h : i _ { 2 } \circ k _ { 2 } \simeq$ id such that $h _ { t } ( U _ { 2 } ) \subset U _ { 2 }$ for all $t$ ,for then $g \in \mathcal { K } ( 2 )$ will imply $h _ { t } \circ g \in \mathcal { K } ( 2 )$ and, via right composition with maps $g , h$ will induce the required homotopies ${ \mathrm { i d } } \simeq \iota \circ \rho$ and $\operatorname { i d } \simeq \rho \circ \iota$ Trivially, we have the following commutative diagram:

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } \frac { i _ { 2 } \oplus \mathrm { i d } } { \hbar } U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } } } \\ { { k _ { 2 } \Big \downarrow } } \\ { { V _ { 2 } \xrightarrow [ i _ { 2 } ] { i _ { 1 } \oplus k _ { 2 } } \Delta U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } . } } \end{array}
$$

We can homotope $i _ { 2 } \oplus \mathrm { i d }$ to $i _ { 1 } \oplus \mathrm { i d }$ by homotoping $i _ { 2 }$ to $i _ { 1 }$ , after which the right composite becomes id $\mathbb { \oplus ( k _ { 2 } | _ { U _ { 2 } } ) }$ . We can then homotope $k _ { 2 } | _ { U _ { 2 } }$ to the identity, after which the right composite becomes the identity. It is clear that $U _ { 2 }$ is carried into $U _ { 2 }$ by these homotopies.

REMARK 2.7. It seems unlikely to us that $\hat { \gamma }$ is actually a homeomorphism.

3. The unit equivalence for the operadic smash product

We here prove I.6.2, and we restate each of its clauses as a lemma.

LEMMA 3.1. For L-spectra $N$ , there is a natural weak equivalence of L-spectra

$$
\omega : \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow N .
$$

ProOF. Let $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ act from the right on $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ by setting $f e = f \circ ( 1 \oplus e )$ for $f \in \mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ and $e \in \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ . Regard $\mathcal { L } ( 2 )$ as a space over $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ via the map $\sigma _ { 2 } : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ specified by $\sigma _ { 2 } ( f ) = f \circ i _ { 2 }$ , where $i _ { 2 } : U \longrightarrow U ^ { 2 }$ is the inclusion of the second summand. Then

$$
\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } N ,
$$

and $\sigma _ { 2 }$ induces the required natural map

$$
\omega = \sigma _ { 2 } \ltimes \mathrm { i d } : \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } N \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes _ { \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) } N \cong N .
$$

Observe that, by I.2.5, $\omega$ is a homotopy equivalence of spectra when $N = \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }$ .

We must prove that $\omega$ induces an isomorphism on homotopy groups. By adjunction, we may identify $\pi _ { n } ( N )$ with $h { \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , N )$ .First, to prove surjectivity, suppose given a map of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra $\alpha : \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \longrightarrow N .$ Write

$$
\hat { \alpha } = \mathrm { i d } \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } \alpha : \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathscr { L } } N .
$$

The following diagram commutes:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \xrightarrow { \hat { \alpha } } } & { \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N } \\ { \omega \Bigg \downarrow } & { \qquad \Bigg \downarrow \omega } \\ { \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \xrightarrow { \alpha } } & { \qquad N . } \end{array}
$$

Since $\omega$ on the left is an equivalence, $\alpha \in \operatorname { I m } ( \omega _ { * } )$ .

To prove injectivity, suppose given an $\mathbb { L }$ -map $\beta : \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \to \mathbb { L } S \land _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ such that $\alpha \equiv \omega \circ \beta \simeq 0$ . Define $\hat { \alpha }$ as above. Since $\alpha \simeq 0$ and $\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - ) : \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ is a homotopy preserving functor, $\hat { \alpha } \simeq 0$ .Define

$$
\hat { \beta } = \beta \circ \omega : \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N .
$$

Since $\omega$ is an equivalence, to prove that $\beta \simeq 0$ , it suffices to prove that $\hat { \alpha } \simeq \hat { \beta }$ . Here, by the naturality of $\omega$ , $\hat { \hat { \beta } }$ coincides with the composite

$$
\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } { \overset { \underset { \mathrm { i d } } { } \wedge \beta } { \longrightarrow } } \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) { \overset { \omega } { \longrightarrow } } \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ,
$$

while $\hat { \alpha }$ coincides with the composite

$$
\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } { \overset { \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } } \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) { \overset { \mathrm { i d } } { \longrightarrow } } \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N .
$$

Thus it suffices to show that $\omega \simeq \mathrm { i d } \wedge \omega$ .Let $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ act from the right on $\mathcal { L } ( 3 )$ by setting $g e = g \circ ( 1 \oplus 1 \oplus e )$ for $g \in \mathcal { L } ( 3 )$ and $e \in \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ . Regard $\mathcal { L } ( 3 )$ as a space over $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ via the map $\sigma _ { 3 } : \mathcal { L } ( 3 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ specified by $\sigma _ { 3 } ( f ) = f \circ i _ { 3 }$ where $i _ { 3 } : U \longrightarrow U ^ { 3 }$ is the inclusion of the third summand. By the proof of the associativity isomorphism I.5.5, we have

$$
{ \mathbb L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( { \mathbb L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) \cong { \mathcal L } ( 3 ) \ltimes _ { { \mathcal L } ( 1 ) } N .
$$

Under the identifications (3.2) and (3.3), the maps $\omega$ and id $\wedge \omega$ in our factorizations of $\hat { \beta }$ and $\hat { \alpha }$ coincide with the maps

$$
\sigma _ { 2 , 3 } \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) } \mathrm { i d } : \mathscr { L } ( 3 ) \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) } N \longrightarrow \mathscr { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) } N
$$

and

$$
\sigma _ { 1 , 3 } \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) } \mathrm { i d } : \mathscr { L } ( 3 ) \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) } N \longrightarrow \mathscr { L } ( 2 ) \ltimes _ { \mathscr { L } ( 1 ) } N ,
$$

where

$$
\sigma _ { 2 , 3 } : \mathcal { L } ( 3 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 ) \quad \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } \quad \sigma _ { 1 , 3 } : \mathcal { L } ( 3 ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 2 )
$$

are the maps that restrict $g \in { \mathcal { L } } ( 3 )$ to the second and third and first and third coordinates, respectively. Thus, it suffices to show that $\sigma _ { 2 , 3 }$ and $\sigma _ { 1 , 3 }$ are homotopic as maps of right $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ spaces over $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ . Since the images under $\sigma _ { 2 , 3 } ( g )$ and $\sigma _ { 1 , 3 } ( g )$ of the first copy of $U$ in $U ^ { 2 }$ are orthogonal and the right action of $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ is on the second copy, to which we restrict when mapping to $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ , we obtain the required homotopy by normalizing the evident linear homotopy

$$
h ( g , t ) ( u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } ) = t g ( u _ { 1 } , 0 , u _ { 2 } ) + ( 1 - t ) g ( 0 , u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } ) . ~ \ \square
$$

LEMMA 3.4. The suspension homomorphism $\Sigma : \pi _ { n } ( N ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { n + 1 } ( \Sigma N )$ is an isomorphism for any L-spectrum $N$ and integer $_ n$ .

PRooF. We shall construct an explicit inverse isomorphism

$$
\Sigma ^ { - 1 } : \pi _ { n + 1 } ( \Sigma N ) \longrightarrow \pi _ { n } ( N ) .
$$

We again think of $\pi _ { n } ( N )$ as $h \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } , N )$ . Since the functors $\Sigma$ and $\mathbb { L }$ commute, we may identify $\mathbb { L } S ^ { n + 1 }$ with $\Sigma \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }$ . Similarly, we have a natural isomorphism

$$
\iota : \mathbb { L } S ^ { - 1 } \wedge \mathscr { L } \Sigma N \cong \mathbb { L } S \wedge \mathscr { L } N .
$$

Since $\mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }$ and $\mathbb { L } S ^ { n }$ are CW L-spectra, the weak equivalence

$$
\omega : \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \mathbb { L } S ^ { n }
$$

is a homotopy equivalence of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, and we choose a homotopy inverse $\nu$ . Suppose given an $\mathbb { L }$ map $\beta : \Sigma \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \longrightarrow \Sigma N$ We define $\Sigma ^ { - 1 } \beta$ to be the composite

$$
\mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \xrightarrow { \nu } \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \xrightarrow { \iota ^ { - 1 } } \mathbb { L } S ^ { - 1 } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \Sigma \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \overset { \iota \mathrm { d } \wedge \beta } { \longrightarrow } \mathbb { L } S ^ { - 1 } \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \Sigma N \xrightarrow { \iota } \mathbb { L } S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \xrightarrow { \omega } N .
$$

If $\beta = \Sigma \alpha$ , then the naturality of $\iota$ and $\omega$ imply that $\Sigma ^ { - 1 } \beta = \alpha \circ \omega \circ \nu \simeq \alpha$ Thus $\Sigma ^ { - 1 } \circ \Sigma = \mathop { \mathrm { i d } }$ To evaluate $\Sigma \circ \Sigma ^ { - 1 }$ , consider the following diagram:

$$
\begin{array}{c} \begin{array} { r l } & { \begin{array} { r l r } & { \Sigma [ \Delta S ^ { n } - } & { \Sigma \nu } \\ & { \vdots } & \\ & { \vdots } & \\ { \Sigma \Sigma ^ { n } } & { \cdots \Sigma } & { \mathbb { L } S \kappa \mathcal { L } \nabla ^ { n } \ : \exp \left( \mathbb { L } S ^ { - 1 } \Lambda \mathcal { L } \exp \left( \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } \right) \right) } \end{array} } \\ & { \begin{array} { r l } & { \Sigma [ \Delta S ^ { n } - \omega } & { \mathbb { L } S \Lambda \mathcal { L } \exp \left( \Sigma \mathbb { L } S ^ { n } - \omega \right) \ : \Sigma ( \mathbb { L } S ^ { - 1 } \Lambda \mathcal { L } S ^ { n } ) } \\ & { \beta ] } & \\ & { \vdots } & \\ { \Sigma \ : N \ : \mathrm { ~ e ~ - ~ } \omega } & { \mathbb { L } S \Lambda \mathcal { L } \exp \left( \mathrm { L } S ^ { n } \right) } \end{array} } \\ & { \begin{array} { r l } & { \Gamma _ { 1 } } & \\ & { \vdots } & \\ { \Sigma \ : N } & { \Sigma \ : N - \omega } & { \Sigma ( \mathbb { L } S \Lambda \mathcal { L } \exp \left( \frac { \lambda } { \sum \omega } \right) ) } \end{array} } \\ & { \begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { ~ y ~ } } \\ & { \Sigma \backslash } & { \Sigma ( \mathbb { L } S \Lambda \mathcal { L } \exp \left( N \right) ) } \end{array} } \end{array}  \end{array}
$$

The upper left dotted arrow is the composite homotopy equivalence dictated by commutativity of the top rectangle. The maps $\Sigma \iota$ and $\iota$ appearing in the bottom rectangle differ by an interchange of circle coordinates, hence we obtain a dotted homotopy equivalence making the bottom rectangle homotopy commute by using a map of degree minus one on the circle coordinate. This implies that the composite $\scriptstyle \sum \circ \Sigma ^ { - 1 }$ is an isomorphism, and it follows formally that it must be the identity.

# CHAPTER XII

# The monadic bar construction

The monadic bar construction was a central tool in earlier drafts of this paper, but it plays a very minor role in this version. It is nevertheless an important construction. We shall say just enough about it to prove the two deferred results that depend on it and to allow rigorous use of it in later work. The essential point is to prove certain lemmas on cofibrations, one of which played a role in our construction of model structures on the categories of $R _ { ☉ }$ -algebras and commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ algebras.

# 1. The bar construction and two deferred proofs

Recall the definitions of an action of a monad on a functor and of a monadic bifunctor from II.6.3.

DEFINITION 1.1. For a triple $( F , \mathbb { S } , R )$ consisting of a monad $( \mathbb { S } , \mu , \eta )$ in a category $\mathscr { C }$ , an $\mathbb { S }$ algebra $( R , \xi )$ , and an $\mathbb { S }$ -functor $( F , \nu )$ in $\mathcal { C } ^ { \prime }$ , define a simplicial object $B _ { \ast } ( F , \mathbb { S } , R )$ in ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \prime }$ by letting the $q \mathrm { . }$ simplices $B _ { q } ( F , { \mathbb S } , R )$ be ${ \cal F S } ^ { q } R$ (where $\mathbb { S } ^ { q }$ denotes $\mathbb { S }$ composed with itself $q$ times); the faces and degeneracies are given by

$$
d _ { i } = \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { \nu \mathbb { S } ^ { q - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } i = 0 } \\ { F \mathbb { S } ^ { i - 1 } \mu \mathbb { S } ^ { q - i - 1 } } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } 1 \leq i < q } \\ { F \mathbb { S } ^ { q - 1 } \xi } & { \mathrm { i f ~ } i = q } \end{array} \right.
$$

and $s _ { i } = F \mathbb { S } ^ { i } \eta \mathbb { S } ^ { q - i }$ . If $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ is a monad in ${ \pmb { \mathcal { C } } } ^ { \prime }$ and $F$ is an $( \mathbb { S } ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { S } )$ -bifunctor, then $B _ { \ast } ( F , { \mathbb S } , R )$ is a simplicial $\mathbb { S } ^ { \prime }$ algebra.

When $\pmb { F }$ takes values in a category with a forgetful functor to $\mathcal { S }$ , we write

$$
B ( F , \mathbb { S } , R ) = | B _ { * } ( F , \mathbb { S } , R ) | .
$$

We use a similar notation for pairs when $F$ takes pairs of spectra as values. All of the bar constructions used earlier, such as $B ^ { R } ( M , A , N )$ , can be interpreted as instances of this general construction. In the context of II.6.4, we have the following standard example.

ExAMPLE 1.2. We have a simplicial $\mathbb { S }$ algebra $B _ { * } ( \mathbb { S } , \mathbb { S } , R )$ associated to an $\mathbb { S } .$ . algebra $R$ Let $\underline { { R } }$ denote $R$ regarded as a constant simplicial object, $\underline { { R } } _ { q } = R$ for all $q$ , with each face and degeneracy the identity map. Iterates of $\mu$ and $\xi$ give a map $\varepsilon _ { * } : B _ { * } ( \mathbb { S } , \mathbb { S } , R ) \longrightarrow \underline { { R } }$ of simplicial $\mathbb { S }$ algebras in $\mathscr { C }$ . Similarly, iterates of $\eta$ give a map $\eta _ { * } : \underline { { R } } \longrightarrow B _ { * } ( \mathbb { S } , \mathbb { S } , R )$ of simplicial objects in $\mathscr { C }$ such that $\varepsilon _ { * } \eta _ { * } = \mathrm { i d }$ . Moreover, there is a simplicial homotopy $\eta _ { * } \varepsilon _ { * } \simeq \mathrm { i d }$ [45, 9.8].

By II.4.1, we have monads $\mathbb { B }$ and $\mathbb { C }$ in $\mathcal { S }$ whose algebras are the $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra. We shall work in the ground category of spectra, rather than that of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra, for definiteness and because we envision more applications in that setting. Recall that geometric realization carries simplicial $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ rings, modules, and algebras to $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ rings, modules, and algebras, by X.1.5. We assume that all given spectra are $\Sigma \cdot$ -cofibrant. In the contrary case, we first apply the cylinder construction $K$ to make them so. By the results of $\mathbf { X \ S 4 }$ , this implies that the spectra of $q$ -simplices in all of our constructions are tame. As we shall explain in the next section, it also implies that our simplicial spectra are proper, so that our homotopical results on geometric realization apply.

DEFINITION 1.3. For an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ ,define an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $U R$ by

$$
\begin{array} { r } { U R = B ( { \mathbb { B } } , { \mathbb { B } } , R ) . } \end{array}
$$

For an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ , define an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $U R$ by

$$
\begin{array} { r } { U R = B ( \mathbb { C } , \mathbb { C } , R ) . } \end{array}
$$

The following result is immediate from Example 1.2 and X.1.2.

LEMMA 1.4. For $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra $\scriptstyle { R }$ there is a natural map of $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra $\varepsilon : U R \longrightarrow R$ that is a homotopy equivalence of spectra, and similarly for $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

We shall prove the following addendum in the next section.

LEMMA 1.5. The unit $\eta : S \longrightarrow U R$ is a cofibration of L-spectra.

REMARK 1.6. The $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ versions of the lemmas are compatible. If $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum, then the natural map

$$
{ \cal B } ( \mathbb { B } , \mathbb { B } , R ) \longrightarrow { \cal B } ( \mathbb { C } , \mathbb { C } , R )
$$

of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra is a map under $S$ and over $R$ and is therefore a homotopy equivalence of spectra.

We now prove our change of operads result II.4.3. The proof is virtually the same as that given on the space level in [45].

PROOF OF II.4.3. We are given an $E _ { \infty }$ operad $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ over $\mathcal { L }$ and an ${ \mathcal { O } } .$ spectrum $R$ Technically, we must assume that the unit element of $\mathcal { O } ( 1 )$ is a nondegenerate basepoint in order to ensure that the arguments of the next section apply to show that the simplicial spectra we use are proper. As in II.4.1, we have a monad $\mathbb { O }$ whose algebras are the $\mathcal { O }$ -spectra together with a map $\mathbb { O } \longrightarrow \mathbb { C }$ of monads. We define $V R$ to be the bar construction $B ( \mathbb { C } , \mathbb { O } , R )$ . By X.4.7, X.4.9, and the equivariant version of I.2.5, $\theta X \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } X$ is a homotopy equivalence of spectra for any tame spectrum $X$ . By X.2.4, there result maps of $\boldsymbol { \mathcal { O } }$ -spectra

$$
R \longleftarrow B ( \mathbb { O } , \mathbb { O } , R ) \longrightarrow B ( \mathbb { C } , \mathbb { O } , R ) = V R
$$

that are homotopy equivalences of spectra.

We have precisely analogous constructions for modules. By II.6.2, we have a monad $\mathbb { C } [ 1 ]$ in the category of pairs of spectra whose algebras are pairs consisting of an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum and a module over it. Its second coordinate is made explicit in II.5.7. We have a similar monad $\mathbb { B } [ 1 ]$ in the $A _ { \infty }$ case.

DEfInitioN 1.7. For an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ and an $R$ -module $M$ , define a $U R$ -module $U M$ by

$$
( U R ; U M ) = B ( \mathbb { C } [ 1 ] , \mathbb { C } [ 1 ] , ( R ; M ) )
$$

Replacing $\mathbb { C }$ by $\mathbb { B }$ , we obtain an analogous functor $U$ on modules over an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum.

LEMMA 1.8. There is a natural map of $U R$ -modules $\varepsilon : U M \longrightarrow M$ that is a homotopy equivalence of spectra.

REMARK 1.9. The $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ interpretations of the lemma are compatible. If $M$ is a module over an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ , then the natural map

$$
B ( \mathbb { B } [ 1 ] , \mathbb { B } [ 1 ] , ( R ; M ) ) \longrightarrow B ( \mathbb { C } [ 1 ] , \mathbb { C } [ 1 ] , ( R ; M ) )
$$

is a map over $( R ; M )$ and is thus a pair of homotopy equivalences of spectra.

PrOOF OF II.5.2. The argument is much the same as the proof of II.4.3. As in II.5.7, we have a monad $\mathcal { O } [ 1 ]$ such that a ${ \mathcal { O } } [ 1 ]$ -algebra is a pair consisting of an ${ \pmb \sigma }$ -spectrum and a module over it, and we have a map of monads $\mathcal { O } [ 1 ] \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } [ 1 ]$ . For an $\pmb { \sigma }$ -spectrum $\scriptstyle { R }$ and an $\scriptstyle R$ -module $M$ , we define $V M$ to be the $V R$ -module given by the second coordinate of $B ( \mathbb { C } [ 1 ] , \pmb { \sigma } [ 1 ] , ( R ; M ) )$ . The second coordinate of the weak equivalence

$$
( R ; M ) \longleftarrow B ( \theta [ 1 ] , \mathcal { O } [ 1 ] , ( R ; M ) ) \longrightarrow B ( \mathbb { C } [ 1 ] , \mathcal { O } [ 1 ] , ( R ; M ) ) = ( V R ; V M )
$$

is the required weak equivalence between the $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $M$ and the $V R$ -module $V M$ ; it is a homotopy equivalence of spectra.

# 2. Cofibrations and the bar construction

We must prove that our simplicial bar constructions are proper and prove Lemma 1.5. Recall the definition of a proper simplicial $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $K _ { * }$ from X.2.2, and remember that the simplicial filtration of $| K _ { * } |$ is then given by a sequence of cofibrations of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra; it follows that the inclusion $K _ { 0 } \longrightarrow | K _ { * } |$ is a cofibration of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra.

ProposiTion 2.1. The simplicial bar constructions used to construct the various functors $U$ and $V$ in the previous section are all proper simplicial L-spectra.

PROOF OF LEMMA 1.5. The unit $S \longrightarrow U R$ is the composite of the inclusion of $s$ as a wedge summand of $\mathbb { C } R$ and the inclusion $\mathbb { C } R \longrightarrow U R$ .

The following two lemmas directly imply Proposition 2.1 in the case of $U R$ . The proofs in the remaining cases are similar. Looking back at $B _ { \ast } ( F , \mathbb { S } , R )$ , we see that all of its degeneracy operators are maps of the form $F ( - )$ , so that the inclusion

$$
s B _ { q } ( F , \mathbb { S } , R ) \subset B _ { q } ( F , \mathbb { S } , R )
$$

is obtained by applying the functor $F$ to an inclusion that we may write

$$
s { \mathbb { S } } ^ { q } R \subset { \mathbb { S } } ^ { q } R .
$$

As explained after X.2.1, pedantic care with pushouts and coends is needed to be precise about this. The following lemma shows that the functor $\mathbb { C }$ converts cofibrations of spectra to cofibrations of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra. Here we are thinking of $\mathbb { C }$ as playing the role of $F$ in our bar construction. The second part of the lemma is more interesting. It was essential to make sense of the Cofibration Hypothesis of VII§4.

LEMMA 2.3. The following statements hold.

(i) The monads T and $\mathbb { P }$ in ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ that define $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra preserve cofibrations of L-spectra. Therefore the monads $\mathbb { B }$ and $\mathbb { C }$ in $\mathcal { S }$ convert cofibrations of spectra to cofibrations of L-spectra.   
(ii) For any commutative $S$ -algebra $R$ , the monads T and $\mathbb { P }$ in $\mathcal { M } _ { R }$ that define $R$ -algebras and commutative $R$ -algebras preserve cofibrations of $R$ -modules.

ProoF. We prove the second statement. The first is similar; its second statement holds because $\mathbb { B } \ = \ \mathbb { T } \mathbb { L }$ , $\mathbb { C } = \mathbb { P } \mathbb { L }$ , and $\mathbb { L }$ carries cofibrations of spectra to cofibrations of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra. Let $f _ { i } : M _ { i } \longrightarrow N _ { i }$ be cofibrations of $R \mathrm { . }$ . modules, $1 \ \leq \ i \ \leq \ j$ . We claim that $f _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \cdot \cdot \cdot \wedge _ { R } f _ { j }$ is a cofibration of $R .$ . modules. There are retractions of $\scriptstyle { R } .$ -modules $r _ { i } : N _ { i } \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow M f _ { i }$ , where $M f _ { i } \ = \ N _ { i } \cup ( M _ { i } \wedge I _ { + } )$ is the mapping cylinder of $f _ { i }$ . The diagonal map $\Delta : I \longrightarrow I ^ { j }$ is a deformation retraction of spaces, with retraction $\rho$ given by the averaging map $\begin{array} { r } { ( t _ { 1 } , \cdot \cdot \cdot , t _ { j } ) \longrightarrow \sum t _ { i } / j } \end{array}$ . The following composite is a retraction of $R$ -modules, proving our claim:

$$
\begin{array}{c} \begin{array} { c } { { ( N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } , \cdots \wedge _ { R } N _ { j } ) \wedge I _ { + } } } \\ { {  \mathrm { d } \Lambda \wedge \Delta _ { + }  } } \\ { {  \begin{array} { c } { {  \mathrm { d } N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } , } } \\ { {  \mathrm { d } N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } , } } \\ { {  \mathrm { d } N _ { 2 } \wedge _ { R } N _ { j }  } } \end{array}  N _ { j } ^ { j } ) _ { + } \cong ( N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { I _ { + } } ) \wedge _ { R } \dots \wedge _ { R } ( N _ { j } \wedge I _ { + } ) } } \\ { {  \mathrm { e } _ { 1 } \wedge \dots \wedge _ { R }  } } \\ { { ( M f _ { 1 } ) \wedge _ { R } \dots \wedge _ { R } ( M f _ { j } ) \cong ( N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \dots \wedge _ { R } N _ { j } ) \cup ( ( M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \dots \wedge _ { R } M _ { j } ) \wedge ( I ^ { j } ) _ { + } ) } } \\ { {  \mathrm { d } \cup \{ \mathrm { d } \Lambda _ { \rho + } \} \qquad } } \\ { {  N _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \dots \wedge _ { R } N _ { j }  \cup ( ( M _ { 1 } \wedge _ { R } \dots \wedge _ { R } M _ { j } ) \wedge I _ { + } ) = M ( f _ { 1 } \wedge \dots \wedge f _ { j } ) . } } \end{array}  \end{array}
$$

Now let $f _ { 1 } = \cdot \cdot \cdot = f _ { j } = f _  $ ,say. Then the $j$ -fold $\wedge _ { R }$ -power $f ^ { j }$ is a cofibration. Moreover, since $\Delta$ and $\rho$ are $\Sigma _ { j }$ -equivariant, $f ^ { j }$ is a $\Sigma _ { j }$ cofibration. Since passage to orbits carries $\Sigma _ { j }$ -cofibrations to cofibrations, $f ^ { j } / \Sigma _ { j }$ is also a cofibration of $R \mathrm { . }$ . modules. By II.7.1, our monads are given by wedges of $j$ -fold smash powers or $j$ -fold symmetric smash powers, and the conclusion follows.

LEMMA 2.4. For each $q \geq 1$ , the inclusion $s \mathbb { C } ^ { q } R \subset \mathbb { C } ^ { q } R$ is a cofibration of spectra.

PROOF. Recall that $\mathbb C R = \bigvee _ { j \ge 0 } \mathcal L ( j ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { j } } R ^ { j }$ Our standing assumption that $R$ is $\Sigma$ cofibrant implies that each $R ^ { j }$ is $\Sigma _ { j }$ -equivariantly $\Sigma$ cofibrant, by X.4.7. This allows us to apply A.8.1 and its equivariant version $\mathtt { A } \ S 9$ to show that maps between twisted half-smash products are cofibrations. The unit map $\eta : R \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } R$ is the composite

$$
\eta \ltimes \mathrm { i d } : R = \{ 1 \} \ltimes R \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \ltimes R \subset \mathbb { C } R .
$$

Here the first map is induced by the inclusion $\{ 1 \} \longrightarrow \mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ and is a cofibration by XI.1.4 and A.8.1, and the second map is the inclusion of a wedge summand. We shall prove that the inclusion $s \mathbb { C } ^ { q } R \subset \mathbb { C } ^ { q } R$ is a wedge of inclusions of the general form $A ^ { \prime } \ltimes _ { G } R ^ { j } \longrightarrow A \ltimes _ { G } R ^ { j }$ , where $G$ is a subgroup of $\Sigma _ { j }$ and $A ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow A$ is a $G$ -cofibration. By A.8.1 and $\mathtt { A } \ S 9$ , this will imply the conclusion. Although the combinatorics of the proof are a bit messy, it is easy to see what is going on by writing out the first few cases explicitly. The subspectrum $s \mathbb { C } ^ { q }$ is the union of the images of the inclusions $\mathbb { C } ^ { \iota } \eta \mathbb { C } ^ { q - \iota - 1 } : \mathbb { C } ^ { q - 1 } R \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } ^ { q } R$ , $0 \leq i \leq q - 1$ We first show by induction on $q$ that $\mathbb { C } ^ { q } R$ is a wedge of spectra of the form $A \bowtie _ { G } R ^ { j }$ This is obvious if $q = 1$ and we assume it for $q - 1$ We first consider $\mathcal { L } ( \mathfrak { j } ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { \mathfrak { i } } } Q ^ { \mathfrak { j } }$ , where $Q$ is a wedge of spectra $Q _ { v }$ indexed on a totally ordered set $\pmb { \mathcal { V } }$ (See [14, II§2] for more details of this analysis of extended powers of wedges.) Let $V$ run through the set of ordered $j$ -tuples of elements of $\pmb { \mathscr { Y } }$ ; these $V$ can be viewed as canonical elements in the distinct orbits of $\mathcal { V } ^ { j }$ under the permutation action of $\Sigma _ { \mathcal { I } }$ . For such a $V$ , let $( v ( 1 ) , \cdots , v ( n ) )$ be the distinct elements of $\pmb { \mathcal { V } }$ appearing in $V$ , let $v ( i )$ appear $j ( i )$ times, so that $\begin{array} { r } { j = \sum j ( i ) } \end{array}$ ; and let $\Sigma _ { V } \subset \Sigma _ { j }$ be the image of $\Sigma _ { \jmath ( 1 ) } \times \cdots \times \Sigma _ { \jmath ( n ) }$ under the block sum homomorphism. Then

$$
\mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma , \mathrm { ~ } } Q ^ { j } \cong \bigvee _ { V } \mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { V } } ( Q _ { v ( 1 ) } ^ { j ( 1 ) } \land \cdot \cdot \cdot \land Q _ { v ( n ) } ^ { j ( n ) } ) .
$$

Now suppose that each spectrum $Q _ { v }$ is of the form $X ( v ) \ltimes _ { G ( v ) } R ^ { k ( v ) }$ for some subgroup $G ( v )$ of $\Sigma _ { k ( v ) }$ and some $G ( v )$ -space $X ( v )$ over $\mathcal { L } ( k ( v ) )$ . Then canonical isomorphisms in I.2.2 imply that

$$
\mathcal { L } ( j ) \ltimes _ { \Sigma _ { V } } ( Q _ { v ( 1 ) } ^ { j ( 1 ) } \land \cdots \land Q _ { v ( n ) } ^ { j ( n ) } ) \cong A ( V ) \ltimes _ { G ( V ) } R ^ { k ( V ) } ,
$$

where $\begin{array} { r } { k ( V ) = \sum j ( i ) k ( v ( i ) ) , G ( V ) \subset \Sigma _ { k ( V ) } } \end{array}$ is the image under the canonical homomorphism of the product of wreath products

$$
\Sigma _ { j ( 1 ) } \int G _ { v ( 1 ) } \times \cdot \cdot \cdot \times \Sigma _ { j ( n ) } \int G _ { v ( n ) } ,
$$

and

$$
A ( V ) = \mathcal { L } ( j ) \times X _ { v ( 1 ) } ^ { j ( 1 ) } \times \cdot \cdot \cdot \times X _ { v ( n ) } ^ { j ( n ) }
$$

with its structural map $\gamma$ to $\mathcal { L } ( k ( V ) )$ . This implies that $\mathbb { C } ^ { q } R$ is a wedge of spectra of the form $A \ltimes _ { G } R ^ { j }$ , and it implicitly gives a complete inductive description of the relevant spaces $A$ and groups $G$ . The wedge summands are indexed by certain directed trees $T$ . Each vertex of $T$ has a prescribed level $_ i$ $1 \leq i \leq q$ . There is a unique vertex of level $q$ , there are directed edges from vertices of level $_ i$ to vertices of level $i - 1$ , and each vertex of level less than $q$ is the target of exactly one edge:

![](images/67573c71743dd94a29178aa6c3c47ccd97a1abf85c63e842b2dcdee97739770e.jpg)

Each vertex is labelled with some $\mathcal { L } ( j )$ , where, if the vertex has level greater than 1, then the vertex is the source of $j$ edges. (We allow $j = 0$ ,when the vertex is the source of no edges.) For such a tree $T$ , the space $A ( T )$ that is used to construct the corresponding wedge summand is the product of the labelling spaces $\mathcal { L } ( j )$ , ordered as prescribed by the tree and the inductive specification of the wedge summands given above. The degeneracy subspace lies in those wedge summands whose corresponding trees have all of their labels $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ at one or more levels, and it is obtained by replacing $\mathcal { L } ( 1 )$ by the point {1} in the labels of vertices at those levels. This proves the lemma.

# CHAPTER XIII

# Epilogue: The category of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra under $S$

In a previous draft of this paper, certain variants of smash products that are defined between L-spectra under $s$ played a central role. In the present version, the only vestige remaining is the definition of free modules over $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra given in II.5.3.

However, the parallel algebraic theory of [35] still requires such variant tensor products. We imagine that there is a functor, like the singular chain complex functor, from topological $A _ { \infty }$ and $E _ { \infty }$ rings and modules to algebraic ones. Such a construction would require the old definitions. The point is that, in algebra, it seems that one cannot hope to have an analogue of the isomorphism $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \cong S$ . The theory of [35] is based on the algebraic operad $\mathcal { C } = C _ { * } ( \mathcal { L } )$ , where $C _ { * }$ is the singular chain complex functor. Hopkins' lemma, I.5.4, carries over since $C _ { * }$ preserves split coequalizers. However, the relation $\mathcal { L } ( 2 ) / ( \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) \times \mathcal { L } ( 1 ) ) = \{ * \}$ does not carry over, and in fact one cannot have the relation $\mathcal { C } ( 2 ) \otimes _ { \mathcal { C } ( 1 ) \otimes \mathcal { C } ( 1 ) } \mathbb { Z } =$ $\mathbb { Z }$ in any $E _ { \infty }$ operad of (connected) chain complexes. Thus the topological theory is intrinsically better behaved algebraically than the parallel algebraic theory.

We explain just enough of the old definitions to give the idea and to explain how the new theory gives homotopical information about the old definitions.

# 1. The modified smash products $\triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } , \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } }$ , and $\star \mathcal { L }$

We return to the prologue and work in the category of $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra in this section. We shall leave all proofs as exercises for the reader. They are easy consequences of results in Chapter I. Let $\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \backslash S$ denote the category of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra under $s$ . We write $\eta$ generically for the given map $S \longrightarrow M$ .

DEFinitioN 1.1. Let $M$ be an L-spectrum under $s$ and let $N$ be an $\mathbb { L } _ { \star }$ spectrum. Define the mixed smash product $M \odot \mathcal { A } \ N$ to be the pushout displayed in the following diagram of L-spectra:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \xrightarrow { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N } \\ { \bigwedge _ { \begin{array} { c } { N } \\ { N } \end{array} } } \end{array}
$$

Define $N \vartriangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } M$ by symmetry.

If we apply the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to the diagram (1.2), we obtain a weakly equivalent pushout diagram whose left arrow is an isomorphism and whose right arrow is therefore also an isomorphism. This implies the basic relation

$$
S \wedge _ { \pm } ( M \odot _ { \pm } N ) \cong ( S \wedge _ { \pm } M ) \wedge _ { S } ( S \wedge _ { \pm } N ) ,
$$

which allows us to deduce homotopical properties of $\vartriangleleft$ from homotopical properties of $\wedge _ { S }$ . It also implies the following result.

PRoPosITioN 1.4. For any L-spectrum $N$ , the canonical map

$$
N \longrightarrow S \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } N
$$

is an isomorphism of L-spectra and the canonical map

$$
M \land \_ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow M \_ { \mathcal { L } } N
$$

is a weak equivalence of L-spectra.

For any $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum $N$ under $S$ , the canonical map

$$
S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow S \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } N
$$

is an isomorphism because $\lambda : S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \longrightarrow S$ is an isomorphism. Composing the inverse of this isomorphism with the unit weak equivalence $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow N$ , we obtain the following result.

PROPOsITION 1.5. For L-spectra $N$ under $S$ , there is a natural weak equivalence of L-spectra $\lambda : S \triangleright \mathcal { L } \ N \longrightarrow N$ .

LEMMA 1.6. Let $M$ and $N$ be L-spectra. Then

$$
( M \vee S ) \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong ( M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) \vee N .
$$

The commutativity and associativity of $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ imply the following commutativity and associativity isomorphisms relating $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ and $\triangleleft { \_ { } }$ ; these isomorphisms imply various others. The monad on ${ \mathcal { S } } [ \mathbb { L } ]$ whose algebras are the $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra under $S$ sends $M$ to $M \lor S$ ,hence the $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra $M \lor S$ are the free L-spectra under $S$ . Results like the following one can be proven by first checking them on the $M \lor S$ and then deducing them in general.

LEMMA 1.7. Let $M$ and $M ^ { \prime }$ be L-spectra under $S$ and let $N$ and $N ^ { \prime }$ be Lspectra. Then there are natural isomorphisms

$$
M \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong N \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } M ,
$$

$$
M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } ( N \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ^ { \prime } ) \cong ( M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N ^ { \prime } ,
$$

and

$$
M \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } ( N \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } M ^ { \prime } ) \cong ( M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } M ^ { \prime } .
$$

With a view towards generalization to arbitrary ground $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra, for which $R \land _ { \mathcal { L } , R } R$ will not be isomorphic to $R$ , we give the following definition in a form that does not rely on the isomorphism $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \cong S$ .

DEFInITION 1.8. Let $M$ and $N$ be $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra under $s$ The coproduct of $M$ and $N$ in $\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \backslash S$ is the pushout $M \cup _ { S } N$ . There is an analogous pushout

$$
( M \land \_ S ) \cup _ { S \land \_ S } ( S \land \_ S - N ) ,
$$

and the unit maps $\lambda$ determine a natural map of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra

$$
\lambda : ( M \wedge _ { \pm } S ) \cup _ { S \wedge _ { \pm } S } ( S \wedge _ { \pm } N ) \longrightarrow M \cup _ { S } N .
$$

The restrictions to $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S$ of the maps

i $\begin{array} { r } { \begin{array} { l l l l l l l } { \mathtt { d } \wedge \_ { \mathcal { L } } \eta : M \wedge \_ { \mathcal { L } } S \longrightarrow M \wedge \_ N } & { \mathrm { a n d } } & { \eta \wedge \_ { \mathcal { L } } \mathrm { i d } : S \wedge \_ S \wedge \longrightarrow M \wedge \_ N } & { } & { } & { } \end{array} } \end{array}$ coincide, hence these maps determine a map

$$
\theta : ( M \wedge _ { \pm } S ) \cup _ { S \wedge _ { \pm } S } ( S \wedge _ { \pm } N ) \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \pm } N .
$$

Define the unital operadic smash product $M \star _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ to be the pushout displayed in the following diagram of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra:

$$
\lim \limits _ { \substack { \emptyset \land \infty \land \infty } } \bigcup \limits _ { \substack { \mathcal { S } \land \mathcal { L } \mid } } \bigcup \substack { \mathcal { S } \land \mathcal { L } N } ) \xrightarrow { \lambda  M \cup _ { S } N }
$$

Then $M \star _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ is an $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum under $S$ with unit the composite of the unit $S \longrightarrow M \cup _ { S } N$ and the displayed canonical map $M \cup _ { S } N  M \star _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ .

The essential, obvious, point is that $S$ is a strict unit for the product $\star _ { \mathcal { L } }$ .

REMARK 1.9. Because $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \cong S ,$ . $\star _ { \mathcal { L } }$ can be defined less conceptually but more succinctly as the pushout in the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { ( M \land \swarrow S ) \lor ( S \land \ne N ) \xrightarrow { \lambda } M \lor N } } \\ { { \theta \brace { \Sigma \ } N \xrightarrow { \theta } N \qquad } } \\ { { M \land \underbrace { \mathcal { S } \ } N \xrightarrow { \Sigma } M \star \ L \land N . } } \end{array}
$$

An immediate comparison of pushout diagrams gives a natural map

$$
{ \cal M } \triangleright \_ { N } \longrightarrow { \cal M } \star \_ { \ell } \ N ,
$$

and a diagram chase shows that the product $\star _ { \mathcal { L } }$ can be constructed in terms of the product $\triangleright \varkappa$ .

LEMMA 1.10. If $M$ and $N$ are $\mathbb { L }$ -spectra under $S$ , then the following diagran is a pushout:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { S \triangleright S \le \ N \xrightarrow { \eta \triangleright \mathrm { i d } } M \triangleright S \le \ N } \\ { \quad \quad \overset { \lambda } { \underset { N } { \bigcup } } \qquad } \end{array}
$$

If $M$ is an L-spectrum and $N$ is an L-spectrum under $S$ , then

$$
( M \lor S ) \star _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong ( M \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) \lor N .
$$

Applying the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to the diagram (1.11) and using (1.3), we find that

$$
S \wedge _ { \pm } ( M \star _ { \pm } N ) \cong ( S \wedge _ { \pm } M ) \wedge _ { S } ( S \wedge _ { \pm } N ) .
$$

Again, homotopical properties of $\star \mathcal { L }$ can be deduced from homotopical properties of $\wedge _ { S }$ , and we have the following result.

PROPOSITION 1.13. The canonical map

$$
{ \cal M } \triangleright \_ { N } \longrightarrow { \cal M } \star \_ { \ell } \ N \quad
$$

is a weak equivalence of L-spectra.

LEMMA 1.14. Let $M$ and $N$ be L-spectra. Then

$$
( M \lor S ) \star _ { \pm } ( N \lor S ) \cong ( M \land _ { \pm } N ) \lor M \lor N \lor S .
$$

LEMMA 1.15. The following associativity relation holds, where $M$ and $M ^ { \prime }$ are L-spectra under $S$ and $N$ is an L-spectrum:

$$
( M \star \_ { \mathcal { L } } M ^ { \prime } ) \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M ^ { \prime } \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } N ) .
$$

THEOREM 1.16. The category $\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \backslash S$ is symmetric monoidal under $\star _ { \mathcal { L } }$ . The categories of monoids and commutative monoids in $\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \backslash S$ are isomorphic to the categories of $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectra and $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectra.

We have a mixed function $\mathbb { L }$ spectrum (but not a unital operadic one).

DEFINITION 1.17. Let $M$ be an $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum under $s$ and $N$ be an L-spectrum. Define $F _ { \mathcal { L } } ^ { \triangleright } ( M , N )$ to be the $\mathbb { L }$ -spectrum displayed in the following pullback diagram:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { F _ { \mathcal { L } } ^ { \triangleright } ( M , N ) \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( M , N ) } \\ & { \everymath { \displaystyle } \int _ { \vphantom { N } } \overline { { \vartheta } } ^ { \triangleright } } \\ & { \everymath { \displaystyle } N \longrightarrow F _ { \mathcal { L } } ( S , N ) ; } \end{array}
$$

here the bottom arrow is adjoint to $\lambda \tau : N \wedge _ { \pm } S \cong S \wedge _ { \pm } N \longrightarrow N .$

PROPOsITION 1.18. Let $M$ be an L-spectrum under $S$ and $\boldsymbol { L }$ and $N$ be Lspectra. Then

$$
\mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \big ( M \mathbin { \odot } \mathscr { L } , N \big ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \big ( L \mathbin { \vartriangle } \mathscr { L } \ M , N \big ) \cong \mathcal { S } [ \mathbb { L } ] \big ( L , F _ { \mathcal { L } } ^ { \flat } \big ( M , N \big ) \big ) .
$$

# 2. The modified smash products $\triangleleft _ { R } , \triangleright _ { R }$ , and $\star _ { R }$

We assume that $R$ is an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum in this section, and we understand modules in the sense of II.3.3. Thus the unit weak equivalences are not required to be isomorphisms. The cell and CW theory of such $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } }$ -modules is developed in the same fashion as for modules over $S$ -algebras. The appropriate definition of a smash product over $\scriptstyle { R }$ in this context reads as follows.

DEfinitioN 2.1. Let $\scriptstyle { R }$ be an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum and let $M$ be a right and $N$ be a left $\scriptstyle { R }$ module. Define $M \land \_ { \pmb { \mathscr { k } } , R } N$ to be the coequalizer displayed in the following diagram of $\mathcal { L }$ -spectra:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left( M \triangleright \_ { \mathcal { L } } \ R \right) \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( R \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \ N \right) \xrightarrow [ \mathrm { i d } \triangle \nu ] { \mu \wedge \mathrm { i d } } \ M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } , R } N , } \end{array}
$$

where $\pmb { \mu }$ and $\nu$ are the given actions of $\scriptstyle { R }$ on $M$ and $N$ ; the canonical isomorphism of the terms on the left is implied by Lemma 1.7.

When $R = S$ . $M \triangleright _ { \mathcal { L } } \ S \cong M$ . $S \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } N \cong N$ , and we are coequalizing the same isomorphism. Therefore our new $M \land _ { \mathcal { L } , S } N$ coincides with our old $M \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } N$ . We have used the notation $\land _ { \mathcal { L } , R }$ to emphasize the conceptual point that we are here generalizing $\wedge _ { \mathcal { L } }$ rather than $\wedge _ { S }$ .

REMARk 2.2. We have given the definition in the form most convenient for proofs, because the displayed coequalizer is split. However it is equivalent to define $M \land _ { \mathcal { L } , R } N$ more intuitively as the coequalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
M \land \_ R \land \_ N \xrightarrow { \mu \land \mathrm { i d } } M \land \_ N \longrightarrow M \land \_ R \longrightarrow M \land \_ R . N .
$$

We can define modified smash products $\triangleleft _ { R } , \triangleright _ { R }$ ,and $\star _ { R }$ when one or both of the variables comes with a given map of $R$ -modules $\eta : R \longrightarrow M$ , copying Definitions 1.1 and 1.8 with $S$ replaced by $R$ .

DEfIniTioN 2.3. Let $M$ be a right $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module under $R$ and let $N$ be a left $\scriptstyle { R } .$ . module. Define the mixed smash product $M \ < _ { R } N$ to be the pushout displayed in the following diagram of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { R \wedge \underbrace { \mathscr { L } . R } _ { \mathcal { L } . R } N \xrightarrow { \eta \wedge \mathrm { i d } } M \wedge \underbrace { \mathscr { L } . R } _ { \mathcal { L } . R } N } \\ { \lambda \Bigg \downarrow \qquad } \\ { N \xrightarrow [ N ] { \qquad } M \ < \mathscr { Q } _ { R } N . } \end{array}
$$

Define $\vartriangleright _ { R }$ by symmetry. Observe that the displayed pushout is a diagram of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules when $\scriptstyle { R }$ is an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum.

DEfinition 2.4. Let $M$ be a right and $N$ a left $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module under $\scriptstyle { R }$ Define the unital smash product $M \star _ { R } N$ to be the pushout displayed in the following

diagram of $\mathbb { L }$ spectra:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { ( M \land _ { \pm , R } R ) \cup _ { R \land \pm , R } ( R \land _ { \pm , R } N ) \xrightarrow { \lambda  M \cup _ { R } N } } \\ { \theta \Bigg \downarrow \qquad } \\ { M \land _ { \pm , R } N \xrightarrow { \lambda  M \star _ { R } N } \qquad M \star _ { R } N . } \end{array}
$$

Here, as in Definition 1.8, $\lambda$ is induced by the unit maps $\lambda$ of $M$ and $N$ and $\theta$ is induced by the structure maps $\eta$ of $M$ and $N$ . Observe that the displayed pushout is a diagram of $R$ -modules when $R$ is an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum.

All of the results of the previous section apply verbatim in the context of $R$ -modules, except for those that depend on the isomorphism $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } S \cong S$ .

LEMMA 2.5. Let $M$ be a right and $N$ be a left $R$ -module. Then

$$
( M \lor R ) \triangleleft _ { R } N \cong ( M \land \pmb { \mathscr { s } } , R ) \lor N
$$

and

$$
( M \lor R ) \star _ { R } ( N \lor R ) \cong ( M \land \_ { \mathcal { L } , R } N ) \lor M \lor N \lor R .
$$

If $N$ is an $R$ -module under $R$ , then

$$
( M \lor R ) \star _ { R } N \cong ( M \triangleright _ { R } N ) \lor N .
$$

If $M$ and $N$ are $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules under $R$ , then the following diagram is a pushout, where the unit map $\lambda : R \triangleright _ { R } N \longrightarrow N$ is constructed by a comparison of pushout diagrams:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { R \triangleright R \ N \xrightarrow { \eta \triangleright \mathrm { i d } } M \triangleright R \ N } \\ { \overset { \lambda } { \underset { N } { \neg } } \Big \downarrow \qquad } \\ { \qquad N \xrightarrow { \qquad } M \star _ { R } N . } \end{array}
$$

Using this, we can deduce alternative expressions for these products in terms of coequalizer diagrams like that which defines $\land _ { \mathcal { L } , R }$ .

LEMMA 2.6. For a right $R$ -module $M$ under $R$ and a left $R$ -module $N , M \triangleleft _ { R }$ $N$ can be identified with the coequalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { ( M \star \mathcal { L } \ R ) \ \triangleleft _ { \mathcal { L } } \ N \cong M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \ ( R \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \ N ) \xrightarrow [ \mathrm { i d } \triangle \nu ] { \mu { \star \mathcal { L } } \vec { \nu } } M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow M \triangle _ { R } \ N . } \end{array}
$$

For a right $R$ -module $M$ under $\scriptstyle { R }$ and a left $R$ -module $N$ under $\scriptstyle { R }$ $\mathrm { ~ ? ~ } , M \star _ { R } N$ can be identified with the coequalizer displayed in the diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left( M \star \_ { \mathcal { L } } \ R \right) \star \_ { \mathcal { L } } \ N \cong M \star _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( R \star _ { \mathcal { L } } \ N \right) \xrightarrow [ \mathrm { i d } \star \nu ] { \frac { \mu \star \mathrm { i d } } { \mathrm { i d } \star \nu } } \ \sum M \star _ { \mathcal { L } } N \longrightarrow M \star _ { R } \ N . } \end{array}
$$

PRoPosITIoN 2.7. The following statements hold.

i) For any $R$ -module $N$ , the canonical map of $R$ -modules

$$
N \longrightarrow R \triangle _ { R } N
$$

is an isomorphism.

(ii) For any R-module $M$ under $R$ , the canonical map of $R$ -modules

$$
M \land _ { \pm , R } N \longrightarrow M \triangle _ { R } N
$$

is a weak equivalence.

(ii) For any $R$ -module $N$ under $\scriptstyle { R }$ , the canonical map of $R$ modules

$$
\lambda : R \triangleright _ { R } N \longrightarrow N
$$

is a weak equivalence.

(iv) For any R-modules $M$ and $N$ under $R$ , the canonical map of R-modules

$$
{ \cal M } \triangleright _ { R } { \cal N } \longrightarrow { \cal M } \star _ { R } { \cal N }
$$

is a weak equivalence.

We have various commutativity and associativity isomorphisms that involve several $A _ { \infty }$ rings, as in III.3.4. Note that an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule is the same thing as an $( R * \mathcal { L } R ^ { \prime 0 \mathcal { P } } )$ -module, and an $( R \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime 0 p } )$ -module under $( R \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime 0 \mathcal { P } } )$ is both a right $R$ -module under $R$ and a left $R ^ { \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime }$ .

PROPOSITION 2.8. Let $M$ be an $( R , R ^ { \prime } )$ -bimodule, $N$ be an $( R ^ { \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule, and $P$ be an $( R ^ { \prime \prime } , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodule.

() If $M$ is an $R ^ { \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime }$ , then

$$
M \triangleleft _ { R ^ { \prime } } N \cong N \triangleright _ { R ^ { \prime o p } } M
$$

as $R \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime \prime } { } ^ { o p }$ -modules and

$$
M \triangle _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( N \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P ) \cong ( M \triangle _ { R ^ { \prime } } N ) \wedge _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P
$$

as $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodules.

ii) If $M$ is an $R ^ { \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime }$ and $P$ is an $\mathcal { R } ^ { \prime \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime \prime }$ , then

$$
M \triangleleft _ { R ^ { \prime } } ( N \triangleright _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P ) \cong ( M \triangleleft _ { R ^ { \prime } } N ) \triangleright _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P
$$

as $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodules.

(ii) If $M$ is an $R ^ { \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime }$ and $N$ is an $( R ^ { \prime } { \star } _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime \prime ^ { o p } } )$ -module under $( R ^ { \prime } { \star } _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime \prime ^ { o p } } )$ , then

$$
( M \star _ { R ^ { \prime } } N ) \triangleleft _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P \cong M \triangleleft _ { R ^ { \prime } } \left( N \triangleleft _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P \right)
$$

as $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodules.

iv) If $M$ is an $R ^ { \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime }$ , $N$ is an $( R ^ { \prime } \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime \prime } { } ^ { o p } )$ -module under $( R ^ { \prime } \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime \prime ^ { o p } } )$ , and $P$ is an $R ^ { \prime \prime }$ -module under $R ^ { \prime \prime }$ , then

$$
\left( M \star _ { R ^ { \prime } } N \right) \star _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P \cong M \star _ { R ^ { \prime } } \left( N \star _ { R ^ { \prime \prime } } P \right)
$$

as $( R , R ^ { \prime \prime \prime } )$ -bimodules.

ProoF. This is a formal exercise in the commutation of coequalizers with coequalizers, starting with the analogous isomorphisms for our various smash products over $S$ . One writes down three-by-three diagrams of coequalizers and uses that the coequalizer of coequalizers is a coequalizer vertically and horizontally to deduce the stated isomorphisms. The top left-hand corner of the diagram needed for the isomorphism of (ii), for example, is

$$
\left( \left( \left( M \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime } \right) \star _ { \mathcal { L } } N \right) \star _ { \mathcal { L } } R ^ { \prime \prime } \right) \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } P \cong M \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( R ^ { \prime } \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( N \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \left( R ^ { \prime \prime } \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } P \right) \right) \right)
$$

Similarly, all other results of $\operatorname { I I I } \ S \ S 3 – 4$ carry over directly to the present context, and we reach the following conclusion in the commutative case.

THEOREM 2.9. The category of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules under $R$ is symmetric monoidal under $\star _ { R }$ .

We can define $R$ -algebras and commutative $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras to be monoids and commutative monoids in this symmetric monoidal category. Of course, if we apply the functor $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } ( - )$ to such algebras we obtain weakly equivalent algebras over the $S .$ algebra $S \wedge _ { \mathcal { L } } R$ .

Again, we have a mixed function $R$ -spectrum giving an adjunction like Proposition 1.18.

DEFInITIoN 2.10. Let $M$ be an $R \mathrm { . }$ -module under $R$ and let $N$ be an $\scriptstyle { R } .$ . spectrum. Define $F _ { R } ^ { \triangleright } ( M , N )$ to be the $\mathbb { L }$ specrum displayed in the following pullback diagram:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } {  { F _ { R } ^ { \triangleright } ( M , N ) \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( M , N ) } } \\ & { \oint _ { \cal N } \psi ^ { \quad } } \\ & { N \longrightarrow F _ { R } ( R , N ) ; } \end{array}
$$

here the bottom arrow is adjoint to $\lambda \tau : N \wedge _ { R } R \cong R \wedge _ { R } N \longrightarrow N$ .If $R$ is an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum, then $F _ { R } ^ { \triangleright } ( M , N )$ is an $R$ -module.

REMARk 2.11. Geometric realization behaves as expected. If $L _ { * }$ and $L _ { * } ^ { \prime }$ are simplicial $R$ -modules under $R$ and $K _ { * }$ is a simplicial $R$ module, then

$$
| L _ { * } | \ \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } \ | K _ { * } | \cong | L _ { * } \triangle _ { \mathcal { L } } K _ { * } |
$$

and

$$
\lvert L _ { * } \rvert \star _ { \mathcal { L } } \lvert L _ { * } ^ { \prime } \rvert \cong \lvert L _ { * } \star _ { \mathcal { L } } L _ { * } ^ { \prime } \rvert .
$$

Similarly our work on enriched model categories carries over to the present framework. There is an analogue of VII.2.8 for the category of $R$ -modules under $R$ .Here we must enrich over the category of unbased spaces. Recall that a colimit is said to be connected if it is indexed on a diagram whose domain category is connected.

PROPosITION 2.12. The category of $R$ -modules under $\scriptstyle { R }$ is topologically cocomplete and complete. The cotensors $F ( X _ { + } , E )$ and all other indexed limits are created in $\mathcal { S }$ ; ordinary connected colimits are also created in $\mathcal { S }$ . For an $\scriptstyle { R }$ . module $N$ , the functor $M \triangleleft _ { R } N$ on $R$ -modules $M$ under $R$ preserves connected colimits. For an $\scriptstyle { R }$ -module $N$ under $\scriptstyle { R }$ , the functor $M \star _ { R } N$ on $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules M under $R$ preserves connected colimits.

PROOF. The colimit, $\mathsf { c o l i m } _ { r e l R } D$ of a diagram $D$ of $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } } .$ modules under $\scriptstyle { R }$ is computed from its colimit as a diagram of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -modules via the pushout

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \ c o \operatorname* { l i m } \underline { { { R } } } \longrightarrow R } } \\ { { \downarrow } } \\ { { \qquad \atop { \mathrm { c o l i m } \ D \mathrm { ~ ( --- ~ c o l i m } _ { r e l R } \ D ; } } } \end{array}
$$

the right vertical arrow gives the unit. Here $\underline { { R } }$ is the constant diagram at $R$ . The top horizontal arrow is an isomorphism if the domain category of $D$ is connected, and the relative and ordinary colimits colimits then agree. Since this holds for coequalizers, VII.2.6 and 2.8 imply the first statement. The remaining statements follow since the products $\vartriangleleft$ and $\star$ are defined in terms of $( - ) \land { \mathcal { \pm } } , R ^ { N }$ and pushouts, which preserve colimits of $\scriptstyle { R }$ modules.

THeoREM 2.13. For any $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ the categories of $\scriptstyle { R }$ -algebras and of commutative $R$ -algebras are topologically cocomplete and complete. Their cotensors and all other indexed limits are created in $\mathcal { S }$ .

Similarly, all categories in sight admit model structures.

THEoREM 2.14. The categories of modules over an $A _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ and of algebras and commutative algebras over an $E _ { \infty }$ ring spectrum $R$ are topological model categories. In all cases, the weak equivalences and $q$ -fibrations are the maps which are weak equivalences or Serre fibrations of underlying spectra.

# APPENDIX A

# Twisted half-smash products and function spectra

by Michael Cole

# 1. Introduction

Let $U , U ^ { \prime }$ be universes, $\pmb { \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ the space of linear isometries $U \longrightarrow U ^ { \prime }$ , $A$ a space, $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ a map, and let $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ be spectra. The twisted half-smash product $\alpha \propto E \in { \mathcal { S } } U ^ { \prime }$ and the twisted function spectrum $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ are fundamental constructions which underly the foundations of stable homotopy theory that were introduced in [38] and developed further in this book. These functors specialize to the change of universe functors that are necessary to define internal smash products and function spectra, and they provide a simple way to prove that the different internal smash products so obtained are canonically and coherently equivalent upon passage to the stable category.

In connection with the research presented in this book, the theory of twisted half-smash products has undergone major clarification and sharpening of the results originally given in [38]. In particular, the improved "untwisting theorem", given as Theorem 5.5 below, has led to corresponding improvements of the theorems concerning the homotopy invariance properties of $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ in the variable $\alpha$ - the crucial point being that for tame spectra $E$ , the homotopical properties of $\alpha \ltimes E$ depend only on the homotopical properties of the space $A$ rather than on the particular map $\alpha$ . In particular, it is vital to the theory that for tame spectra $E$ the functor $\alpha \times E$ is well-behaved homotopically before passage to the stable category.

We present new definitions of twisted half-smash products and function spectra which have considerable advantages, both conceptually and expositionally, compared to the definitions in [38]. In particular, our definitions do not require choosing arbitrary cofinal sequences of representations and therefore our treatment avoids the technical complications concerning the behavior of colimits found in the approach of [38]. We shall recast much of the material of [38, $\mathrm { V I } \ S \ S 1 - 3 ]$ in a simpler, more user-friendly, form.

The treatment of twisted half-smash products and function spectra in [38] is written in the equivariant context, with a compact Lie group $G$ acting on all spaces and spectra in sight. We present our definitions and theorems nonequivariantly here and note that virtually everything that we say carries over mutatis mutandis to the equivariant case. We will point out the small exceptions to this at the end.

We give preliminary definitions and constructions in Sections 2-4, reaching the definition of twisted half-smash products and function spectra in Section 5. That section also gives a simple proof of the untwisting theorem, which describes an isomorphism $\alpha \ltimes E \cong E \land A _ { + }$ when the spectrum $\boldsymbol { \varepsilon }$ is a finite desuspension of a space. Our proof relies on an idea of Neil Strickland. We give some formal properties of our functors in Section 6 and consider their homotopical properties in Section 7. In Section 8 we prove a cofibration theorem that plays an important technical role in the theory of $S _ { \ l }$ -modules. We shall derive it from the untwisting theorem. We conclude in Section 9 with a brief discussion of the equivariant versions of our constructions; more details will appear in [16].

# 2. The category $\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$

To define our constructions we introduce a category $\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ . An object $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ is a family of spectra $\mathcal { \delta } _ { V } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ , one for each indexing space $V \subset U$ We require isomorphisms $\rho _ { V , W } : \Sigma ^ { W - V } { \widehat { \mathcal { O } } } _ { W } \longrightarrow { \widehat { \mathcal { O } } } _ { V }$ , $V \subset W$ ,that satisfy the evident transitivity relation. Here we are mixing universes since we are suspending the spectrum $\pmb { \mathcal { O } } _ { W }$ which is indexed on $U ^ { \prime }$ by the indexing space $W - V$ which is in $U$ , but in any case $\Sigma ^ { W - V } \mathcal { E } _ { W }$ just means the usual smash product of the spectrum $\pmb { \mathcal { O } } _ { W }$ with the space $S ^ { W - V }$ . We think of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } }$ as the spectrum $\mathcal { E } _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ , an object stable with respect to suspension by indexing spaces in $U ^ { \prime }$ , but also equipped with a choice of compatible desuspensions by indexing spaces in $U$ .

Example 2.1. If $U ^ { \prime } = U$ and $X$ is a space, we get an associated object ${ \mathcal { E } } ( X ) \in { \mathcal { S } } ( U ; U )$ by setting ${ \mathcal { E } } ( X ) _ { V } ~ = ~ \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X$ and considering the canonical natural isomorphisms $\Sigma ^ { W - V } \Sigma _ { W } ^ { \infty } X \cong \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X$ as structure maps.

ExAMPLE 2.2. Generalizing Example 2.1, let $f : U \longrightarrow U ^ { \prime }$ be a linear isometry and $X$ a space. The specification ${ \mathcal { E } } _ { f } ( X ) _ { V } = \Sigma _ { f V } ^ { \infty } X$ defines an object $\pmb { \mathscr { E } } _ { f } ( X ) \in$ $\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ with structure maps given by $\Sigma ^ { W - \bar { V _ { \ L } } } \Sigma _ { f W } ^ { \infty } X \cong \Sigma ^ { f W - f V } \Sigma _ { f W } ^ { \infty } X \cong$ $\Sigma _ { f V } ^ { \infty } X$ .

We will prove shortly that a map $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ gives rise to an object $\mathcal { M } \alpha \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ for which $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { 0 } \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } A _ { + }$ . The construction is natural in spaces over $\pmb { \mathcal { S } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ . If $_ \alpha$ is the constant map at $f \in { \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ then $\mathcal { M } \alpha$ is the object discussed in Example 2.2 with $X = A _ { + }$ .

We will define a smash product

$$
\wedge : \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U ) \times \mathcal { S } U \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }
$$

and a function spectrum

$$
F ( - , - ) : \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U ) ^ { \circ \circ } \times \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U
$$

satisfying an adjunction isomorphism

$$
\mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { \ell } \wedge E , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { S } U ( E , F ( \mathcal { \ell } , E ^ { \prime } ) ) ,
$$

where $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ , $\pmb { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ . For a map $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ and spectra $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ we will define

$$
\alpha \ltimes E = { \mathcal { M } } \alpha \wedge E
$$

and

$$
F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) = F ( \mathcal { M } \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )
$$

and show that this agrees with the definitions of [38].

# 3. Smash products and function spectra

We first record the following obvious fact.

PRoPosITION 3.1. For an object $\mathcal { E } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ and a space $X \in \mathcal { T }$ there is a smash product $\mathcal { E } \wedge X \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ defined by $( \mathcal { E } \wedge X ) _ { V } = \mathcal { E } _ { V } \wedge X$ and the evident structure maps. There is an adjunction isomorphism

$$
\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U ) ( \mathcal { \epsilon } \wedge X , \mathcal { D } ) \cong \mathcal { T } ( X , \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U ) ( \mathcal { \epsilon } , \mathcal { D } ) )
$$

where the morphism set $\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U ) ( \mathscr { E } , \mathscr { D } )$ is given the evident topology and basepoint.

Although not obvious from the definitions, it can be shown that there is a function object" $F ( X , { \mathcal { D } } ) \in { \mathcal { S } } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ such that the adjunction extends in the expected way.

We now define the object $F ( \mathcal { \epsilon } , E ^ { \prime } )$ .

DEfInition 3.2. For objects $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ and $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ the spectrum $F ( \mathcal { E } , E ^ { \prime } ) \in \mathcal { S } U$ is given by $F ( \acute { \varepsilon } , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V ) = \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \acute { \varepsilon } _ { V } , E ^ { \prime } )$ Abbreviating $\mathcal { S } ^ { \prime } =$ ${ \mathcal { S } } U ^ { \prime }$ , the structure maps are given by the sequences of isomorphisms

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathcal { S } ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { E } _ { V } , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { S } ^ { \prime } ( \Sigma ^ { W - V } \mathcal { E } _ { W } , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { S } ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { E } _ { W } , \Omega ^ { W - V } E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \Omega ^ { W - V } \mathcal { S } ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { E } _ { W } , E ^ { \prime } ) . } \end{array}
$$

For $\mathcal { E } ^ { \mathrm { ~ \scriptsize ~ \in ~ } } \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ and $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , our definition of $\mathcal { E } \wedge E \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ is dictated by the desired adjunction (2.3). Recall that for a spectrum $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ and prespectrum $D \in { \mathcal { P } } U$ , the morphism set $\mathcal { P } U ( D , E )$ may be described by

$$
{ \mathcal { P } } U ( D , E ) = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V \subset U } { \mathcal { T } } ( D V , E V ) ,
$$

where the limit is taken over the maps

$$
{ \mathcal { I } } ( D W , E W ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { I } } ( \Sigma ^ { W - V } D V , E W ) \cong { \mathcal { I } } ( D V , \Omega ^ { W - V } E W ) \cong { \mathcal { I } } ( D V , E V ) .
$$

It follows that

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { I } U ( E , F ( \mathcal { E } , E ^ { \prime } ) ) = \operatorname* { l i m } \mathcal { I } ( E V , F ( \mathcal { E } , E ^ { \prime } ) V ) } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad = \operatorname* { l i m } \mathcal { I } ( E V , \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { E } _ { V } , E ^ { \prime } ) ) } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad = \operatorname* { l i m } \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { E } _ { V } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } ) } \\ & { \quad \quad \quad = \mathcal { I } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } \ \mathcal { E } _ { V } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } ) , } \end{array}
$$

where the colimit is taken over the maps

$$
\acute { \varepsilon _ { V } } \wedge E V \cong \Sigma ^ { W - V } \acute { \sigma _ { W } } \wedge E V \cong \acute { \varepsilon _ { W } } \wedge \Sigma ^ { W - V } E V \longrightarrow \acute { \sigma _ { W } } \wedge E W .
$$

Hence our definition of $\mathcal { E } \wedge E$ .

DEfinition 3.5. If $\pmb { \mathcal { \mathcal { G } } } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ and $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , then $\mathcal { \mathcal { E } } \wedge E \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ is the spectrum $\scriptstyle \operatorname { o l i m } _ { V \subset U } { \mathcal { E } } _ { V } \wedge E V$ where the colimit is taken over the maps (3.4).

We have contrived our definitions to make the following true.

PRoPosiTioN 3.6. There is an adjunction isomorphism

$$
\mathcal { H } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { E } \wedge E , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { S } U ( E , F ( \mathcal { E } , E ^ { \prime } ) ) .
$$

The following result is easy.

PRopositIon 3.7. If $\pmb { \mathcal { \mathcal { E } } } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ , $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , and $X \in \mathcal { T }$ there are natural isomorphisms

$$
( \mathcal { E } \wedge E ) \wedge X \cong \mathcal { E } \wedge ( E \wedge X ) \cong ( \mathcal { E } \wedge X ) \wedge E .
$$

In practice if a spectrum $E$ is the spectrification $_ { L D }$ of a prespectrum $D$ , it is often useful to describe $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } \wedge E$ as a colimit involving the spaces $D V$ rather than the spaces $E V$ . A simple adjunction argument together with (3.3) proves the following result.

PROpositION 3.8. If $D \in { \mathcal { P } } U$ is a prespectrum and $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ then

$$
\boldsymbol { \mathcal { E } } \wedge L D \cong \operatorname { c o l i m } _ { V \subset U } \ \boldsymbol { \mathcal { E } } _ { V } \wedge D V
$$

where the colimit is taken over the maps (3.4) (replacing $E$ by $D$ ).

In particular, if $E$ is the desuspension spectrum $\Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X$ of a space $X$ , this has the following consequence.

PRoPosiTion 3.9. For a fixed indexing space $V \subset U$ there is an isomorphism

$$
{ \mathcal { E } } \wedge \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X \cong { \mathcal { E } } _ { V } \wedge X
$$

that is natural in $\hat { \mathcal { O } }$ and $X$ .

ProoF. By Proposition 3.8 we see that

$$
\boldsymbol { \mathcal { E } } ^ { \circ } \wedge \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X \cong \operatorname { c o l i m } _ { W \subset U } \ \boldsymbol { \mathcal { E } } _ { W } \wedge \Sigma ^ { W - V } X .
$$

But clearly, for $V \subset W$ the structure map

$$
\oint _ { \cal V } \wedge X \cong \Sigma ^ { W - V } \mathcal { E } _ { W } \wedge X \cong \mathcal { E } _ { W } \wedge \Sigma ^ { W - V } X
$$

is an isomorphism. Hence the colimit stabilizes at $V$ and the claim follows.

# 4. The object $\mathcal { M } \alpha \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$

Let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { g } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be a map. For any $V \subset U$ and $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ , let ${ \mathit { A } } _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ denote the pullback

$$
\begin{array} { c c } { { A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \xrightarrow { A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \xrightarrow { A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } } } \mathcal { I } ( V , V ^ { \prime } ) } } \\ { { \downarrow } } & { { } } \\ { { A \xrightarrow { \downarrow } \xrightarrow { } \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { I } } ( V , U ^ { \prime } ) } } \end{array}
$$

Thus $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ is the set of $a \in A$ such that $\alpha ( a ) V \subset V ^ { \prime }$ Note that $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ will often be empty (for example, if $V ^ { \prime }$ is too small). Note that $A _ { 0 , V ^ { \prime } } = A$ for any $V ^ { \prime }$ . If $V \subset W$ then $A _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } \subset A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ . If $V ^ { \prime } \subset W ^ { \prime }$ then $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \subset A _ { V , W ^ { \prime } }$ and, for fixed $V$ , $\textstyle A = \bigcup _ { V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime } } A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ . In particular, if $A$ is compact, or more generally has compact image, then, for fixed $V$ , we see that $A = A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ for large $V ^ { \prime }$ .

Now for any $V , V ^ { \prime }$ let $\eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ denote the vector bundle over $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ with total space

$$
E ( \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } ) = \{ ( a , v ) \in A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \times V ^ { \prime } \mid v ^ { \prime } \perp \alpha ( a ) V \} .
$$

Let $T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ be the Thom space of $\eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ . In the case that $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } = \emptyset$ then $\eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ is the empty bundle $\emptyset \longrightarrow \emptyset$ and, by convention, the Thom space is a single point.

OBSERVATION 4.1. For fixed $V$ . $\left\{ T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \right\}$ is a prespectrum indexed over $U ^ { \prime }$ , which we will denote by ${ \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { V }$ The structure maps $\Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow T \alpha _ { V , W ^ { \prime } }$ are induced by the evident vector bundle morphisms

$$
\eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \oplus ( W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } ) \cong \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , W ^ { \prime } } | _ { A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } } \longrightarrow \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , W ^ { \prime } } .
$$

OBSERVATION 4.2. For fixed $V ^ { \prime }$ we have maps $\Sigma ^ { W - V } T \alpha _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ that are induced by the evident vector bundle morphisms

$$
\eta ( \alpha ) _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } \oplus ( W - V ) \cong \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } | _ { A _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } } \longrightarrow \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } .
$$

Thus for each $V \subset U$ we have a prespectrum $\mathcal { T } \alpha _ { V } \in \mathcal { P } U ^ { \prime }$ and we have maps of prespectra $\Sigma ^ { W - V } { \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { W } \longrightarrow { \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { V }$ that satisfy the evident transitivity condition. Let $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ be the spectrification of ${ \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { V }$ .Then we have maps of spectra $\Sigma ^ { W - V } \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { W ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V }$ that satisfy the transitivity condition.
