PROPOSITION 4.3. The maps $\Sigma ^ { W - V } \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { W } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V }$ are isomorphisms of spectra. Hence $\mathcal { M } \alpha$ is an object in the category $\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ .

PROOF. For a compact $K \subset A$ let $\alpha | _ { K }$ be the composite $K \longrightarrow A { \stackrel { \alpha } { \longrightarrow } } { \mathcal { S } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ Since, as always, we are working in the category of compactly generated spaces, $A$ is topologized as the union of its compact subspaces and it follows that in the category of spaces over $\pmb { \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ , $\alpha = \mathsf { c o l i m } _ { K } \alpha | _ { K }$ where the colimit is taken over all compact $K \subset A$ . All constructions we have made are natural in spaces over ${ \pmb { \mathcal { I } } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ , and they commute with filtered colimits. In particular, ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { V } = { \mathsf { c o l i m } } _ { K }$ $\mathcal { M } ( \alpha \vert _ { K } ) _ { V }$ and, of course, $\Sigma ^ { W - V }$ commutes with colimits. Hence it suffices to show that the map $\Sigma ^ { W - V } \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { W } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V }$ is an isomorphism when $A$ is compact.

Assume then that $A$ is compact. Fix $V \subset W \subset U$ and choose $V ^ { \prime }$ large enough that $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } = A _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } = A$ Then for $V ^ { \prime } \subset W ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ the structure maps $\Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow T \alpha _ { V , W ^ { \prime } }$ are isomorphisms. Hence ${ \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { V }$ agrees cofinally with the desuspension prespectrum $\{ \Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \}$ and thus $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ . Similarly $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { W } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { W , V ^ { \prime } }$ and we have the sequence of isomorphisms

$$
\begin{array} { r } { { \Sigma } ^ { W - V } \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { W } \cong { \Sigma } ^ { W - V } { \Sigma } _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } \cong { \Sigma } _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } { \Sigma } ^ { W - V } T \alpha _ { W , V ^ { \prime } } \cong { \Sigma } _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \cong \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } . } \end{array}
$$

LEMMA 4.4. There is an isomorphism $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { 0 } \cong \Sigma ^ { \infty } A _ { + }$ that is natural in $\alpha$ .

Proor. It is immediate from the definitions that $\eta ( \alpha ) _ { 0 , V ^ { \prime } }$ is the trivial bundle $A \times V ^ { \prime }$ over $A$ and hence ${ \cal T } \alpha _ { 0 , V ^ { \prime } } \cong \Sigma ^ { V ^ { \prime } } A _ { + }$ .

Together with the fact that $\Sigma ^ { V } \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { 0 }$ , this leads one to suspect that ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { V }$ is isomorphic to a shift desuspension of the space $A _ { + }$ . That turns out to be the case and, in our version of twisted half-smash products, this is the explanation for the untwisting theorem which we will discuss in the next section.

LEMMA 4.5 (UNTWISTING). Let $V \subset U$ and $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ be indexing spaces such that $V \cong V ^ { \prime }$ and let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be a map. There is an isomorphism $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } A _ { + }$ that is natural in $\alpha$ .

PROOF. Let ${ \mathcal { O } } ( U ^ { \prime } )$ be the orthogonal group of linear isometric isomorphisms $U ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow U ^ { \prime }$ that are the identity of a finite subspace. Since the restriction map $r ^ { \prime } : { \mathcal { O } } ( U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow { \mathcal { I } } ( V ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime } )$ is a bundle over the contractible paracompact space ${ \mathcal { I } } ( V ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime } )$ , it admits a section $s ^ { \prime } : \mathcal { I } ( V ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { O } ( U ^ { \prime } )$ Fixing an isomorphism $f : V \longrightarrow V ^ { \prime }$ , we obtain a homeomorphism $f ^ { * } : \mathcal { I } ( V ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( V , U ^ { \prime } )$ Hence we have a composite

$$
s = s ^ { \prime } \circ ( f ^ { * } ) ^ { - 1 } : \mathcal { I } ( V , U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \mathcal { O } ( U ^ { \prime } )
$$

with the property that $s ( g ) ( f v ) = g v$ for any $g \in \mathcal { I } ( V , U ^ { \prime } )$ and $v \in V$

Now $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ passes to a map $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathcal { S } } ( V , U ^ { \prime } )$ which we also write as $\pmb { \alpha }$ .For $W ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ , let $A _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] }$ denote $\varnothing$ unless $V ^ { \prime } \subset W ^ { \prime }$ , in which case $\pmb { A } _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] }$ denotes the pullback

$$
\begin{array} { l c r } { { A _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } \longrightarrow \bar { \sigma } ( W ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \bar { \sigma } ( W ^ { \prime } ) } } \\ { { \mid } } \\ { { \nonumber _ { \bar { A } \longrightarrow \bar { \sigma } ( V , U ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow \bar { \sigma } ( U ^ { \prime } ) . } } } \end{array}
$$

Thus when $V ^ { \prime } \subset W ^ { \prime }$ , ${ \cal A } _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] }$ is the set of all $a \in A$ such that $s ( a ) \in { \mathcal { O } } ( W ^ { \prime } )$ . Note that ${ \cal A } _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } \subset { \cal A } _ { V , W ^ { \prime } }$ and $\begin{array} { r } { A = \bigcup _ { W ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime } } A _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } } \end{array}$ .Let $\zeta ( \alpha ) _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] }$ denote the trivial bundle $A _ { \vert { V } , { W } ^ { \prime } ] } \times \left( W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } \right)$ Taking Thom spaces, we obtain a prespectrum $\mathcal { T } \alpha _ { [ V ] } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ with

$$
\mathcal { T } \alpha _ { [ V ] } V ^ { \prime } = T ( \zeta ( \alpha ) _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } ) \cong \Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } ( A _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } ) _ { + }
$$

ad the vius ucu aps.Let ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { \{ V \} }$ be the spectrification of ${ \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { [ V ] }$ Note that ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { [ V ] }$ is functorial in $_ \alpha$ and commutes with filtered colimits of spaces over $\pmb { \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ . We will show that 1) $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { [ V ] } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } A _ { + }$ and 2) $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { [ V ] } \cong \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V }$ , naturally in $_ \alpha$ .

For 1) we observe that ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { [ V ] } = \dim _ { K } { \mathcal { M } } ( \alpha | _ { K } ) _ { [ V ] }$ where the colimit is taken over all compact $K \subset A$ .Hence it suffices to establish 1) when $A$ is compact. But for compact $A$ , $A _ { \left[ V , W ^ { \prime } \right] } = A$ for $W ^ { \prime }$ large enough, so we see that ${ \mathcal { T } } \alpha _ { [ V ] }$ agrees cofinally with the desuspension prespectrum $\big \{ \Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } A _ { + } \big \}$ . The conclusion follows.

For we construct bundle morphisms $\zeta ( \alpha ) _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } \longrightarrow \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , W ^ { \prime } }$ . Recall that

$$
E ( \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , W ^ { \prime } } ) = \{ ( a , w ^ { \prime } ) \in A _ { V , W ^ { \prime } } \times W ^ { \prime } \mid w ^ { \prime } \perp \alpha ( a ) V \} .
$$

We define a map

$$
\theta _ { W ^ { \prime } } : A _ { [ V , W ^ { \prime } ] } \times ( W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow E ( \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , W ^ { \prime } } )
$$

by $\theta _ { W ^ { \prime } } ( a , w ^ { \prime } ) = ( a , s ( \alpha ( a ) ) ( w ^ { \prime } ) )$ . The properties of s ensure that these maps are well-defined and induce a map of prespectra $\theta : \pmb { \mathscr { T } } \alpha _ { [ V ] } \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { T } } \alpha _ { V }$ on passage to Thom spaces. To show that the resulting map of spectra $\theta : \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { [ V ] } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V }$ is an isomorphism, the colimit argument again lets us reduce the problem to the case when $A$ is compact. But for compact $A$ , with $W ^ { \prime }$ large enough so that $A _ { \left[ V , W ^ { \prime } \right] } = A$ , the bundle map $\theta _ { W } ,$ is an isomorphism. Therefore the map of prespectra $\theta : \mathfrak { T } \alpha _ { [ V ] } \longrightarrow \mathcal { T } \alpha _ { V }$ is cofinally a spacewise isomorphism, and the conclusion follows.

Observe that the isomorphism $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } A _ { + }$ depends on the choice of $f$ and of the section $\pmb { s } ^ { \prime }$ . The idea of exploiting such a section, although proposed in the context of a very different proof of the untwisting theorem, is due to Neil Strickland.

# 5. Twisted half-smash products and function spectra

DEfInITion 5.1. For $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ , $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ , and $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ , the twisted half-smash product $\alpha \ltimes E \in { \mathcal { S } } U ^ { \prime }$ is the spectrum ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha \wedge E$ and the twisted function spectrum $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) \in \mathcal { S } U$ is the spectrum $F ( \mathcal { M } \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ .

PRoPosiTIoN 5.2. The above definitions agree with the definitions of [38].

PRooF. Consider first the case of $A$ compact. Then for a given $V \subset U$ one can find $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ large enough that $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ Thus

$$
F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) V = \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong F ( T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) ,
$$

which is the definition of [38]. Therefore, by uniqueness of adjoints, the definitions of $\alpha \ltimes E$ agree for compact $A$ For general $A$ one simply observes that our constructions and the definitions of [38] behave "properly" with respect to colimits. Specifically,

$$
\alpha \ltimes E = \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { K } \alpha | _ { K } \ltimes E \quad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \quad F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { K } F [ \alpha | _ { K } , E ^ { \prime } ) ,
$$

where $\kappa$ runs through the compact subspaces of $A$ .

The difference between our approach and the approach of [38] is this: The definitions in [38] of $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $f [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ are simple and concrete when $A$ is compact, but they depend on arbitrary choices of cofinal sequences of indexing spaces in $U$ and $U ^ { \prime }$ . Hence naturality in $\alpha$ is difficult to prove. To get around this, the authors of [38] develop an elaborate theory of "connections" that allows them to show that the definitions of $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ are independent of the choices involved and, therefore, that the constructions are natural in $_ { \alpha }$ Only after proving all this is it possible to define $\alpha \ltimes E$ for non-compact $A$ as the relevant spectrum-level colimit and $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ as the limit. The advantage of our treatment is that the objects $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $F \{ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ are defined once and for all for arbitrary $A$ and naturality in $\alpha$ is immediate from the definitions.

We show in the following pair of results that twisted half-smash products and function spectra generalize change of universe functors and are in a sense built up out of them.

PROPOSITION 5.3. If $f : U \longrightarrow U ^ { \prime }$ is a linear isometry regarded as a map $* \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ then $f \ltimes E = f _ { \ast } E$ and $F [ f , E ^ { \prime } ) = f ^ { * } E ^ { \prime }$ , where $f _ { * } : \mathcal { S } U \longrightarrow$ ${ \mathcal { S } } U ^ { \prime }$ and $f ^ { * } : \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U$ are the standard change of universe functors. In particular, if $U = U ^ { \prime }$ and id denotes the identity map then i ${ \mathrm { ~ d ~ } } \ltimes E \cong E$ .

PRoOF. For any $V \subset U$ we have $T \alpha _ { V , f V } \cong \{ f \} _ { + } \cong S ^ { 0 }$ and thus $\mathcal { M } f _ { V } =$ $\Sigma _ { f V } ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 }$ Therefore

$$
\begin{array} { r } { F [ f , E ^ { \prime } ) V = \mathcal { S } \bar { U } ^ { \prime } ( \Sigma _ { f V } ^ { \infty } S ^ { 0 } , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong F ( S ^ { 0 } , E ^ { \prime } ( f V ) ) \cong E ^ { \prime } ( f V ) = ( f ^ { \ast } E ^ { \prime } ) V . } \end{array}
$$

The structure maps work properly and thus $F \lbrack f , E ^ { \prime } ) = f ^ { * } E ^ { \prime }$ . The fact that $f \ltimes E = f _ { \ast } E$ follows by the uniqueness of adjoints, or by an easy inspection.

One should think of $\alpha \ltimes E$ intuitively as the union over $a \in A$ of the spectra $\alpha ( a ) _ { * } E$ , suitably topologized. Similarly, $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ should be thought of as a suitably structured object that arises from the collection of spectra $\{ \alpha ( a ) ^ { * } E ^ { \prime } \}$ . More precisely, we have the following result [38, VI.2.7], which admits an easy proof in our setup.

PROPOsITION 5.4. Let $\alpha : \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be a map and let $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ and $\dot { E } ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ be spectra. $A$ map $\xi : \alpha \propto E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ determines and is determined by maps $\xi ( \alpha ) : E \longrightarrow \alpha ( a ) ^ { * } E ^ { \prime }$ for points $a \in A$ such that the functions

$$
\zeta _ { V . V ^ { \prime } } : T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \wedge E V \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime }
$$

specified by $\zeta _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } ( ( a , v ^ { \prime } ) \wedge y ) = \sigma ( \xi ( a ) ( y ) \wedge v ^ { \prime } )$ for $a \in A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }$ , $v ^ { \prime } \in V ^ { \prime } - \alpha ( a ) V .$ . and $y \in E V$ are continuous, where $\sigma$ denotes the structure map

$$
\sigma _ { \alpha ( a ) V , V ^ { \prime } } : \Sigma ^ { V ^ { \prime } - \alpha ( a ) V } E ^ { \prime } ( \alpha ( a ) V ) \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } .
$$

PROOF. Since for any $V \subset U , { \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { V }$ is the spectrification of $\mathcal { T } \alpha _ { V } = \{ T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \}$ we can write ${ \mathcal { M } } \alpha _ { V }$ as the spectrum level colimit

$$
\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } = \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime } } \ \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } .
$$

Therefore

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \alpha \ltimes E , E ^ { \prime } ) = \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { V \subset { U ^ { \prime } } } \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } ) } \\ & { \qquad = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V \subset { U ^ { \prime } } } \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } ) } \\ & { \qquad = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V \subset { U ^ { \prime } } } \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { V ^ { \prime } \subset { U ^ { \prime } } } \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } ) } \\ & { \qquad = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V \subset U } \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V ^ { \prime } \subset { U ^ { \prime } } } \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } ) } \\ & { \qquad = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V \subset U } \operatorname* { l i m } _ { V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime } } \mathcal { T } ( T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \wedge E V , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) . } \end{array}
$$

This gives the connection between maps of spectra $\alpha \propto E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ and families of maps $\zeta _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } : T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \land E V \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime }$ .

By restriction to points $a \in A$ , a given map $\xi : \alpha \ltimes E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ induces maps $\alpha ( a ) _ { * } E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ which are adjoint to maps $\xi ( a ) : E \longrightarrow \alpha ( a ) ^ { * } E ^ { \prime }$ . By projection from the double limit, $\xi$ induces the map $\zeta _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } : T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \land E V \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime }$ , which may be checked to have the claimed description.

Conversely, given maps $\xi ( a ) : E \longrightarrow \alpha ( a ) ^ { * } E ^ { \prime }$ satisfying the hypotheses, the maps $\zeta _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } : T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } { \wedge } E V \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime }$ specified by $\zeta _ { V . V ^ { \prime } } ( ( a , v ^ { \prime } ) \wedge y ) = \sigma ( \xi ( a ) ( y ) \wedge v ^ { \prime } )$ are easily checked to be compatible with the maps over which the double limit is taken, and therefore they specify a map of spectra $\alpha \propto E \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ .

The following untwisting theorem is an important sharpening of the result originally given in [38] and is vital to the theory of $S _ { \ l }$ -modules. The original proof of Elmendorf, Kriz, May, and Mandell, although not long or difficult, is rather technical in that it relies heavily on Elmendorf's category of spectra [20].

THEOREM 5.5 (UNTWISTING). Let $V \subset U$ and $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ be indexing spaces such that $V \cong V ^ { \prime }$ , let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be a map, and let $X$ be a based space. There are isomorphisms

$$
\alpha \ltimes \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X \cong A _ { + } \land \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } X
$$

and

$$
\Omega _ { V } ^ { \infty } F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong F ( A _ { + } , \Omega _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } E ^ { \prime } )
$$

that are natural in $\alpha$ and $X$ .

PRooF. By Proposition 3.9 there is an isomorphism ${ \mathcal { E } } \wedge \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X \cong { \mathcal { E } } _ { V } \wedge X$ that is natural in spaces $X \in \mathcal { T }$ and objects $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ Thus

$$
\alpha \ltimes \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X = \mathcal { M } \alpha \land \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X \cong \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \land X .
$$

By the untwisting lemma, $\mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } A _ { + }$ , so it follows that

$$
\alpha \ltimes \Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X \cong ( \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } A _ { + } ) \land X \cong A _ { + } \land \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } X .
$$

The isomorphism $\Omega _ { V } ^ { \infty } F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) \cong F ( A _ { + } , \Omega _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } E ^ { \prime } )$ follows by uniqueness of adjoints.

# 6. Formal properties of twisted half-smash products

We here give three basic formal properties of twisted half-smash products and function spectra. They have been used over and over in the text of the book. The first is an easy direct consequence of Proposition 3.7.

ProposItion 6.1. For a map $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ spectra $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ and $E ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ and a based space $X$ , there are natural isomorphisms

$$
( \alpha \times E ) \wedge X \cong \alpha \ltimes ( E \wedge X )
$$

and

$$
{ \cal F } [ \alpha , F ( X , E ^ { \prime } ) ) \cong F ( X , F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) ) .
$$

Moreover, if $Y$ is an unbased space and we denote by $\alpha \times Y$ the map

$$
A \times Y \ { \stackrel { \pi } { \longrightarrow } } \ A \ { \stackrel { \alpha } { \longrightarrow } } \ g ( U , U ^ { \prime } ) ,
$$

there are natural isomorphisms

$$
( \alpha \times Y ) \ltimes E \cong ( \alpha \ltimes E ) \land Y _ { + } \cong \alpha \ltimes ( E \land Y _ { + } ) .
$$

The following two results relate twisted half-smash products to the naturality properties of spaces of linear isometries.

PROPOSITION 6.2. Let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { g } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ and $\beta : B \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { g } } ( U ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime \prime } )$ be maps and let $\beta \times _ { c } \alpha$ denote the composite

$$
\begin{array} { r } { B \times A \xrightarrow { \beta \times \alpha } \mathcal { I } ( U ^ { \prime } , U ^ { \prime \prime } ) \times \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } ) \xrightarrow { c } \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime \prime } ) , } \end{array}
$$

where c denotes composition. Then there are isomorphisms

$$
( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) \ltimes E \cong \beta \ltimes ( \alpha \ltimes E )
$$

and

$$
F [ \beta \times _ { c } \alpha , E ^ { \prime \prime } ) \cong F [ \alpha , F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime \prime } ) )
$$

that are natural in $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ and $E ^ { \prime \prime } \in \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime \prime }$ .

PROPOSITION 6.3. Let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow { \pmb \mathscr { g } } ( U _ { 1 } , U _ { 1 } ^ { ' } )$ and $\beta : B \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U _ { 2 } , U _ { 2 } ^ { ' } )$ be maps and let $\alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta$ denote the composite

$$
A \times B \xrightarrow { \alpha \times \beta } \mathcal { I } ( U _ { 1 } , U _ { 1 } ^ { ' } ) \times \mathcal { I } ( U _ { 2 } , U _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ) \xrightarrow { \oplus } \mathcal { I } ( U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } , U _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus U _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ) .
$$

There is a natural isomorphism

$$
( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) \ltimes ( E _ { 1 } \wedge E _ { 2 } ) \cong ( \alpha \times E _ { 1 } ) \wedge ( \beta \ltimes E _ { 2 } ) ,
$$

where $\wedge$ denotes the external smash product of spectra.

Proof OF PRoposition 6.2. For $\mathcal { D } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime \prime } ; U ^ { \prime } )$ and $\pmb { \mathscr { E } } ^ { \circ } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ , we define the composition smash product $\mathcal { D } \wedge _ { c } \mathcal { E } \in \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime \prime } ; U )$ by $( \mathcal { D } \wedge _ { c } \mathcal { E } ) _ { V } = \mathcal { D } \wedge \mathcal { E } _ { V }$ . It is elementary to check that

$$
( \mathcal { D } \land _ { c } \pmb { \mathcal { E } } ) \land E \cong \mathcal { D } \land ( \pmb { \mathcal { E } } \land E )
$$

for $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ Thus it suffices to show that $\mathcal { M } ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) \cong \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge _ { c } \mathcal { M } \alpha$ .

We begin by constructing bundle morphisms

$$
\eta ( \beta ) _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \times \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow \eta ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } } .
$$

By definition,

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { \Xi ( \eta ( \beta ) _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } ) = \{ ( b , v ^ { \prime \prime } ) \in B _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \times V ^ { \prime \prime } \mid v ^ { \prime \prime } \perp \beta ( b ) V ^ { \prime } \} , } & \\ & { \quad E ( \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } ) = \{ ( a , v ^ { \prime } ) \in A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \times V ^ { \prime } \mid v ^ { \prime } \perp \alpha ( a ) V \} , } & \end{array}
$$

and

$$
E ( \eta ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } } ) = \{ ( b , a , v ^ { \prime \prime } ) \in ( B \times A ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \times V ^ { \prime \prime } \mid v ^ { \prime \prime } \perp \beta ( b ) \alpha ( a ) V \} .
$$

It is easily seen that $B _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \times A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \subset ( B \times A ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } }$ Consider the map

$$
E ( \eta ( \beta ) _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } ) \times E ( \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } ) \longrightarrow E ( \eta ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } } )
$$

that takes $( b , v ^ { \prime \prime } ) \times ( a , v ^ { \prime } )$ to $( b , a , v ^ { \prime \prime } + \beta ( b ) v ^ { \prime } )$ . Passing to Thom spaces, we obtain maps

$$
T \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow T ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } }
$$

which define a map of prespectra

$$
\Im { \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } } } \land T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow \Im ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V }
$$

and therefore a map of spectra

$$
\mathcal { M } \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V } .
$$

One checks that, for $V ^ { \prime } \subset W ^ { \prime }$ , the diagram

$$
\overbrace  \phantom { \left. \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { M \beta _ { V } , \wedge } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \right. } \longrightarrow \int _ { \mathbb { X } ( \beta \times \mathscr { c } _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V } } \longrightarrow \int _ { V } \beta _ { W ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } }
$$

commutes, where the top row is the composite isomorphism

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { M } \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \cong \Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } \mathcal { M } \beta _ { W ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } } \\ & { \qquad \cong \mathcal { M } \beta _ { W ^ { \prime } } \wedge \Sigma ^ { W ^ { \prime } - V ^ { \prime } } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \cong \mathcal { M } \beta _ { W ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , W ^ { \prime } } . } \end{array}
$$

Thus we obtain a map of spectra

$$
( \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge _ { c } \mathcal { M } \alpha ) _ { V } = \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime } } \ \mathcal { M } \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V } .
$$

One checks that these maps are compatible with the structure maps of our objects and so define a morphism $\theta : \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge _ { c } \mathcal { M } \alpha \longrightarrow \mathcal { M } ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha )$ in the category $\mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime \prime } ; U )$ .

To show that $\theta$ is an isomorphism, we invoke the familiar colimit argument. We have

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \pmb { \mathcal { M } } \beta \wedge _ { c } \pmb { \mathcal { M } } \alpha = \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { K , L } \ \pmb { \mathcal { M } } \beta | _ { L } \wedge _ { c } \pmb { \mathcal { M } } \alpha | _ { K } } \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \pmb { \mathcal { M } } ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) = \mathrm { c o l i m } _ { K , L } \ \pmb { \mathcal { M } } ( \beta \times _ { \mathrm { c } } \alpha ) | _ { L \times K } , } \end{array}
$$

where the colimits are taken over all compact $K \subset A$ and all compact $L \subset B$ . Thus it suffices to show that $\theta$ is an isomorphism when $A$ and $B$ are compact. In that case, for a fixed $V$ , we may choose $V ^ { \prime }$ large enough that $A _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } = A$ and we may then choose $V ^ { \prime \prime }$ large enough that $B _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } = B$ It follows that $( B \times A ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } } = B \times A$ and the map $T \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \longrightarrow T ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } }$ is a homeomorphism. Therefore

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { ( \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge _ { c } \mathcal { M } \alpha ) _ { V } = \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V } \cong \mathcal { M } \beta \wedge \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } } \\ & { \cong \mathcal { M } \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \cong ( \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } ) \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } } \\ & { \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime \prime } } ^ { \infty } T \beta _ { V ^ { \prime } , V ^ { \prime \prime } } \wedge T \alpha _ { V , V ^ { \prime } } \cong \Sigma _ { V ^ { \prime \prime } } ^ { \infty } T ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V , V ^ { \prime \prime } } } \\ & { \cong \mathcal { M } ( \beta \times _ { c } \alpha ) _ { V } . ~ \Omega } \end{array}
$$

Proof OF PRoposition 6.3. For $\mathcal { D } \in \mathcal { S } ( U _ { 1 } ^ { ' } ; U _ { 1 } )$ and $\mathcal { E } \in \mathcal { S } ( U _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ; U _ { 2 } )$ we define the external direct sum smash product $\mathcal { D } \hat { \setminus } _ { \oplus } \mathcal { E } \in \mathcal { S } ( U _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus U _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ; \bar { U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } } )$ by

$$
( \mathop { \mathcal { D } } \wedge _ { \oplus } \hat { \mathcal { O } } ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } } = \mathop { \mathcal { D } } _ { V _ { 1 } } \wedge \hat { \mathcal { O } } _ { V _ { 2 } } .
$$

Since the set $\{ V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } \}$ is cofinal in $U _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 }$ this specifies an object in the category $\mathcal { S } ( U _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus U _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ; \dot { U } _ { 1 } \oplus U _ { 2 } )$ . It is easily checked that, for spectra $E _ { i } \in \mathcal { S } U _ { \ i }$ ,

$$
( \neg \neg \neg \emptyset \land \emptyset ^ { \circ } ) \wedge ( E _ { 1 } \wedge E _ { 2 } ) \cong ( \emptyset \wedge E _ { 1 } ) \wedge ( \mathscr { E } \wedge E _ { 2 } ) .
$$

Thus it suffices to show that $\mathcal { M } \alpha \wedge _ { \oplus } \mathcal { M } \beta \cong \mathcal { M } ( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) ,$

By definition,

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { E ( \eta ( \alpha ) _ { V _ { 1 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } } ) = \{ ( a , v _ { 1 } ^ { ' } ) \in A _ { V _ { 1 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } } \times V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \mid v _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \bot \alpha ( a ) V _ { 1 } \} , } \\ & { } & { E ( \eta ( \beta ) _ { V _ { 2 } , V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } } ) = \{ ( b , v _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ) \in B _ { V _ { 2 } , V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } } \times V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } \mid v _ { 2 } ^ { ' } \bot \beta ( b ) V _ { 2 } \} , } \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array} { c } { E ( \eta ( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \oplus V _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } } ) = } \\ { \{ ( a , b , v _ { 1 } ^ { ' } , v _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ) \in ( A \times B ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \oplus V _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } } \times ( V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } ) \mid v _ { 1 } ^ { ' } + v _ { 2 } ^ { ' } \perp \alpha ( a ) V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus \beta ( b ) V _ { 2 } \} . } \end{array}
$$

It follows immediately that there is a bundle isomorphism

$$
\eta ( \alpha ) _ { V _ { 1 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } } \times \eta ( \beta ) _ { V _ { 2 } , V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } } \cong \eta ( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } } .
$$

Thus we have isomorphisms of Thom spaces

$$
T \alpha _ { V _ { 1 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } } \wedge T \beta _ { V _ { 2 } . V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } } \cong T ( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } , V _ { 1 } ^ { ' } \oplus V _ { 2 } ^ { ' } }
$$

and therefore an isomorphism of prespectra

$$
\mathcal { T } \alpha _ { V _ { 1 } } \wedge \mathcal { T } \beta _ { V _ { 2 } } \cong \mathcal { T } ( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } } .
$$

It follows that

$$
( \mathcal { M } \alpha \wedge _ { \oplus } \mathcal { M } \beta ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } } \cong \mathcal { M } \alpha _ { V _ { 1 } } \wedge \mathcal { M } \beta _ { V _ { 2 } } \cong \mathcal { M } ( \alpha \times _ { \oplus } \beta ) _ { V _ { 1 } \oplus V _ { 2 } } .
$$

# 7. Homotopical properties of $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$

We now turn our attention to the homotopy preservation properties of our constructions. It is evident from the definitions that $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ preserve homotopies in $E$ and $E ^ { \prime }$ and homotopies over ${ \pmb \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ in $\pmb { \alpha }$ However, it is vital to the theory that many homotopical properties of $\alpha \ltimes E$ and $F \{ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ depend only on the homotopical properties of the space $A$ .

ProposITIon 7.1. If A has the homotopy type of a CW complex, then the functor $\alpha \ltimes E$ preserves CW homotopy types in $\boldsymbol { \varepsilon }$ and the functor $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ preserves weak equivalences in $E ^ { \prime }$ .

ProoF. It is a standard categorical observation that the two assertions are equivalent since $\alpha \ltimes \mathrm { ~ - ~ }$ and $F [ \alpha , - )$ are an adjoint pair of functors. Let $E _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow E _ { 2 } ^ { \prime }$ be a weak equivalence in ${ \mathcal { S } } U ^ { \prime }$ Then each $E _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow E _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime }$ is a weak equivalence of spaces. Since $A$ has CW homotopy type, $F ( A _ { + } , E _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow F ( A _ { + } , E _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } )$ is a weak equivalence for any $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ . It follows from the untwisting theorem that $F [ \alpha , E _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow F [ \alpha , E _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } )$ is a spacewise weak equivalence and therefore a weak equivalence of spectra.

COroLlarY 7.2. If $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ is a map and $A$ has CW homotopy type then the functors $\alpha \ltimes \mathrm { ~ - ~ }$ and $F [ \alpha , - )$ pass to an adjoint pair of functors on the stable categories.

As always, a functor such as $\alpha \kappa E$ that does not preserve weak equivalences in $\boldsymbol { E }$ is defined on the stable category by first replacing $E$ with a CW approximation. We can strengthen Proposition 6.1 by obtaining a CW structure on $\alpha \ltimes E$ from CW structures on $A$ and $E$ .

PROPOSITION 7.3. Let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be a map, where A is a CW complex with skeletal filtration $\left\{ A ^ { n } \right\}$ . Let $\boldsymbol { E }$ be a CW spectrum with skeletal filtration $\left\{ E ^ { n } \right\}$ and sequential filtration $\left\{ E _ { n } \right\}$ . Then $\alpha \ltimes E$ is a CW spectrum with skeletal filtration

$$
( \alpha \ltimes E ) ^ { n } = \bigcup _ { p + q = n } ( \alpha | _ { A ^ { p } } ) \ltimes E ^ { q } , \ n \in \mathbb { Z } ,
$$

and sequential filtration

$$
( \alpha \ltimes E ) _ { n } = \bigcup _ { p + q = n } ( \alpha | _ { A ^ { p } } ) \ltimes E _ { q } , \ n \geq 0 .
$$

PRooF. We induct up the sequential filtration. Since $E _ { 0 } = *$ , $( \alpha \ltimes E ) _ { 0 } = \ast$ . Assume that $( \alpha \propto E ) _ { n - 1 }$ is a CW spectrum. Let $\varepsilon : D ^ { \mathfrak { p } } \longrightarrow A$ be a $p$ -cell with restriction $\sigma$ to $S ^ { p - 1 }$ . Let $C S ^ { r - 1 } \longrightarrow E _ { q }$ be an $\boldsymbol { r }$ -cell of $E _ { q }$ with attaching map $S ^ { r - 1 } \longrightarrow E _ { q - 1 }$ , where $p + q = n$ and $r \in \mathbb { Z }$ Then $( \alpha \ltimes E ) _ { n }$ is obtained from $( \alpha \ltimes E ) _ { n - 1 }$ by attaching "twisted cells" of the form $( \alpha \circ \varepsilon ) \ltimes C S ^ { r - 1 }$ along attaching maps

$$
\begin{array} { r } { ( \alpha \circ \varepsilon ) \ltimes S ^ { r - 1 } \cup ( \alpha \circ \sigma ) \ltimes C S ^ { r - 1 } \longrightarrow ( \alpha \ltimes E ) _ { n - 1 } . } \end{array}
$$

It follows from the untwisting theorem and inspection on the space level that the pair $( ( \alpha \circ \varepsilon ) \ltimes C S ^ { r - 1 } , ( \alpha \circ \varepsilon ) \ltimes S ^ { r - 1 } )$ is isomorphic to the pair $( C S ^ { p + r - 1 } , S ^ { p + r - 1 } )$ . Therefore $( \alpha \times E ) _ { n }$ is constructed from $( \alpha \ltimes E ) _ { n - 1 }$ by attaching genuine cells along cellular maps and is thus a CW spectrum.

Recall that a prespectrum $D$ is said to be $\Sigma$ -cofibrant if the structure maps $\Sigma ^ { W - V } D V \longrightarrow D W$ are cofibrations. A spectrum is $\Sigma \cdot$ nt  it i to $_ { L D }$ for some $\Sigma$ cofibrant prespectrum $\mathcal { D }$ A spectrum is tame if it is homotopy equivalent to a $\Sigma$ -cofibrant spectrum. Roughly speaking, tame spectra are to spacewise homotopy equivalences of spectra as spectra with CW homotopy type are to weak equivalences of spectra. Thus, if $D$ is tame and $f : E _ { 1 } \longrightarrow E _ { 2 }$ is a map of spectra such that each $f V : E _ { 1 } V \longrightarrow E _ { 2 } V$ is a homotopy equivalence, then

$$
f _ { * } : h \mathcal { S } U ( D , E _ { 1 } ) \longrightarrow h \mathcal { S } U ( D , E _ { 2 } )
$$

is a bijection. It follows formally that a spacewise homotopy equivalence of tame spectra is a genuine homotopy equivalence. Pursuing the analogy further, the cylinder construction $K E$ may be thought of as a $^ { 6 6 } \Sigma$ -cofibrant approximation" to the spectrum $E$ , and there is a map $K E \longrightarrow E$ that is a spacewise homotopy equivalence. Categorically, it is generally true that if ${ \cal L } : \mathcal { S } U \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ and $R : \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } \longrightarrow \mathcal { S } U$ are a left-right adjoint pair of functors, then $L$ preserves tameness if and only if $R$ preserves spacewise homotopy equivalences.

$$
F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) _ { * } : h \mathcal { S } U ( E , F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ) \longrightarrow h \mathcal { S } U ( E , F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) )
$$

is a bijection. It is now formal that $\phi \ltimes E$ is a homotopy equivalence.

COROLLARY 7.5. Let $\alpha _ { 1 } , \alpha _ { 2 } : A \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be maps. If $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ is tame, then $\alpha _ { 1 } \ltimes E$ and $\alpha _ { 2 } \ltimes E$ are homotopy equivalent. Since CW spectra are tame, this implies the following result.

CoROLLARY 7.6. If A has CW homotopy type, the functors αx - obtained by varying the map α are canonically and coherently equivalent upon passage to the stable category. Similarly the functors $F [ \alpha , - )$ are canonically and coherently equivalent as functors on the stable category.

# 8. The cofibration theorem

In this section we prove the following analog for cofibrations of Theorem 7.4. The original proof of Elmendorf, Kriz, May, and Mandell relied heavily on the properties of Elmendorf's category of spectra [20], using bundle theoretic arguments about the morphism sets in that category. We shall show that it actually follows formally from the untwisting theorem and some elementary homotopy theory.

THEOREM 8.1. Let $\phi : A \longrightarrow B$ be a cofibration, let $\beta : B \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be a map, and let $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ be the composite $\beta \circ \phi$ . If $E \in { \mathcal { S } } U$ is $\Sigma$ -cofibrant or is a CW spectrum, then ${ \phi } \ltimes { E } : { \alpha \ltimes { E } } \longrightarrow { \beta \ltimes { E } }$ is a cofibration.

PRooF. Consider a test diagram αE 20 (αE)^I+ >E βE i0 (βxE)I+

for which we must prove that the dotted arrow exists making the diagram commute. By adjunction, we may consider instead the test diagram

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { E \longrightarrow F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) } } \\ { { \imath _ { 0 } \Bigg \downarrow } } \\ { { E \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow \qquad \quad \qquad \qquad \quad \sqrt { F ( \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) } } } \end{array}
$$

and try to prove that $h$ exists. We are not claiming that $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) : F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow$ $F \{ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } )$ is a fibration of spectra, but we will show that it does have the covering homotopy property (CHP) with respect to $\Sigma \mathrm { \mathrm { \cdot } }$ -cofibrant spectra and CW spectra.

It is easily seen that $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ is a spacewise fibration. For if $V \subset U$ and $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ are such that $V \cong V ^ { \prime }$ , then $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ is equivalent to a map

$$
F ( \phi _ { + } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) : F ( B _ { + } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow F ( A _ { + } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) ,
$$

which is clearly a fibration. It follows now that $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ has the CHP with respect to spectra of the form $\Sigma _ { V } ^ { \infty } X$ for any $V \subset U$ . In particular, $F \lbrack \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ has the CHP with respect to sphere spectra and hence $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ may be thought of as a spectrum level Serre fibration. The standard arguments now show that $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ has the CHP with respect to CW spectra.

The argument for $\Sigma$ -cofibrant spectra is a bit more delicate. Let $E = L D$ where $D$ is a $\Sigma \mathrm { \mathrm { \cdot } }$ -cofibrant prespectrum. We want to use the cofibration condition on the structure maps $\Sigma ^ { W - V } D V \longrightarrow D W$ to construct the lift $h$ for the diagram of prespectra

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { D  F ( \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) } } \\ { { i _ { 0 } { \Bigg \downarrow } _ { { \Bigg . } } { - \frac { h ^ { * } } { g } - \frac { \mathcal { F } } { \sqrt { F ( \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) } } } { \Bigg . } } } \\ { { D \wedge I _ { + } - \frac { \mathcal { F } } { g } F ( \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) . } } \end{array}
$$

We do this by choosing a cofinal sequence $V _ { 0 } \subset V _ { 1 } \subset \ldots$ of representations in $U$ and then arguing that after having constructed the map $h V _ { i } : D V _ { i } \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow$ $F \{ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i } )$ we can construct the map $h V _ { i + 1 }$ in a compatible way so as to obtain a map of prespectra.

The problem reduces to the following: Define

$$
k : D V _ { i + 1 } \wedge \{ 0 \} _ { + } \cup \Sigma ^ { V _ { 1 + 1 } - V _ { 1 } } D V _ { i } \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i + 1 } )
$$

to be the pushout of the maps

$$
\begin{array} { r } { D V _ { i + 1 } \xrightarrow { f V _ { i + 1 } } F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i + 1 } ) } \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \Sigma ^ { V _ { * + 1 } - V _ { * } } D V _ { * } \wedge I _ { + } \xrightarrow { \Sigma ^ { V _ { * + 1 } - V _ { * } } h V _ { * } } \Sigma ^ { V _ { * + 1 } - V _ { * } } F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { * } ) \longrightarrow F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { * + 1 } ) . } \end{array}
$$

Then $h V _ { i + 1 } : D V _ { i + 1 } \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i + 1 } )$ must be a solution to the lifting problem

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { D V _ { \iota + 1 } \wedge \{ 0 \} _ { + } \cup \Sigma ^ { V _ { \iota + 1 } - V _ { \iota } } D V _ { \iota } \wedge I _ { + } \stackrel { k _ { * } } { \longrightarrow } F [ \beta , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i + 1 } ) } & { } \\ { \Big \downarrow } & { \qquad - \stackrel { h V _ { \iota + 1 , - } ~ \smile ~ \smile ~ } { \longrightarrow ~ } \Big \{ F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { \iota + 1 } ) } \\ { D V _ { \iota + 1 } \wedge I _ { + } \stackrel { \smile ~ \smile ~ \to ~ \dots ~ } { g V _ { \iota + 1 } } } & { \qquad \Rightarrow F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i + 1 } ) . } \end{array}
$$

By the untwisting theorem, if $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ is such that $V _ { i + 1 } \cong V ^ { \prime }$ then we can replace $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V _ { i + 1 } )$ with the map

$$
F ( \phi _ { + } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) : F ( B _ { + } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) \longrightarrow F ( A _ { + } , E ^ { \prime } V ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

The existence of the lift $h V _ { \imath + 1 }$ follows from our next lemma.

LEMMA 8.2. If $X \longrightarrow Y$ is a cofibration of based spaces, $A \longrightarrow B$ is a cofibration of unbased spaces, and $z$ is a based space, then any diagram of the form

$$
\begin{array} { r } { Y \wedge \{ 0 \} _ { + } \cup X \wedge I _ { + } \longrightarrow F ( B _ { + } , Z ) } \\ { \Big . \downarrow _ { \substack { X \wedge I _ { + } = - \sum ^ { - } F ( A _ { + } , Z ) } } \Big . } \end{array}
$$

can be completed by the dotted arrow.

Proor. By playing with adjunctions we can replace the above diagram with an equivalent diagram

$$
\begin{array} { r } { B _ { + } \wedge \{ 0 \} _ { + } \cup A _ { + } \wedge I _ { + } \xrightarrow { \dotsc } F ( Y , Z ) } \\ { \Big \downarrow _ { \substack { -  -  } } \Big . \Big . } \\ { B _ { + } \wedge I _ { + } \xrightarrow { \dotsc - f - } F ( X , Z ) . } \end{array}
$$

But this diagram of based spaces is equivalent to the diagram of unbased spaces

$$
\begin{array} { r } { B \times \{ 0 \} \cup A \times I \xrightarrow { \quad \longrightarrow F ( Y , Z ) } } \\ { \downarrow \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \downarrow } \\ { B \times I \xrightarrow { \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad } \quad F ( X , Z ) . } \end{array}
$$

The based fibration $F ( Y , Z ) \longrightarrow F ( X , Z )$ is also an unbased fibration and the solution now follows from the fact that $( B \times I , B \times \{ 0 \} \cup A \times I )$ is a DR pair.

# 9. Equivariant twisted half-smash products

As we have stated previously, all of our definitions and results on twisted half-smash products and function spectra generalize to the equivariant context with little change. In this section we discuss briefly the few exceptions to this assertion. More details will appear in [16].

The basic source material on categories of equivariant spectra is found in [38] and we briefly summarize that setup. For a compact Lie group $G$ ,a $G .$ . universe $U$ is defined to be a countably infinite dimensional real inner product space on which $G$ acts smoothly through linear isometries. We require that $\boldsymbol { U }$ contain a trivial representation and we require that whenever $V \subset U$ is a finite dimensional representation then $U$ must contain an isomorphic copy of $V ^ { \oplus \infty }$ . At the one extreme, a $G \mathrm { . }$ -universe is called complete if it contains copies of all the irreducible $G$ -representations; at the other extreme, a $G .$ -universe is called $G .$ -trivial if it contains only the trivial representations.

By a $G .$ -spectrum indexed on a $G \mathrm { . }$ -universe $U$ we mean a spectrum $E$ indexed on the finite dimensional subrepresentations of $\boldsymbol { U }$ such that each component space $\pmb { { \cal E V } }$ is a $G$ space and the structure maps $\Sigma ^ { W - V } E V \longrightarrow E W$ are $G \mathrm { . }$ maps. The resulting category is denoted $G \mathcal { S } U$ . Thus for non-isomorphic $G$ universes $U , U ^ { \prime }$ we have associated non-equivalent categories of $G$ -spectra that pass to non-equivalent equivariant stable categories.

For $G \mathrm { . }$ universes $U , U ^ { \prime }$ , we let $G$ act on ${ \pmb \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ by conjugation. For a $G .$ . map $\alpha : A \longrightarrow \pmb { \mathscr { s } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ and $G .$ spectra $E \in G { \mathcal { S } } U$ , $E ^ { \prime } \in G \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ there is a twisted half-smash product $\alpha \ltimes E \in G \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ and a twisted function spectrum $F [ \alpha , E ^ { \prime } ) \in G \mathcal { S } U$ whose definitions and properties are wholly analogous to the nonequivariant constructions. Sections 2 to 6 generalize in a straightforward way that requires little comment. The category $G \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ is defined in the evident way. In the equivariant version of Definition 3.2, for an object $\mathcal { E } ^ { \circ } \in G \mathcal { S } ( U ^ { \prime } ; U )$ and $G .$ spectrum $E ^ { \prime } \in G \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime }$ the $G \mathrm { . }$ space $F ( \pmb { \mathscr { e } } , E ^ { \prime } ) ( V ) = \mathcal { S } U ^ { \prime } ( \pmb { \mathscr { e } } _ { V } , E ^ { \prime } )$ must be understood to mean the space of all nonequivariant maps of spectra $\mathcal { E } _ { V } \longrightarrow E ^ { \prime }$ with $G$ acting by conjugation. The equivariant versions of the untwisting results, Lemma 4.5 and Theorem 5.5, are true, except that the existence of a $V ^ { \prime } \subset U ^ { \prime }$ isomorphic to the given $G$ indexing space $V \subset U$ is a non-trivial hypothesis that may not be satisfied.

Fix a copy of $\mathbb { R } ^ { \infty }$ in $U$ . It is a crucial fact [38, I.4.6] that for a map $f :$ $E _ { 1 } \longrightarrow E _ { 2 }$ of $G \mathrm { \mathrm { \Omega } }$ -spectra indexed on $U$ to be a spacewise weak equivalence of $G$ -spaces, it suffices that $f \mathbb { R } ^ { n } : E _ { 1 } \mathbb { R } ^ { n } \longrightarrow E _ { 1 } \mathbb { R } ^ { n }$ be a weak equivalence for all $\boldsymbol { n }$ . Since all $G$ -universes contain the trivial representations, the untwisting theorem applies to the trivial representations, and we see that the equivariant versions of Proposition 7.1 and Corollary 7.2 hold. The equivariant version of Proposition 7.3 only holds when $G$ is finite. In analogy with [38, I.4.6], it can be shown that in order for a map of $G$ -spectra to be a spacewise homotopy equivalence, it is sufficient to consider only the trivial representations. Hence the equivariant version of Theorem 7.4 holds. While $\pmb { \mathcal { I } } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ is not a $G \mathrm { . }$ contractible $G .$ space, it does have the property that if $A$ is a $G$ -CW complex then any two maps $\alpha _ { 1 } , \alpha _ { 2 } : A \longrightarrow \mathcal { I } ( U , U ^ { \prime } )$ are $G \mathrm { . }$ -homotopic. Hence the equivariant versions of Corollaries 7.5 and 7.6 hold.

The equivariant version of Theorem 8.1 holds in full generality for the case that $E$ is a $G$ -CW spectrum. The reason is that the sphere spectra relevant to a $G$ CW structure are of the form $S ^ { n } \wedge ( G / H ) _ { + }$ where $_ n$ is an integer. Hence the problem of lifting cells over the map $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } )$ only requires consideration of the maps $F [ \phi , E ^ { \prime } ) ( { \mathbb R } ^ { n } )$ for which the untwisting theorem applies. For general universes $U , U ^ { \prime }$ , if $E$ is tame, but not $G$ CW, I do not know whether the map ${ \phi } \ltimes { E } : { \alpha \ltimes { E } } \longrightarrow { \beta \ltimes { E } }$ must always be a cofibration. Our proof of Theorem 8.1, like the original proof given by Elmendorf, Kriz, May, and Mandell, works equivariantly only if we add the additional assumption that the universe $U ^ { \prime }$ is "as big or bigger" than $U$ in the sense that there exists a $G$ -linear isometry $U \longrightarrow U ^ { \prime }$ .
